      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>The Bulletin of the British Museum (Natural History), instituted in 1949, is issued in four<tei:lb/>scientific series, Botany, Entomology, Geology (incorporating Mineralogy) and Zoology, and<tei:lb/>an Historical series.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Papers in the Bulletin are primarily the results of research carried out on the unique and<tei:lb/>ever-growing collections of the Museum, both by the scientific staff of the Museum and by<tei:lb/>specialists from elsewhere who make use of the Museum's resources. Many of the papers are<tei:lb/>works of reference that will remain indispensable for years to come.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Parts are published at irregular intervals as they become ready, each is complete in itself,<tei:lb/>available separately, and individually priced. Volumes contain about 300 pages and several<tei:lb/>volumes may appear within a calendar year. Subscriptions may be placed for one or more of<tei:lb/>the series on either an Annual or Per Volume basis. Prices vary according to the contents of<tei:lb/>the individual parts. Orders and enquiries should be sent to:</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Publications Sales,</tei:p>
         <tei:p>British Museum (Natural History),<tei:lb/>Cromwell Road,</tei:p>
         <tei:p>London SW7 5BD,<tei:lb/>England.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>World List abbreviation: Bull. Br. Mus. nat. Hist. (Zool.)</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Trustees of the British Museum (Natural History), 1986</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The Zoology Series is edited in the Museum's Department of Zoology</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Keeper of Zoology : MrJ. F. Peake<tei:lb/>Editor of Bulletin : Dr C. R. Curds<tei:lb/>Assistant Editor : Mr C. G. Ogden</tei:p>
         <tei:p>ISBN 0565 0501 8 4</tei:p>
         <tei:p>ISSN 0007 1498 Zoology series</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Vol 50 No. 1 pp 1-57<tei:lb/>British Museum (Natural History)<tei:lb/>Cromwell Road<tei:lb/>London S W7 5BD Issued 24 April 1 986</tei:p>

      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>A revision of the genus Vorticella (Ciliophorp*<tei:lb/>Peritrichida)</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. Warren</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Department of Zoology, British Museum (Natural History), Cromwell Road, London</tei:p>
         <tei:p>2 4 APR 1986</tei:p>
         <tei:p/>
         <tei:p>Contents</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Introduction .</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Systematics</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Key to species .</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Species descriptions</tei:p>
         <tei:p>References</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Appendix (includes index of extant species and annotated list of nominal species)</tei:p>
         <tei:p>PRESENTED</tei:p>
         <tei:p>1<tei:lb/>1</tei:p>
         <tei:p>7<tei:lb/>11</tei:p>
         <tei:p>48<tei:lb/>52</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Introduction</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Anyone who has had experience with the identification of free-living ciliates will be familiar with<tei:lb/>the difficulty of distinguishing the species of Vorticella. Their plastic body shape, variable size and<tei:lb/>highly contractile nature has made them among the most difficult of ciliates to study and identify.<tei:lb/>This has resulted in the erection of numerous species and varieties many of which are of doubtful<tei:lb/>taxonomic status.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The last major revision of the genus Vorticella was that of Noland &amp; Finley (1931) who found<tei:lb/>in the literature over 200 names and descriptions. Many were clearly not congeneric, the majority<tei:lb/>being either rotifers or other protozoa but even after the rejection of these, some 94 nominal<tei:lb/>species still remained. Over the intervening 50 years another 113 species and varieties have been<tei:lb/>added giving a total of 209. With so many taxa, the descriptions of which are spread throughout<tei:lb/>the literature, the task of the ecologist trying to identify individual isolates has become particu-<tei:lb/>larly onerous even with the aid of identification keys such as those of Stokes (18850), Kahl (1935),<tei:lb/>Curds (1969), Stiller (1971) and Green (1974).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The aim of this paper is to provide both the ecologist and the specialist in peritrichs, with a<tei:lb/>functional classification of the species of Vorticella. For this purpose Noland &amp; Finley (1931)<tei:lb/>have been imitated in that a checklist of the 209 named taxa has been compiled and the taxo-<tei:lb/>nomic status of each determined. Drawings and descriptions of the extant species of Vorticella<tei:lb/>are given and a key to their identification has been constructed.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Systematics</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In the scheme adopted by the Committee on Systematics and Evolution of the Society of<tei:lb/>Protozoologists (Levine et al, 1980), based on the classification of Corliss (1979), the taxonomic<tei:lb/>position of the genus Vorticella is given as follows:</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Subkingdom: Protozoa Goldfuss, 1818 emend, von Siebold, 1845</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Phylum: Ciliophora Doflein, 1901</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Class: Oligohymenophora de Puy torac et al. , 1 974</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Subclass: Peritrichia Stein, 1859</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Order: Peritrichida Stein, 1859</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Suborder: Sessilina Kahl, 1933</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Family Vorticellidae Ehrenberg, 1838</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Genus: Vorticella Linnaeus, 1 767</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Bull. Br. Mus. not. Hist. (Zool.) 50(1): 1-57</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Issued 24 April 1986</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>2 A. WARREN</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Diagnosis</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The following list of characters serve to define the genus Vorticella. Body borne upon a contrac-<tei:lb/>tile stalk, always solitary although many species are gregarious. Zooids commonly inverted<tei:lb/>bell-shape but may also be spherical, cylindrical or cone-shape. Oral cilia in three rows, two inner<tei:lb/>(polykineties) and one outer (haplokinety) which wind counter-clockwise around the peristome<tei:lb/>when viewed from above. Somatic cilia absent in the normal adult cell. Each zooid contains a<tei:lb/>single macronucleus accompanied by a small, oval micronucleus. The macronucleus is typically<tei:lb/>long and sinuous. Impregnation by silver reveals a pattern of equally spaced horizontal lines<tei:lb/>which encircle the body. The stalk is oval in cross-section and contains a helically coiled contrac-<tei:lb/>tile myoneme called the spasmoneme which causes contraction to take place in a spiral not a<tei:lb/>zig-zag manner. Asexual reproduction is by binary fission with one cell remaining attached to the<tei:lb/>stalk while the other becomes the migratory telotroch which has an extra row of cilia, the aboral<tei:lb/>ciliary wreath, near the aboral pole. Sexual reproduction is by conjugation and involves total<tei:lb/>fusion of the mobile microconjugant with the sessile macroconjugant.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Taxonomic characters</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Traditionally the taxonomy of the vorticellae is based on the morphology of the living organism<tei:lb/>as revealed by light microscopical examination. The principal characters used are shown in<tei:lb/>Figure 1. In recent years special techniques such as silver impregnation, scanning and trans-<tei:lb/>mission electron microscopy (SEM &amp; TEM) have been increasingly used to study pellicular and<tei:lb/>sub-pellicular structures in greater detail (Hobbs &amp; Lang, 1964; Reid, 1967; Kawamura, 1973;<tei:lb/>Barlow &amp; Finley, 1976a; Foissner, 1979; Carey &amp; Warren, 1983). As a result the taxonomic<tei:lb/>importance of structures such as the pellicular striations, pores and tubercles has increased<tei:lb/>accordingly. Furthermore evidence is accumulating that ecological and behavioural factors such<tei:lb/>as host specificity (for epibionts), substrate preference, pseudocolony formation and telotroch<tei:lb/>swimming patterns may also be useful diagnostic characters (Nenninger, 1948; Barlow &amp; Finley,<tei:lb/>1976ft; Horikami &amp; Ishii, 1981). The most important taxonomic features are discussed below and<tei:lb/>it is recommended that as many of these as possible should be considered when describing a new<tei:lb/>isolate.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>BODY SHAPE. The outline form is the first and most readily recognisable feature of the Vorticella<tei:lb/>zooid. Although commonly inverted bell-shape, a range of body forms occur throughout the<tei:lb/>genus including species which are spherical (V. globularia, V. sphaerica), cylindrical ( V. aequilata,<tei:lb/>V. elongatd) and conical ( V. kenti, V. patellind). This variety of form introduces a problem of<tei:lb/>definition since the interpretation of body shape can be highly subjective: what is elongate to one<tei:lb/>observer can be ellipsoidal to another. Methods of quantifying body shape for more objective<tei:lb/>mathematical analyses were investigated by Roberts et al (1983). It should also be noted that the<tei:lb/>body contour of some species is readily changeable (e.g. V. inconstantans) and that nearly all<tei:lb/>vorticellae alter their shape as conditions change in a culture or in a drop under examination. In<tei:lb/>view of this only freshly isolated and healthy specimens should be used when describing body<tei:lb/>shape.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>SIZE. Size is usually expressed in terms of the overall length and maximum width of the zooid.<tei:lb/>The length is measured from the scopula, i.e. the stalk-zooid junction, to the upper rim of the<tei:lb/>peristomial lip and the width is measured at the widest part of the body below the peristome.<tei:lb/>There is often considerable variability within, and overlap between the various taxa in terms of<tei:lb/>size, and dimensions are frequently given as average values within defined ranges. Kahl (1935)<tei:lb/>has suggested that size ratios, such as zooid length: zooid width or peristomial lip width: zooid<tei:lb/>width, might be a more useful method of expressing these dimensions. Clearly, taken alone size<tei:lb/>does not constitute a particularly secure criterion of species but it does serve to reinforce other<tei:lb/>more distinctive features.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>COLOUR. There are three main sources of colour within the zooid; the cytoplasm, the contents of<tei:lb/>the food vacuoles and cytoplasmic inclusions. The first two were rejected by Noland &amp; Finley</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>A REVISION OF THE GENUS VORTICELLA</tei:p>
         <tei:p>\ \</tei:p>
         <tei:p>My</tei:p>
         <tei:p>_TB</tei:p>
         <tei:p>AWC</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 1. A generalised Vorticella: (a) zooid; (b) buccal infraciliature; (c) telotroch. AWC aboral wreath<tei:lb/>of cilia; CR.^3 1st, 2nd &amp; 3rd ciliary rows; CV contractile vacuole; E epistomial membrane; FR<tei:lb/>filamentous reticulum; FV food vacuole; G germinal row of kinetics; H haplokinety; I infundibulum;<tei:lb/>Ma macronucleus; Mi micronucleus; My myoneme; N nucleolus; P t _ 3 1st, 2nd &amp; 3rd peniculus; PD<tei:lb/>peristomial disc; PL peristomial lip; Po polykinety; S striations; Sh sheath; Sp spasmoneme; TB<tei:lb/>telotroch band; TG thecoplasmic granule.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>(1931) as being of little taxonomic significance since both are known to vary in colour according<tei:lb/>to ecological conditions and food source respectively. The cytoplasmic inclusions consist of<tei:lb/>endosymbiotic zoochlorellae, waste and reserve granules. Noland &amp; Finley (1931) recommended<tei:lb/>that species diagnoses should be established on grounds other than the presence of intracellular<tei:lb/>zoochlorellae pending further studies on the stability of the symbiotic relationship. Following<tei:lb/>their ultrastructural studies of two species of C/z/ore//a-containing Vorticella, Graham &amp; Graham<tei:lb/>(1978 &amp; 1980) concluded that the relationship is stable and that the presence of endosymbiotic<tei:lb/>zoochlorellae may be accepted as a reliable taxonomic character.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>PERISTOMIAL LIP. The peristomial lip, also referred to as the peristome border or bell margin, is<tei:lb/>the contractile rim that encompasses the anterior end of the cell. Upon contraction the lip con-<tei:lb/>stricts enclosing the peristomial disc and cilia which are withdrawn below. The peristomial lip<tei:lb/>usually takes the form of a simple rim but in some species, such as V. campanula, it projects from<tei:lb/>the periphery of the body in a shelf-like manner while in others it is ornamented by frill-like<tei:lb/>undulations (V. crater d), spine-like projections ( V. pulchrd) or tubercles and granules (V. vernalis,<tei:lb/>V. verrucosd).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The diameter, thickness and degree of eversion of the peristomial lip have a considerable<tei:lb/>influence on the overall appearance of the zooid and are commonly included in species descrip-<tei:lb/>tions. However, Noland &amp; Finley (1931) highlighted the variability of these parameters with</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>4 A. WARREN</tei:p>
         <tei:p>changing environmental conditions observing that 'nearly all vorticellae alter the degree of<tei:lb/>eversion and even the width of the border as conditions change in the surrounding medium'.<tei:lb/>They concluded that the variability of the lip was too great to justify placing much weight on it.<tei:lb/>Conversely, in a study of symphoriont peritrichs, Vavra (1961) found that the peristome<tei:lb/>remained constant over a range of environmental conditions. Clearly further studies on<tei:lb/>variability are required but it is generally accepted that the peristomial lip of a healthy, freshly<tei:lb/>mounted specimen constitutes a useful diagnostic feature.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>PERISTOMIAL DISC. The peristomial disc (epistomial disc) is the area at the apical end of the cell<tei:lb/>encircled by the rows of cilia. In some species (e.g. V. bivacuolata, V. elongata) the disc is typically<tei:lb/>arched high above the peristome whilst in others it is flattened or only slightly elevated. It has<tei:lb/>been reported that most species react to unfavourable conditions by becoming increasingly<tei:lb/>globular and developing a protruding disc (Noland &amp; Finley, 1931). Therefore the shape of the<tei:lb/>peristomial disc should only be described for healthy, freshly mounted specimens.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Silver staining reveals the presence on the disc of concentric rings of silver lines similar to those<tei:lb/>found on the rest of the zooid (Klein, 1926 &amp; 1927) although these have not been studied in<tei:lb/>detail. Ridges or undulations radiating from the centre to the periphery and pellicular secretions<tei:lb/>may also be present but these are considered to be transitory features of little taxonomic value<tei:lb/>(Noland &amp; Finley, 1931).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MACRONUCLEUS. Vorticella has a single macronucleus which is typically long and sinuous. The<tei:lb/>shape and position within the zooid of the macronucleus are useful diagnostic features although<tei:lb/>variation throughout the genus is limited. The two conformations most commonly found are J-<tei:lb/>and C-shaped. The J-shaped macronucleus lies longitudinally with respect to the major axis of<tei:lb/>the zooid, its upper arm curved horizontally across the peristome and its distal end bent upwards<tei:lb/>(Fig. 1). The C- or horse shoe-shaped macronucleus is often shorter and is usually situated in the<tei:lb/>central or anterior part of the zooid. Its orientation may be either longitudinal (e.g. V. longifilum,<tei:lb/>V.pulchrd) or horizontal (e.g. V. striata, V. communis) with respect to the major axis of the zooid.<tei:lb/>The length and degree of curvature of the C-shaped macronucleus vary considerably from short<tei:lb/>and only slightly curved (e.g. V. exilis) to highly elongate and curved in a ring-like manner (e.g.<tei:lb/>V. operculariformis). Some vorticellae have macronuclei which are irregular in shape (e.g. V.<tei:lb/>muralis, V. nutans) while in others (e.g. V. macrophya, V. quadrangularis) the macronucleus has<tei:lb/>yet to be described.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Macronuclei are readily demonstrated using standard nuclear stains such as the Feulgen<tei:lb/>Reaction (Mackinnon &amp; Hawes, 1961) and methyl green (Curds et al, 1983). The macronucleus is<tei:lb/>usually accompanied by a small, oval micronucleus. The micronucleus shows little variety among<tei:lb/>the different species and is difficult to observe in the living animal. Therefore it is not considered<tei:lb/>to be a particularly useful diagnostic feature.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>BUCCAL APPARATUS AND ciLiATURE (Fig. lb). Infraciliary buccal patterns, which have been<tei:lb/>extensively studied in most ciliate groups and often with far reaching taxonomic consequences,<tei:lb/>have received comparatively little attention among the peritrichs. A notable exception was the<tei:lb/>exhaustive study carried out by Lorn (1964) and it is from this work that much of our current<tei:lb/>knowledge is derived.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The ciliature of the adult Vorticella zooid is confined to the anterior end of the cell. It consists<tei:lb/>of three rows of cilia, two inner rows or polykineties (PO) and one outer row or haplokinety (H).<tei:lb/>These are situated around the edge of the peristomial disc where they perform a 1-1| turn of the<tei:lb/>peristome before plunging down into the infundibulum. Here the two separate, the PO passing<tei:lb/>directly downwards whereas the H follows the border of the infundibulum before sinking<tei:lb/>downwards. The PO and H are now 180 out of phase as they continue their counter-clockwise<tei:lb/>spiral down the funnel-shape infundibulum (Fig. lb). At the base of the infundibulum is the<tei:lb/>cytostome which leads to the cytopharynx. The cytopharynx is a non-ciliated tube-like structure<tei:lb/>not usually visible in the living animal although its length and position can be ascertained by<tei:lb/>watching food vacuoles pass through. The overall length and orientation within the cell of the<tei:lb/>infundibulum and cytopharynx are often included in species diagnoses. However, Lorn (1964)</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>A REVISION OF THE GENUS VORTICELLA</tei:p>
         <tei:p>a</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 2. The three types of ribbing between the striations (S) on the pellicle of Vorticella: (a) normal; (b)<tei:lb/>concave; (c) convex (b &amp; c, after Foissner &amp; Schiffmann, 1974).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>concluded that the uniformity of peritrich buccal structures limits their use in the differentiation<tei:lb/>of closely related species.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>CONTRACTILE VACUOLE (C.V.). Most vorticellae have a single c.v. and this is usually situated just<tei:lb/>beneath the peristome, close to the infundibulum. In some species a second c.v. is present and this<tei:lb/>is also located in the anterior part of the cell usually on the opposite side of the infundibulum to<tei:lb/>the first c.v. Thus while the position of the c.v.(s) is comparatively uniform throughout the genus,<tei:lb/>the number of c.v's per zooid is a useful diagnostic feature. It has also been suggested that the<tei:lb/>diameter and rate of pulsation of the c.v. may be valuable aids for distinguishing species (Noland<tei:lb/>&amp; Finley, 1931). However these parameters should only be considered in the light of the osmotic<tei:lb/>conditions of the surrounding medium.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>PELLICLE. The structure of the pellicle of Vorticella corresponds closely with that of other ciliates.<tei:lb/>It consists of a single plasma membrane overlaying a system of membrane-bound alveolar sacs<tei:lb/>which in turn lie between transversely orientated pellicular ridges (Kawamura, 1973). In many<tei:lb/>species these ridges are well developed and are clearly visible under the light microscope as<tei:lb/>transverse striations encircling the body. These striations, along with the pellicular pores which<tei:lb/>are also found all over the zooid surface, have become increasingly important in peritrich taxo-<tei:lb/>nomy in recent years. Special techniques such as silver staining and SEM have been employed to<tei:lb/>study pellicular structures in detail (Hobbs &amp; Lang, 1964; Small &amp; Ranganathan, 1970; Davidson<tei:lb/>&amp; Finley, 1972; Barlow &amp; Finley, 19760 &amp; b; Carey &amp; Warren, 1983). The distribution patterns<tei:lb/>formed by the pores and striations have been analysed biometrically (Reid, 1967; Foissner &amp;<tei:lb/>Schiffmann, 1974 &amp; 1975; Foissner, 1979 &amp; 1981).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Further variations in the pellicle occur in those areas between the striations. For the majority<tei:lb/>of vorticellae the pellicle in these areas follows the contour of the zooid when the animal is<tei:lb/>viewed in profile. However in some species the pellicle curves inwards while in others it bulges out<tei:lb/>(Fig. 2). Foissner &amp; Schiffmann (1974) referred to these two pellicle types as concave and convex<tei:lb/>respectively. The configuration of the pellicle between the striations, or 'ribbing', is determined<tei:lb/>by the shape of the underlying alveolar sacs.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Other pellicular structures which are rather less common and are found in relatively few species<tei:lb/>include tubercles (e.g. V, perlata), spines (V. voeltzkowi), mucus layers (V. rhabdophord) and<tei:lb/>membranous sacs (V. vestitd). Although the functions of these structures are generally not<tei:lb/>known, they are useful characters for recognising species.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>STALK. The stalk is probably the most characteristic structure of Vorticella. A comprehensive<tei:lb/>study of its ultrastructure was made by Randall &amp; Hopkins (1962) but basically it consists of an<tei:lb/>outer wall or sheath enclosing a fibrillar matrix which supports a contractile cord. The cord<tei:lb/>consists of a membrane-bound layer of cytoplasm, or 'thecoplasm', and placed excentrically<tei:lb/>within this is the contractile myoneme or 'spasmoneme'. The thecoplasm often contains numer-<tei:lb/>ous small refractile bodies called thecoplasmic granules which lie alongside the spasmoneme in<tei:lb/>longitudinally arranged spiral rows. When present, these granules are usually colourless but in<tei:lb/>two species (V. picta and V. rubristigmd) they are coloured either green or red. The function of the<tei:lb/>thecoplasmic granules is not known and in several species they are absent altogether.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>6 A. WARREN</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Stalk length has long been used as a diagnostic feature for species of Vorticella. However<tei:lb/>although stalk length does tend towards a modal value in many species, individual variation is<tei:lb/>too great to justify much taxonomic reliance. Other parameters which appear to be more con-<tei:lb/>sistently uniform are stalk width and the length of one complete turn of the spasmoneme spiral,<tei:lb/>i.e. the internode distance. Noland &amp; Finley (1931) found a direct mathematical relationship<tei:lb/>between the two, the internode distance being approximately ten times the stalk width.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The stalk sheath shows some variability within the genus. For most vorticelliae the sheath is<tei:lb/>untwisted when the stalk is fully extended but in a few species (e.g. V.flexuosa and V. gracilis) it is<tei:lb/>twisted and in some cases has an even greater spiral curvature than the contained spasmoneme.<tei:lb/>Occasionally the sheath may also have distinct rings or annulations (e.g. V. annulatd). According<tei:lb/>to Noland &amp; Finley (1931) these annuli represent points of cell division and are transitory<tei:lb/>features of little taxonomic value.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>TELOTROCHS. The telotroch is the free-swimming stage of the Vorticella life cycle and is formed as<tei:lb/>a result of asexual reproduction (binary fission). Telotrochs are usually cylindrical or cone-shape<tei:lb/>and possess an extra row of cilia, the aboral ciliary wreath, near the scopular region (Fig. Ic).<tei:lb/>They swim with the aboral end facing forwards and the peristome, which remains closed, facing<tei:lb/>backwards.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Although studied in relatively few species, telotrochs exhibit several potentially useful<tei:lb/>taxonomic characters, both morphological and behavioural. Morphological features of<tei:lb/>diagnostic value include size, shape and pellicular structures. Telotroch behaviour is even less<tei:lb/>well documented than morphology, a notable exception being Barlow &amp; Finley 's (19766) study of<tei:lb/>swimming patterns and settling activities of four Vorticella spp. It was noted that each of the four<tei:lb/>species had its own characteristic behaviour pattern.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>It has long been known that certain species of Vorticella form pseudocolonies while others<tei:lb/>remain solitary (Kent, 1880-1882; Spoon, 1974). Horikami &amp; Ishii (1981) reported that the adult<tei:lb/>zooids of pseudocolony-forming species such as V. convallaria, V. campanula and V. vestita<tei:lb/>secrete a transparent viscous compound which acts as an attractant to the telotrochs. As a result<tei:lb/>of this positive chemotactic response the telotrochs settle on the substrate close to the attached<tei:lb/>adult cells.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>CYSTS. Cysts and the processes of encystation and excystation have been described in detail for<tei:lb/>only one species, V. microstoma (Brand, 1923; Finley &amp; Lewis, 1960). The presence of cysts has<tei:lb/>been reported in few other species. Although Corliss &amp; Esser (1974) have highlighted the<tei:lb/>importance of cysts in peritrich ecology, our knowledge of the Vorticella cyst is meagre and its<tei:lb/>taxonomic application has yet to be explored.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>ECOLOGICAL FACTORS. It has long been recognised that different species of Vorticella often have a<tei:lb/>predilection for different ecological conditions (Kolkwitz &amp; Marsson, 1909; Noland, 1925).<tei:lb/>Various physico-chemical and biological factors have been identified as being of primary<tei:lb/>importance in determining the distribution of peritrichs. These include the concentration of<tei:lb/>organic pollution (BOD); food source; flow rate; substrate preference, and relationships with<tei:lb/>predators and parasites.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The ability of different species of Vorticella to tolerate different levels of organic pollution was<tei:lb/>used by Kolkwitz &amp; Marsson (1909) to form the basis of the first saprobic system for measuring<tei:lb/>water quality. Originally four saprobic zones were designated according to the species of<tei:lb/>Vorticella which predominate in each. More recently Sladecek (1971) defined the saprobic<tei:lb/>sequence of thirty-two species of Vorticella.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Of the other ecological factors food source (Curds &amp; Vandyke, 1966), flow rate (Zimmerman,<tei:lb/>1961), substrate preference (Sladeckova, 1962) and relationships with predators and parasites<tei:lb/>(Noland, 1925; Spoon, 1975) may all provide useful information to aid species diagnoses<tei:lb/>although in most cases these have yet to be studied in sufficient detail. Taken alone these factors<tei:lb/>would be a very insecure criteria of species but they may serve to reinforce other more distinctive<tei:lb/>features.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>

         <tei:p>Species descriptions</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The recognised species with their synonyms are given below.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>y.aequilataKahl, 1939</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DIAGNOSIS (Fig. 5a): zooid 40-50 um long x 20 um wide, peristomial lip 15um in diameter; peristomial<tei:lb/>region constricted giving zooid the appearance of a somewhat elongated barrel; peristomial disc bulges<tei:lb/>prominently above lip; pellicle clearly striated; macronucleus C-shaped and situated longitudinally along<tei:lb/>the axis of the body; one c.v. situated near the cytopharynx; stalk up to 200 um long.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>HABITAT. Freshwater and marine.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>REMARKS. For biometric analysis see Reid (1967).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>V. alba Fromentel, 1874</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DIAGNOSIS (Fig. 5b). Zooid 60-80 um long x 25 um wide, inverted bell-shaped and constricted below<tei:lb/>peristomial lip which measures 25 um across; disc convex; infundibulum reaches one-third body length;<tei:lb/>C.V. situated in upper one-third of zooid; macronucleus C-shaped and lies longitudinally in centre of body;<tei:lb/>pellicular striations not visible; stalk 180-300 um long.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>HABITAT. Freshwater, solitary.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>V. alpestris Foissner, 1979</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DIAGNOSIS (Figs 6a &amp; b). Zooid 35^45 urn long x 20 um wide; body constricted beneath peristomial lip<tei:lb/>which measures 20 um across; infundibulum nearly reaches centre of zooid; C.V. lies just above centre of<tei:lb/>body and empties into ventral wall of infundibulum; macronucleus J-shaped and situated longitudinally<tei:lb/>with respect to major axis of zooid; pellicle distinctly striated with concave ribbing between striations.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>HABITAT. Freshwater.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>REMARKS: Foissner's (1979) original description includes a biometric analysis.</tei:p>

Zooid 50 um long x 15 um wide, elongate and slightly constricted beneath well<tei:lb/>developed peristomial lip which measures 15-20um across; infundibulum short; C.V. situated in upper<tei:lb/>one-third of body; macronucleus C-shaped and lies longitudinally in centre of zooid; pellicular striations not<tei:lb/>visible but there are two characteristic transverse ridges on the pellicle and below each of these, the body<tei:lb/>becomes abruptly narrower; during contraction, these portions of the zooid overlap; stalk slender and up to<tei:lb/>50 um long.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>HABITAT. Freshwater or marine; solitary.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>44</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. WARREN</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 44. (a) V. telescopica (after Kent, 1880-82), bar = 25um; (b) V. telescopica, contracted (after<tei:lb/>Kahl, 1935); (c) V. telescopoides (after Sramek-Husek, 1948), bar = 25 um.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>V. telescopoides (Kahl, 1935) Sramek-Husek, 1948<tei:lb/>V. telescopica Kahl, 1935</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DIAGNOSIS (Fig. 44b). Zooid elongate 55-70 um long x 17-25 urn wide; peristomial lip narrow, 15-20 urn in<tei:lb/>diameter; disc flat or slightly convex; c.v. situated just beneath peristome; macronucleus short, C-shaped<tei:lb/>and situated in upper one-third of zooid; pellicle striated; two transverse pellicular ridges are present on<tei:lb/>lower \ of zooid and upon contraction the area of the zooid above each overlaps that below.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>HABITAT. Freshwater.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>REMARKS. V. telescopoides is the name given by Sramek-Husek (19486) to the animal which Khal (1935)<tei:lb/>isolated in Hamburg and tentatively identified as "V. telescopiaT . Sramek-Husek (19486) redescribed this<tei:lb/>species from specimens collected from Czechoslovakian rivers.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>V. turgicula Wang Jiaji, 1977</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DIAGNOSIS (Fig. 45a). Zooid 60-70 um long x 52-64 um wide, the middle portion being distinctly swollen;<tei:lb/>disc dome-shaped and prominently elevated above peristomial lip which measures 32-42 um in diameter;<tei:lb/>one c.v. situated just beneath the peristome close to the infundibulum; macronucleus C-shaped and lies<tei:lb/>transversely across centre of zooid; pellicle faintly striated; stalk 180-315 um long.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>HABITAT. Freshwater, originally found attached to aquatic mosses.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>V. venusta Nenninger, 1948<tei:lb/>V. rotunda var. asellicola Stiller, 1953</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DIAGNOSIS (Fig. 45b &amp; c). Zooid 36 um long x 25-30 um wide, inverted bell-shaped but not constricted<tei:lb/>beneath peristomial lip which measures 30 um in diameter; disc convex; c.v. lies in upper one-third of body<tei:lb/>in the niche formed by the C-shaped macronucleus; pellicle distinctly striated and has convex ribbing<tei:lb/>between striations; stalk up to 250 urn long; upon contraction the peristomial lip is drawn up in the shape of<tei:lb/>a narrow cylinder (Fig. 45c).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>HABITAT. Freshwater, originally isolated as an epibiont on the crustacean Asellus sp.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>A REVISION OF THE GENUS VORTICELLA</tei:p>
         <tei:p>45</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 45. (a) V. turgicula (after Wang Jiaji, 1977), bar = 25um; (b) V. venusta relaxed zooid; (c)<tei:lb/>contracted zooid (after Nenninger, 1948), bar = 25 um.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>y. vernalis Stokes, 1887</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DIAGNOSIS (Fig. 46a). Zooid 50 urn long x 30 urn wide, elongated trumpet-shaped with no constriction<tei:lb/>beneath well developed peristomial lip which measures 35 um across; disc prominently arched above<tei:lb/>peristome; two C.V.'s situated in upper one-third of zooid; macronucleus long, C-shaped and lies longi-<tei:lb/>tudinally with respect to major axis of body; pellicular striations only visible in lower one-third of zooid;<tei:lb/>pellicle covered with irregularly arranged tubercles and granules; stalk up to 350 um long.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>HABITAT. Freshwater ponds.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>y. verrucosa Dons (1915), 1918</tei:p>
         <tei:p>V. singularis Kahl, 1933</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DIAGNOSIS (Fig. 46b). Zooid 60 um long x 55 um wide, conical in shape and with no constriction beneath<tei:lb/>peristomial lip which measures 60 um across; C.V. situated in upper one-third of body; macronucleus<tei:lb/>J-shaped with proximal region lying horizontally across peristome; pellicle is furnished with numerous<tei:lb/>randomly arranged tubercles of various sizes; pellicular striations not visible; stalk 8-0 um wide and up to<tei:lb/>40 um long.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>HABITAT. Marine.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>y. vestita Stokes, 1883</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Pseudovorticella vestita (Stokes, 1883) Jankowski, 1976<tei:lb/>V. chlamydophora Penard, 1922</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DIAGNOSIS (Fig. 47a). Zooid 50-75 um long x 40-50 urn wide, inverted bell-shaped and not constricted<tei:lb/>beneath peristomial lip which measures 55 um across; disc flat and slightly elevated above peristome;<tei:lb/>infundibulum reaches two-thirds body length; C.V. situated in upper one-third of zooid; macronucleus</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>46</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A. WARREN</tei:p>
         <tei:p>ig. 46. (a) V. vernalis (after Stokes, 1887), bar = 25mn; (b) V. verrucosa (after Kusters, 1974),</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 47. (a) V. vestita (after Stokes, 1883), bar = 25 |im; (b) V. voeltzkowi cyst; (c) zooid (after King,<tei:lb/>1931), bar = 25 urn.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>A REVISION OF THE GENUS VORTICELLA 47</tei:p>
         <tei:p>lies longitudinally with respect to major axis of body; pellicle finely striated; entire zooid covered by<tei:lb/>transparent, membranous investment which is divided into compartments; stalk up to 400 um long.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>HABITAT. Freshwater, often forming large pseudocolonies.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>REMARKS. According to Noland &amp; Finley (1931) V. vestita has two C.V.'s but in his original description,<tei:lb/>Stokes (1883) indicated that only one C.V. is present.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>V. voeltzkom Sondheim, 1929</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Pseudovorticella voeltzkowi (Sondheim, 1929) Jankowski, 1976<tei:lb/>V. echina King, 1933</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DIAGNOSIS (Fig. 47b). Zooid 30-40 um long x 30-40 um wide, somewhat rotund, but slightly elongated<tei:lb/>distally and constricted below peristomial lip which measures 1 5-20 urn across; disc flat; infundibulum short<tei:lb/>and broad; C.V. situated in upper one-third of body; macronucleus short, thick, C-shaped and lies<tei:lb/>longitudinally in centre of zooid; pellicle covered in short, pointed projections, 3-0-4-0 um long; pellicular<tei:lb/>striations not visible; stalk up to 140 um long.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>HABITAT. Freshwater.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Addendum</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Four new species of Vorticella were described by Banina (1983) from activated sludge at the<tei:lb/>Experimental Aeration Station, Petrodvorets, Old Peterhof, USSR. These are:</tei:p>
         <tei:p>V. aerotenci Banina, 1983</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DIAGNOSIS Zooid 90-140 um long x 32-60 urn wide, elongate with upper part cylindrical and lower part<tei:lb/>rounded; not constricted beneath peristomial lip, the diameter of which is greater than the maximum body<tei:lb/>width; disc convex; single large c.v. situated in upper one-third of zooid; macronucleus C-shaped and lies<tei:lb/>longitudinally with respect to major body axis; cytoplasm granular; pellicle finely striated; stalk thick and up<tei:lb/>to 140 um long.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>V. geispicae Banina, 1983</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DIAGNOSIS Zooid 6B-122 um long x 24-48 um wide, upper part cylindrical and lower part cone-shaped; not<tei:lb/>constricted beneath peristomial lip, the diameter of which is slightly greater than the maximum body width;<tei:lb/>two c.v.'s situated in upper part of body; macronucleus slender and elongate with the upper end curved<tei:lb/>across the peristome and the lower end extending longitudinally down the body; pellicle finely striated;<tei:lb/>bundles of fibrils conspicuous in lower part of zooid radiating from scopula; stalk somewhat shorter than<tei:lb/>body length.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>V.peterhoffi Banina, 1983</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DIAGNOSIS Zooid 62-80 um long x 35-46 um wide, inverted bell-shaped and held at an angle on stalk; con-<tei:lb/>stricted beneath peristomial lip, the diameter of which is less than the maximum body width; disc slightly<tei:lb/>convex; infundibulum lies horizontally across upper part of zooid; single c.v. situated just below peristome;<tei:lb/>macronucleus thick, irregular or dumb-bell shaped and lies either obliquely or longitudinally with respect to<tei:lb/>major body axis; pellicular striations not observed; stalk length greater than zooid length.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>V. hyalina Banina, 1983</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DIAGNOSIS Zooid 96-1 00 um long x 48-54 urn wide, inverted bell-shaped and constricted beneath peri-<tei:lb/>stomial lip, the diameter of which is equal to the maximum body width; disc flat; single c.v. situated in<tei:lb/>upper one-third of zooid; macronucleus slender and J-shaped; cytoplasm clear and transparent; pellicular<tei:lb/>striations not observed; stalk slender and several times longer than its body.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Appendix 1</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Index of extant species; annotated list of nominal species.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The following list is composed of those species which were not rejected by Noland &amp; Finley<tei:lb/>(1931) together with the 113 species and varieties described since. Haplocaulis Precht, 1935 and<tei:lb/>Pseudovorticella Foissner &amp; Schiffmann, 1974, two genera which closely resemble Vorticella,<tei:lb/>have been erected since Noland &amp; Finley 's review and many species previously belonging to<tei:lb/>Vorticella have been transferred to these two. Instances of synonymy, homonymy and the<tei:lb/>rejection of taxa due to a lack of sufficient information are also indicated.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>V. abbreviata (Keiser, 1921) Kahl, 1935 was regarded as a distinct species by both Kahl (1935), and Foissner</tei:p>
         <tei:p>&amp; Schiffmann (1974), but is considered here to be a variant of V. infusionum.<tei:lb/>V. aequilata Kahl 1935 (p. 1 1).<tei:lb/>V. alba Fromentel, 1874 was erroneously moved to the genus Haplocaulis by Stiller (1971). It appears to be a</tei:p>
         <tei:p>valid species of Vorticella (p. 1 1).</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>A REVISION OF THE GENUS VORTICELLA 53</tei:p>
         <tei:p>V. aldabrandina Kaas, 1921 was so poorly figured and described that it cannot be recognised as a distinct</tei:p>
         <tei:p>species. It therefore is considered to be a nomen dubium.<tei:lb/>V. alpestris Foissner, 1979 (p. 1 1).<tei:lb/>V. amphitricha Schmarda, 1854 was described from a specimen in the process of telotroch formation. In the</tei:p>
         <tei:p>absence of a detailed description of a normal vegetative cell, it is now considered to be a nomen dubium.<tei:lb/>V. amphiurae Cuenot, 1891 was described as having a straight spasmoneme which does not coil up in spiral</tei:p>
         <tei:p>fashion when it contracts. It should be therefore transferred to the genus Haplocaulis<tei:lb/>V. anabaenae Stiller, 1940 was transferred to the genus Haplocaulis by Stiller (1971).<tei:lb/>V. annulata (Gourret &amp; Roeser, 1888) Kahl, 1935 was originally thought to be a variant of V. brevistyla but</tei:p>
         <tei:p>was elevated to the rank of species by Kahl (1935) (p. 13).<tei:lb/>V. anomala Gourret &amp; Roeser, 1886 (p. 13).<tei:lb/>V. aperta Fromentel, 1874 (p. 13).<tei:lb/>V. artemiae Lepsi, 1965. No diagram was given with this poorly described freshwater form which now</tei:p>
         <tei:p>becomes a nomen dubium.<tei:lb/>V. astyliformis Foissner, 1981 (p. 13).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>V. banatica Lepsi. The original reference could not be located but Stiller (1971) gives a detailed description</tei:p>
         <tei:p>and diagram of what appears to be a valid species (p. 14).<tei:lb/>V. bidulphae Stiller, 1939 (p. 14).<tei:lb/>V. bivacuolata Fukui &amp; Morishita, 1961 (p. 14).<tei:lb/>V. bosminae Sramek-Husek, 1948 (p. 15).<tei:lb/>V. brevistyla d'Udekem, 1864 was transferred to the genus Pseudocarchesium by Stiller (1971).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>V. calciformis Kahl, 1933 (p. 15).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>V. campanula Ehrenberg, 1831 (p. 15).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>V. campanula f. monilata Stiller, 1963 has on its pellicle rows of regularly arranged tubercles and is therefore</tei:p>
         <tei:p>transferred to the genus Pseudovorticella.<tei:lb/>V. campanula f. minor Stiller, 1953 is an epizooic variant of V. campanula and is not sufficiently distinct to be</tei:p>
         <tei:p>known by a separate name.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>V. campanula f. citrina var. minor Stiller, 1953 = V. campanula.<tei:lb/>V. campanulata (Kahl, 1933), Sramek-Husek, 1948. This species was redescribed and renamed by</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Sramek-Husek (1948) (p. 17).<tei:lb/>V. carinogammari Stiller, 1953. Although redescribed by Piesik (1975) under that name it had already been</tei:p>
         <tei:p>transferred to the genus Haplocaulis by Stiller (1971).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>V. chinensis Tai, 1931 strongly resembles V. convallaria but may be distinguished by its spheroidal macro-<tei:lb/>nucleus. However, Kirby (1942) has shown that Vorticella macronuclei also typically assume a</tei:p>
         <tei:p>round-oval shape TFparasitised by bacteria. Therefore V. chinensis is considered to be a parasitised</tei:p>
         <tei:p>V. convallaria.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>V. chlamydophora Penard, 1922 appears to be identical to V. vestita Stokes, 1883.<tei:lb/>V. chlorellata Stiller, 1940= V. chlorostigma Ehrenberg, 1831.<tei:lb/>V. chlorostigma Ehrenberg, 1831 (p. 18).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>V. chydoricola Sramek-Husek, 1946 is an epizooic variant of V. microstoma.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>V. citrina Miiller, 1773 is, according to Faure-Fremiet (1904), a physiological variant of V. convallaria.<tei:lb/>V. citrina var. turgescens Stiller, 1931 = V. convallaria.<tei:lb/>V. claparedei Andrussowa, 1886. The mode of contraction of this species suggests that it should be</tei:p>
         <tei:p>transferred to the genus Haplocaulis.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>V. claudicans Penard, 1922 was transferred to the genus Haplocaulis by Stiller (1971).<tei:lb/>V. coeni Lepsi, 1948 was so poorly figured and described that it cannot be recognised as a valid species. It</tei:p>
         <tei:p>therefore becomes a nomen dubium.<tei:lb/>V. color ata Mereschkowsky, 1877 was distinguished on the basis of its pink cytoplasm but, according to</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Noland &amp; Finley (1931), colour alone is insufficient for the separation of species. In the absence of other</tei:p>
         <tei:p>distinguishing features this species is now considered to be a nomen dubium.<tei:lb/>V. communis Fromentel, 1874 (p. 18).<tei:lb/>V. conica Dons, 1915. Kahl (1935) renamed this species V. obconica in order to solve the problem of</tei:p>
         <tei:p>homonymy with Stoke's (18876) V. conica.<tei:lb/>V. conosoma Stokes, 1889 was described by Gajewskaja (1933) who indicated that the spasmoneme is</tei:p>
         <tei:p>straight and that upon contraction the stalk folds rather than coils. Therefore this species is transferred to</tei:p>
         <tei:p>the genus Haplocaulis.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>V. constricta Fromentel, 1 874 = V. microstoma.<tei:lb/>V. constricta Kahl, 1933 = V. campanulata.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>54 A. WARREN</tei:p>
         <tei:p>V. constricta Gajewskaja, 1933 is clearly not a Vorticella since it forms colonies. Therefore, this species is</tei:p>
         <tei:p>transferred to the genus Zoothamnium and in order to prevent synonymy with Z. constricta it is renamed</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Zoothamnium gajewskajum nom. nov.<tei:lb/>V. convallaria Linnaeus, 1758 (p. 18).<tei:lb/>V. convallaria var. compacta Nenninger, 1948 is an epizooic form which only differs from V. convallaria by</tei:p>
         <tei:p>the arrangement of its food vacuoles. This feature was rejected by Noland &amp; Finley (1931) as a basis for</tei:p>
         <tei:p>separating species.<tei:lb/>V. costata (Sommer, 1951) Foissner, 1979 was originally thought to be a variety of V. octavo but biometric</tei:p>
         <tei:p>studies by Foissner (1979) have shown that it is a distinct species (p. 19).<tei:lb/>V. crassicaulis Kent, 1881 was transferred to the genus Paravorticella by Kahl (1935), and subsequently to</tei:p>
         <tei:p>the genus Haplocaulis by Stiller (1971).<tei:lb/>V. crassicaulis Nenninger, 1948 appears to be identical to V. longifilum Kent, 1881. Synonymising these two</tei:p>
         <tei:p>also solves the problem of homonymy with the previous species.<tei:lb/>V. crater a Kent, 1881 is the name which Kent applied to Ehrenberg's (1838) V. patellina. However, Kent's</tei:p>
         <tei:p>animal is quite unlike any other species of Vorticella and V. cratera is now designated as a distinct species</tei:p>
         <tei:p>(p. 20).<tei:lb/>V. cupifera Kahl, 1935 appears to be identical to V. microstoma. Pratt &amp; Rosen (1983) have also remarked</tei:p>
         <tei:p>on the similarity of these two species.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>V. cyclopicola Kahl, 1935 is probably an epibiotic variety of V. microstoma.<tei:lb/>V. cyclopis Kahl, 1933 is a marine form which closely resembles V. convallaria.<tei:lb/>V. cylindrica Dons, 1915 appears to be a marine variety of V. campanula.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>V. delicatulata Biernacka, 1963 is probably a marine -variety of V. hamata.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>V. difficilis Kahl, 1933 was transferred to the genus Pseudovorticella by Jankowski (1976).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>V. dimorpha Stiller, 1940 (p. 20).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>V. dipneumon Penard, 1922 was transferred to the genus Haplocaulis by Stiller (1971).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>V. dubia Fromentel, 1874 was so poorly described and figured that it cannot be recognised as a distinct</tei:p>
         <tei:p>species. It therefore becomes a nomen dubium.<tei:lb/>V. dudekemi Kahl, 1933 = V. cratera.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>V. echina King, 1933 = V. voeltzkowi Sondheim, 1929.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>V. eforiana Tucolesco, 1962. In the original diagram this species is shown as having a short, straight</tei:p>
         <tei:p>spasmoneme. It should therefore be transferred to the genus Haplocaulis.<tei:lb/>V. elegans Dons, 1915 was transferred to the genus Intranstylum by Nenninger (1948), and later to the genus</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Haplocaulis by Stiller (1971).<tei:lb/>V. elongata Fromentel, 1874 (p. 20).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>V. ephemerae Kahl, 1935 is an epizooic form which closely resembles V. convallaria.<tei:lb/>V. epizoica Sramek-Husek, 1948 was transferred to the genus Haplocaulis by Stiller (1971).<tei:lb/>V. exilis Nenninger, 1948 (p. 21).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>V. extensa Kahl, 1935 was transferred to the genus Haplocaulis by Sommer (1951).<tei:lb/>V. extensa var. macronucleata Nenninger, 1948 was transferred to the genus Haplocaulis by Stiller (1971).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>V.fasciculata Mxiller, 1773 is distinguished principally on the basis of its green colouration, a character</tei:p>
         <tei:p>rejected by Noland &amp; Finley (1931) for the separation of vorticellae. This is now considered to be a nomen</tei:p>
         <tei:p>dubium.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>V.flexuosa Nenninger, 1948 (p. 21).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>V.fluvialis Fromentel, 1874 is a morphological variant of V. microstoma.<tei:lb/>V.floridensis Stokes, 1886 closely resembles and is probably synonymous with V. kenti (Kent, 1881) Kahl,</tei:p>
         <tei:p>1935.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>V.fromenteli Kahl, 1935 (p. 21).<tei:lb/>K/MSOZ Precht, 1935 (p. 22).<tei:lb/>V.fusca Biernacka, 1963. This species has a short, straight spasmoneme and should therefore belong to the</tei:p>
         <tei:p>genus Haplocaulis. This also solves the problem of homonymy with the previous species.<tei:lb/>V.fusiforma Nenninger, 1948 was transferred to the genus Haplocaulis by Sommer (1951).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>V. globosa Ghosh, 1922 (p. 22).<tei:lb/>V. globularia Muller, 1773 (p. 22).<tei:lb/>V. gracilis Dujardin, 1841 (p. 23).<tei:lb/>V. granulata Kahl, 1933 (p. 23).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>V. halophila Stiller, 1941 (p. 23).<tei:lb/>V. hamata Ehrenberg, 1831 (p. 25).</tei:p>

In other respects V. intermissa closely resembles V. pyriforme Stiller, 1939<tei:lb/>with which it is here synonymised.</tei:p>

      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>The littorinid molluscs of mangrove forests in the<tei:lb/>Indo-Pacific region</tei:p>
         <tei:p>D. G. Reid</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Periwinkles (family Littorinidae) are amongst the most intensively studied of marine gastropods,<tei:lb/>because of their worldwide distribution and abundance in the intertidal environment. This<tei:lb/>monograph is a comprehensive account of the taxonomy, biology and biogeography of the<tei:lb/>'Littorina scabra ' species complex, a hitherto poorly-known group of littorinids ubiquitous in<tei:lb/>mangrove habitats throughout the Indo-Pacific region. Twenty species are recognized, where<tei:lb/>most previous authors have distinguished only three. Detailed accounts of the anatomy of each<tei:lb/>species are given, with particular attention to the reproductive system, by which the species can<tei:lb/>be reliably distinguished. A key to shells is provided, and 100 figures and plates illustrate the<tei:lb/>range of shell variation, anatomical characters and geographical distribution of each species.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Drawing on comparisons with ten littorinid genera, evolutionary trends in the morphology of<tei:lb/>male and female reproductive tracts, sperm nurse cells, egg capsules, reproductive modes and<tei:lb/>radulae are discussed. These features are assessed as taxonomic characters, and the large<tei:lb/>literature on the morphology of the family is reviewed. Cladistic analysis of anatomical<tei:lb/>characters supports a reclassification of the Littorinidae, including placement of the 'scabra'<tei:lb/>group in the genus Littoraria.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In addition to molluscan systematists, this monograph should be of interest to marine<tei:lb/>biogeographers and ecologists working in the mangrove environment. In recent years much<tei:lb/>ecological and genetical research has been stimulated by the discovery of sibling species of<tei:lb/>Littorina in Europe. The Littoraria species have a similar potential in the Indo-Pacific, where up<tei:lb/>to ten species may occur sympatrically. Several of the species are polymorphic for shell colour<tei:lb/>and have already been used as material for the study of mechanisms of natural selection.<tei:lb/>1986, 240pp, 99 illustrations, 1 colour plate. 565 00978 8 35.00.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>

         <tei:p>By B. Brewster</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A taxonomic revision of the Southern Arabian Enidae sensu lato (Mollusca:<tei:lb/>Pulmonata). By P. B. Mordan</tei:p>

         <tei:p>The Bulletin of the British Museum (Natural History], instituted in 1949, is issued in four<tei:lb/>scientific series. Botany, Entomology, Geology (incorporating Mineralogy) and Zoology and<tei:lb/>an Historical series.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Papers in the Bulletin are primarily the results of research carried out on the unique and<tei:lb/>ever-growing collections of the Museum, both by the scientific staff of the Museum and by<tei:lb/>specialists from elsewhere who make use of the Museum's resources. Many of the papers are<tei:lb/>works of reference that will remain indispensable for years to come.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Parts are published at irregular intervals as they become ready, each is complete in itself,<tei:lb/>available separately, and individually priced. Volumes contain about 300 pages and several<tei:lb/>volumes may appear within a calendar year. Subscriptions may be placed for one or more of<tei:lb/>the series on either an Annual or Per Volume basis. Prices vary according to the contents of<tei:lb/>the individual parts. Orders and enquiries should be sent to:</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Publications Sales,</tei:p>
         <tei:p>British Museum (Natural History),<tei:lb/>Cromwell Road,</tei:p>

         <tei:p>A revision of the Suctoria (Ciliophora,<tei:lb/>Kinetofragminophora) 4. Podophrya and its<tei:lb/>morphological relatives</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Colin R. Curds</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Zoology Department, British Museum (Natural History), Cromwell Road, London SW7 5BD</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Contents</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Introduction 59</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Genus Podophrya 59</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Genus Parapodophrya 82</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Genus Mucophrya 87</tei:p>
         <tei:p>References 88</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Index to species 91</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Introduction</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The species of the two well-known genera Podophrya Ehrenberg, 1833 and Sphaerophrya<tei:lb/>Claparede and Lachmann, 1859 and the two lesser known Parapodophrya Kahl, 1931 and<tei:lb/>Mucophrya Gajewskaja, 1933 are the subjects for consideration in this the fourth part of the<tei:lb/>series. It will immediately be noticed that the genus Sphaerophrya has here been treated as a<tei:lb/>synonym of Podophrya. The two genera were originally distinguished by the presence of a stalk in<tei:lb/>Podophrya and the absence of one in Sphaerophrya. However it has long been known that the<tei:lb/>stalk is often lost by several species of Podophrya and apparently some Sphaerophrya species<tei:lb/>occasionally develop one. The presence of a cyst with a stalk has also been used as a distinguish-<tei:lb/>ing character but it should be appreciated that this is based on few observations and a similar<tei:lb/>stage may yet be found in Sphaerophrya. Another possible method of distinguishing the two<tei:lb/>genera investigated by the present author was based on the observation that the ciliary rows in<tei:lb/>Podophrya species are arranged transversely while those in many traditional Sphaerophrya<tei:lb/>species are arranged longitudinally. However examination of the description of bud formation in<tei:lb/>the type species Podophrya fixa Ehrenberg, 1833 by Penard (1920) clearly shows that while the<tei:lb/>kinetics are initially transverse they become longitudinally orientated as the bud elongates. It is<tei:lb/>clear therefore that there is no stable character by which to distinguish the two genera and under<tei:lb/>such circumstances they must be synonymised, indeed Corliss (1979) indicated that such a step<tei:lb/>might be necessary in his taxonomic review of the ciliates.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Genus PODOPHRYA Ehrenberg, 1833</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Sphaerophrya Claparede and Lachmann, 1 859<tei:lb/>Kystopus Jankowski, 1967</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The morphology of Podophrya is simple in comparison to many other suctoria and several<tei:lb/>features indicate that they could be regarded as primitive. Most are simple spheres with an even<tei:lb/>distribution of capitate tentacles radiating out from the body. Some are without a stalk and float<tei:lb/>freely in the water while others are attached by means of a stalk to vegetation and inanimate<tei:lb/>objects, only rarely are they found attached to aquatic arthropods. Several species have become<tei:lb/>endo- or ectoparasites of ciliated protozoa and in some cases the species may only be dis-<tei:lb/>tinguished by its association with a particular host. However, it should be noted that the degree</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Bull. Br. Mus. nal. Hist. (Zool.) 50(2): 59-91 Issued 26 June 1986</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>60</tei:p>
         <tei:p>COLIN R. CURDS</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 1. Podophrya fixa: (a,b) adult and cyst after Collin, 1912; (c) after Fukui &amp; Morishita, 1962<tei:lb/>(called P. variabile); (d) after Ghosh, 1929 (called P. bengalensis); (e) after Sand, 1899 (called P.<tei:lb/>brevipoda); (f,g,h) budding and transformation of adult into motile form after Penard, 1920; (ij,k)<tei:lb/>adult, embryo and cyst after Collin, 1912.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>of specificity of these parasites has not been determined and some may be further synonymised as<tei:lb/>more information becomes available.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The relatively primitive nature of Podophrya is shown by its method of reproduction which, in<tei:lb/>contrast to the other genera so far considered (Curds, 19850, b, c), is by a type of external<tei:lb/>budding known as 'pseudo-scissiparite' (Batisse, 1975). In this type of budding the mother cell</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>PODOPHRYA 61</tei:p>
         <tei:p>enlarges and divides across the cell as is the normal method of division in ciliates but here a field<tei:lb/>of kinetosomes migrates from the mother to become the ciliary bases of the motile bud (see Fig.<tei:lb/>If)- The cilia initially develop as transverse rows but in some cases these may later develop into<tei:lb/>longitudinal rows because of elongation by the bud in that axis. Some species have been reported<tei:lb/>to have the ability for the whole cell to transform itself (Fig. Ig, h) into a motile bud-like form<tei:lb/>which is rather reminiscent of telotroch formation in the peritrichs and presumably plays a simi-<tei:lb/>lar role in enabling the organism to move away from one attachment site to another if necessary.<tei:lb/>Cyst formation is well documented in several species of Podophrya. Cysts are generally stalked<tei:lb/>and heavily ribbed and the numbers of ribs have been used as criteria for species identification.<tei:lb/>Multiple fission of the suctorian within the cyst has been reported in Podophrya.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Diagnosis of Podophrya</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Typically freshwater, rarely marine, suctorians whose outline shape varies from spherical to<tei:lb/>ovoid. Lorica absent, some species borne upon a stalk but this may often be absent. Some species<tei:lb/>free-living, others are parasitic in or on other ciliates. Free-living species usually attached to<tei:lb/>aquatic vegetation or inanimate objects and only rarely to invertebrates. Tentacles reduced in the<tei:lb/>parasitic species but are capitate, long and numerous in the free-living ones. Tentacles distributed<tei:lb/>all over body, not in fascicles. Actinophores absent. Cysts sometimes stalked and often heavily<tei:lb/>ribbed. Reproduction by external buds (division into parts), buds ciliated with 3-12 rows of cilia<tei:lb/>that initially develop transversely. In some cases the ciliary rows become transformed into<tei:lb/>longitudinal rows as the bud elongates. Buds usually with rudimentary tentacles. Whole cell may<tei:lb/>convert into a motile bud-like state.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Species descriptions of Podophrya<tei:lb/>Podophryafixa (Muller, 1786) Ehrenberg, 1833</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Trichodafixa Muller, 1786<tei:lb/>Peritricha cometa Bory, 1825<tei:lb/>Actinophrys pedicellata Dujardin, 1841<tei:lb/>Orcula trochas Weisse, 1 847 (the cyst)<tei:lb/>Actinophrys difformis Perty, 1852<tei:lb/>Actinophrys sol Stein, 1 854 pro pane<tei:lb/>Podophrya brevipoda Sand, 1899<tei:lb/>Podophrya bengalensis Ghosh, 1929<tei:lb/>Podophrya variabile Fukui and Morishita, 1962</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION (Fig. 1). This the type species is a small to medium (50-75 um diameter), freshwater<tei:lb/>or marine, aloricate suctorian that is spheroidal in shape. Typically free-living but there is one</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>PODOPHRYA</tei:p>
         <tei:p>63</tei:p>
         <tei:p>report (Penard, 1920) of it as an endoparasite of the hypotrichous ciliate Stylonychia mytilus<tei:lb/>although perhaps it could have been P. grelli. Often, but not always, borne upon a stalk that is<tei:lb/>variable in length but usually about the same length as the body diameter. Tentacles capitate,<tei:lb/>variable in length, radiating out from all over surface of body. Free-living forms attached to<tei:lb/>vegetation and inanimate objects. Macronucleus ovoid and centrally located. Single contractile<tei:lb/>vacuole. Adult has ability to totally transform into a ciliated bud-like form (Fig. If) enabling it to<tei:lb/>swim away from its stalked attachment to a more favourable site.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Reproduction by pseudo-scissiparity producing a bud that is approximately the same size as<tei:lb/>the mother cell with about 12 longitudinal rows of cilia and several rudimentary tentacles. Well<tei:lb/>developed stalked cyst produced with 4 prominent transverse ribs. Fission can occur within cyst<tei:lb/>to produce up to 4 daughter cells (Fig. Ib).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Podophrya amoeboides (Sand, 1899) n. comb.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Trichophrya amoeboides Sand, 1 899<tei:lb/>Trichophrya variabilis Sand, 1899</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION (Fig. 2). Small (15-40um long), freshwater or marine, aloricate suctorian that is<tei:lb/>oval, lozenge, pyriform or irregular with lobes. Body able to slowly change its shape. Free-living,<tei:lb/>without stalk, found amongst algae and hydroid colonies. Tentacles capitate, of variable length<tei:lb/>and radiating out from all over the body surface. Movement is achieved by means of tentacles<tei:lb/>and body changes. One tentacle is extended forward and attaches itself distally. It then contracts<tei:lb/>and pulls the entire organism forward. The action apparently deforms the body by elongating<tei:lb/>that part of the cytoplasm to which the contracting tentacle is attached, although Penard (1920)<tei:lb/>states that this species can also move by means of its body in a manner comparable to leucocytes.<tei:lb/>Central spherical macronucleus, single anterior or central contractile vacuole. Reproduction and<tei:lb/>budding not described but Penard (1920) saw a bud, which he suspected to derive from this</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 2. Podophrya amoeboides: (a,b) after Sand, 1899 (called Trichophrya amoeboides); (c,d) after<tei:lb/>Penard, 1920 (called Trichophrya variabilis); (e,f,g) after Sand, 1899 (called Trichophrya variabilis).</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>64 COLIN R. CURDS</tei:p>
         <tei:p>species, that was an elongated ovoid covered in ciliary rows. Cyst formation also thought to<tei:lb/>occur (Penard, 1920).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>NOTE. Since Penard (1920) discovered T. variabilis in salt water there seems to be no reason for<tei:lb/>distinguishing between it and T. amoeboides. The latter mentioned species takes precedence since<tei:lb/>it was described on an earlier page than T. variabilis.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Podophrya atypica (Gonnert, 1935) n. comb.<tei:lb/>Parapodophrya atypica Gonnert, 1935</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION (Fig. 3). Small (10 urn diameter), freshwater, aloricate suctorian that is highly<tei:lb/>irregular in shape. Ectoparasitic on the ciliate Colpoda. Tentacles prominently capitate, length<tei:lb/>about three times body diameter, bases greatly expanded. One tentacle firmly embedded into<tei:lb/>cytoplasm of host cell. Ovoid macronucleus and single contractile vacuole centrally positioned.<tei:lb/>Mode of reproduction and bud morphology undescribed.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>NOTE. Until the bud and budding are fully described the true taxonomic position for this species<tei:lb/>will be unknown. However it was thought more advisable to transfer it to Podophrya since all<tei:lb/>other species of Parapodophrya are free-living and the similarity in tentacles was not considered<tei:lb/>to be sufficient to retain it in the latter genus.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 3. Podophrya atypica after Gonnert, 1935 (called Parapodophrya atypica).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Podophrya canelli (Clement, 1967) n. comb.<tei:lb/>Sphaerophrya canelli Clement, 1967</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION (Fig. 4). Small (10-25 um diameter), freshwater, spherical, aloricate suctorian.<tei:lb/>Endoparasite of the hypotrichous ciliates Euplotes eurystomus and E. patella, usually located in<tei:lb/>the peristomial depression of the host cell. Stalk absent. Some short capitate tentacles present<tei:lb/>randomly distributed over body surface. Central spherical macronucleus, single contractile<tei:lb/>vacuole. Reproduction by pseudo-scissiparity. Bud ovoid with 4 or 5 parallel longitudinal rows<tei:lb/>of cilia. Cyst not formed.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>NOTE. Originally described by Canella (1957) but not given a specific name.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Podophryaflexilis Kellicott, 1887<tei:lb/>Tokophryaflexilis(Ke\\icott, 1887) Butschli, 1889</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION (Fig. 8). Small (25-50 urn diameter), freshwater, ovoid, aloricate suctorian. Free-<tei:lb/>living, borne upon a stalk that is shorter than the body diameter.          <tei:p>Podophrya gretti Dieckmann, 1985</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION (Fig. 11). Small (25-50 jam diameter), freshwater, spherical, aloricate suctorian.<tei:lb/>Free-living or endoparasitic in the hypotrichous ciliate Stylonychia lemnae in which it lies just<tei:lb/>beneath the surface in cytoplasmic pockets that remain open to the external environment.<tei:lb/>Parasite is apparently host-specific. Few short capitate tentacles when inside host. Ciliated buds<tei:lb/>produced by unequal to equal exogenous budding. Conjugation of parasite occurs after degener-<tei:lb/>ation of host. Stalked cysts with 5 ribs similar to those of P.fixa are produced outside host after<tei:lb/>conjugation. The final rib in P. grelli is directed posteriorly (Fig. 1 1) towards the stalk while that<tei:lb/>of P. fixa is directed posterio-laterally (Figs Ib, Ik). Multiple fission within cyst produces<tei:lb/>infective buds that are indistinguishable from buds of the asexual generation. Buds or swarmers<tei:lb/>small (15-20 um long), oval with few rudimentary tentacles. There are 6-7 kinetics and a bunch<tei:lb/>of posterior caudal cilia.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>NOTE. This may be the suctorian parasite seen by Penard (1920) and thought to be P.fixa which<tei:lb/>is similar but not usually parasitic.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION (Fig. 14). Medium (50-80 urn diameter), freshwater, spherical, aloricate suctorian.<tei:lb/>Endoparasite of the ciliate Bursaria truncatella. Stalk and cyst absent. Several short capitate<tei:lb/>tentacles randomly distributed over body surface. Centrally located macronucleus. Reproduction<tei:lb/>by pseudo-scissiparity producing daughters that possess tentacles but not cilia. Synchronous<tei:lb/>total transformation of daughter cells into motile forms called 'trophotomites' enables the infes-<tei:lb/>tation to be transmitted from host to host. Young trophotomites with above five transverse<tei:lb/>ciliary rings elongate into mature laterally flattened oval tomites with longitudinal kinetics and<tei:lb/>some rudimentary tentacles.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION (Fig. 29). Small (10 urn long), freshwater, ovoid, aloricate suctorian. Free-living,<tei:lb/>borne upon stalk that may be branched to form a colony. Stalks two or three times length of<tei:lb/>body. Tentacles restricted to anterior body surface, few, 4-12, and capitate. Several contractile<tei:lb/>vacuoles. Reproduction and buds not described.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>NOTE. This is a most doubtful species, as there are no other records of a branched stalk in the<tei:lb/>suctoria and it could be suspected that the organisms seen were perhaps not suctorians but<tei:lb/>peritrichous ciliates.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 29. Podophrya spenceri: (a,b) colonial forms (called Tokophrya sp. 1); (c) solitary form (called</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Tokophrya sp. 2). All after Spencer, 1917.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Podophrya stentoris (Maupas, 1881) n. comb.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Sphaerophrya stentoris Maupas, 1881<tei:lb/>Sphaerophrya stentorea Sand, 1901</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION (Fig. 30). Small (35-45 um diameter), freshwater, spherical, aloricate suctorian.<tei:lb/>Parasitic in Stentor roeseli and S. coeruleus. Adult with few short tentacles. Centrally located<tei:lb/>spherical macronucleus, single eccentric contractile vacuole. Reproduction by pseudo-<tei:lb/>scissiparity. Ovoid bud with anterior transverse ciliary rows, several posterior tentacles<tei:lb/>contractile vacuoles and is prominently waisted by the presence or two transverse grooves.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Podophrya stokesii (Mamaeva, 1979) n. comb.<tei:lb/>Sphaerophrya stokesii Mamaeva, 1979</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION (Fig. 31). Small (30-35 um diameter), freshwater, spherical, aloricate suctorian.<tei:lb/>Ectoparasitic on the exterior of the ciliate Stokesia vernalis. Capitate tentacles distributed all over<tei:lb/>surface of body. Spherical macronucleus, single marginal contractile vacuole. Reproduction by<tei:lb/>pseudo-scissiparity.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Podophrya urostylae (Maupas, 1881) Jankowski, 1963</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Sphaerophrya urostylae Maupas, 1881<tei:lb/>Sphaerophrya stylonychiae Kent, 1 882<tei:lb/>Podophrya stylonychiae Foissner, 1980</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION (Fig. 32). Small (30-40 urn diameter), freshwater, spherical, aloricate suctorian.<tei:lb/>Free-living or endoparasitic in hypotrichous ciliates such as Urostyla grandis and Stylonychia.<tei:lb/>Few short capitate tentacles distributed all over surface of body. Central spherical macronucleus,<tei:lb/>single marginal contractile vacuole. Adult also able to live outside host when it is attached to<tei:lb/>inanimate objects by means of a stalk. Reproduction by pseudo-scissiparity. Buds ciliated by<tei:lb/>about six closely packed longitudinal rows of cilia and there are several rudimentary tentacles<tei:lb/>distributed over the bud. Stalked cyst, approximately ovoid with 4 or 5 transverse ridges.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Dubious Species</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Podophrya tortuosa Dons, 1918</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION (Fig. 33). Small (25 urn long), marine organism that is potentially a suctorian. Only<tei:lb/>the cyst has been described and for that reason it cannot be certain that it is ciliate of any kind.<tei:lb/>Cyst ovoid, anterior pointed, posterior flattened with a rim by which it is attached to the marine<tei:lb/>polychaete worm Spirorbis. Cyst striated obliquely. Single contractile vacuole.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Genus PARAPODOPHRYA Kahl, 1931</tei:p>
         <tei:p>While members of this genus are superficially similar to certain species of Podophrya there is a<tei:lb/>fundamental character that separates the two genera. In Parapodophrya an elongated bud carry-<tei:lb/>ing an anterior corona of transverse ciliary rows develops by pseudo-scissiparity which is quite</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>PODOPHRYA 83</tei:p>
         <tei:p>unlike anything produced by Podophrya. Furthermore all Parapodophrya species are free-living<tei:lb/>since no. parasitic podophryid capable of producing the typical Parapodophrya bud has been<tei:lb/>described. In some cases the tentacles may widen towards their bases but this should not be<tei:lb/>regarded as evidence on its own that the species in question is a member of the above genus.<tei:lb/>However in most other respects the two genera resemble each other quite closely since their<tei:lb/>modes of reproduction, cysts and abilities for total transformation into a free-swimming bud-like<tei:lb/>state are similar.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Diagnosis of Parapodophrya</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fresh to brackish-water suctorians whose outline shape is typically spherical. Lorica absent,<tei:lb/>some species borne upon a stalk but this may often be absent. Free-living, usually attached to<tei:lb/>aquatic vegetation or inanimate objects. Never parasitic. Tentacles capitate and frequently widen<tei:lb/>markedly towards their base which may give body a serrated to star-like appearance. Tentacles<tei:lb/>distributed all over body, not in fascicles. Actinophores absent. The only cyst that has been<tei:lb/>described is stalked and heavily ribbed. Reproduction by pseudo-scissiparity, buds ciliated by a<tei:lb/>corona of several transverse rows of cilia. Bud typically elongated, wider at the anterior end that<tei:lb/>bears the ciliary rows. Some buds with rudimentary tentacles in the posterior body half. Whole<tei:lb/>cell may convert into a motile bud-like state.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Key to Species of Parapodophrya</tei:p>
         <tei:p>1 Tentacles noticeably widen at base 2</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Tentacles do not noticeably widen at base 4</tei:p>
         <tei:p>2 Without stalk, bases of tentacles very wide giving body a star-like appearance ... P. sparganium</tei:p>
         <tei:p>With stalk, tentacle bases only moderately wide 3</tei:p>
         <tei:p>3 Macronucleus spherical, zooid spherical P. soliformis</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Macronucleus lemon-shaped, zooid pyriform P.palmigera</tei:p>
         <tei:p>4 Tentacles simply capitate 5</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Tentacle ends broadly spatulate P. typha</tei:p>
         <tei:p>5 Zooid covered in short denticle-like projections P. denticulata</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Zooid without surface projections</tei:p>
         <tei:p>With stalk P. soliformis</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Without stalk P.nigricans</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Species descriptions of Parapodophrya</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Parapodophrya soliformis (Lauterborn, 1908) Kahl, 1931</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Sphaerophrya sol Lauterborn, 1901 non Mechnikov, 1864<tei:lb/>Sphaerophrya soliformis Lauterborn, 1908<tei:lb/>Podophrya soliformis Penard, 1918</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION (Fig. 34). This the type species is a small to medium (40-1 00 um diameter),<tei:lb/>freshwater, spherical, aloricate suctorian. Capitate tentacles numerous, projecting from all over<tei:lb/>body surface, fine at the tips and widening towards their bases giving the cell surface a rather<tei:lb/>serrated appearance. Free-living, usually borne upon a stalk that is at least as long as the body<tei:lb/>diameter. Stalk joins body via a short cytoplasmic projection. Central spherical macronucleus,<tei:lb/>single contractile vacuole slightly eccentric. Reproduction by pseudo-scissiparity producing a<tei:lb/>ciliated embryo which has a corona of several transverse kinetics near to the anterior end of the<tei:lb/>cell and sometimes a few rudimentary tentacles behind the cilia. Similar ciliated larvae can be<tei:lb/>produced by the total transformation of the adult. Spherical cyst with four transverse ribs, borne<tei:lb/>upon short stalk.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>NOTE. This species has been the subject of several papers including Penard (1920) and Kormos<tei:lb/>(1960) in addition to those given above.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Genus MUCOPHRYA Gajewskaja, 1933</tei:p>
         <tei:p>This single species genus closely resembles some of the stalkless species ofPodophrya and perhaps<tei:lb/>will eventually become submerged in that genus. However the description of budding is not<tei:lb/>sufficiently detailed to positively identify it as pseudo-scissiparity or as simple exogenous budding<tei:lb/>and until that feature is elucidated it should remain as a separate genus. The possession of a thick<tei:lb/>mucous coat is not in itself thought to be sufficient to sustain its separate generic status since this<tei:lb/>is possibly a recent adaptation to the planktonic way of life. The saucer-shaped lorica is rather<tei:lb/>different from other podophryid cysts and this feature tends to strengthen the case for retaining it<tei:lb/>as a separate genus.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Diagnosis of Mucophrya</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Freshwater suctorian whose outline shape is irregular, triangular to heart-shape. Lorica absent<tei:lb/>but there is a thick layer of mucous covering the only species described to date. Stalk absent.<tei:lb/>Free-living, planktonic, never parasitic. Tentacles capitate and retractile, distributed all over<tei:lb/>body, not in fascicles. Actinophores absent. Cyst saucer-shaped with frilled edge, cytoplasmic<tei:lb/>portion reduced, spherical lying centrally within cyst. Reproduction by exogenous buds.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Key to species of Mucophrya</tei:p>
         <tei:p>1 Planktonic, covered in thick layer of mucous M.pelagica</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Species description of Mucophrya</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Mucophrya pelagica Gajewskaja, 1933</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION (Fig. 40). Medium (65-1 lOum diameter), freshwater, irregular heart-shape to<tei:lb/>triangular, aloricate suctorian. Tentacles capitate, projecting out from all over body surface,<tei:lb/>retractile. Free-living, planktonic, without stalk. Body covered in thick mucilaginous coat.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>88 COLIN R. CURDS</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 40. Mucophrya pelagica: (a) adult; (b) cyst. Both after Gajewskaja, 1933.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Central ovoid macronucleus, two micronuclei, single marginal contractile vacuole. Reproduction<tei:lb/>by exogenous buds. Cyst saucer-shape, up to 1 30 um in diameter, with a frilled edge. Cytoplasm<tei:lb/>in cyst centrally positioned and spherical.</tei:p>
