      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>The Identity of Cribrilaria innominata (Couch, 1844)<tei:lb/>(Bryozoa, Cheilostomata)</tei:p>
         <tei:p>J. D. D. Bishop</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Departments of Palaeontology and Zoology, British Museum (Natural History), Cromwell<tei:lb/>Road, London SW7 5BD</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Synopsis</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The universally accepted identity of Lepralia innominata Couch, 1844, i.e. that of a cribrilinid species com-<tei:lb/>monly placed in the genus Cribrilaria, was established by Johnston in the second edition (1847) of A History<tei:lb/>of the British Zoophytes but may not have been that intended by Couch. In the absence of the type series,<tei:lb/>from which the true nature of the species might have been determined, Johnston's concept of the taxon is<tei:lb/>accepted and a neotype is selected from his material. A redescription based on the neotype is given. It is<tei:lb/>hoped thereby to restrict and stabilise the usage of the name, which has been applied to a number of Recent<tei:lb/>and fossil cribrilinid species. The occurrence of the species as a fossil in the European Neogene is regarded as<tei:lb/>unproven; a record from the Pliocene Crags of eastern England is specifically rejected.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Introduction</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Studies in the last few years have revealed considerable present-day diversity within the cribri-<tei:lb/>morph genus Cribrilaria, particularly in the NE. Atlantic region (Harmelin, 1970; 1978; 1984).<tei:lb/>This realisation has accompanied a narrowing of species concepts within the genus, necessitating<tei:lb/>a re-examination of the commonly-cited older nominal species in order to establish their precise<tei:lb/>identity. Following the view of Hincks (1880), Cribrilaria innominata (Couch, 1844) was regarded<tei:lb/>by many workers as merely a form or variety of C. radiata (Moll, 1803). Recently, however, the<tei:lb/>separate identity of the two species has been reaffirmed, for instance by Harmelin (1970) and<tei:lb/>Hayward &amp; Ryland (1979). The apparent intergradation exhibited by C. radiata and C. innomi-<tei:lb/>nata seems to have resulted, at least in part, from the confusion of several species under each<tei:lb/>name. A neotype of C. radiata was selected by Harmelin (1970) who also distinguished two<tei:lb/>forms, A and B, of C. innominata. Harmelin attributed a relatively restricted geographical distri-<tei:lb/>bution (perhaps exclusively Mediterranean) to C. radiata, but regarded C. innominata as cos-<tei:lb/>mopolitan. Gordon (1984) also considered C. innominata to be cosmopolitan. However, type<tei:lb/>material of C. innominata has not been recognised, and it seems highly probable that the<tei:lb/>name is still being used for several different but related species. The present paper is an attempt to<tei:lb/>examine the origin of the concept of Cribrilaria innominata and to clarify its identity by reference<tei:lb/>to type material.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Development of the concept of C. innominata</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The original description and figure (R. Q. Couch, 1844) of Lepralia innominata are inadequate to<tei:lb/>define the species. There is, indeed, little to suggest a cribrimorph identity for the taxon. Couch<tei:lb/>did not place any other taxon in synonymy with his new species. The provenance of his material<tei:lb/>was given as 'On stones, rare. Goran, Mr. Peach. Polperro. Mount's bay' (Couch, 1844: 114).<tei:lb/>Charles W. Peach's assistance in providing specimens for Part 3 of A Cornish Fauna was warmly<tei:lb/>acknowledged by Richard Couch (1844; iv-v, preface dated August 1844). Peach worked as a<tei:lb/>Customs Officer in Cornwall; from October 1834 to March 1845 he was based at Goran (or<tei:lb/>Gorran) Haven, south of Mevagissey, then he moved to Fowey before transfer to Scotland in<tei:lb/>December 1849 (Boase &amp; Courtney, 1878; Lee, 1895).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Bull. Br. Mus. nat. Hist. (Zool.) 50(2): 93-102 Issued 26 June 1986</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>94 J. D. D. BISHOP</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Peach himself announced the discovery of two new species of Lepralia to a meeting of the<tei:lb/>British Association for the Advancement of Science, at York in September 1 844. The published<tei:lb/>summary of his communication (Anon., 1845: 65) cited these as 'Lepralia catenata and Lepralia<tei:lb/>pectinata, which [Peach had stated] Dr. Johnston of Berwick-on-Tweed and Mr. Couch of<tei:lb/>Penzance have pronounced new and good species'. No descriptions or figures were given and, if<tei:lb/>this report (in the third person, by an anonymous editor) of his remarks qualifies as a publication<tei:lb/>of the names by Peach, they must be regarded as nomina nuda. The report of the Royal Institution<tei:lb/>of Cornwall for 1845 lists donations to their Museum for the period 3 December 1844 to 7<tei:lb/>November 1845. These included specimens of 18 species of coelenterates and bryozoans from<tei:lb/>Goran and Fowey Harbour presented by Peach; amongst them were Lepralia catenata and L.<tei:lb/>pectinata, but not L. innominata. (L. catenata was Chorizopora brongniartii (Audouin) according<tei:lb/>to later authors. L. pectinata will be discussed below.) Peach's donation did not include material<tei:lb/>referred to any of the new species described by Couch (1844). None of Peach's bryozoan<tei:lb/>specimens are now to be found in the collections of the Royal Institution of Cornwall (R. D.<tei:lb/>Penhallurick, Assistant Curator, pers. comm., 1985).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Johnston (1847) redescribed Lepralia innominata in his History of the British Zoophytes. In the<tei:lb/>preface to this work (i.e. to the second edition) he acknowledged the assistance of both Peach and<tei:lb/>Couch. However, Peach's name alone was placed after the diagnosis of L. innominata, the signifi-<tei:lb/>cance of this convention being explained by Johnston (1847: 30, footnote) as follows: 'The name<tei:lb/>affixed to the specific character is that of the person who, so far as I have been able to ascertain<tei:lb/>the fact, added the species to the British Fauna'. Johnston placed 'Lep. pectinata, Peach MS' in<tei:lb/>synonymy with L. innominata; his listed material of the species was provided by Peach from<tei:lb/>Cornwall and by G. C. Hyndman from W. Scotland. Johnston's diagnosis, description and figure<tei:lb/>(1847: 319-320 and pi. 55 fig. 12) appear to contradict those of Couch (1844: 1 14-1 15 and pi. 22<tei:lb/>fig. 4) on several points. Thus 'cells short, sub-orbicular or ovate' (Johnston) contrasts with<tei:lb/>Couch's figure showing slender zooids about three times as long as broad (although Couch's own<tei:lb/>text also says 'oval'). Johnston states 'aperture . . . armed with several short denticles or spines<tei:lb/>not longer than its diameter' (perhaps describing spine bases left after spines had been lost)<tei:lb/>whereas Couch indicates long oral spines clearly exceeding the dimensions of the orifice. Most<tei:lb/>significantly, Johnston specifies ridges on the zooids radiating from the midline, but Couch de-<tei:lb/>scribes and illustrates a series of short, sub-parallel transverse bands. Perhaps not surprisingly,<tei:lb/>Johnston characterised Couch's figure (and description?) as 'very bad'. The cribrilinid concept of<tei:lb/>L. innominata adopted by later workers seems clearly to have been established by Johnston rather<tei:lb/>than Couch. However, Johnston's account is insufficient to allow the separation of one particular<tei:lb/>species from other, related, cribrimorphs.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A large collection, presented by Johnston to the British Museum in 1847, was described in<tei:lb/>the handwritten accessions book as 'The authentic specimens from which the descriptions in<tei:lb/>"Johnston's British Zoophytes" were taken'. Material of L. innominata from the locations listed<tei:lb/>by Johnston (1847) is represented by only two specimens: BM(NH) 1847.9.16.32 (Goran,<tei:lb/>Cornwall, C. W. Peach a label with the specimen indicates that this was collected in September<tei:lb/>1843) and 1847.9.16.122 (Sana Island, W. Scotland, G. C. Hyndman). These specimens are con-<tei:lb/>specific; they are both labelled L. pectinata in Johnston's handwriting. The collection included<tei:lb/>six additional specimens listed in the accessions book as L. pectinata (as were the two just<tei:lb/>mentioned), for which the locality was given simply as 'British'. This poorly localised material<tei:lb/>was not specifically referred to by Johnston (1847). Some of these specimens are conspecific with<tei:lb/>those from Cornwall and Scotland; others belong to a closely related (possibly conspecific) form<tei:lb/>of similar zooidal dimensions sharing the large suboral lacuna regarded as characteristic of L.<tei:lb/>innominata by later authors, but differing in details of the frontal wall calcification. The status of<tei:lb/>the latter form will not be further discussed here.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Busk (1854: pi. 86 fig. 2) illustrated Lepralia innominata from one of Johnston's specimens,<tei:lb/>1847.9.16.32, collected at Goran Haven by Peach. This was the most detailed and informative<tei:lb/>figure of L. innominata yet published, clearly showing a distinct triangular area, proximal to the<tei:lb/>D-shaped orifice, pierced by a large suboral pore or lacuna. Busk's diagnosis of the species also<tei:lb/>noted this suboral pore. Lepralia pectinata Peach MS was listed in the synonymy for the species.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>IDENTITY OF CRIBRILARIA INNOMINATA 95</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In addition, Busk provided a second figure of L. innominata (pi. 86 fig. 3). The illustrated<tei:lb/>specimen, 1847.9.16.79, was also part of Johnston's collection. However, there is no record that<tei:lb/>Johnston himself identified this colony as L. innominata. The colony encrusts a bivalve shell also<tei:lb/>colonised by several other species of bryozoan; Johnston's labels, and the accession details, refer<tei:lb/>to these other species only. The species illustrated in pi. 86 fig. 3 would today be placed in the<tei:lb/>genus Puellina; it agrees with the description by Hincks (1880: 186) of Cribrilina radiata var. a,<tei:lb/>which has been taken to be Puellina setosa (Waters).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Busk ( 1 860: 282) later noted the possibility that L. innominata (referred to as 'L. innominata,<tei:lb/>Johnst.') might be a synonym of Eschara radiata Moll, 1803, a species described from the<tei:lb/>Mediterranean Sea. Smitt (1873: 22-23) transferred both species to the genus Cribrilina but dis-<tei:lb/>counted the possibility of synonymy, giving 'the presence, on Cribrilina innominata, of a lunate<tei:lb/>pore in the triangular or semicircular space, proximally of the zooecial aperture' as a character<tei:lb/>distinguishing the two species.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Hincks (1880: 185) dismissed Couch's figure (and description?) as 'worthless' and followed<tei:lb/>Johnston (1847) and later authors in his concept of Lepralia innominata. Hincks regarded the<tei:lb/>species, referred to (1880: 187) as 'Lepralia innominata, Johnston', as merely a form of Cribrilina<tei:lb/>radiata (Moll). His confusing account recorded both the 'radiata form' and the 'innominata form'<tei:lb/>from Britain. The two forms were considered to intergrade.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Peach (1882) updated the work of Couch (1844) by adding subsequent Cornish records and<tei:lb/>revising nomenclature with reference to Hincks (1880). Lepralia innominata Couch, 1844 was<tei:lb/>listed in synonymy with Cribrillina radiata (lapsus pro Cribrilina), thereby endorsing Hincks'<tei:lb/>treatment of the species. L. pectinata was not mentioned.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The genus Cribrilaria was founded by Canu &amp; Bassler (1928; 1929: see Lagaaij, 1952) with C.<tei:lb/>radiata as type-species; it was regarded by Gordon (1984) as a sub-genus of Puellina Jullien, 1886.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>From all this it is apparent that the accepted concept of Cribrilaria innominata may not be that<tei:lb/>intended by Couch (1844), but was established later by Johnston (1847) and partially clarified by<tei:lb/>Busk (1854) and Smitt (1873). Johnston apparently based his concept of the taxon on Peach's<tei:lb/>undescribed Lepralia pectinata, but treated this as a synonym of Couch's L. innominata. The<tei:lb/>source of Johnston's belief that L. innominata and L. pectinata were conspecific has not been<tei:lb/>ascertained. Peach reportedly checked the identity of L. pectinata as a new species with Couch<tei:lb/>during or before 1844 (Anon., 1845), but also provided part of the type material for L. innomi-<tei:lb/>nata. The name L. pectinata was not mentioned by Couch (1844), but was apparently still being<tei:lb/>used by Peach and Johnston in a public talk and for the labelling of specimens around the time of<tei:lb/>publication of Couch's paper. Peach (1882) accepted Hincks' (1880) treatment of L. innominata,<tei:lb/>implying that he did not question the concept of the taxon that then prevailed.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Non-availability of original type material</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A Cornish Fauna, of which Richard Couch's account of the 'zoophytes' formed Part 3, was sub-<tei:lb/>titled 'Intended to form a Companion to the Collection in the Museum of the Royal Institution<tei:lb/>of Cornwall'; it was published in Truro by the Institution. Type material of L. innominata might<tei:lb/>therefore be sought in the Institution's Museum. In the faunal lists of Parts 1 and 2 (J. Couch,<tei:lb/>1838; 1841), species represented in the Museum collection were marked with an asterisk. How-<tei:lb/>ever, no such convention was adopted for Part 3. This may imply that little relevant material was<tei:lb/>present in the collection at the time. The present author has been unable to find any record in<tei:lb/>the Institution's Reports from 1829 to 1871 of the accession to their Museum of a substantial<tei:lb/>collection of 'zoophytes' that might have been the basis for Richard Couch's work. The Reports<tei:lb/>for this period include detailed lists of donations compiled approximately once a year; major<tei:lb/>purchases are noted in the body of the Report. Clear mention is made of material relating to<tei:lb/>Parts 1 and 2 (particularly in Reports for the years 1837, 1838, 1840 and 1850).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The collection of the Institution's Museum was moved to new buildings during 1917 and 1918.<tei:lb/>Some time before this move was scheduled to be completed, the former premises were comman-<tei:lb/>deered by the Army Council and it was necessary to vacate the old building hurriedly (as detailed</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>96 J. D. D. BISHOP</tei:p>
         <tei:p>in the Report for 1918). Time did not permit the orderly relocation of the remaining collections,<tei:lb/>and a mass of material was stored in the basement of the new premises. Much of this material had<tei:lb/>not yet been unpacked when a flood destroyed it during the 1950s. None of Richard Couch's<tei:lb/>'zoophyte' specimens are now to be found in the collection of the Institution (R. D. Penhallurick,<tei:lb/>Assistant Curator, pers. comm. to P. J. Chimonides, 1976 and to P. F. S. Cornelius, 1977). Any<tei:lb/>part of Couch's collection at the Royal Institution of Cornwall (if such a collection existed) that<tei:lb/>survived into the 1950s was apparently destroyed by the flood.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A second possible location for material studied by Couch would have been the small Museum<tei:lb/>of the Penzance Natural History and Antiquarian Society. Richard Couch lived in Penzance, and<tei:lb/>held the honorary post of Curator of the Museum from 1845 to 1855 (as shown by the Society's<tei:lb/>Reports). However, the Museum no longer exists, and its collections have been scattered and, in<tei:lb/>part, destroyed. No bryozoan collection attributable to R. Q. Couch is now to be found amongst<tei:lb/>material known to have been transferred from the Society's Museum, either to Penlee House<tei:lb/>Museum (Penzance) or elsewhere (Stella M. Turk, Biological Records Unit, Institute of Cornish<tei:lb/>Studies, pers. comm., 1985).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Couch did not mention Lepralia innominata in his subsequent papers. It is concluded that no<tei:lb/>recognisable type material of L. innominata survives.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Selection of neotype</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In the absence of material from the type series, there seems to be no hope that the true identity<tei:lb/>of Couch's taxon can ever be determined. The present paper therefore establishes a neotype<tei:lb/>for Cribrilaria innominata in accordance with accepted usage of the name. Since the specimen<tei:lb/>selected came from one of the type localities of Couch's species, was amongst those before<tei:lb/>Johnston (1847), and was one of those illustrated as L. innominata by Busk (1854), it is hoped<tei:lb/>thereby to ensure the stability of the name. The author is not aware that this choice of neotype<tei:lb/>results in any other nominal taxon passing into synonymy with C. innominata. A redescription of<tei:lb/>the species based on the neotype is provided in an attempt to define the species precisely and thus<tei:lb/>facilitate future revisory work.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The colony encrusting a shell registered as BM(NH) 1847.9.16.32 is selected here as the<tei:lb/>neotype of Lepralia innominata Couch, 1 844; the specimen was part of the Johnston collection<tei:lb/>donated to the British Museum in 1847. Only two of the labels accompanying the specimen<tei:lb/>appear to pre-date its donation. They read: 'Goran. Sep. 1843 Lepralia pectinata' (with small<tei:lb/>sketch of two zooids) and '64d Lepralia pectinata' (with the BMNH registration number added<tei:lb/>in different handwriting). The first label was very probably written by C. W. Peach. The second is<tei:lb/>in Johnston's handwriting. The specimen is believed to be part of the material listed as Lepralia<tei:lb/>innominata by Johnston (1847); it was listed by Gray (1848: 121) as specimen 'a' of L. innomi-<tei:lb/>nata; it was illustrated, again as L. innominata, by Busk (1854: pi. 86 fig. 2; cf. present paper, Fig.<tei:lb/>1). The single colony, c. 5 x 5mm, encrusts the inner (concave) surface of a broken Venerupis<tei:lb/>pullastra (bivalve mollusc) shell, close to its dorsal margin. It is situated between the tube of<tei:lb/>a serpulid polychaete (Pomatoceros sp.) and the colony of another cheilostome bryozoan,<tei:lb/>Escharoides sp., which was also illustrated by Busk (1854: pi. 88 fig. 5), as Lepralia coccinea.<tei:lb/>Many of the zooecial chambers of the cribrimorph are occupied by a folliculinid ciliate (Figs 7,<tei:lb/>8), indicating that most or all of the colony was dead at the time of collection. The colony has c.<tei:lb/>200 autozooids, of which c. 45 are ovicellate. There are five avicularia, three of which are badly<tei:lb/>damaged.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Redescription of Cribrilaria innominata based on the neotype</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Colony encrusting, consisting of single layer of clearly delimited zooids. Frontal wall of auto-<tei:lb/>zooid convex in transverse section. Shape of autozooid in frontal view variable: often irregular-<tei:lb/>ovoid, sometimes broadly bifid proximally (with duplication of radiating pattern of costae) when</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>Figs 1-4 Scanning electron micrographs of Cribrilaria innominata, neotype (1847.9. 16.32): (1) part of<tei:lb/>colony x 39, the left-central ovicellate zooid and those surrounding it being those illustrated by Busk<tei:lb/>(1854); (2) pore chambers visible in damaged zooids x 73; (3) proximally bifid zooid with duplication<tei:lb/>of radiating pattern of costae x 72; (4) regenerated zooid with oblique polarity axis x 105.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>98 J D- D. BISHOP</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Table 1 Measurements on neotype, in mm, excluding periancestrular zooids</tei:p>
         <tei:p>No. of<tei:lb/>Range Mean Observns Comments</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Length of autozooid 0-35-0-53 0-44 30 Up to 0-63 if long proximal extension</tei:p>
         <tei:p>of gymnocyst present. (Measurement<tei:lb/>excluded ovicell if present.)</tei:p>
         <tei:p>passing to either side of proximal zooid (Fig. 3). Exposed lateral gymnocyst narrow. Proximal<tei:lb/>gymnocyst more extensive, often forming narrow proximal extension(s) between neighbouring<tei:lb/>zooids. Pericyst (= costate frontal shield) with nine to 14 costae (most commonly 12; fewer in<tei:lb/>periancestrular zooids; up to 16 in proximally bifid zooids). Costae raised, distinct, each with<tei:lb/>steeply inclined basal portion bearing minute pelmatidium (Fig. 7); variably developed tubercle<tei:lb/>or short ridge borne at angle of basal portion of costa and more shallowly inclined subsequent<tei:lb/>portion. Pericyst with blunt suboral median mucro passing into variably developed median ridge.<tei:lb/>One to three (? rarely four) very small intercostal pores between successive costae; additional<tei:lb/>single larger pore, with distinct lip along basal margin, between bases of successive costae.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Orifice (Fig. 6) D-shaped, broader than long, proximal edge straight. Five evenly-spaced spine-<tei:lb/>bases around lateral and distal margin of orifice in non-ovicellate autozooids (only four in one<tei:lb/>regenerated zooid, Fig. 4). Ovicellate zooids with two closely-spaced spine bases on each side of<tei:lb/>orifice (the more distal sometimes partially obscured by ovicell). Strongly inclined triangular area<tei:lb/>between orifice and umbo (not included in counts of costae given above), pierced by large lacuna<tei:lb/>of variable shape. Each autozooid with three or four pairs of distolateral pore chambers plus<tei:lb/>one distal pore chamber (? sometimes double) (Fig. 2). Uncalcified external openings of pore<tei:lb/>chambers (seen in zooids on edge of colony) relatively large, as wide as or wider than calcification<tei:lb/>separating them.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Ovicell roughly globular, often partially embedded in distal autozooid, with variably devel-<tei:lb/>oped low ridges or elongate tubercles in more or less radiating pattern on frontal surface, but no<tei:lb/>pores; median suture line sometimes discernible (Fig. 5). Avicularium (Figs 7, 8) interzooidal,<tei:lb/>without pivotal bar; rostrum elongate-triangular, edges converging towards tip at angle of<tei:lb/>c. 25-30, slightly raised (i.e. neither closely adpressed to frontal wall of adjacent autozooid nor<tei:lb/>lying along interzooecial sulcus). Combined length of palate and frontal non-calcified area<tei:lb/>c. 0-17 mm (two measurements only); proximal gymnocyst of avicularian chamber clearly shorter<tei:lb/>than this. Ancestrula obscured.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Discussion</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Successive generations of bryozoologists have shown a remarkable willingness to indulge in<tei:lb/>debate on the status and identity of Lepralia innominata without pausing to define the species<tei:lb/>adequately by reference to type material. As a result, many of the numerous records in the</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Figs 5-8 Scanning electron micrographs of Cribrilaria innominata, neotype (1847.9.16.32): (5) part<tei:lb/>of colony viewed at angle to emphasise surface sculpture x 72; (6) orifice of autozooid x 350; (7)<tei:lb/>avicularium and adjacent autozooids x 1 37, arrows indicate pelmatidia; (8) avicularium (same zooid<tei:lb/>as Fig. 7) x 230, arrows indicate folliculinid ciliates in orifices of adjacent autozooids (also visible in<tei:lb/>Fig. 7).</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>IDENTITY OF CRIBRILARIA INNOMINATA</tei:p>
         <tei:p>99</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>100 J. D. D. BISHOP</tei:p>
         <tei:p>literature of this supposedly cosmopolitan species are at best equivocal, yet may not be firmly dis-<tei:lb/>counted without examination of source material. However it can be stated that, amongst records<tei:lb/>from Recent seas, Busk (1854: pi. 86 fig. 3 only) and Manzoni (1871) do not refer to Cribrilaria<tei:lb/>innominata as defined by the neotype. Similarly, from the respective descriptions, Colletosia<tei:lb/>innominata subsp. bifida d'Hondt, 1970 and Puellina innominata var. vicariata Waters, 1923 do<tei:lb/>not in fact belong within Cribrilaria innominata. The fossil species Lepralia mitrata Seguenza,<tei:lb/>1879 and L. elegantissima Seguenza, 1879 were both referred to Cribrilina radiata form innomi-<tei:lb/>nata by Hincks (1884). Comparison of Seguenza's figures (1879: pi. 15 fig. 8 and pi. 8 fig. 11) with<tei:lb/>the neotype of Cribrilaria innominata clearly indicates the rejection of this synonymy (a conclu-<tei:lb/>sion already reached by Neviani (1900) in the case of L. elegantissima). The record of Lepralia<tei:lb/>innominata from the Pliocene Coralline Crag of eastern England by Busk (1859) is discounted;<tei:lb/>the accuracy of the published account was checked in this case by examination of part of the<tei:lb/>relevant material (BMNH B1697, D6754, D6799 and D6934). Since Manzoni (1869) based his<tei:lb/>concept of L. innominata on Busk's (1859) account and apparently copied Busk's figure, his<tei:lb/>record from the Italian succession must also be questioned. Indeed, the occurrence of Cribrilaria<tei:lb/>innominata, as defined by the neotype, as a fossil in the European Neogene is regarded as<tei:lb/>unproven.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The account of Cribrilaria innominata given by Hayward &amp; Ryland (1979) agrees in all relevant<tei:lb/>details with the neotype. BM(NH) 1899.5.1.723, from which at least part of Hayward &amp; Ryland's<tei:lb/>figure was drawn, is considered to be conspecific with BM(NH) 1847.9.16.32.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The neotype colony shows an example (Fig. 4) of 'total regeneration with oblique polarity axis'<tei:lb/>sensu Jebram (1978: 259 and fig. 4). In this case, the regenerated zooid appears to have been<tei:lb/>budded from the left distolateral neighbour of its damaged predecessor; it is abnormal in having<tei:lb/>four rather than five oral spine bases.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The proximally bifid zooids found in the colony (Fig. 3) may represent 'lateral cystid fusions'<tei:lb/>sensu Jebram (1978: 260 and fig. 4). It is probable that three parent zooids (one proximal, two<tei:lb/>proximolateral) contribute to the development of a bifid zooid. In the observed examples, the<tei:lb/>orifice shows the same orientation as those of surrounding zooids. An example of a bifid zooid<tei:lb/>with the orifice oblique (i.e. aligned with one of the proximal branches of the zooid) in an Upper<tei:lb/>Cretaceous cribrimorph is illustrated by Jebram &amp; Voigt (1977). These authors also list other<tei:lb/>fossil occurrences of 'heart-shaped' zooids recorded in the literature. In less well developed<tei:lb/>cases, cribrimorph zooids may simply show extreme proximal widening with partial duplication<tei:lb/>of the radiating pattern of costae. Waters (1923: 558) recorded specimens referred to Puellina<tei:lb/>innominata 'with the proximal part [of some zooids] spreading out' and cited a similar occurrence<tei:lb/>in Castanopora castanea illustrated by Lang (1922: pi. 5 fig. 2). Proximally bifid zooids and zooids<tei:lb/>with extreme proximal widening appear to be relatively common in cribrimorphs.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>As noted in the redescription above, the most peripheral of the pores between adjacent costae<tei:lb/>differ in size and morphology from the others. They may prove to be bounded on their outer or<tei:lb/>more basal margin by gymnocyst rather than intercostal calcification. The term intercostal pores<tei:lb/>should not, perhaps, be used to include these outer pores. Norman (1903: 96-98), noting that<tei:lb/>they were distinct, used the term 'papillae-pores' (or 'papillae-holes'), since a series of uncalcified<tei:lb/>papillae emerge through them (in material in which soft parts are preserved); the most distal and<tei:lb/>longest pair of these are commonly called setiform papillae. The papillae are found in many<tei:lb/>species of Cribrilaria and Puellina, and were cited in the diagnosis of Puellina by Levinsen (1909)<tei:lb/>and Gordon (1984). They were discussed by Smitt (1873), Harmer (1902; 1926), Norman (1903),<tei:lb/>Levinsen (1909), Waters (1923), Canu &amp; Bassler (1928) and Gordon (1984), and illustrated using<tei:lb/>SEM by Harmelin (1970: pi. 2 fig. 5; 1984: figs 4, 5, 6 and 7).</tei:p>

      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>A revision of the spider genus Phyaces<tei:lb/>(Araneae: Salticidae)</tei:p>
         <tei:p>F. R. Wanless</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Department of Zoology, British Museum (Natural History), Cromwell Road, London SW7 5BD</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Introduction</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The genus Phyaces Simon 1 902, was comprised of two taxa, P. comosus Simon, the type species<tei:lb/>from Sri Lanka and P.furiosus Hogg from Sumatra. The latter species lacks the ornate hair tufts<tei:lb/>of P. comosus and is therefore transferred to the genus Simaetha Thorell to which it clearly<tei:lb/>belongs. The species is not redescribed in the present work, but will be dealt with when Simaetha<tei:lb/>is revised.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Interest in P. comosus stems from the unusual behaviour, cryptic appearance and bizarre<tei:lb/>ornamentation of this small salticid spider. They are detritus mimics in life and specimens have<tei:lb/>been collected from ground litter and litter trapped in the shrub layer of bushes. Several examples<tei:lb/>were preserved in spirit by the author before their unusual features were fully appreciated and it is<tei:lb/>unfortunate that only one live female was available for behaviour studies (see Jackson, 1986).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The standard abbreviations and measurements are those used by Wanless (19780), but for the<tei:lb/>leg spination the system adopted is that used by Platnick and Shadab (1975).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Taxonomy</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Genus Phyaces Simon</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Phyaces Simon, 1902: 399. Type species Phyaces comosus Simon, by original designation and monotypy.<tei:lb/>Simon, 1903: 836, 839, 840, 843. Waterhouse, 1912: 230. Petrunkevitch, 1928: 191. Neave, 1940, III: 736.<tei:lb/>Roewer, 1954: 1009. Bonnet, 1958: 3647. Proszyhski, 1971: 459; 1984: 10. Brignoli, 1983: 628.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DEFINITION. Small spiders adorned with pronounced hair tufts and with legs I enlarged; total<tei:lb/>length between 2-0-4-0 mm; sexes alike in general habitus. In life, cryptic detritus mimics.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Carapace: of medium height, slightly longer than broad, widest and somewhat bulbous at level<tei:lb/>of coxae II-III; fovea absent; cuticle sculptured, papillate-falsifoveate with setae, i.e. scattered<tei:lb/>setose pits with raised papillae-form rims posteriorly; adorned with lateral fringes and dorsal<tei:lb/>hair tufts. Eyes: with moderately pronounced lenses set on low tubercles; laterals with black<tei:lb/>surrounds; anteriors unequally spaced, medians contiguous, laterals separated from medians by<tei:lb/>about half their diameter, apices slightly recurved; posterior medians minute, set closer to and<tei:lb/>well outside optical axis of anterior laterals; posterior laterals about as large as anterior laterals,<tei:lb/>set just inside lateral margins of carapace when viewed from above; posterior ocular quadrangle<tei:lb/>clearly wider behind; entire quadrangle about 56% of carapace length. Clypeus: low. Chelicerae:<tei:lb/>of medium size, slightly more robust in male; bulbous but with anterior surface somewhat<tei:lb/>flattened; slightly inclined anteriorly, more or less parallel; fang moderately strong and curved;<tei:lb/>promargin with two teeth, retromargin with bicuspid tooth. Maxillae: moderately long, parallel<tei:lb/>with outer distal margins more or less rounded. Labium: longer than broad, greater than half<tei:lb/>maxillar length. Sternum: scutiform with markedly bulbous central area. Pedicel: short.<tei:lb/>Abdomen: set so that anterior part lies over thoracic slope; more or less ovoid and somewhat<tei:lb/>flattened with pronounced basal hair tuft; spinnerets relatively short, subequal in length,<tei:lb/>anteriors robust others slender; former position of colulus indicated by scanty group of setae<tei:lb/>between anterior spinnerets and tracheal spiracle; tracheal system not examined, spiracle an</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Bull. Br. Mus. not. Hist. (Zool.) 50(2): 103-108 Issued 26 June 1986</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>104 F. R. WANLESS</tei:p>
         <tei:p>indistinct slit near base of anterior spinnerets. Legs: first pair enlarged with scanty fringes, others<tei:lb/>relatively short and moderately robust; claws pectinate, tufts present, scopulae absent; spines<tei:lb/>few, but vary from normal to robust strongest on metatarsi I. In males spines present on<tei:lb/>metatarsi, tibiae and femora of legs I-II and femora of legs III-IV, in females only present on<tei:lb/>metatarsi and tibiae of legs I-II. Female palp: moderately long and hairy.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Epigyne: moderately simple with median triangular pouch not always evident in uncleared<tei:lb/>specimens; copulatory openings indistinct and associated with large disc-like surrounds<tei:lb/>(arrowed, Fig. 2D, F); introductory ducts short and relatively broad; spermathecae rounded with<tei:lb/>large fertilization ducts.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Male palps: simple; femora bowed and laterally flattened; tibiae short with simple unmodified<tei:lb/>retrolateral apophysis; cymbium with distal scopula; embolus short, slender, arising from distal<tei:lb/>prolateral margin of tegulum; tegulum rounded with marginal seminal duct; expanded palps not<tei:lb/>examined.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DIAGNOSIS. The ornate hair tufts readily separate Phyaces from other salticid genera.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>AFFINITIES. Simon (1903) was correct when he placed Phyaces in his subgroup Simaetheae. The<tei:lb/>general habitus and structure of the genitalia suggesting a relationship with the nominate genus<tei:lb/>Simaetha Thorell and furthermore, as Proszynski (1984) noted, it also shares affinities with<tei:lb/>Ligurra Simon, also in Simaetheae. A casual survey of other genera currently included in the sub<tei:lb/>group and unidentified specimens in the collections of the British Museum (Natural History),<tei:lb/>indicates that some genera may be synonymous and that there are possibly many new species to<tei:lb/>be described. It is also probable that diversity, in respect of habitus may be considerable.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>For the present, Phyaces can only be treated as valid on the basis of its ornate hair tufts which<tei:lb/>are evidently autapomorphic for the genus. It could, however, fall into synonymy when the<tei:lb/>morphological peculiarities of Simaetheae are fully known.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Phyaces comosus Simon</tei:p>

      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>The biology of Phyaces comosus (Araneae: Salticidae),<tei:lb/>predatory behaviour, antipredator adaptations and silk<tei:lb/>utilization</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Robert R. Jackson</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Department of Zoology, University of Canterbury, Christchurch 1, New Zealand.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Introduction</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The salticids are generally considered to be the classic examples of cursorial hunting spiders<tei:lb/>which, instead of building webs to ensare their prey, use acute vision to stalk, chase, and leap on<tei:lb/>active insects (Land, \969a,b). However, the evolutionary origins of the salticids and their<tei:lb/>unique, complex eyes are poorly understood.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Recent studies of Portia (Jackson &amp; Blest, 1982; Jackson &amp; Hallas, in press), an unusual<tei:lb/>salticid genus from Africa, Asia, and Australia, suggest that questions about salticid evolution<tei:lb/>may not be as intractable as they formerly seemed. Although it moves with apparent ease across<tei:lb/>open ground and captures prey as a cursorial predator, Portia* also spins prey-capture webs and<tei:lb/>invades diverse types of alien webs to prey on the host spiders. Lacking acute vision, typical web-<tei:lb/>building spiders detect and localize prey and predators by interpreting vibratory disturbances of<tei:lb/>their webs. Using specialized movements of its legs and palps, Portia creates vibrations of the silk<tei:lb/>that deceive the host spider and assist with predation.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Portia also feeds kleptoparasitically on insects ensnared in alien webs and eats the eggs of the<tei:lb/>host spider. Eggs are an unusual prey for a salticid, since they are non-motile and salticids are<tei:lb/>generally envisaged as predators of motile arthropods.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Morphological specializations give Portia the appearance of detritus in webs, probably<tei:lb/>affording it protection from visually hunting predators. Normally, locomotion is slow and<tei:lb/>'mechanical', rendering Portia difficult to recognize even when moving. When inactive in a web,<tei:lb/>Portia adopts a specialized posture, the cryptic rest posture, with palps retracted to beside the<tei:lb/>chelicerae and legs retracted to beside and under the body, thus obscuring the outlines of<tei:lb/>appendages. Away from webs, P.fimbriata from Queensland has a specialized means of stalking<tei:lb/>and catching typical cursorial salticids, a prey which, like Portia, has acute vision. This behaviour<tei:lb/>is unique to this species. When stalking salticids, but not other prey, P.fimbriata exaggerates the<tei:lb/>slow, mechanical nature of its locomotion, retracts its palps as in the cryptic rest posture and<tei:lb/>ceases to advance when the salticid faces. Apparently, as a result of these behaviours and the<tei:lb/>cryptic morphology of P. fimbriata, salticids fail to recognize P. fimbriata as an approaching<tei:lb/>predator.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Although typical cursorial salticids neither spin webs nor use silk in prey capture, they build<tei:lb/>silken nests in which they moult, oviposit, mate, and generally stay at night and during other<tei:lb/>periods of inactivity. A salticid in a nest is probably safe from attacks by many of its predators.<tei:lb/>However, P. fimbriata preys on salticids it locates in nests; it vibrates on the silk, enticing the<tei:lb/>salticids to come out, or waits patiently until the salticid leaves the nest spontaneously.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Although it is a specialized and complex animal, Portia has some morphological characters<tei:lb/>which are apparently pleisiomorphic (Wanless, 1978, 1984). The occurrence of pleisiomorphic<tei:lb/>traits in Portia raised the intriguing possibility that some of the behaviours of Portia are also<tei:lb/>pleisiomorphic. Recognition of this possibility led to a hypothesis, presented in detail elsewhere<tei:lb/>(Jackson &amp; Blest, 1982), that the Salticidae evolved from web-building spiders with poorly</tei:p>
         <tei:p>*Except when ambiguity is likely to result Portia species will be referred to simply by genus i.e. Portia instead of<tei:lb/>Portia spp.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Bull. Br. Mas. nat. Hist. (Zool.) 50(2): 109-1 16 Issued 26 June 1986</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>110</tei:p>
         <tei:p>ROBERT R. JACKSON</tei:p>
         <tei:p>developed vision and that acute vision evolved originally in a spider similar to Portia which<tei:lb/>became an araneophagic predator proficient at invading diverse types of webs.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>It was proposed that some, but not all, of the unusual traits of Portia have been inherited from<tei:lb/>a common ancestor of Portia and all other salticids, and that these traits have been lost by most<tei:lb/>salticids but conserved in members of the genus Portia. The specialized manner in which P.<tei:lb/>fimbriata stalks ordinary salticids may have evolved secondarily and uniquely in this species.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Varied types of information are potentially useful for evaluating the Jackson &amp; Blest<tei:lb/>hypothesis, comparative studies of predatory behaviour and silk utilization in other salticid<tei:lb/>genera being especially important. Phyaces comosus Simon is of special interest because this small<tei:lb/>salticid, like Portia, is a highly cryptic detritus mimic; but Portia and Phyaces belong to different<tei:lb/>subfamilies (Wanless, 1986). An opportunity arose to study P. comosus, and the results of this<tei:lb/>study will be reported here.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Materials and Methods</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Observations were carried out in the laboratory in Christchurch on a female P. comosus collected<tei:lb/>at Peradeniya, Sri Lanka by F. R. Wanless. Maintenance and testing procedures, terminology,<tei:lb/>and conventions used in describing behaviour were as in earlier studies (Jackson &amp; Hallas, in<tei:lb/>press). Various potential nest sites were provided in the form of green leaves, dry dead leaves,<tei:lb/>stems, pieces of bark, and similar objects spread about in the cage of the P. comosus. The spiders<tei:lb/>and insects with which P. comosus was tested and the types of tests for which each species was<tei:lb/>used are indicated in Table 1 (for additional information, see: Jackson &amp; Hallas, in press).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Cryptic appearance</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Because of its unusual morphology and a specialized posture, P. comosus was exceedingly well<tei:lb/>concealed from human observers while standing on dead brown leaves; and on green leaves it was</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Table 1. Spiders and insects (Drosophila only) used as potential prey or as sources of eggs, webs, and nests in<tei:lb/>tests with Phyaces comosus, listed alphabetically by species. A: adult spider or insect; body lengths: salticids<tei:lb/>(10-14 mm), insect (c. 2 mm). CF: P. comosus put into cage with spider or insect; cage free of nests and webs.<tei:lb/>CP: spider or insect put in cage with P. comosus in its nest. CS: spins cribellate sticky web. Cu: non-salticid<tei:lb/>cursorial spider. ES: ecribellate sticky web. J: juvenile spider; body length: c. 2 mm. NE: eggs in vacant nest.<tei:lb/>NO: nest occupied by host spider, but no eggs present. NS: non-sticky web. NSE: nest with eggs, occupied<tei:lb/>by host spider. NV: vacant nest with no eggs. Or: Oregon strain (functional wings). S: typical cursorial salti-<tei:lb/>cid. SS: social species; builds communal web. Vg: vestigial winged strain (flightless). WB: web-building<tei:lb/>spider. WE: eggs in vacant web. WO: web occupied by host spider. WI: insect on vacant web of S. mimo-<tei:lb/>sarum. WV: vacant web with no eggs.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Species</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Family</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Description</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Types of Tests</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>PHY ACES COMOSUS 1 1 1</tei:p>
         <tei:p>conspicuous but unanimal-like in appearance, resembling instead a speck of dust or a minute bit<tei:lb/>of detritus.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>P. comosus always stood with its legs and palps held very close to the body. Often tarsi II and<tei:lb/>HI were held slightly under the body. Tarsi I tended to be just in front of the chelicerae. The palps<tei:lb/>were held very close to the front of the chelicerae, sometimes pressing against them. The femur<tei:lb/>of each palp angled up, with the remainder of the palp angling straight down. When mildly<tei:lb/>disturbed, P. comosus often pulled its appendages in even closer to the body and remained<tei:lb/>inactive.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Locomotion</tei:p>
         <tei:p>While walking, P. comosus remained highly cryptic. Its legs moved in short, slow, barely notice-<tei:lb/>able steps and remained close to the body. Sometimes legs and palps were held closer to the<tei:lb/>body while stepping than while standing. While stepping, the spider's body rocked forward and<tei:lb/>backward in a characteristic 'bouncing' fashion (Fig. 1). Usually, the spider rocked forward-<tei:lb/>backward-forward, making a net advance of c. ^ a body length, and a pause of 1-10 s occurred<tei:lb/>before the next bout started.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Although it occasionally leapt as far as 4 body lengths across barriers such as spaces of a few<tei:lb/>millimetres between stems, P. comosus usually made detours by, for example, returning to the<tei:lb/>base of the stem then walking across to the base of the other stem.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>No waving of legs occurred either while stepping or during pauses between bouts of stepping;<tei:lb/>but an inconspicuous form of palp waving sometimes occurred. As the waving palps moved up<tei:lb/>and down, in matching phase, a fraction of a millimetre, they remained close to the front of the<tei:lb/>chelicerae. When chased vigourously with a brush, P. comosus stopped rocking and stepped more<tei:lb/>rapidly than during normal locomotion. Nevertheless, its movements continued to be much<tei:lb/>slower then the normal locomotion of typical salticids.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>If forced on to a web, P. comosus always left immediately. Locomotion across non-sticky webs<tei:lb/>appeared laboured, and P. comosus adhered to cribellate and ecribellate sticky webs, eventually<tei:lb/>freeing itself after biting at threads and pulling forcefully with its legs.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Nests</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The spider never built a nest unless it had access to two dead leaves, with their surfaces only a few<tei:lb/>millimetres apart, between which to spin. A thick layer of silk was spun against the surface of<tei:lb/>each leaf. On each of two opposing sides, a more sparsely woven sheet connected the two thick<tei:lb/>sheets and held the leaves together. The resulting nest had the shape of a flattened tube, c. 10 mm<tei:lb/>long, 7 mm wide and 2 mm deep and open at both ends. Two to six rivets (bands of silk c. 2 mm<tei:lb/>long, 0-5-1-0 mm wide, strung between the leaves) were widely spaced about the nest. The two<tei:lb/>leaves were very tightly bound by the silk of the rivets and the sides of the nest.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Predatory behaviour</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Although Drosophila were pursued only occasionally (vestigial: 9 or 16 tests; Oregon: 3 of 12<tei:lb/>tests), P. comosus readily pursued salticids (6 of 9 tests). However, essentially the same predatory<tei:lb/>behaviours occurred with both prey-types. Dolomedes were ignored by the P. comosus (8 tests).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>P. comosus usually began to approach its prey, in its usual rocking gait, from 25-35 mm away;<tei:lb/>and its rocking movements became progressively slower and of lower amplitude as it got closer,<tei:lb/>especially as it came to within 5-10 mm. There was no tendency for P. comosus to behave differ-<tei:lb/>ently depending on prey orientation. Generally, P. comosus did not wave its palps while pursuing<tei:lb/>its prey.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>112</tei:p>
         <tei:p>ROBERT R. JACKSON</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 1. Phyaces comosus Simon. Walking pattern on a stem: (a) Initial position as begins to rock body<tei:lb/>forward, (b) Maximum forward position before begins to rock backward, (c) Maximum rearward<tei:lb/>position before starting to move forward again. This position is slightly forward of the initial<tei:lb/>position, (d) Maximum forward position at the end of the second rock forward. This position is<tei:lb/>slightly beyond the maximum forward position during the first motion. Barely perceptible shifting of<tei:lb/>legs across the substrate occurred as the spider rocked forward and backward, (e) Legs are shifted<tei:lb/>forward as the spider prepares for the next bout of rocking.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>PHY ACES COMOSUS</tei:p>
         <tei:p>113</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 2. Phyaces comosus Simon eating an egg of a much larger salticid (Bavia aericeps Koch). Its fangs<tei:lb/>are piercing the chorion of the egg. The eggs are almost equal in size to the P. comosus.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>When 2-1 body length away, P. comosus stood inactive for 1-1 Os, occasionally longer, then<tei:lb/>suddenly lunged forward and grasped its prey. There were no evident preliminaries to lunging<tei:lb/>(legs were not lifted, etc.). Prey was grasped with chelicerae alone. P. comosus was observed to<tei:lb/>capture a D. melanogaster only twice (vestigial winged in both cases), but all pursued salticids<tei:lb/>were captured.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>P. comosus never entered webs voluntarily and never attacked juvenile web spiders (24 tests),<tei:lb/>eggs (4 tests), or ensnared Drosophila (10 tests). If it accidently contacted the edge of a web, it<tei:lb/>moved away immediately (2 tests).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>P. comosus always responded distinctively to the nests of cursorial spiders, whether occupied<tei:lb/>(8) or vacant (3). P. comosus intermittently chewed (opened and closed its chelicerae against<tei:lb/>the silk) or tugged (lifted its cephalothorax once or twice while holding onto the silk with its<tei:lb/>chelicerae) and gradually made a hole into which it placed legs I. The hole was slowly enlarged as<tei:lb/>P. comosus burrowed its way into the interior of the nest (time elapsing from initial contact with</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>1 1 4 ROBERT R. JACKSON</tei:p>
         <tei:p>the nest: 0-2-3 h). The 4 small juvenile saltjcids tested left their nests as P. comosus entered, but<tei:lb/>there were no evident responses by the 3 adult salticids and the one adult clubionid tested. If no<tei:lb/>eggs were present in the nest, the P. comosus eventually left (maximum latency: 4h); but P.<tei:lb/>comosus fed if eggs were present. Additional layers of silk separated eggs from the inner chamber<tei:lb/>of the nest; and to feed, P. comosus burrowed through this layer then walked on to and pierced an<tei:lb/>egg with its fangs. After feeding, P. comosus remained in the nest near or on the eggs for up to 6<tei:lb/>days, sometimes feeding on one or two additional eggs. Host spiders were 8-14 mm in body<tei:lb/>length, and their eggs were almost as large as the P. comosus (Fig. 2).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Discussion<tei:lb/>Crypsis</tei:p>
         <tei:p>As a result of its specialized locomotory behaviour and its unusual morphology and posture, P.<tei:lb/>comosus is a decidedly cryptic spider, closely resembling small particles of detritus that are<tei:lb/>common on the ground and on leaves in the understory of the Sri Lankan rain-forest. By<tei:lb/>choosing dead, dried leaves for nest sites, P. comosus is able to move out on to a background<tei:lb/>against which it is maximally concealed when it ventures forth from its nest.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Locomotion</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Functionally, the locomotory behaviour of P. comosus is comparable to that of Portia and<tei:lb/>many other highly cryptic, leaf-mimicking and stick-mimicking arthropods (Robinson, 1969).<tei:lb/>Locomotion occurs in a manner that minimizes the extent to which the animal's specialised<tei:lb/>camouflage is sacrificed, but there are major differences in the motor patterns employed. The legs<tei:lb/>and palps of Portia move in a unique 'choppy' fashion; P. comosus steps in a completely different<tei:lb/>forward-and-backward rocking gait.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The normal locomotion of most salticids is rapid and agile, and leaps across barriers are made<tei:lb/>readily. Portia and especially P. comosus are reluctant to leap. Portia, however, unlike P.<tei:lb/>comosus, is quite capable of moving agilely and rapidly if stressed. Although it walks faster and<tei:lb/>abandons its bouncy gait when chased vigorously, P. comosus continues to move atypically<tei:lb/>slowly for a salticid. P. comosus, like African chameleons (Guppy &amp; Davison, 1982) seems to be<tei:lb/>incapable of running. It is difficult to understand the evolution of an inability to retreat rapidly<tei:lb/>from predators. Perhaps crypsis is so extreme in P. comosus that rapid escape is rarely required. It<tei:lb/>would be interesting to look for structural or physiological constraints which might hinder rapid<tei:lb/>movements by P. comosus.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Predatory behaviour</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The predatory behaviour of P. comosus consists primarily of using its normal locomotory gait to<tei:lb/>approach the prey, slowing down when close, and making a sudden lunge from close range. The<tei:lb/>manner in which P. comosus stalks prey, like its specialized gait during normal locomotion is<tei:lb/>consistent with crypsis and most probably maintains its concealment from visually hunting<tei:lb/>predators; but it is inefficient as a means of capturing motile insects (Drosophild). It is efficient,<tei:lb/>however, against a type of prey which is itself a visually hunting predator, namely other salticids.<tei:lb/>Both P. comosus and Portia fimbriata have a pronounced tendency to pursue salticids and a<tei:lb/>relatively weak tendency to pursue insects, suggesting that salticids are a primary prey of P.<tei:lb/>comosus. Crypsis most probably evolved initially as an antipredatory rather than a predatory<tei:lb/>adaptation, antipredatory crypsis possibly preadapting both species as specialized predators on<tei:lb/>salticids. Compared to P. comosus, the behaviour used by P. fimbriata when pursuing a salticid as<tei:lb/>prey is more discrete from normal locomotion and from predatory behaviour used against other<tei:lb/>types of prey. With its shorter legs and more hirsute body, P. comosus may be a more thoroughly<tei:lb/>cryptic spider than P. fimbriata. Thus it might have less to gain by 'refinements' of the types used<tei:lb/>by P. fimbriata. Also the normally larger salticid prey of the larger (c. 10mm) P. fimbriata may</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>PHY A CES COMOSUS 1 1 5</tei:p>
         <tei:p>tend to have vision of greater acuity than the minute salticids with minute eyes preyed on by the<tei:lb/>small P. comosus in the dimly lit understory of the forest (Blest, in press).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Considering the differences in the behaviour of P. fimbriata and P. comosus when pursuing<tei:lb/>salticids and the disparate taxonomic placements of these two species, specialized stalking of<tei:lb/>salticids most likely evolved convergently. Cursorial salticids are extraordinarily abundant in<tei:lb/>the Queensland habitat of P. fimbriata, and this may have been a major factor favouring the<tei:lb/>evolution of specialized predation on salticids by this species. Salticids did not seem compara-<tei:lb/>tively abundant in the habitat of P. comosus, although small salticids easily could have been over-<tei:lb/>looked. P. comosus and P. fimbriata have another behaviour in common, which is shared by all<tei:lb/>studied Portia: feeding on the eggs of other spiders. Differences in the oophagic behaviours of<tei:lb/>Portia and P. comosus are evidently related to differences in the sizes of these spiders. Portia<tei:lb/>either bites into the egg sac and eats out its contents as a single entity, or it opens up egg sacs,<tei:lb/>rakes out the eggs, and feeds on them one at a time. The much smaller P. comosus bores into egg<tei:lb/>sacs, stands on the eggs, and eats them one at a time.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Salticids and clubionids will attack if they detect intruders at the nest (Eberhard, 1974;<tei:lb/>Jackson, 1976; Pollard, 1983). However, with its small size, stealthy movements, and cryptic<tei:lb/>morphology, P. comosus avoids attracting the attention of and eliciting attacks from the<tei:lb/>maternal spider. As a distinctly araneophagic spider, Portia is in frequent proximity of spider egg<tei:lb/>sacs, and this was probably an important factor in the evolution of oophagy in this genus.<tei:lb/>Oophagy probably evolved independently in P. comosus, crypsis and small size being important<tei:lb/>preadaptations. Each egg of a large spider is almost as large as the P. comosus, and the egg mass<tei:lb/>in a single egg sac is a veritable bonanza. The adaptive advantage of oophagic behaviours for P.<tei:lb/>comosus are readily apparent.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Evolutionary implications</tei:p>
         <tei:p>To evaluate the proposal that the unique combination of behaviours that occurs in Portia is<tei:lb/>partially a reflection of salticid ancestry, it will be important to ascertain the distribution of these<tei:lb/>behaviours within the Salticidae. Detritus mimicry, oophagy and specialized predation on<tei:lb/>salticids are three unusual characteristics that probably evolved independently in P. comosus and<tei:lb/>Portia. However, web-building and web-invading with the use of aggressive mimicry, which were<tei:lb/>proposed as characteristics of salticid ancestors, do not occur in P. comosus, suggesting that these<tei:lb/>are evolutionarily conservative characters, as implied by the Jackson &amp; Blest hypothesis.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A more exhaustive study of Phyaces would be interesting; but the prospects for this are dim<tei:lb/>because of the remoteness, for most arachnologists, of the natural habitats of these spiders and<tei:lb/>the considerable difficulty of locating Phyaces in the field, which is at least partly due to its<tei:lb/>mimicry of detritus and its small size.</tei:p>

      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>Capitella caribaeorum sp. nov., a new capitellid<tei:lb/>polychaete from the Caribbean</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Lynda M. Warren</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Department of Life Sciences, Goldsmiths' College, Rachel McMillan Building, Creek Road,<tei:lb/>London SE8 3BU</tei:p>
         <tei:p>J. David George</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Department of Zoology, British Museum (Natural History), Cromwell Road, London SW7 5BD</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Introduction</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The capitellid polychaete described below has been found intertidally amongst decaying<tei:lb/>mangrove leaves in southern Florida, Cuba and St Lucia. It forms a network of galleries, lined<tei:lb/>with mucus and faeces (Fig. la), through the surface layers of organic debris accumulated around<tei:lb/>mangrove roots. The worm has been cultured in agar through many generations (George, 1975)<tei:lb/>and its behaviour and life history have been elucidated from observations made both in the field<tei:lb/>and in the laboratory (George, 1984). Initially it was thought that the worm could be assigned to<tei:lb/>Capitella giardi (Mesnil, 1897), but subsequent study has shown that sufficient morphological,<tei:lb/>behavioural and physiological differences exist to warrant erection of a new species.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The description given below is based on adults collected from the natural habitat and<tei:lb/>supplemented by observations made on specimens from laboratory culture. No morphological<tei:lb/>differences were detected between worms from the wild populations and those from a culture that<tei:lb/>had been maintained in the laboratory for five years.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Specimens collected in the field were fixed in 10% neutralized commercial formalin in sea water<tei:lb/>for 48 hours before storage in 80% ethyl alcohol. Specimens derived from the cultures were either<tei:lb/>relaxed by slow addition of alcohol to sea water containing the worms prior to formalin fixation<tei:lb/>or were anaesthetized by slow addition of glutaraldehyde before fixation in 2-5% glutaraldehyde<tei:lb/>in 0-1 M phosphate buffer (pH 7-2) with 0-5 M sucrose. Material was dehydrated through the<tei:lb/>alcohols prior to examination by optical or scanning electron microscopy (SEM). Specimens to<tei:lb/>be examined by SEM (Hitachi S-800) were either air-dried using diethyl ether or acetone, or dried<tei:lb/>in an Edwards-Pearse tissue dryer EPD3, using acetone and liquid CO 2 , before coating with<tei:lb/>gold/palladium in a Polaron sputter coating unit.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Description of new species</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Capitella caribaeorum sp. nov.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>TYPES. The holotype is a fully grown specimen containing oocytes. The paratypes include<tei:lb/>specimens that have not reached physical maturity. The holotype (registration no. BM(NH) ZB.<tei:lb/>1985. 191) and paratypes (registration nos. BM(NH) ZB. 1985. 192-196) are deposited in the<tei:lb/>collections of the British Museum (Natural History). Further paratypes have been deposited<tei:lb/>in the collections of the National Museum of Natural History, Smithsonian Institution,<tei:lb/>Washington, D.C., U.S.A. and in the Australian Museum, Sydney.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>TYPE LOCALITY. Southern Florida red mangrove (Rhizophora mangle Linnaeus) swamp,<tei:lb/>Matheson Hammock, Miami, Florida, U.S.A.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MORPHOLOGY. The species is small with fully grown individuals measuring up to 20 mm in length<tei:lb/>and 0-7 mm in width across the mid-thorax. The body is divided into a relatively muscular</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Bull. Br. Mus. not. Hist. (Zool.) 50(2): 1 17-125 Issued 26 June 1986</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>1 1 8 LYNDA M. WARREN &amp; J. DAVID GEORGE</tei:p>
         <tei:p>thorax followed by a longer thinner abdomen which, unlike that of other capitellids such as<tei:lb/>Mediomastus, remains uncoiled when the worm is handled and when preserved (Fig. la). Living<tei:lb/>worms are light brown in colour and more or less transparent, at least in the abdominal region.<tei:lb/>Gravid worms show a distinctive banding pattern when stained with methyl green in alcohol<tei:lb/>(Banse, 1970). Segments 5-8 are stained all over, segment 4 shows only slight colouration and<tei:lb/>segment 9 does not take up the stain at all. As the stain is gradually washed out the sides of<tei:lb/>segments 7 and 8 retain the green colour longer than other parts of the worm. This staining<tei:lb/>pattern is less obvious in non-gravid individuals.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The prostomium is roughly conical, as broad at the base as it is long, and without a palpode.<tei:lb/>Its dorsal surface is flattened or slightly concave and there is a marked longitudinal ventral cleft<tei:lb/>(Fig. Ib). There are no obvious nuchal organs. Eyes are not visible in the adult, but a pair of eyes<tei:lb/>can be seen in a dorso-lateral position to the rear of the prostomium in larvae and recently<tei:lb/>metamorphosed juveniles.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The peristomium is about the same length as the prostomium but broader, with a large mouth<tei:lb/>situated ventrally (Fig. Ib). In living worms the demarcation between prostomium and peristo-<tei:lb/>mium is clearly visible, but in preserved material the distinction may be less obvious. There is a<tei:lb/>large eversible proboscis without obvious papillation.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>There are nine thoracic chaetigers followed by an abdominal region with up to 40 segments.<tei:lb/>Thoracic segments are about three times as wide as long anteriorly, narrowing to twice as wide as<tei:lb/>long posteriorly. The surface is distinctly reticulated. There are no parapodial lobes, but there are<tei:lb/>widely separated notopodial and neuropodial chaetal bundles positioned half way to two-thirds<tei:lb/>back on each segment.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Both capillary chaetae and hooded hooks are present in the thorax. In fully grown worms<tei:lb/>chaetigers 1-6 bear capillaries exclusively in both notopodia and neuropodia. Chaetiger 7 typi-<tei:lb/>cally has capillaries in the notopodia and hooded hooks in the neuropodia (Figs Ib; 2a). In<tei:lb/>chaetigers 8 and 9 there are no notopodial capillaries or hooded hooks; instead these segments<tei:lb/>bear stout genital hooks mid-dorsally and hooded hooks in the neuropodia (Figs Ic; 2a). This<tei:lb/>arrangement gives a thoracic chaetal formula of l-6c 7 8-9. However, this formula varies with<tei:lb/>age and size (see CHAETAL VARIATION).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The number of chaetae in any one bundle is small. There are usually 3 capillaries or 3 hooded<tei:lb/>hooks per bundle in the thorax (range 2-4) (Fig. Ib). Genital hooks develop in all individuals:<tei:lb/>usually 1-2 pairs point posteriorly on chaetiger 8 (Fig. Ic) and 1 pair points anteriorly on<tei:lb/>chaetiger 9. Older individuals have 3 pairs on chaetiger 8 and 2 pairs on chaetiger 9 but the<tei:lb/>second or third pairs are often very small (presumably replacements) and may be overlooked.<tei:lb/>The genital hooks on chaetiger 8 slightly overlap those on chaetiger 9 (Fig. Ic). The genital hooks<tei:lb/>surround a glandular pit similar to that found in male Capitella capitata.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The capillary chaetae are prominent. Using optical microscopy each capillary looks winged<tei:lb/>and has a distinct shoulder slightly above the exit point from the body. Using scanning electron<tei:lb/>microscopy it can be seen that the winged appearance is caused by the arrangement of the<tei:lb/>cylinders of which the chaeta is comprised (Figs Id, e).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The hooded hooks are much shorter than the capillaries and only protrude a short way from<tei:lb/>the body (Fig. 3a). They each have a shoulder like that of the capillary chaeta, but in this case the<tei:lb/>shaft terminates in a pointed hook surrounded by a hood which is transparent in transmitted</tei:p>

(Fig. la,<tei:lb/>transmitted light photograph; Figs. Ib-e, scanning electron micrographs).</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Fig. 2. a: Dorsal view of anterior end showing arrangement of thoracic chaetae. b: TS through an<tei:lb/>anterior abdominal segment, c: TS through a posterior abdominal segment, ag accessory gut;<tei:lb/>b body wall; cap capillary chaeta; c coelom; g gut; gh genital hook; h hooded hook;<tei:lb/>nc nerve cord; ne neuropodium; nep neuropodial pad; no notopodium; nop notopodial pad;<tei:lb/>o oocyte; om oblique muscle; per peristomium; pr prostomium; 1st ab first abdominal segment.<tei:lb/>Numbers refer to chaetiger number.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 3. a: A bundle of four hooded hooks protruding from the body wall (scale bar = 7 urn), b: Detail<tei:lb/>of the head of a hooded chaeta showing the main fang-like tooth overlain by smaller teeth (scale<tei:lb/>bar = 1 urn), c: A view of the abdomen showing the rectangular box-shaped segments with widely<tei:lb/>spaced bundles of chaetae (scale bar = 85 um). d: The posterior end of the body showing the glandu-<tei:lb/>lar pygidium and the area of segment proliferation immediately in front of it (scale bar = 65 um).<tei:lb/>(Figs. 3a-d, scanning electron micrographs).</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>CAPITELLA CARIBAEORUM</tei:p>
         <tei:p>121</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>1 22 LYNDA M. WARREN &amp; J. DAVID GEORGE</tei:p>
         <tei:p>light. Using scanning electron microscropy it can be seen that the hood consists of several layers<tei:lb/>of small diameter cylinders which form the outer layer of the chaeta below the shoulder (Fig. 3a).<tei:lb/>The hook itself is comprised of a large fang-like tooth overlain by several rows of smaller teeth<tei:lb/>(Fig. 3b). The fang and teeth are the ends of the larger cylinders forming the core of the chaeta.<tei:lb/>The number and arrangement of the teeth as viewed using light microscopy are not reliable<tei:lb/>taxonomic characters.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>As in most capitellids the junction of thorax and abdomen is not always clearly defined<tei:lb/>externally, although the groove between segments 9 and 10 is usually more obvious than grooves<tei:lb/>between preceding thoracic segments. Abdominal segments are thin-walled and transparent<tei:lb/>when compared with those of the thorax in both living and preserved animals (Fig. la). The<tei:lb/>anterior abdominal segments are about the same length as posterior thoracic ones and are at least<tei:lb/>as long as wide, but the length:width ratio increases posteriorly until segments become two to<tei:lb/>three times longer than wide. In cross section the anterior abdominal segments are roughly<tei:lb/>triangular (Fig. 2b), but posterior ones are more like squares, with each segment looking like a<tei:lb/>rectangular box (Figs 2c; 3c).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Table 1. Thoracic chaetal formulae in a sample of<tei:lb/>Capitella caribaeorum collected from southern Florida</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Formula No. of individuals</tei:p>
         <tei:p>c, capillaries only in notopodia and neuropodia; H,<tei:lb/>hooded hooks only in notopodia and neuropodia; ,<tei:lb/>capillaries only in notopodia, hooded hooks only in<tei:lb/>neuropodia; ^/ H , mixed bundles of capillaries and<tei:lb/>hooded hooks in notopodia, hooded hooks only in<tei:lb/>neuropodia; jj, genital hooks only in notopodia, hooded<tei:lb/>hooks only in neuropodia.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Data on total length and chaetiger number are not included<tei:lb/>because some specimens had rear ends missing. It is important<tei:lb/>to note that the relative frequency of individuals with any par-<tei:lb/>ticular chaetal formula will vary according to the age structure<tei:lb/>of the population. This table merely gives an indication of the<tei:lb/>range of formulae to be encountered in a sample of worms.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>CAPITELLA CARIBAEORUM</tei:p>
         <tei:p>123</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Chaetae are arranged in four widely-spaced bundles situated to the rear of each segment (Fig.<tei:lb/>3c). In posterior segments the chaetae arise from glandular pads of which the neuropodial ones<tei:lb/>are more obvious (Fig. Ib). All abdominal segments normally bear chaetae (an average of two<tei:lb/>per bundle) except for developing segments in front of the pygidium (Fig. 3d). All abdominal<tei:lb/>chaetae are hooded hooks that are similar in structure to those found on the thorax.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The body terminates as a prominent glandular pygidium which takes the form of a swollen ring<tei:lb/>partly divided into two lobes by a posteroventral cleft containing the anus (Fig. 3d).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In gravid forms large yolky eggs are visible from segment 1 1 . The number of ovigerous<tei:lb/>segments increases with age, ranging from eleven to thirteen. In young worms each ovary con-<tei:lb/>tains a single oocyte, but this number may increase to two or three in older worms. The species is<tei:lb/>hermaphroditic and under laboratory conditions is capable of self-fertilization (George, 1984).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>CHAETAL VARIATION. George (1984) has traced the development of this species from larva to<tei:lb/>mature adult. At metamorphosis only the first 3 chaetigers have capillary chaetae; all others bear<tei:lb/>hooded hooks exclusively. At this stage there are 13 chaetigers. With increasing age capillaries<tei:lb/>gradually replace the hooks progressively backwards along the thorax. When the worms reach<tei:lb/>the 27-33 chaetiger stage there are only capillaries on segment 5, with mixed bundles of<tei:lb/>capillaries and hooded hooks on segment 6 and sometimes segment 7. At about this stage the<tei:lb/>genital hooks begin to develop and eventually the notopodial hooded hooks of segments 8 and 9<tei:lb/>drop out.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Table 1 records the range of thoracic chaetal formulae of a field sample of 35 worms. The table<tei:lb/>gives some indication of the variation likely to be encountered in a natural population of this<tei:lb/>species. Such variability makes the use of thoracic chaetal formulae as a character for identifi-<tei:lb/>cation purposes very unreliable if used in isolation. However, there is a clear relationship between</tei:p>
         <tei:p>1-7C</tei:p>
         <tei:p>1-6C 7M</tei:p>
         <tei:p>1-6C</tei:p>
         <tei:p>g 1-5C 6M</tei:p>
         <tei:p>E<tei:lb/>o</tei:p>
         <tei:p>1-5C</tei:p>
         <tei:p>- 1-4C 5M</tei:p>
         <tei:p>03<tei:lb/>| 1 - C</tei:p>
         <tei:p>O 1-3C 4M<tei:lb/>1-3C</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Fig. 4. Relationship between thoracic chaetal formula and chaetiger number in a sample of complete<tei:lb/>specimens. C segment with capillaries only; M segment with capillaries and hooded hooks.<tei:lb/>George (1984) found that no worms with fewer than 36 chaetigers were gravid. The 36-chaetiger<tei:lb/>point is indicated by a dotted line in the figure.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>1 24 LYNDA M. WARREN &amp; J. DAVID GEORGE</tei:p>
         <tei:p>thoracic chaetal formula and total number of chaetigers as can be seen by analysis of a sample of<tei:lb/>complete worms (Fig. 4). Whenever possible, therefore, identifications should be based on a large<tei:lb/>sample of worms, including many complete individuals, and the relationship between thoracic<tei:lb/>chaetal formula and total chaetiger number checked against Fig. 4.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Discussion</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Four genera of capitellids with nine thoracic chaetigers and genital hooks have been described.<tei:lb/>Of these Branchiocapitella Fauvel, 1932 is readily distinguished by the presence of cirriform<tei:lb/>branchiae. Warren (1976) referred Capitellides Mesnil, 1897 to the genus Capitella Blainville,<tei:lb/>1828. Capitellides had been distinguished principally by the presence of genital hooks in both<tei:lb/>sexes. The description of Capitomastus Langerhans, 1880 is also very similar, but it includes refer-<tei:lb/>ence to sexual dimorphism in the thoracic chaetal formula. The relationships of these three<tei:lb/>genera are very uncertain and a reappraisal of the Capitella/ 'Capitellides y 'Capitomastus complex is<tei:lb/>currently under investigation by one of us (LMW).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Capitella caribaeorum appears to have many morphological and developmental features in<tei:lb/>common with Capitomastus minimus sensu Hauenschild (1954) collected from Rho'des in the<tei:lb/>Mediterranean. In the absence of either a detailed description or reference material it is<tei:lb/>impossible to draw any firm conclusion as to the status of this material.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Four species of Capitella with genital hooks in ovigerous individuals have been described. Of<tei:lb/>these Capitella ( = Capitellides) jonesi (Hartman, 1959) and Capitella ( Capitellides) teres<tei:lb/>(Treadwell, 1939) have been recorded from the Caribbean province. Neither of these, however,<tei:lb/>could be confused with C. caribaeorum as they have different thoracic chaetal formulae: in C.<tei:lb/>caribaeorum the capillary chaetae occur in the first six or seven segments, whereas in C. jonesi<tei:lb/>they are restricted to the first three segments and in C. teres are present on the first eight<tei:lb/>segments.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In Capitella hermaphrodita Boletzky and Dohle, 1967 not all adults possess genital hooks and<tei:lb/>there are indications of a sex change from female to male (Warren, 1976). In C. caribaeorum<tei:lb/>the male reproductive system develops in advance of the female (George, 1984). Furthermore,<tei:lb/>capillary chaetae occur only on the first four chaetigers in C. hermaphrodita.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>C. caribaeorum is most similar to Capitella ( = Capitellides) giardi which was first described<tei:lb/>from the northern coast of France. The thoracic chaetal formula of this species is l-6c In<tei:lb/>8-9^, although capillaries are sometimes present in segment 7, giving it an identical formula to<tei:lb/>that of C. caribaeorum. C. caribaeorum differs most markedly in the number of hooded hooks,<tei:lb/>having on average 3 hooks per bundle in the thorax and 2 per bundle in the abdomen, whereas C.<tei:lb/>giardi has 4-5 hooks per bundle in the thorax and 7-10 per bundle in the abdomen. Adult C.<tei:lb/>giardi are slightly smaller than C. caribaeorum (Mesnil quoted a length of 10 mm) with a compar-<tei:lb/>able number of chaetigers (35-45) so these differences cannot be attributed to size variations.<tei:lb/>Like C. caribaeorum, C. giardi produces a few large eggs. Mesnil (1897) quoted a dimension of<tei:lb/>500 um and Day (1937) gave a range of 290-370 um for the largest dimension, compared with an<tei:lb/>average of 300 um for C. caribaeorum (George, 1984). Although the egg sizes are similar, Day, in<tei:lb/>his description of the development of C. giardi, reported that at metamorphosis larvae had the<tei:lb/>first three segments with capillaries followed by thirteen segments with hooks. C. caribaeorum, on<tei:lb/>the other hand, has three segments bearing capillaries followed by ten hooked segments at meta-<tei:lb/>morphosis (George, 1984). There is no evidence in the literature that C. giardi is hermaphroditic.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>There are marked ecological differences between these two species. Mesnil found C. giardi in<tei:lb/>the intertidal zone at Wimereux in sediment covering clayey rock accompanied by Polydora<tei:lb/>ciliata (Johnston, 1838), Pygospio elegans Claparede, 1863 and Fabricia sabella (Ehrenberg,<tei:lb/>1837), and amongst a thick encrustation of Lithothamnion polymorphum (Linnaeus) in rock pools<tei:lb/>in the granite bedrock at St Martin's Bay near to Cap de la Hague (n.b. It is unlikely that the<tei:lb/>species forming encrustations was L. polymorphum as stated by Mesnil. It is more likely to<tei:lb/>have been Lithophyllum incrustans Philippi). This is in marked contrast to the habitat of<tei:lb/>C. caribaeorum amongst decaying mangrove leaves.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>CAPITELLA CARIBAEORUM 125</tei:p>
         <tei:p>George (1975) investigated the effects of temperature on the development of C. caribaeorum<tei:lb/>and found that 10C was near the lower lethal limit for the species. At 15C adults survived, but<tei:lb/>larvae did not develop beyond metamorphosis. The occurrence of C. giardi on the north coast of<tei:lb/>France indicates that this species survives far lower temperatures in the winter months.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In view of the apparent physical similarity between the species we attempted to obtain the type<tei:lb/>material of C. giardi, but this could not be traced. We made collections at both of its type<tei:lb/>localities in order to try to obtain specimens for comparison with C. caribaeorum but were<tei:lb/>unsuccessful.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Differential diagnosis</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Capitella caribaeorum sp. nov. may be distinguished from the other species in the genus by the<tei:lb/>following characters: length 20mm; 50 chaetigers; thoracic chaetal formula l-6c ? 8-9^;<tei:lb/>average of 3 chaetae per bundle in thorax, 2 per bundle in abdomen; hermaphroditic; habitat<tei:lb/>decaying mangrove leaves; distribution Caribbean province.</tei:p>
