      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>Relationships of the laticaudine sea snakes (Serpentes:<tei:lb/>Elapidae: Laticaudinae)</tei:p>
         <tei:p>C. J. McCarthy</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Department of Zoology, British Museum (Natural History), Cromwell Road, London SW7 5BD</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Introduction</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The genus Laticauda is considered by some workers to constitute the most primitive group of<tei:lb/>the sea snake family Hydrophiidae. However, others consider that Laticauda (Laticaudinae) and<tei:lb/>the true sea snakes (Hydrophiinae) are more likely to have had separate origins within the<tei:lb/>front-fanged proteroglyphous snakes (family Elapidae).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The paper considers morphological evidence for the relationships of laticaudines at a number<tei:lb/>of levels. Firstly, variation within the currently recognized species of Laticauda is discussed and<tei:lb/>clinal variation within the wide-ranging forms L. laticaudata, L. colubrina, and L. semifasciata/<tei:lb/>schistorhynchus is reviewed. The status of L. crockeri, a form endemic to a brackish water lake<tei:lb/>on Rennell Island (Solomon Islands), is considered and its relationships with L. laticaudata, to<tei:lb/>which it is sometimes regarded as being only subspecifically related, are discussed. Next, the rela-<tei:lb/>tionships between Laticauda species are analysed using both phenetic and phylogenetic methods<tei:lb/>of analysis. In terms of overall (phenetic) resemblance, L. colubrina is closer to the L. laticaudata/<tei:lb/>crockeri lineage than to the divergent L. semifasciataj schistorhynchus lineage. Under phylogenetic<tei:lb/>analysis however, L. colubrina emerges as being somewhat transitional between the two lineages.<tei:lb/>Finally, the wider relationships of Laticauda are investigated, again using both phenetic and<tei:lb/>phylogenetic methods. Laticauda clearly shares more overall similarity with terrestrial elapines<tei:lb/>than with the hydrophiine sea snakes examined. However, when the same data are subjected<tei:lb/>to phylogenetic analyses (parsimony and compatibility methods) a rather conflicting picture<tei:lb/>emerges, but, in spite of the incompatibilities, the balance of evidence seems to support the<tei:lb/>hypothesis of comparatively close association between laticaudines and hydrophiines (a scheme<tei:lb/>that is also congruent with recent immunological studies).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Malcolm Smith (1926: xi), in his classic work on sea snakes (Hydrophiidae), regarded Lati-<tei:lb/>cauda as the most primitive sea snake genus and suggested a dual origin of the Hydrophiidae; the<tei:lb/>Laticaudinae (in which he placed Laticauda, Aipysurus and Emydocephalus} from Australia and<tei:lb/>the Hydrophiinae (containing the remaining sea snakes) from Indo-Malaya. Later he appeared<tei:lb/>to slightly modify his opinion by stating (Smith 1943: 439) that the Laticaudinae and Hydro-<tei:lb/>phiinae 'are united through Ephalophis'. Underwood (1967: 1 10) however mentioned that a case<tei:lb/>still had to be made that Laticauda is related to the other sea snakes and McDowell (1967, 1969,<tei:lb/>1972, 1974) has argued that Laticauda is not closely related to the true sea snakes (Hydrophiinae<tei:lb/>including Aipysurus and Emydocephalus) but represents an independent marine adaptation of<tei:lb/>a group of elapids comprising the Asiatic coral snakes (Calliophis, Maticora), the American<tei:lb/>coral snakes (Micrurus and Micruroides} and an elapid from Bougainville, Solomon Islands<tei:lb/>(Parapistocalamus) .</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Some workers have accepted McDowell's theory, for example Smith et al. (1977) even pro-<tei:lb/>posed a radical change in classification with Laticauda being placed in the subfamily Elapinae of<tei:lb/>the family Elapidae and remaining sea snakes being assigned to the subfamily Hydrophiinae of<tei:lb/>the family Hydrophiidae. Others have, however, regarded the position of Laticauda as equivocal<tei:lb/>e.g. Voris (1977) grouped Laticauda and true sea snakes together in the same family (Hydro-<tei:lb/>phiidae) but stated that Laticauda is 'a group of very closely related species distinct from all other<tei:lb/>sea snakes and either represent an independent evolutionary line or a very early separation from<tei:lb/>all other sea snakes'.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Bull. Br. Mus. Nat. Hist. (Zool.) 50(2): 127-161 Issued 26 June 1986</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>128</tei:p>
         <tei:p>C. J. MCCARTHY</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Recent immunological evidence (e.g.Cadle &amp; Gorman, 1981; Mao et al., 1983) suggests that<tei:lb/>Laticauda and true sea snakes are relatively closely related although Mao et al. (1983: 870) state<tei:lb/>that 'the Hydrophiinae are much closer immunologically to the Australian elapids than is the<tei:lb/>genus Laticauda 1 .</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In the belief that this somewhat confused picture could possibly be clarified by phylogenetic<tei:lb/>methods of analysis, the author undertook a study of the morphological characters which bear<tei:lb/>upon the relationships of Laticauda (McCarthy, 1982). While no very clear answer to the central<tei:lb/>problem was obtained the resulting data set has a number of interesting aspects which are<tei:lb/>presented here.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Intraspecific variation in Laticauda</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The genus Laticauda has an extensive distribution from the Bay of Bengal through the Indo-<tei:lb/>Australian area, north to Japan and west to some South Pacific islands (Niue, Tonga and<tei:lb/>Samoa); there are even unconfirmed reports of the occurrence of one species on the west coast of<tei:lb/>Central America (Fig. 1).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Five species are currently assigned to the genus although two of these (L. crockeri and L.<tei:lb/>schistorhynchus) are regarded by some authorities as being only subspecifically distinct. Two of<tei:lb/>the species (L. laticaudata and L. colubrind) together occupy almost the entire known range of the<tei:lb/>genus. L. semifasciata and L. schistorhynchus appear very closely related to each other but have<tei:lb/>peculiarly disjunct distributions (see below). L. crockeri, a close relative of L. laticaudata, is<tei:lb/>confined to a land-locked lagoon on Rennell Island, Solomons.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 1 Distribution of laticaudine sea snakes. Stippled shading = Joint distributions of Laticauda<tei:lb/>colubrina and L. laticaudata (there are also some reports of L. colubrina from Central America,<tei:lb/>p. 1 34). Vertical line shading = Distribution of L. semifasciata. Oblique line shading = Distribution<tei:lb/>of L. schistorhynchus. Asterisk = Distribution of L. crockeri.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>RELATIONSHIPS OF THE LATICAUDINE SEA SNAKES</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Laticauda laticaudata (Linnaeus, 1 758)</tei:p>
         <tei:p>129</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A wide-ranging species regarded (e.g. Klemmer, 1963) as comprising two subspecies:- L. I.<tei:lb/>laticaudata (Linnaeus, 1758) from Philippines, Indo-Australian Archipelago, New Guinea,<tei:lb/>Australia, Oceania and L. I. affinis (Anderson, 1871) from India, Malay Peninsula, South China,<tei:lb/>Taiwan and Ryukyu Retto (Japan).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>According to Stejneger (1907: 404) the two forms may be distinguished on colour pattern.<tei:lb/>L. laticaudata laticaudata has a light horse-shoe shaped mark, on top of the head, which<tei:lb/>bends down behind the eye to reach the lip. In contrast, the light horse-shoe shaped mark in<tei:lb/>L. laticaudata affinis does not curve down behind the eye (Fig. 2). Additional differences between<tei:lb/>the two forms, cited by Stejneger, include: light coloured rings on belly 4-5 ventrals wide in L. /.<tei:lb/>laticaudata which also has no or one incomplete light ring on the neck; in L. I. affinis the light<tei:lb/>belly rings are 1-3 ventrals wide and there are usually two incomplete light neck rings. Stejneger<tei:lb/>also commented that the extent to which these characters held good in a large series required<tei:lb/>investigation.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 2 Head coloration in Laticauda laticaudata. (a) Typical 'eastern' form (Loo Choo); note that the<tei:lb/>light mark does not turn down posterior to the eye. (b) Typical 'western' form (Tasmania); note the<tei:lb/>down-turned light mark.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>1 30 C. J. MCCARTHY</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Table 1. Laticauda laticaudata, geographical variation.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>'BM = British Museum (Natural History), London; RML = Rijksmuseum van Natuurlijke Historic, Leiden;<tei:lb/>ZMC = Zoologisk Museum, Copenhagen.<tei:lb/>2 + indicates presence; indicates absence.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Table 1 shows the distribution of the above characters in the 34 specimens of L. laticaudata<tei:lb/>examined in the present study. It appears that the head pattern can indeed be used to partition L.<tei:lb/>laticaudata broadly into eastern and western populations however some specimens in the inter-<tei:lb/>mediate area (Ternate and Deli) show asymmetry, the head mark being down-turned on one side<tei:lb/>of the head but not on the other. Additional features do not correlate absolutely with the con-<tei:lb/>dition of the head mark but there is a degree of correspondence; 8 of the 1 3 specimens that<tei:lb/>show some down-turning of the head mark also have light body rings that are in excess of three<tei:lb/>ventrals wide whereas only 3 of the 21 specimens that lack the down-turning have light bands as<tei:lb/>broad as this. Additionally the number of incomplete neck bands is generally greater in eastern<tei:lb/>than in western specimens (17 of the 21 eastern forms have two incomplete neck rings whereas</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>RELATIONSHIPS OF THE LATICAUDINE SEA SNAKES 1 3 1</tei:p>
         <tei:p>none of the 1 1 western forms have this condition; the intermediate specimens from Ternate and<tei:lb/>Deli have two and one incomplete neck rings respectively). If subspecies of L. laticaudata are<tei:lb/>deemed worthy of recognition, which seems unwarranted owing to the apparently clinal nature of<tei:lb/>the variation between eastern and western forms, Enderman (1970 unpublished ms.) observes<tei:lb/>that Linnaeus's type of laticaudata was probably based on an Asiatic specimen; he would there-<tei:lb/>fore relegate affinis (Anderson, 1871) to the synonymy of L. /. laticaudata. In this event the name<tei:lb/>available for the Pacific population appears to be L. I. muelleri (Boulenger, 1896).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Laticauda crockeri Slevin, 1934</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Endemic to Lake Tegano ( = Te-Nggano), Rennell Island, this species resembles L. laticaudata in<tei:lb/>several respects. The main points of difference between the two forms are outlined below:</tei:p>
         <tei:p>L. crockeri L. laticaudata</tei:p>
         <tei:p>1. Midbody scale rows 19; 1. Midbody scale rows</tei:p>
         <tei:p>occasionally 21 invariably 19</tei:p>
         <tei:p>2. Ventral scales 192-207 2. Ventral scales 219-252</tei:p>
         <tei:p>3. Strong but variable tendency 3. Not melanistic, head pattern and</tei:p>
         <tei:p>to melanism; some individuals body banding always clearly</tei:p>
         <tei:p>almost uniformly dark. visible.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Slevin's (1934) description of L. crockeri was based on one uniformly dark brown specimen, with<tei:lb/>21 midbody scale rows, collected in Lake Tegano in 1933. In 1956, Vols0e described a new sub-<tei:lb/>species from the lake, L. laticaudata wolffi based on three individuals all with 19 midbody scale<tei:lb/>rows and with a degree of melanism i.e. 'head entirely black above and dark brown below'<tei:lb/>ground colour of body 'dark slate grey dorsally (almost merging with the black bands)'. He<tei:lb/>further noted that L. laticaudata wolffi appeared to differ from Slevin's description of L. crockeri<tei:lb/>in only two respects:- in the number of scale rows (19 vs. 21) and in coloration. In addition,<tei:lb/>Vols0e reported that L. colubrina also occurred in the lake. He therefore described the following<tei:lb/>situation:</tei:p>
         <tei:p>This freshwater lake is inhabited by no less than three different species of sea<tei:lb/>snakes all belonging to the same genus Laticauda namely:</tei:p>
         <tei:p>1. L. colubrina. The lake population is undifferentiated from the typical form<tei:lb/>which occurs also along the shores of the island.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>2. L. laticaudata wolffi. Subspecifically distinct from the nominate form which<tei:lb/>has not been taken from the shores of Rennell Island . . .</tei:p>
         <tei:p>3. L. crockeri. An endemic species with unknown relationships to other species<tei:lb/>of Laticauda.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Later, Vols0e (1958) described nine further specimens of L. 'laticaudata' from the lake. He<tei:lb/>recorded that eight had 19 midbody scale rows (like L. I. wolffi) whereas one had 21 midbody<tei:lb/>scale rows reducing to 19 a little distance posteriorly. The aberrant specimen was very dark (and<tei:lb/>therefore in agreement with the description of L. crockeri) but three of the other specimens<tei:lb/>matched it in colour. He concluded that 'there can be no doubt, therefore, that they all belong to<tei:lb/>the same form'. The correct name to be applied to the Lake Tegano endemic, following Vols0e<tei:lb/>(1958) is L. laticaudata crockeri with L. I. wolffi being reduced to a synonym of that form.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Recently, Cogger (1975: 124) implied that the status of L. laticaudata wolffi and L. crockeri<tei:lb/>was uncertain, citing a suggestion by Voris (1969 unpublished thesis) to the effect that 'wolffi<tei:lb/>may represent the product of recent hybridization between crockeri and immigrant laticaudata'.<tei:lb/>Kharin (1984) treats L. laticaudata wolffi and L. crockeri as separate entities. However, McCoy<tei:lb/>(1980: 70) is of the opinion that ' crockeri and laticaudata wolffi are almost certainly synonymous'.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Wolff (1969, 1970) agreed with Vols0e and recognized only two forms from the lake; L. lati-<tei:lb/>caudata crockeri and L. colubrina. He commented that whilst the lake population of L. colubrina<tei:lb/>appears indistinguishable from individuals occurring outside the lake, 'L. crockeri has become<tei:lb/>clearly differentiated from the ancestral form' (Wolff, 1970: 20), suggesting that either L.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>132 C. J. MCCARTHY</tei:p>
         <tei:p>colubrina is a recent invader or 'for some unknown reason is the only one of the two species which<tei:lb/>migrates to and from the lake through the subterranean channel'.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Among a large series of crockeri collected by him in 1977, McCoy (1980: 70) found that 'most<tei:lb/>specimens had 19 scale rows at midbody, several had 21 and one individual had 19, 20 and 21<tei:lb/>rows in an area around midbody', moreover 'there was no relation between the number of scale<tei:lb/>rows and the degree of distinction of the dark banding'. In the present study of relatively large<tei:lb/>series of Laticauda crockeri has been examined in the collection in the Zoologisk Museum,<tei:lb/>Copenhagen (including the holotype and paratypes of L. I. wolffi.). The holotype of L. crockeri<tei:lb/>(from the California Academy of Sciences) and a paratype of L. I. wolffi in the British Museum<tei:lb/>(Natural History) have also been available.          <tei:p>The marked irregularity of the above formulae together with the rare occurrence of 21 midbody<tei:lb/>scale rows is strong evidence for the atypical nature of the condition.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Complete melanism is also rather rare and not necessarily correlated with the number of<tei:lb/>midbody scale rows (McCoy, 1980). It seems certain that Vols0e (1958) was correct in regarding<tei:lb/>L. crockeri and L. I. wolffi as synonyms.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Whether to treat the endemic form as being only subspecifically distinct from L. laticaudata is<tei:lb/>problematical. L. laticaudata and L. crockeri certainly resemble each other rather closely in many<tei:lb/>features that are not shared with other Laticauda species e.g. azygous prefrontal scale is absent;<tei:lb/>first rank of marginal lower lip scales is elongate; usually 19 midbody scale rows; heart tip in a<tei:lb/>more anterior position than L. colubrina; tracheal lung absent; vestigial left lung present (like all<tei:lb/>L. laticaudata and only some L. colubrina).</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>RELATIONSHIPS OF THE LATICAUDINE SEA SNAKES<tei:lb/>Table 2. Laticauda crockeri, scale row reduction and extent of melanism.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Most of the above characters appear to be relatively primitive and therefore unlikely to be<tei:lb/>strong indicators of close relationship. However it is also true that very few characters have yet<tei:lb/>been discovered that can effectively discriminate between laticaudata and crockeri; only the<tei:lb/>ventral count seems entirely to do so (192-210 in crockeri, 219-252 in laticaudata). Additionally,<tei:lb/>Tamiya, et al. (1983), in their analysis of neuro toxins, found that 'L. laticaudata and L. crockeri<tei:lb/>are very closely related, although there are some genetic differences'. It seems therefore reason-<tei:lb/>able to suggest that L. laticaudata and L. crockeri are more closely related to each other than<tei:lb/>either is to any other extant species. L. crockeri may only be a subspecies of L. laticaudata but as<tei:lb/>the forms are allopatric this hypothesis is difficult to confirm.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Voris's (1969: 368-369) hypothesis that an invasion of L. laticaudata resulted in hybridization<tei:lb/>between it and L. crockeri to produce L. laticaudata wolffi is hardly supported by the evidence.<tei:lb/>Perhaps the most likely explanation for the situation in Lake Tegano is that a population<tei:lb/>(possibly of L. laticaudata) became isolated when the lake was formed. According to Wolff (1970:<tei:lb/>20) the age of the lake as a brackish water body is unknown, 'although it seems probable that<tei:lb/>the lagoon was cut off from the sea not long after the elevation of the land started in the late<tei:lb/>Pliocene'. L. crockeri may have evolved its pecularities as a result of: selection processes in the<tei:lb/>lake environment, inheritance from a somewhat aberrant founder population, or genetic drift<tei:lb/>due to isolation in a small population.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>An enigma still remaining to be considered is: why has the crockeri population in the lake<tei:lb/>become distinct whereas L. colubrina appears not to have formed a discrete lake form? As men-<tei:lb/>tioned earlier, Wolff (1970) suggests that crockeri is unable to navigate through an inferred sub-<tei:lb/>terranean passage to the sea whereas colubrina is able to do so. However, a simpler explanation is</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>1 34 C. J. MCCARTHY</tei:p>
         <tei:p>afforded by knowledge of L. colubrina, a species that appears to be more terrestrial in its habits<tei:lb/>than other species of Laticauda. This suggests that L. colubrina may be able to cross the high<tei:lb/>ground surrounding the lake and thus maintain genetic flow with the marine population. Wolff<tei:lb/>(1970: 13) observes that the lake is 'surrounded on all sides by a rim with a width of 0-9-2 km and<tei:lb/>a continuous height of about 100 m'; the only exception is at the extreme eastern end of the lake<tei:lb/>where the height of the rim is only 45 m.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The climbing ability of L. colubrina is demonstrated by a record of some specimens from<tei:lb/>Taiwan that were found on top of a solitary coral reef about 50 m high (Mao &amp; Chen, 1980).<tei:lb/>Although L. crockeri 'has been found moving amongst short grass surrounding the lake'<tei:lb/>(McCoy, 1980: 70) it may be less terrestrial in its habits than L. colubrina and a barrier of the<tei:lb/>dimensions described by Wolff might well prevent it commuting between the lake and the sea.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Laticauda colubrina (Schneider, 1 799)</tei:p>
         <tei:p>This species has a very widespread distribution which is largely shared with L. laticaudata.<tei:lb/>Additionally there are some reports of L. colubrina from Nicaragua (Villa, 1962), Mexico<tei:lb/>(Alvarez del Toro, 1982) and El Salvador (Villa, pers. comm.). Unfortunately these records are<tei:lb/>based on material that is no longer available for examination; the presence of L. colubrina in<tei:lb/>tropical America therefore requires substantiation (Villa, pers comm.).*</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Through its range, L. colubrina shows considerable variation in neck and body coloration and<tei:lb/>in some aspects in its scalation. Enderman (1970) considers that six populations of L. colubrina<tei:lb/>are worthy of subspecific recognition: (i) New Caledonia; (ii) Fiji, Tonga, Society Islands; (iii)<tei:lb/>New Hebrides ( = Vanuatu), Solomons, Bismarck Archipelago; (iv) Geelvink Baai (northern<tei:lb/>Irian, New Guinea); (v) Lesser Sunda Islands and S.W. New Guinea to Ryukyus, Japan; (vi)<tei:lb/>Sumatra, Malaya, Bay of Bengal. The features which Enderman cites to support the recognition<tei:lb/>of these subspecies comprise mainly relatively minor differences in neck and body banding; he<tei:lb/>also observes that populations (i)-(i v ) usually lack a postmental scale whereas populations (v)<tei:lb/>and (vi) normally have one (Fig. 3).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Amino acid sequencing of neuro toxins reveals at least three genetically different populations of<tei:lb/>L. colubrina: the difference in the structure of long-chain neurotoxins, between populations from<tei:lb/>Japan and the Philippines in contrast with those from the Solomons, Fiji and New Caledonia, is</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 3 Chin shields of Laticauda colubrina. (a) Typical 'western' form (Solomon Is); postmental scale<tei:lb/>absent, (b) Typical 'eastern' form (Singapore); postmental scale present (stippled).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>*A female specimen in Stuttgart Museum (SMNS-4203) is alleged to have been collected in Guatemala in 1877 (donor<tei:lb/>unknown); the collecting locality is thought doubtful (Wermuth &amp; Schluter, pers. comm.). In most respects the specimen<tei:lb/>appears to be a fairly typical western form having a fairly low ventral count (226) and lacking a postmental scale. It has<tei:lb/>one incomplete light band on its neck and 28 dark bands on its body.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>especially well-marked (Tamiya et al., 1983). Very recently a population in Vanuatu, with low<tei:lb/>ventral scale count and unusual neck coloration, has been studied, which appears to be a distinct<tei:lb/>species, occurring sympatrically with more typical L. colubrina. A related population occurs in<tei:lb/>the northern part of the Tonga island group (Cogger, pers. comm.).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A full analysis of population variation in L. colubrina is beyond the scope of the present study.<tei:lb/>However, examination of some morphological characters of 72 specimens (Table 3) indicates<tei:lb/>that, like L. laticaudata, the main division appears to be into eastern and western forms. The<tei:lb/>trend in ventral counts of L. colubrina is for them to be generally higher in eastern specimens than<tei:lb/>western specimens. This tendency in ventral counts is also reflected in some L. colubrina literature<tei:lb/>records. Guinea (1981), for instance, give mean ventral counts of 223 (n = 6) and 228-7 (n = 10),<tei:lb/>for males and females respectively, from Fiji, whereas Mao &amp; Chen (1980) give mean counts of<tei:lb/>231 (n = 9) and 236 (n = 16), for males and females respectively, from Taiwan.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Some other characters are only variably present in L. colubrina i.e. azygous prefrontal scale<tei:lb/>and vestigal left lung, but these features appear to show no obvious correlation with geographic<tei:lb/>distribution.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Laticauda semifasciata (Reinwardt in Schlegel, 1837) and<tei:lb/>Laticauda schistorhynchus (Giinther, 1874)</tei:p>
         <tei:p>L. semifasciata and L. schistorhychus closely resemble each other, indeed they mainly appear to<tei:lb/>have only average differences, i.e. number of ventrals and bands on the body and in maximum<tei:lb/>adult size (Smith, 1926).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Tamiya et al. (1983: 447) analysed neurotoxins and found that L. semifasciata and L. schisto-<tei:lb/>rhynchus are genetically homogeneous as far as these components are concerned. Guinea,<tei:lb/>Tamiya &amp; Cogger (1983) concluded that there is no justification for treating L. semifasciata and<tei:lb/>L. schistorhynchus as separate species.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>There do indeed seem reasonable grounds for considering the two forms conspecific. However,<tei:lb/>there is no proof that they interbreed; L. semifasciata and L. schistorhynchus are completely<tei:lb/>allopatric being separated by an enormous gap. L. semifasciata has been recorded from South<tei:lb/>Japan, Riu Kius, Philippines, Moluccas and Lesser Sunda Islands, whereas L. schistorhynchus<tei:lb/>is found in the Pacific at Niue, Tonga and Samoa. There is however a single dubious record of<tei:lb/>L. schistorhynchus from 'Bertrand' Island ( = Tendanye Island), New Guinea (a specimen in the<tei:lb/>Hamburg Museum cited by Smith 1926).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The circumstances that led to splitting of L. semifasciataj schistorhynchus populations are<tei:lb/>unknown but it is possible that competition from similar forms might, at least in part, be<tei:lb/>responsible. In the area where members of the semifasciata group are absent, for instance, there<tei:lb/>occur members of the genus Aipysurus. Aipysurus comprises several species of hydrophiines that<tei:lb/>are confined (with the exception of A. eydouxi) to the continental shelf waters of Australia and<tei:lb/>New Guinea, they also occur in parts of the extreme south west Pacific Ocean (Cogger, 1975: 72).<tei:lb/>This diversity of shallow-water sea snakes might well fill niches that are thus unavailable for the<tei:lb/>semifasciata group; this theory might also account for the relative rarity of other Laticauda<tei:lb/>species in Australian waters.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Characters examined Data matrix</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The Elapidae are a relatively large family (comprising 244 species according to Dowling &amp;<tei:lb/>Duellman, 1978; McCarthy, 1985), therefore there are practical difficulties in examining all elapid<tei:lb/>species for many characters that might have relevance to the question of laticaudine relationship.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>In order to cope with this problem it was decided to carry-out methods of analysis on a sample<tei:lb/>comprising all species of Laticauda together with a selection of ten other elapids.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The non-laticaudines in the sample were selected on the basis of a survey of the Elapidae for<tei:lb/>seven characters of Laticauda that were hitherto believed to have a restricted distribution outside<tei:lb/>the genus. The results of the survey are summarised in Table 4. The resulting sample of elapids<tei:lb/>was then examined for 45 binary and 20 multistate characters (Table 5) and a data matrix was<tei:lb/>compiled (Table 7).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Polarity of the characters (Table 6) was mainly determined on the criterion of out-group com-<tei:lb/>parison i.e. if a character state is common (widespread) among Caenophidia (higher snakes), it is<tei:lb/>regarded as primitive within the Elapidae. A more detailed account of the characters analysed,<tei:lb/>and polarity criteria used, is given by McCarthy (1982).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The problem of relationship is considered from a number of perspectives i.e. from the view-<tei:lb/>point of overall ('phenetic') resemblance (see Sneath &amp; Sokal, 1973) and from the viewpoint of<tei:lb/>various phylogenetic ('cladistic' or 'evolutionary') approaches (see review by Felsenstein, 1982).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Two basic levels of affinity will be considered here: (a) The relationships of Laticauda species to<tei:lb/>each other, (b) The relationships of the genus Laticauda to other elapids.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Interspecific relationships within genus Laticauda<tei:lb/>Phenetic analysis</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Overall (phenetic) similarity can be measured in many ways; one of the most commonly used<tei:lb/>coefficients is the simple-matching coefficient and it is this expression of resemblance that is used<tei:lb/>here. Simple-matching coefficients are calculated (e.g. Sneath &amp; Sokal, 1973: 132) according to<tei:lb/>the formula:</tei:p>
         <tei:p>m</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Ssm =<tei:lb/>n</tei:p>
         <tei:p>(where m = the number of matches between pairs of taxa and n = the number of characters)</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The overall similarity matrix is shown in Table 8. From this the scheme illustrated in Fig. 4 was<tei:lb/>constructed using the 'unweighted pair-group' method of clustering (as discussed by Sneath &amp;<tei:lb/>Sokal, 1973: 230-234).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>From Figure 4 it can be seen that Laticauda semifasciata and L. schistorhynchus are very close<tei:lb/>to each other phenetically; similarly L. crockeri and L. laticaudata cluster together. L. colubrina<tei:lb/>resembles L. laticaudataj crockeri more than it does L. semifasciata/ L. schistorhynchus. Overall,<tei:lb/>the topology is the same as in the phenogram presented by Voris (1977: 91) who also used simple<tei:lb/>matching coefficients on his data.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Phylogenetic analysis</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Phylogenetic (cladistic) analysis involves considering the relationships between taxa in terms of<tei:lb/>shared derived characters ('synapomorphies'). Reference to the data matrix (Table 7) shows that,<tei:lb/>when only derived states are considered, there is a conflict between 9 states (7-2; 12-1; 29; 32; 56;<tei:lb/>57-2; 58; 60; 64-2) supporting the association of L. colubrina with the L. laticaudata lineage and 4<tei:lb/>states (1-1; 8-2; 14; 15) supporting the clustering of L. colubrina with the semifasciata lineage. The<tei:lb/>principle of parsimony ('democratic method' Arnold, 1981: 21) leads one to accept the scheme<tei:lb/>that is supported by most evidence i.e. the same topology as suggested by phenetic analysis (Fig.<tei:lb/>4) with colubrina associated with laticaudata and crockeri. However 6 of the 9 states supporting<tei:lb/>this association have rather arbitrary polarity determinations (12-1; 29; 32; 58; 60; 64-2) and<tei:lb/>perhaps should not be used as primary evidence. If arbitrarily scored states are discounted, the<tei:lb/>balance of evidence shifts marginally in favour of a scheme associating colubrina with the semi-<tei:lb/>fasciata lineage. Two of the 4 states supporting the association of colubrina with semifasciata are<tei:lb/>variable the primitive state occurring in some individuals i.e. in colubrina, the azygous prefrontal</tei:p>

         <tei:p>scale (character 1-1) is absent in some individuals also a vestigial left lung (character 14) is vari-<tei:lb/>ably present. A possible explanation is that such characters may have been variable in ancestral<tei:lb/>Laticauda, became relatively fixed in semifasciata and laticaudata lineages but remain variable in<tei:lb/>L. colubrina; a similar scenario is postulated by Arnold (1981: 19).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In conclusion, L. colubrina appears to be, in some ways, transitional between the L. laticaudata<tei:lb/>lineage and the divergent L. semifasciata lineage and cannot be unequivocally associated with<tei:lb/>either group on the basis of a phylogenetic analysis of its morphological characters. Biochemical<tei:lb/>evidence seems consistent with this conclusion. In terms of albumin immunological distance<tei:lb/>(Cadle &amp; Gorman, 1981; Mao et ai, 1983), L. semifasciata appears rather divergent although, in<tei:lb/>tests with antisera to L. semifasciata, L. colubrina emerges as slightly closer to L. semifasciata<tei:lb/>than is L. laticaudata.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>With regard to karyology, Gorman (1981) finds that L. colubrina has a diploid count of 34<tei:lb/>which he believes corresponds with the primitive elapid condition; L. laticaudata and L. semi-<tei:lb/>fasciata have higher counts (40 and 38 respectively) and these are assumed to have evolved via<tei:lb/>centric fissions.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Kharin (1984) has recently suggested that L. semifasciata and L. schistorhynchus are suf-<tei:lb/>ficiently different from other Laticauda species to warrant being placed in a separate genus<tei:lb/>(Pseudolaticauda). The above evidence demonstrates that while the L. semifasciata lineage is</tei:p>

It is therefore<tei:lb/>thought best to here retain the genus Laticauda in its former broad sense.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Wider relationships of genus Laticauda<tei:lb/>Phonetic analysis</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Simple matching coefficients and unweighted pair group clustering were used to depict phenetic<tei:lb/>relationships between all the elapids in the sample (Table 8 and Fig. 4).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Laticauda and other sea snakes appear to share only a relatively low level of phenetic simi-<tei:lb/>larity (see also Voris, 1977: 91). Additionally Laticauda shares more overall similarity with the<tei:lb/>terrestrial elapines in the sample than with the hydrophine sea snakes.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>number of derived characters are added last. However Felsenstein (1981) recommends perform-<tei:lb/>ing a number of runs, altering the order of input of taxa, as a trial &amp; error strategy for finding the<tei:lb/>shortest tree.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In all four runs were undertaken:</tei:p>
         <tei:p>1. Entire data set; advancement-index sequenced.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>2. Entire data set; taxa in order of Le Quesne test labelling (see p. 1 56).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>3. Characters with more arbitrarily scored polarities excluded; advancement-index sequenced.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>4. Characters with more arbitrarily scored polarities excluded; Le Quesne test labelling<tei:lb/>governing the order of input of taxa (see p. 156).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>With the full data set, examined by the unmodified sequence method (run 1), Laticauda and the<tei:lb/>true sea snakes appear not especially closely related; Parapistocalamus is sister to Laticauda (Fig.<tei:lb/>5a). Altering the program to treat taxa in a modified order based on Le Quesne test labelling (run<tei:lb/>2) produced a less parsimonious tree (213 steps vs. 204 steps). The topology of this tree (Fig. 5b)<tei:lb/>suggests that Laticauda, 'true' sea snakes and kraits (Bungarus) are all relatively closely related.<tei:lb/>Reducing the data set to consider only the more confidently scored characters (runs 3 and 4)<tei:lb/>produced two more topologies for consideration. In the advancement sequenced run (3) Fig. 5c,<tei:lb/>the tree was similar to that produced in run 1 in the sense that Laticauda and the true sea snakes<tei:lb/>appear only remotely related. However, run 3 estimates the sister of Laticauda to be not only<tei:lb/>Parapistocalamus but also Micrurus. Repeating the run, this time with Le Quesne test sequencing<tei:lb/>(run 4) Fig. 5d produced an equally parsimonious tree (the trees produced by both runs 3 and 4<tei:lb/>are 139 steps). However, the topology of the tree resulting from run 4 resembles that of rejected<tei:lb/>run 2 in closely relating Laticauda and the 'true' sea snakes with each other, Bungarus is not<tei:lb/>considered close to the sea snakes though, instead Parapistocalamus and Micrurus together form<tei:lb/>the sea snake sister group.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Detection of homoplasies in Wagner trees<tei:lb/>(i) Procedural estimate</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The general amount of homoplasy (reversals and parallels) present in Wagner trees may be esti-<tei:lb/>mated by dividing the number of derived character states by the total number of character state<tei:lb/>changes (steps) in the tree. The result, expressed as a percentage, is termed the consistency ratio<tei:lb/>or homoplasy index. The homoplasy indices of various Wagner tree runs in the present study are<tei:lb/>as follows:</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Homoplasy Index</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Run 1 89/204 x 100 43-63%</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Run 2 89/213 x 100 41-78%</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Runs3&amp;4 65/139 x 100 46-76%</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In comparison, Kluge (1976: 45) derived a scheme for pygopodid lizards that has rather less<tei:lb/>'noise' than is detected in the present study (its homoplasy index is 57-2%) whereas Moody's<tei:lb/>(1980: 124) Wagner tree for agamid lizards has almost twice as much homoplasy as the trees in<tei:lb/>the present study (the index is 24-7%).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The Wagner tree algorithm, by creating hypothetical intermediates at nodes, assesses the impli-<tei:lb/>cations of each topology for every character state so that the number of reversals and parallels<tei:lb/>hypothesized for each character may be compared. Table 9 shows the changes of each character<tei:lb/>implicit in Wagner trees produced by runs 1, 3 and 4. However the Wagner tree algorithm gives a<tei:lb/>purely procedural estimate of homoplasies; some of the decisions, when judged by biological cri-<tei:lb/>teria, may appear unrealistic. For example, character 14 (vestigial left lung loss) is hypothesized<tei:lb/>to have been reversed three times in runs 3 and 4 but, biologically, a more likely explanation may<tei:lb/>be that such a vestige would have instead been lost, in parallel, on several occasions.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>(ii) 'Fours' analysis</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Underwood (1982) recently suggested a method for detecting parallelism. This program enables<tei:lb/>taxa to be selected in groups of four and gives the distribution of derived states among these taxa;<tei:lb/>in this way conflicts of evidence are clearly exposed.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>1 52 C. J. MCCARTHY</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Table 9. Number of parallels (P) &amp; reversals (R) hypothesized for some Wagner tree runs.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Seven species were selected to test the robustness of the various Wagner trees produced in the<tei:lb/>present study: Laticauda crockeri, Laticauda semifasciata, Parapistocalamus hedigeri, Micrurus<tei:lb/>surinamensis, Ephalophis greyi, Aipysurusfuscus, Bungarusflaviceps.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The topologies of these taxa in various Wagner tree runs are shown in Fig. 6. Table 10 gives an<tei:lb/>indication of the character states supporting various combinations of the seven species. Table</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Fig. 6 'Fours' analysis. Topologies, in various Wagner tree runs, of seven taxa selected for 'fours'<tei:lb/>analysis. Taxa numbered as in Fig. 4. (a) Wagner tree run 1. (b) Wagner tree run 2. (c) Wagner tree<tei:lb/>run 3. (d) Wagner tree run 4.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>10(a) deals with Micrurus surinamensis (10), Parapistocalamus hedigeri (6), Laticauda crockeri (2)<tei:lb/>and Laticauda semifasciata (4). Wagner run 1 suggests an asymmetric dichotomy pattern for<tei:lb/>these taxa whereas run 3 suggests symmetric dichotomies. Of the non-arbitrary characters<tei:lb/>supporting the alternatives there is a tie; one character 51-2 (saddle-joint between palatine and<tei:lb/>pterygoid) supporting run 1 while character 45-1 (position of the anterior vidian foramen) sup-<tei:lb/>ports run 3. Three non-arbitrary characters are incompatible with either of these arrangements:<tei:lb/>56 (parietal ridge) and 57-2 (prefrontal/frontal articulation) associate Micrurus surinamensis (10)<tei:lb/>and Laticauda crockeri (2), while 14 (absence of a vestigial left lung) occurs in Micrurus (10),<tei:lb/>Parapistocalamus (6) and Laticauda semifasciata (4). However the small number of characters<tei:lb/>suggesting that L. crockeri and L. semifasciata might have closer affinities with taxa outside the<tei:lb/>genus are outweighed by thirteen non-arbitrary derived characters shared by these two species of<tei:lb/>Laticauda.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Another fours print-out (Table lOb), this time replacing Parapistocalamus with Aipysurus<tei:lb/>fuscus (14) brings into consideration some of the characters supporting the affinities of Laticauda<tei:lb/>with other sea snakes. The proposition that Micrurus surinamensis (10) is more closely related to<tei:lb/>Laticauda (4) than is Aipysurus (14) (runs 1 and 3) is supported by five non-arbitrary characters<tei:lb/>but out- voted by twelve non-arbitrary characters supporting the close affinity of Laticauda with<tei:lb/>Aipysurus. Additionally, the assumption that L. crockeri is closely related to L. semifasciata<tei:lb/>(supported by characters 9 and 65) is challenged by three characters (1-1, 15 and 35) which<tei:lb/>suggest that L. semifasciata is more closely related to Aipysurus, a topology not suggested by any<tei:lb/>of the Wagner runs in the present study.</tei:p>

         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>RELATIONSHIPS OF THE LATICAUDINE SEA SNAKES 1 55</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Introducing another hydrophiine sea snake into the fours analysis and withdrawing Laticauda<tei:lb/>crockeri leads to the print-out given in Table 10 (c). Seven non-arbitrary characters support the<tei:lb/>inclusion of Laticauda semifasciata (4) in the same group as Aipysurus (14) and Ephalophis (13)<tei:lb/>whereas only three suggest that L. semifasciata is more closely related to Micrurus surinamensis<tei:lb/>(10) than it is to hydrophiine sea snakes. Relationships within the sea snakes are balanced between<tei:lb/>the seven non-arbitrary characters supporting the affinities of Aipysurus with Ephalophis and the<tei:lb/>eight non-arbitrary characters supporting the association of Aipysurus with L. semifasciata.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>As L. semifasciata and Aipysurus fuscus are the respective laticaudines and hydrophiines that<tei:lb/>seem to have most derived characters in common, a fourth print-out (Table lOd) was obtained to<tei:lb/>assist in evaluating the evidence supporting laticaudine/hydrophiine relationships. In addition<tei:lb/>to L. semifasciata (4) and A. fuscus (14) the elapines Bungarus flaviceps (15) (a species which is<tei:lb/>associated with hydrophiine sea snakes in Wagner tree runs 1-3) and Micrurus surinamensis (10)<tei:lb/>are also included.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>If it be assumed that Laticauda and hydrophiine sea snakes do not share close common ances-<tei:lb/>try it might be anticipated that the majority of derived features shared by the two groups would<tei:lb/>be parallel marine adaptations. However, of the twelve non-arbitrary derived states shared by<tei:lb/>Laticauda semifasciata and Aipysurus fuscus, only three appear to be likely aquatic adaptations,<tei:lb/>namely: 10 (paddle-shaped tail), 15 (tracheal lung present), 16 (muscular air sac). Of the other<tei:lb/>characters, 1-1 (azygous prefrontal) and 7-1 (marginal lower lip scales) are possibly not homo-<tei:lb/>logous in the two groups. Aipysurus fuscus is inclined to have its head shields irregularly<tei:lb/>fragmented therefore the inference that these are truly derived characters, shared by Aipysurus<tei:lb/>and Laticauda, may in fact be spurious. Absence of a palatine medial wing (49) and presence<tei:lb/>of a saddle-joint between palatine and pterygoid (51) are both found in a number of other taxa<tei:lb/>(Marx &amp; Rabb, 1972 and McCarthy, 1982), and appear likely to have occurred independently<tei:lb/>several times in snake evolution. Broad flaring of the quadrato-maxillary ligament (35) has been<tei:lb/>found additionally in Parapistocalamus. The unusual hyoid muscle conditions in Laticauda and<tei:lb/>Aipysurus (characters 36, 37 and 38) do appear to be restricted to these two genera and may<tei:lb/>be potentially robust indicators of phyletic affinity but the extent of the distribution of these<tei:lb/>characters remains to be more fully investigated (McCarthy, in prep.). Character 48 (caudal<tei:lb/>haemapophyses fused) is unusual for snakes and might be a significant similarity shared between<tei:lb/>Laticauda, Aipysurus, Emydocephalus , and Ephalophis; the only elapines in which this state has<tei:lb/>been found are two species of Calliophis (McCarthy, 1982: 146).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Characters shared by Laticauda semifasciata and Micrurus surinamensis are:- venom gland<tei:lb/>down-turning (25-2), quadrate attachment of the superficialis muscle (26-1, 26-2) and a small<tei:lb/>dorsal extension of the dentary in comparison with the ventral extension (41-2). None of these<tei:lb/>characters are exclusively shared by Laticauda and Micrurus but at least 25-2 and 41-2 are rather<tei:lb/>restricted in their distributions among other elapids (McCarthy, 1982: 101 and 132). Characters<tei:lb/>26-1 and 26-2 have rather wider distributions, occurring for example in a number of Australasian<tei:lb/>elapines (McDowell, 1967: 536 if.).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Compatibility</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In contrast to parsimony methods, which aim to find the shortest tree thereby minimizing<tei:lb/>assumptions of homoplasy, compatibility methods exist to find a tree which is compatible with<tei:lb/>the largest number of characters irrespective of the number of changes that may need to be<tei:lb/>assumed in other characters (Felsenstein, 1982). When pairs of binary characters are compared<tei:lb/>and all four possible combinations of states are found it is a logical consequence that at least one<tei:lb/>of the character states is not uniquely derived (Le Quesne, 1969).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The program used in the present study is that devised by Underwood, which compiles a<tei:lb/>character-pair matrix, works out the number of Le Quesne test incompatibilities per character<tei:lb/>and then computes the ratio between actual and expected failure rates. Additionally the number<tei:lb/>of times the occurrence of a character state in a particular species is uniquely responsible for Le<tei:lb/>Quesne test incompatibility is noted; this procedure is termed 'labelling' by Guise, Peacock &amp;<tei:lb/>Gleaves (1982). The number of labelling events for each species is totalled (Table 11) and the</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>1 56 C. J. MCCARTHY</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Table 11. Le Quesne test; the number of labelling<tei:lb/>events per taxon.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Taxon Number of times labelled</tei:p>
         <tei:p>results used to decide the order of input of taxa in some Wagner parsimony test runs (p. 151). This<tei:lb/>procedure for input of taxa may be desirable because initial construction of the Wagner tree can<tei:lb/>be accomplished using taxa with the fewest incompatibilities. 'Problem' taxa are later added to a<tei:lb/>relatively robust structure rather than being allowed to influence the topology of that structure at<tei:lb/>an early stage.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The Le Quesne test failure rates of the characters are shown in Table 12. An observed: expected<tei:lb/>ratio of 0-5 indicates those characters that have survived the test twice as well as could have been<tei:lb/>anticipated on a null hypothesis of random distribution of states of the characters (Le Quesne,<tei:lb/>1972). Fourteen characters having a ratio of 0-5 or better are shown in Figure 7 together with<tei:lb/>the cladograms described by them. Ten of the fourteen characters that best survive the test can<tei:lb/>be nested within a single cladogram (Fig. 7a). These ten states suggest relationships within<tei:lb/>Laticauda, the distinctiveness of the genus and also its possible relationships with some hydro-<tei:lb/>phiine sea snakes. Two character states (17-1 and 26-1) not entirely congruent with the arrange-<tei:lb/>ment suggest the relationships of Laticauda with particular groups of terrestrial elapines and one<tei:lb/>hydrophiine (Figs 7b &amp; 7c). Two other character sites (55 and 59) suggest possible relationships<tei:lb/>between American and Asiatic elapines (Fig. 7d).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>It may be concluded that those characters which best survive the Le Quesne test and which<tei:lb/>indicate the affinities of Laticauda with taxa outside the genus are:- 7-1 (marginal lower lip<tei:lb/>scales), 16-1 (muscular pulmonary air sac), 49 (absence of palatine medial wing). If Le Quesne's<tei:lb/>procedure is to be viewed as a weighting method (Arnold, 1981) these characters would be given<tei:lb/>relatively high weight in attempts to reconstruct a phylogeny of laticaudine sea snakes. Out of the<tei:lb/>various Wagner parsimony runs, run 4 (Fig. 5d) appears to be closest to the topology that would<tei:lb/>be preferred on the grounds of Le Quesne test results.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Conclusion</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Following a study of the morphological evidence using most methods of analysis (some parsi-<tei:lb/>mony runs, 'fours', compatibility) it may be concluded that laticaudines and hydrophiines do<tei:lb/>indeed seem to be comparatively closely related. There is however still a residue of conflicting<tei:lb/>data which tend to support McDowell's contrasting proposition that Laticauda is more closely</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>

         <tei:p>related to particular groups of terrestrial elapines than to hydrophiines (a topology suggested<tei:lb/>both by phenetic analysis and by some parsimony runs).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Evidence from immunology (e.g. Cadle &amp; Gorman, 1981; Mao et al., 1983) and karyology<tei:lb/>(Mengden pers. comm.) again indicates that the affinities of laticaudines lie with hydrophiines<tei:lb/>(and Australian terrestrial elapids) although samples tested so far appear not to have included<tei:lb/>some taxa which McDowell hypothesises to be closely related to Laticauda i.e. Calliophis,<tei:lb/>Maticora and Parapistocalamus.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The method of sample selection in the present study (Table 4) has led to the terrestrial sample<tei:lb/>to be rather biased towards Asiatic and American elapids. Given some of the recent evidence<tei:lb/>provided by biochemical and chromosomal data it would be instructive for future studies further</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>RELATIONSHIPS OF THE LATICAUDINE SEA SNAKES 1 59</tei:p>
         <tei:p>to investigate Australian terrestrial elapids to assess the degree to which the apparent affinity of<tei:lb/>this group with laticaudines and hydrophiines is corroborated by morphological evidence.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The present assessment of the relationships of laticaudine sea snakes must therefore be that,<tei:lb/>while the bulk of the evidence examined supports the affinity of laticaudines with hydrophiines,<tei:lb/>more information is required about the distribution of some characters in order to be able to<tei:lb/>resolve precisely the relationships of either sea snake group with particular groups within the<tei:lb/>largely terrestrial Elapinae. The classification recommended by McCarthy (1985) with Elapinae,<tei:lb/>Laticaudinae and Hydrophiinae treated as equivalent subfamilies of the Elapidae reflects the<tei:lb/>present lack of clear resolution in the relationships between the three subfamilies, a situation that<tei:lb/>hopefully will be improved as more morphological, biochemical and karyological information<tei:lb/>becomes available.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>The Littorinid molluscs of mangrove forests in the<tei:lb/>Indo-Pacific region</tei:p>
         <tei:p>D. G. Reid</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Periwinkles (family Littorinidae) are amongst the most intensively studied of marine gastropods,<tei:lb/>because of their worldwide distribution and abundance in the intertidal environment. This<tei:lb/>monograph is a comprehensive account of the taxonomy, biology and biogeography of the<tei:lb/>'Littorina scabra ' species complex, a hitherto poorly-known group of littorinids ubiquitous in<tei:lb/>mangrove habitats throughout the Indo-Pacific region. Twenty species are recognized, where<tei:lb/>most previous authors have distinguished only three. Detailed accounts of the anatomy of each<tei:lb/>species are given, with particular attention to the reproductive system, by which the species can<tei:lb/>be reliably distinguished. A key to shells is provided, and 100 figures and plates illustrate the<tei:lb/>range of shell variation, anatomical characters and geographical distribution of each species.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Drawing on comparisons with ten littorinid genera, evolutionary trends in the morphology of<tei:lb/>male and female reproductive tracts, sperm nurse cells, egg capsules, reproductive modes and<tei:lb/>radulae are discussed. These features are assessed as taxonomic characters, and the large<tei:lb/>literature on the morphology of the family is reviewed. Cladistic analysis of anatomical<tei:lb/>characters supports a reclassification of the Littorinidae, including placement of the 'scabra'<tei:lb/>group in the genus Littoraria.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In addition to molluscan systematists, this monograph should be of interest to marine<tei:lb/>biogeographers and ecologists working in the mangrove environment. In recent years much<tei:lb/>ecological and genetical research has been stimulated by the discovery of sibling species of<tei:lb/>Littorina in Europe. The Littoraria species have a similar potential in the Indo-Pacific, where up<tei:lb/>to ten species may occur sympatrically. Several of the species are polymorphic for shell colour<tei:lb/>and have already been used as material for the study of mechanisms of natural selection.<tei:lb/>1986, 240pp, 99 illustrations, 1 colour plate. 565 00978 8 35.00.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>

         <tei:p>The Bulletin of the British Museum (Natural History), instituted in 1949, is issued in four<tei:lb/>scientific series, Botany, Entomology, Geology (incorporating Mineralogy) and Zoology, and<tei:lb/>an Historical series.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Papers in the Bulletin are primarily the results of research carried out on the unique and<tei:lb/>ever-growing collections of the Museum, both by the scientific staff of the Museum and by<tei:lb/>specialists from elsewhere who make use of the Museum's resources. Many of the papers are<tei:lb/>works of reference that will remain indispensable for years to come.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Parts are published at irregular intervals as they become ready, each is complete in itself,<tei:lb/>available separately, and individually priced. Volumes contain about 300 pages and several<tei:lb/>volumes may appear within a calendar year. Subscriptions may be placed for one or more of<tei:lb/>the series on either an Annual or Per Volume basis. Prices vary according to the contents of<tei:lb/>the individual parts. Orders and enquiries should be sent to:</tei:p>

      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>A review of the genus Hydrocynus CuvidSf 1 1<tei:lb/>(Teleostei: Characiformes)</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Bernice Brewster</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Department of Zoology, British Museum (Natural History), Cromwell</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Contents</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Introduction ....<tei:lb/>Note on nomenclature<tei:lb/>Materials ....<tei:lb/>Abbreviations used in figures .<tei:lb/>Osteology of the genus Hydrocynus<tei:lb/>Character analysis .<tei:lb/>Interrelationships of Hydrocynus<tei:lb/>Revision of Hydrocynus species<tei:lb/>Key to species .<tei:lb/>Concluding remarks<tei:lb/>Acknowledgements .<tei:lb/>References<tei:lb/>Appendix 1</tei:p>
         <tei:p>163<tei:lb/>164<tei:lb/>165<tei:lb/>165<tei:lb/>166<tei:lb/>184<tei:lb/>189<tei:lb/>193<tei:lb/>201<tei:lb/>202<tei:lb/>203<tei:lb/>203<tei:lb/>205</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Introduction</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The genus Hydrocynus is a member of the Characidae (Order Characiformes) one of the largest<tei:lb/>families of freshwater fishes found in both Africa and the Neotropics. Hydrocynus is, however,<tei:lb/>endemic to Africa, (see p. 201 for details of distribution). As noted by Weitzman &amp; Fink (1983:<tei:lb/>340), the current classification of the characids is problematical. Roberts (1969: 443) divided the<tei:lb/>African Characidae into the sub-families Hydrocyninae, (to include only Hydrocynus) and<tei:lb/>Alestiinae (to include all remaining African characids). Gery (1977: 18) subsequently erected the<tei:lb/>family Alestidae to include Roberts' s sub-families Hydrocyninae and Alestiinae and restricted<tei:lb/>the Characidae to include only Neotropical taxa. Gery's (1977) work was not based on a cladistic<tei:lb/>analysis of the Characidae (sensu Greenwood et al. 1966) and the characters he used to define the<tei:lb/>Alestidae appear to be a mixture of plesiomorphies and apomorphies. Vari (1979: 342) included<tei:lb/>Roberts's Hydrocyninae with the Alestiinae because the latter represented a non-monophyletic<tei:lb/>assemblage with some of its members being more closely related to Hydrocynus than to members<tei:lb/>of their own sub-family. The current classification of the characids is clearly unsatisfactory but<tei:lb/>pending further phylogenetic analysis the concept of the Characidae sensu Greenwood et al.<tei:lb/>(1966) is followed here.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Species of Hydrocynus are pike-like predators, commonly termed 'tigerfishes' for their<tei:lb/>prominent dentition and dark lateral stripes. The genus was first described by Cuvier (1819) and<tei:lb/>characterized by him as follows:</tei:p>
         <tei:p>. . . par des dents peu nombreuses, longues, coniques, tres-aigues tranchantes par les bords,<tei:lb/>qui se croisent quant la bouche estfermee, et qui ne sontpas recouvertes par les levres. J'en<tei:lb/>trouve 7 a chaque intermaxillaire , ce qui fait en tout 14 pour la machoire superieure, mais<tei:lb/>les trois dernier es de chaque cote sont beaucoup plus petites que les autres; il n'y ena que 6<tei:lb/>de chaque cote a la machoire inferieure, et ce sont les deux dernieres qui sont petites. On<tei:lb/>compte done 8 grandes dents a chaque machoire. La gueule n 'est pas tres f endue, et le<tei:lb/>maxillaire qui n 'a pas de dents se recourbe transversalement sur la commissure. La langue<tei:lb/>et lepalais sont lisses, et les os pharyngiens garnis de dents en velours.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Bull. Br. Mus. Nat. Hist. (Zool.) 50 (3): 163-206</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Issued 31 July 1986</tei:p>
         <tei:p>163</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>164 B. BREWSTER</tei:p>
         <tei:p>With the exception of the teeth overlapping the jaws when the mouth is closed, these characters<tei:lb/>are applicable to all other members of the Characidae. Valenciennes (1849) added to this list, the<tei:lb/>presence of an adipose eye-lid, a feature found in other members of the Characidae, such as the<tei:lb/>African genus Alestes, in other characiforms for example the Neotropical families Curimatidae<tei:lb/>and Hemiodontidae and in unrelated teleosts, notably members of the Clupeidae and Mugilidae.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Boulenger (1907) redefined the genus Hydrocynus using the following principal characters:<tei:lb/>teeth in a single series; a moderately large maxilla which slips beneath the second infraorbital; an<tei:lb/>elongate body; a large supraorbital bone; a fontanelle present in juveniles, separating the frontals<tei:lb/>posteriorly and the parietals entirely; a large air-bladder with a posterior chamber about three<tei:lb/>times the length of the anterior chamber, and a strongly ossified skull. Again, with one exception<tei:lb/>(see below) these characters are applicable to other members of the Characidae.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Of those characters given by Cuvier (1819) and Boulenger (1907), only two are synapomorphic<tei:lb/>for species of the genus Hydrocynus; viz: moderately large maxilla which slips beneath the second<tei:lb/>infraorbital; uniserial teeth that overlap the opposing jaw. Roberts (1966, 1967 &amp; 1969) remarked<tei:lb/>on certain features such as jaw and tooth structure, tooth replacement, posttemporal fossae and<tei:lb/>presence of an orbitosphenoid tube. On the basis of these characters, Roberts (1966: 215; 1967:<tei:lb/>242; 1969: 443) postulated Hydrocynus to be related to the African characid genus Alestes.<tei:lb/>However, in the absence of any osteological definition of either Hydrocynus or Alestes, no<tei:lb/>evidence could be presented to either corroborate or refute Roberts's hypothesis of relationship<tei:lb/>between these genera.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>There is also confusion concerning the validity of certain currently recognized species of<tei:lb/>Hydrocynus. Boulenger (1909) recognized five species solely on the basis of meristic characters for<tei:lb/>which there was overlap in the ranges. Boulenger (1901, 1907) commented that two of the recog-<tei:lb/>nized species of Hydrocynus were so alike that he hesitated to retain them as distinct species.<tei:lb/>Svensson (1933) also noted the close resemblance between some species; Johnels (1954) also<tei:lb/>found it very difficult to distinguish the species of Hydrocynus and concluded that the taxonomy<tei:lb/>of the genus was unsatisfactory. Nonetheless, all five species have continued to be recognized<tei:lb/>despite various authors' misgivings concerning their validity.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The aims of this paper are three-fold. Firstly, to define the genus on osteological characters;<tei:lb/>secondly to present an hypothesis of relationship to other characiforms by adopting a cladistic<tei:lb/>approach, and thirdly, to review the taxonomy of the contained species.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Note on the nomenclature</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The application of the name Hydrocynus to the African tigerfishes has been a subject of<tei:lb/>controversy. Hasselquist (1757) described a taxon Salmo dentex from the Nile, which is now<tei:lb/>recognized as Alestes dentex. Forsskal (1775: 66) identified a Nilotic fish as Salmo dentex of<tei:lb/>Hasselquist. Unfortunately, this specimen is no longer extant (Klausewitz &amp; Nielsen, 1965: 12)<tei:lb/>but the description and meristic data given by Forsskal are without doubt those of a tigerfish.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Lacepede (1803: 272-273) further confounded the issue as he regarded S. dentex of both<tei:lb/>Hasselquist and Forsskal to be synonyms of Characinus dentex (Geoffrey Saint Hilaire in<tei:lb/>Lacepede 1803). Geoffroy Saint Hilaire (1809) wrote at length of the differences between<tei:lb/>Hasselquist's Salmo dentex and the specimen identified as the same by Forsskal, although he did<tei:lb/>nothing to resolve the problem. In 1817, Cuvier introduced a new genus, Hydrocynus which<tei:lb/>included in a footnote Forsskal's Salmo dentex (non Hasselquist). In a later paper, Cuvier (1819:<tei:lb/>353-357) gave Forsskal's Salmo dentex the name 'HydrocyonforskahliC in an attempt to resolve<tei:lb/>the confusion that had arisen over the identity of this fish.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Riippell (1829: 5) subsequently used the binomen Hydrocyon dentex for the tigerfish in<tei:lb/>question. However, Hasselquist's S. dentex is not available as the description precedes the<tei:lb/>starting point of zoological nomenclature (International Code of Zoological Nomenclature 1985:<tei:lb/>article 3). Salmo dentex (as of Forsskal) is not available as a senior synonym for Hydrocynus<tei:lb/>forskahlii under article 49 of the International Code of Zoological Nomenclature.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>A REVIEW OF THE GENUS HYDROCYNUS 1 65</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Materials</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The osteology and soft anatomy were studied using ethanol preserved specimens, double stained<tei:lb/>alcian blue/alizarin red 'S' transparencies (following the procedure detailed by Dingerkus &amp;<tei:lb/>Uhler, 1977), dry skeleton preparations, and radiographs.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Most of the material used in the preparation of this paper, is held in the British Museum<tei:lb/>(Natural History) (BMNH) collection, but additional specimens have been examined from the<tei:lb/>following institutions: Museum National d'Histoire Naturelle, Paris (MNHN): Musee Royal<tei:lb/>de 1'Afrique Centrale (MRAC); Institut Royal des Sciences Naturelles, Brussels (IRSN);<tei:lb/>Rijksmuseum van Natuurlijke Historic, Leiden RMNH) and California Academy of Sciences<tei:lb/>(SU). A detailed list of the material examined is given in the species descriptions, and outgroup<tei:lb/>material examined is given in Appendix 1 .</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Note on the measurements</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The standard length (SL) is measured from the tip of the snout, including premaxilla, to the<tei:lb/>region of the caudal peduncle approximating to the posterior margin of the hypural bones. Body<tei:lb/>depth is measured at the deepest part of the body anterior to the dorsal fin; head length is<tei:lb/>measured from the tip of the snout, including the premaxilla, to the posterior edge of the<tei:lb/>operculum; snout length is measured in the horizontal plane from the tip of the snout, including<tei:lb/>premaxilla, to the anterior margin of the eye. The interorbital width is measured as the greatest<tei:lb/>width of the frontals between the eyes. The 4th infraorbital is measured at the greatest width<tei:lb/>from the posterior margin of the eye to the posterior margin of this element. The depth of the<tei:lb/>premaxilla is measured in the mid-line from its junction with the supraethmoid to the base of the<tei:lb/>teeth. All measurements are made in millimetres and proportions are expressed as a percentage of<tei:lb/>the standard length.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The vertebrae are counted as abdominal (including the 4 Weberian elements) and caudal<tei:lb/>(including the first fused ural and preural centra).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Lateral line scale counts are taken from the first pore-bearing scale posterior to the supra-<tei:lb/>cleithrum to the origin of the caudal fin.</tei:p>
