         <tei:p>Osteology of the genus Hydrocynus</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The description is based on Hydrocynus forskahlii Cuvier 1819, type species of the genus. The<tei:lb/>terminology of Weitzman (1962) is followed, with the following exceptions: vomer for prevomer<tei:lb/>and intercalar of opisthotic (Roberts, 1969); supraethmoid for ethmoid, and epioccipital for<tei:lb/>epiotic (Patterson, 1975); anguloarticular for articular, and retroarticular for angular (Nelson,<tei:lb/>1973).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Kampf (1961) described the osteology and myology of H. forskahlii in a paper concerned with<tei:lb/>the comparative functional morphology of this species and Gasterosteus aculeatus, Spinachia<tei:lb/>spinachia and Esox lucius. I disagree with Kampf s identification and nomenclature of the cranial<tei:lb/>bones, those labelled prefrontal, prootic, epioccipital, ectethmoid and palatine by Kampf are<tei:lb/>identified here as supraethmoid, sphenotic, extrascapular, lateral ethmoid, and ectopterygoid<tei:lb/>respectively. In his figure 27 (p. 424) the bones labelled 'ectopterygoid' and 'endopterygoid' seem<tei:lb/>to be an endopterygoid that has fractured giving the appearance of two bones. Subsequent<tei:lb/>figures show this bone to be entire, and labelled as the 'ectopterygoid'.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Neurocranium</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In dorsal view the neurocranium is tapered, being widest posteriorly.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The supraethmoid is a 'T'-shaped bone (Fig. 1A), its stem being slightly shorter than the broad<tei:lb/>lateral wings forming the cross-bar of the 'T'. The anterior border bears a median rostral process,<tei:lb/>with a shallow depression in the vertical mid-line. This depression accepts the ascending<tei:lb/>premaxillary processes while the lateral wings articulate with facets on the premaxillae (see pp.<tei:lb/>171-172). The lateral supraethmoid wings are thick, blunt, transverse processes with a ventral<tei:lb/>curvature. A ligament connects the ventral portion of the lateral supraethmoid wing to the<tei:lb/>palatine (see p. 175). Each lateral supraethmoid wing rests laterally in the dorsomedial premaxill-<tei:lb/>ary facet and contacts the anterior tip of the ascending limb of the maxilla; the whole complex is<tei:lb/>tightly bound by connective tissue. Ventrally, the supraethmoid and vomer meet at a straight,<tei:lb/>transverse juncture. The nasals are rod-shaped and lie lateral to the supraethmoid.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The vomer is a rectangular bone with an elliptical, posteriorly directed process (Fig. 1C).<tei:lb/>Posterodorsally, the main part of this bone contacts the lateral ethmoid; the dorsal surface of the</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>A REVIEW OF THE GENUS HYDROCYNUS<tei:lb/>PT</tei:p>
         <tei:p>167</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MRP</tei:p>
         <tei:p>5mm<tei:lb/>Fig. 1 Hydrocynus forskahlii neurocranium: A, dorsal view; B, right side, lateral view; C, ventral view.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>posterior process contacts the parasphenoid. Ventrally, the vomer is formed into a 'V shaped<tei:lb/>ridge (Fig. 1C), although the development of this ridge appears to be variable.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The lateral ethmoid lies beneath the antero ventral portion of the frontal. The paired lateral<tei:lb/>ethmoid elements contact the supraethmoid cartilage in the mid-line. Each bone has a medially<tei:lb/>situated foramen for the passage of the olfactory nerve. In some specimens larger than 400 mm<tei:lb/>SL a tubular ossification extends posteriorly from around the olfactory foramen and contacts the<tei:lb/>posterior part of the orbitosphenoid process.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>168</tei:p>
         <tei:p>B. BREWSTER</tei:p>
         <tei:p>SO PA</tei:p>
         <tei:p>RTF</tei:p>
         <tei:p>5mm</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 2 Hydrocynus forskahlii neurocranium, posterior view.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The lateral ethmoid wing projects ventrally from beneath the anterolateral margin of the<tei:lb/>frontal, forming the anterior wall of the orbit.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The rectangular frontals form most of the cranial roof. They are flat dorsally with well-<tei:lb/>developed striae. The posterolateral part of the frontal contributes extensively to the long<tei:lb/>dilatator fossa and partially overlaps the fossa's medial border.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In juveniles and specimens up to 300 mm SL, there is usually a medial fontanelle separating the<tei:lb/>parietals and dividing the posterior margins of the frontals. Posteriorly, the fontanelle is<tei:lb/>bordered by the supraoccipital. In specimens greater than 300mm SL, the frontals abut one<tei:lb/>another posteriorly although the parietals remain separated by a fontanelle which is covered by a<tei:lb/>thick sheet of connective tissue.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The parietals contribute most of the posterior cranial roof and are comma-shaped bones (Fig.<tei:lb/>1A). Each parietal contacts the supraoccipital posteromedially the epioccipital posteriorly and<tei:lb/>the pterotic laterally.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The supraoccipital forms the posteromedial part of the cranial roof; it is deeply sulcate and<tei:lb/>contacts the parietals as described above; its posterolateral borders contact the epioccipitals.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The medial border of each epioccipital (Figs 1A &amp; 2) contacts the supraoccipital, while the<tei:lb/>ventromedial and ventral borders make extensive contacts with the exoccipital. Laterally, the<tei:lb/>epioccipital makes narrow contact with the pterotic and dorsally it contacts the parietal.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>There are three openings into the posttemporal fossa, situated dorsally, laterally and medially<tei:lb/>(Fig. 2). The dorsal opening is bounded by the parietal anteriorly, by the supraoccipital medially,<tei:lb/>and by the epioccipital laterally; the lateral opening is bounded by the pterotic and epioccipital;<tei:lb/>the medial opening is contained entirely within the epioccipital.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The exoccipitals are irregular in shape (Fig. 2). Each contacts the supraoccipital dorsally and<tei:lb/>epioccipital dorsolaterally, the pterotic laterally, the basioccipital ventrally and the prootic<tei:lb/>anteroventrally. Medially, both exoccipitals contact each other dorsal and ventral to the foramen<tei:lb/>magnum and cavum sinus imparis. The intercalar (see below) lies ventral to the suture between<tei:lb/>the exoccipital and pterotic. The exoccipital is pierced posterolaterally by the large foramen for<tei:lb/>the tenth cranial (vagus) nerve. Together, the ventral part of the exoccipital and the dorsal part<tei:lb/>of the basioccipital form the small, slightly inflated lagenar capsule (Fig. IB). In specimens up<tei:lb/>to 120mm SL the capsule is greatly inflated, a characteristic of all characiforms (see Fink &amp;<tei:lb/>Fink, 1981: 315). Species of Hydrocynus, however, attain a much larger size than most other<tei:lb/>characiforms, thus the apparently reduced inflation is a negatively allometric phenomenon.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>A REVIEW OF THE GENUS HYDROCYNUS</tei:p>
         <tei:p>169</tei:p>
         <tei:p>F PS OS PTS SPST PRO PT</tei:p>
         <tei:p>5mm</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 3 Hydrocynus forskahlii orbito-otic region, ventral view.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The intercalar has a flask-shaped outline (Fig. 1C) and lies ventral to the suture between the<tei:lb/>pterotic and exoccipital, contacting the prootic anteriorly. The intercalar bears a short, thorn-like<tei:lb/>posterior process which is connected ligamentously to the posttemporal (see p. 179).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The basioccipital contributes to the lagenar capsule (see above) and is bordered by the prootics<tei:lb/>anteriorly; the parasphenoid ventromedially, and the exoccipitals dorsally (Fig. IB &amp; C). It is<tei:lb/>divided bilaterally into bony lamellae which contact the parasphenoid ventrally. These lamellae,<tei:lb/>together with the prootics and posterior part of the parasphenoid form the floor of the large<tei:lb/>posterior myodome. In specimens up to 600 mm SL the myodome opens ventrally through a deep<tei:lb/>notch in the posterior section of the parasphenoid (Fig. 1C). This ventral opening closes<tei:lb/>ontogenetically, in specimens above 600 mm SL, the notched parasphenoid is progressively less<tei:lb/>evident and in the largest specimens examined is just visible.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Each pterotic (Figs IB &amp; C) forms the posterolateral portion of the cranium and contacts on its<tei:lb/>side, the parietal and epioccipital posterodorsally, the frontal anterodorsally, the sphenotic<tei:lb/>anteriorly and the exoccipital, intercalar and prootic ventromedially. The posterolateral surface<tei:lb/>of the bone contributes to the dilatator fossa, which continues posteriorly as a groove. The<tei:lb/>pterotic has a long posteroventrally directed spiny prpcess from which originate, anteriorly, the<tei:lb/>levator operculi muscle; ventrally it provides the site of origin for the protractor pectoralis muscle,<tei:lb/>and posterodorsally an insertion for part of the epaxial body musculature.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The ventrolateral surface of the pterotic is deeply idented by the hyomandibular fossa, which<tei:lb/>articulates with the posterodorsally situated hyomandibular facet.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The sphenotic contacts the frontal dorsally, and forms the anterior surface of the long, deep<tei:lb/>dilator fossa (Fig. Ib). The sphenotic spine forms the posterior margin of the orbit; ventrally it is<tei:lb/>expanded to form a short thick process (Fig. IB) from which the levator arcus palatini muscle<tei:lb/>dilator fossa (Fig. IB). The sphenotic spine forms the posterior margin of the orbit; ventrally it is<tei:lb/>bone (Fig. 3), the posteromedial edge of which contacts the sphenotic facet. The sphenotic facet<tei:lb/>articulates with the anterodorsal surface of the hyomandibula.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>170</tei:p>
         <tei:p>B. BREWSTER</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The prootics are large and irregularly shaped, forming most of the cranial floor and wall (Fig.<tei:lb/>1C). Anteriorly, each prootic is produced into a ridge which contacts the sphenotic laterally and<tei:lb/>the ascending process of the parasphenoid medially. The anterior face of the prootic contributes<tei:lb/>to the posteroventral wall of the orbit, and is pierced by the trigemino-facialis foramen. The<tei:lb/>anterior faces of the prootics, are connected to one another medially by a sheet of connective<tei:lb/>tissue that forms the anterior roof to the posterior myodome and separates it from the optic<tei:lb/>nerve.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The prootic is pierced by a number of foramina, of which, the circular jugular foramen<tei:lb/>continues posteriorly as the jugular groove across the posterior surface of the prootic and<tei:lb/>anterior margin of the exoccipital (Fig. 1C).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The subtemporal fossa is a shallow depression extending from the posterior part of the prootic<tei:lb/>to the suture between the prootic, exoccipital, intercalar and pterotic bones, the deepest part of<tei:lb/>the fossa lying at the point of this suture. The adductor hyomandibulae and adductor operculi<tei:lb/>muscles originate from the subtemporal fossa.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The pterosphenoid bones are roughly hexagonal in shape, contact each other anteromedially<tei:lb/>and form part of the posterior wall of the orbit. Posteromedially, each pterosphenoid is separated<tei:lb/>from its partner by the foramen for the optic nerve, which is floored ventrally by the horizontal<tei:lb/>sheet of connective tissue that extends between the prootics (see above).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The orbitosphenoid is a rectangular bone which contacts the pterosphenoid posteriorly and<tei:lb/>frontal dorsally; a tubular process (Fig. IB &amp; C), encloses the olfactory bulb and posterior end of<tei:lb/>the olfactory nerve. In some large specimens (400 mm SL or more), the orbitosphenoid process is<tei:lb/>sutured to a similar tubular process that extends from the lateral ethmoid posteriorly (see p. 167).<tei:lb/>The olfactory nerve is thus entirely enclosed.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The parasphenoid is a stout, elongate bone that contacts the vomer anteroventrally, and the<tei:lb/>prootic and basioccipital posterolaterally (Fig. 1C). The ascending processes of the parasphenoid<tei:lb/>are convex where they meet the prootic.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Jaws</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In lateral view, the lower jaw is elongate, roughly triangular and deepest posteriorly, where the<tei:lb/>dorsal surface is rather convex (Fig. 4). The dentary tapers anteriorly and is joined to its partner<tei:lb/>by an interlocking symphysial hinge (described by Eastman, 1917). The medial wall of the<tei:lb/>dentary contains a roofed over replacement tooth trench, that is pierced by large foramina</tei:p>
         <tei:p>1mm</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 4 Hydrocynus forskahlii lower jaw, right side, lateral view.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>A REVIEW OF THE GENUS HYDROCYNUS</tei:p>
         <tei:p>171</tei:p>
         <tei:p>immediately above each replacement tooth (see Roberts, 1967: 233). The trench is referred to<tei:lb/>herein as 'replacement cavities' in preference to 'trench' because the individual replacement teeth<tei:lb/>are compartmentalized as opposed to being compressed together in an open trench, as in other<tei:lb/>African characids. There are five tooth replacement cavities, the anterior four each containing a<tei:lb/>single large tooth, whereas the fifth may contain one or two small, usually tricuspid teeth. The<tei:lb/>replacement teeth are directed posterodorsally with their tips just protruding above the rim of the<tei:lb/>replacement cavity. The functional teeth are widely and evenly spaced along the dorsal border of<tei:lb/>the dentary.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Posteriorly, the dentary is sutured to the anguloarticular, the latter having a diarthritic joint<tei:lb/>with the quadrate. Meckel's cartilage lies between a small posterior process on the anguloarticu-<tei:lb/>lar and an anterior dentary process. The small triangular coronomeckelian bone lies dorsal to<tei:lb/>Meckel's cartilage on the anteromedial surface of the anguloarticular. The posteroventral edge of<tei:lb/>the anguloarticular is grooved to accommodate the small, wedge-shaped retroarticular.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The posterolateral surfaces of the dentary and anguloarticular are broadly recessed to<tei:lb/>accommodate the maxilla.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The premaxillae are elongate bones, each bearing 6 or 7 functional teeth, and are united<tei:lb/>anteromedially by a symphysial hinge. Unlike the halves of the lower jaw, the premaxillae do not<tei:lb/>interlock but have a syndesmotic connection. As in the dentary, the tooth replacement trench is<tei:lb/>roofed over and partitioned into usually five replacement cavities. The posterior cavity may con-<tei:lb/>tain two or more small, tricuspid replacement teeth; the other four cavities each contain a single<tei:lb/>large conical tooth. The replacement teeth are orientated posterodorsally within the cavities, with<tei:lb/>their tips just projecting beyond the rim. The functional teeth are widely and evenly spaced along<tei:lb/>the ventral border of the premaxilla.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The ascending process of the premaxilla is a short, square blade with a small dorsal notch (Fig.<tei:lb/>5). Posteromedial to the ascending process there is an articular fossa, which articulates with the<tei:lb/>lateral border of the supraethmoid process. A second, dorsomedially situated facet accommo-<tei:lb/>dates the lateral supraethmoid wing. This coupling of the supraethmoid and premaxillae forms a<tei:lb/>condylar joint permitting a limited dorsoventral rotation of the upper jaw.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Distally, the premaxilla is very deep and its dorsomedial surface is grooved to accommodate the<tei:lb/>ascending limb of the maxilla. Posteroventrally, the premaxilla and maxilla are ankylosed,<tei:lb/>although they can be separated in specimens up to 100 mm SL.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>1mm</tei:p>
         <tei:p>M mmlF</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 5 Hydrocynusforskahlii upper jaw, right side, lateral view.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>1 72 B. BREWSTER</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The maxilla is an edentulous, lamellar bone with an anteriorly curved, ascending process that<tei:lb/>rests on the premaxilla (see above; Fig. 5). The anterolateral face of the maxilla bears a small<tei:lb/>depression to which the maxillary-mandibular ligament is attached. Dorsomedially, the posterior<tei:lb/>margin of the maxilla has a small cup-shaped protuberance onto which insert some dorsomedial<tei:lb/>fibres of the adductor mandibularis muscle.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Teeth</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The smallest alizarin/alcian blue stained specimens examined (16 mm, 22 mm, 23 mm and 25 mm<tei:lb/>SL (BMNH 1981.2.17: 2600-2609)) display a transition from a conical to tricuspid dentition. The<tei:lb/>16 mm SL specimen is heterodont, with 14 upper and 8 lower jaw teeth, of which 2 on either side<tei:lb/>of the upper jaw and a single tooth on the lower jaw are tricuspid. The remaining teeth are all<tei:lb/>conical in the upper jaw and right dentary (Fig. 6), but are bicuspid in the left dentary. The<tei:lb/>conical juvenile teeth are simple, uniseriate, translucent and, in the upper jaw they contact one<tei:lb/>another, thus closely resembling the conical dentition of the dwarf characid, Lepidarchus adonis.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>This 16 mm specimen has 8 upper and 6 lower tricuspid replacement teeth, which lie medial to<tei:lb/>the functional dentition. Each tricuspid replacement tooth can be separated into three conical<tei:lb/>elements, thus suggesting that such teeth are formed by the fusion of 'juvenile' conical elements<tei:lb/>(see also p. 187).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The 22 mm SL specimen retains a conical tooth on each side of the upper jaw, which closely<tei:lb/>contacts the anterior second tricuspid tooth; all other functional and replacement teeth are<tei:lb/>tricuspid. Diastemata separate the functional teeth.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The 23 and 25 mm SL specimens have a functional and replacement dentition of 12 upper and<tei:lb/>8 lower tricuspid teeth. A specimen of 40 mm SL has conical teeth anteriorly in both jaws but</tei:p>
         <tei:p>B</tei:p>
         <tei:p>0-5mm</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 6 Hydrocynusforskahlii 16 mm SL specimen: A, premaxilla, right side, lateral view; B, lower jaw,</tei:p>
         <tei:p>right side, lateral view.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>A REVIEW OF THE GENUS HYDROCYNUS</tei:p>
         <tei:p>173</tei:p>
         <tei:p>each tooth has lateral cusps in the form of very small, nipple-like protuberances. The posterior<tei:lb/>teeth of this specimen are all tricuspid with well-developed lateral cusps.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In specimens larger than 50 mm SL the lateral cusps on the anterior teeth are difficult to detect<tei:lb/>but the teeth at the posterior margin of each jaw are always tricuspid (see also p. 187).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The attachment of the teeth to the jaw is type 1 (sensu Fink, 1981), that is they are completely<tei:lb/>ankylosed to the bone.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Circumorbital series</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The ant orbital is a small ovate bone tightly bound by connective tissue to the antero ventral<tei:lb/>margin of the supraorbital and the posterodorsal margin of the first infraorbital.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Theirs/ infraorbital, or lachrymal, is a triangular bone that lies lateral to the posterior third<tei:lb/>of the premaxilla. Dorsally it contacts the antorbital (see above), and ventrally the second<tei:lb/>infraorbital. The laterosensory canal runs through the orbital margin of the circumorbitals.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The second infraorbital is very large and covers the posteroventral surface of the upper and<tei:lb/>posterodorsal surface of the lower jaws, and lying lateral to the posterior surface of the maxilla<tei:lb/>(Fig. 7). Ventromedially, the second infraorbital is secured to the posterior margin of the<tei:lb/>anguloarticular by a sheet of connective tissue which also covers the maxillary-mandibular<tei:lb/>ligament. Posteriorly, the second infraorbital contacts the third infraorbital.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The third infraorbital is extensive, covering most of the cheek musculature. The fourth and fifth<tei:lb/>infraorbitals are also large but, unlike the others, are rectangular. The fifth infraorbital contacts<tei:lb/>the sphenotic spine anteromedially.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The dermosphenotic (sixth infraorbital) is ovoid, posterodorsally curved, and contacts the<tei:lb/>frontal dorsally, where that bone is indented by the dilatator fossa. The dermosphenotic covers<tei:lb/>the posterior part of the dilatator operculi muscle. The laterosensory canal bifurcates in the<tei:lb/>dermosphenotic; the anterior branch is continuous with the supraorbital canal, the posterior<tei:lb/>branch contacts the parietal canal and the ventral branch communicates with the canal of the 5th<tei:lb/>infraorbital.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The supraorbital is an elongate bone that lies anterior to the dermosphenotic. It has extensive<tei:lb/>contact with the lateral margin of the frontal and contacts the antorbital anteriorly (see above).</tei:p>

         <tei:p>DSP</tei:p>
         <tei:p>104-5</tei:p>
         <tei:p>AO</tei:p>

         <tei:p>B. BREWSTER</tei:p>
         <tei:p>P ECT END HS</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Hyopalatine arch</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The hyomandibula is roughly rectangular in outline, with a strong posterodorsally inclined shaft<tei:lb/>(Fig. 8). It has a long dorsal condylar articulation with the cranium, and posteriorly, a condylar<tei:lb/>articulation with the operculum. There are two points of contact between the hyomandibula and<tei:lb/>metapterygoid (Fig. 8). The anterior edge contacts the short posterior arm of the metapterygoid,<tei:lb/>and the anteroventral surface of the shaft contacts the posteroventral angle of the metapterygoid.<tei:lb/>An elongate and narrow, near crescentic foramen separates the hyomandibula and metaptery-<tei:lb/>goid between these points of contact (Fig. 8). The posterodorsal edge of the hyomandibular<tei:lb/>shaft is deeply grooved to accommodate the anterodorsal edge of the ascending arm of the<tei:lb/>preoperculum. Ventrally, the hyomandibular shaft lies medial to the preoperculum.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>There is a distinct anterolaterally directed dorsolateral spine on the hyomandibula (Fig. 8),<tei:lb/>which separates the dilatator operculi and levator arcus palatini muscles.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The metaptergoid is axe-shaped in outline (Fig. 8), with a short, rounded posterior arm, a<tei:lb/>broad ventral arm and a long, anteriorly expanded anterior arm; it contacts the hyomandibula in<tei:lb/>two places (see above). Its ventral edge is capped with cartilage and contacts the margin of the<tei:lb/>symplectic and the cartilaginous interface between the latter and the hyomandibula (Fig. 8). A<tei:lb/>strip of cartilage connects the posterodorsal margin of the quadrate to the cartilage of the ventral<tei:lb/>arm. The anterior arm lies lateral to the posterior surface of the endopterygoid (Fig. 8), and<tei:lb/>anteriorly contacts the posterodorsal edge of the vertical limb of the quadrate at a cartilage<tei:lb/>interface.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The symplectic (Fig. 8) is an elongate, tapered bone that lies in a groove on the medial surface<tei:lb/>of the horizontal limb of the quadrate. Posteriorly, it contacts both the hyomandibula and<tei:lb/>metapterygoid (see above).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The quadrate is an 'L'-shaped element (Fig. 8). The horizontal limb lies lateral to the<tei:lb/>anterodorsal surface of the preopercular horizontal arm. Posteromedially, there is a deep groove<tei:lb/>in the quadrate which accommodates the symplectic (see above). The ventrolateral surface of the<tei:lb/>horizontal limb of the quadrate is formed into a prominent ridge which, anteriorly, forms the<tei:lb/>lateral facet of the stout articulatory condyle. A strip of cartilage connects the anterodorsal edge<tei:lb/>of the quadrate to the palatine (Fig. 8).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The metapterygoid-quadrate fenestra is ovate and bordered by the metapterygoid and quadrate.<tei:lb/>The fenestra is filled by a sheet of connective tissue.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>A REVIEW OF THE GENUS HYDROCYNUS</tei:p>
         <tei:p>175</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The endopterygoid is a fan-shaped bone. Its borders lie medial to the metapterygoid, quadrate<tei:lb/>and to the cartilaginous strip connecting the quadrate and palatine (Fig. 8). A sheet of connective<tei:lb/>tissue joins the dorsal edge of the endopterygoid to the ventral border of the parasphenoid.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The ectopterygoid is a small, edentulous and thin disc, which lies on the anteroventral margin<tei:lb/>of the palatoquadrate arch (Fig. 8). Anteriorly, it contacts, and is ligamentously joined to,<tei:lb/>the posteroventral edge of the palatine. The ectopterygoid and palatine are tightly bound by<tei:lb/>connective tissue.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The palatine is an approximately rectangular block of bone, its cartilaginous head articulating<tei:lb/>with the lateral facet on the lateral wing of the supraethmoid (see p. 166). The palatine head is<tei:lb/>also ligamentously secured to the supraethmoid and to the ventral surface of the vomer.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Opercular series</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The operculum is roughly triangular, with an anteromedially situated condyle for articulation<tei:lb/>with the hyomandibula. Its ventral surface lies lateral to the suboperculum, whereas the anterior<tei:lb/>margin lies medial to the vertical limb of the preoperculum. Anteroventrally, the operculum<tei:lb/>contacts and is slightly overlapped by the interoperculum.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The preoperculum is crescentic, with a well-developed lateral sensory canal in both the vertical<tei:lb/>and horizontal arms (Fig. 9). The anterodorsal surface of the horizontal arm lies medial to<tei:lb/>the quadrate. A short ligament connects the anteroventral tip of the horizontal arm to the<tei:lb/>retroarticular. Ventrally, the horizontal preopercular arm lies lateral to the interoperculum.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The vertical arm of the preoperculum lies lateral to the hyomandibular shaft anteriorly, and to<tei:lb/>the operculum posteriorly.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The interoperculum is a long, anteriorly tapered bone (Fig. 9). The dorsal margin has two slight<tei:lb/>protuberances which contact the preoperculum posteriorly and the ventral surface of the<tei:lb/>quadrate anteriorly. Posteriorly the interoperculum lies lateral to the anterior edge of the<tei:lb/>suboperculum. Medially, the interoperculum is held in place by a connective tissue sheet which<tei:lb/>surrounds the opercular series.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The suboperculum is a long posteriorly tapered bone, with a small anterodorsal process (Fig. 9).<tei:lb/>The posterior and ventral edges of the subopreculum are poorly ossified.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Hyoid and branchial arches</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The basihyal is fragmented; anteriorly a dome-shaped cartilage bloc articulates with the larger,<tei:lb/>partially ossified posterior section of this element (Fig. 10). In some specimens larger than<tei:lb/>100 mm SL the anterior basihyal element is represented by two cartilage blocs; the anterior one is<tei:lb/>dome-shaped and articulates with a second and square cartilage bloc which, in turn, articulates<tei:lb/>with the larger posterior section (Fig. 11). The posterior element is connected to the anterior<tei:lb/>cartilage bloc(s) by dense connective tissue. A ligament connects the dorsolateral surface of the<tei:lb/>ventrohyal to the connective tissue of the posterior basihyal element and anterior cartilaginous<tei:lb/>element or elements.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The posterior section of the basihyal is spatulate, ossified posteriorly but cartilaginous<tei:lb/>anteriorly (Fig. 10); its anterodorsal surface lies below a large elongate, ovoid edentulous tooth-<tei:lb/>plate (Fig. 10). Posteriorly, this section of the basihyal articulates laterally with both the first<tei:lb/>basibranchial and with each dorsohyal.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The dorsohyal (Fig. 12) is an axe-shaped bone, which articulates anteromedially with the<tei:lb/>posterior basihyal element, just anterior to the articulation between the latter and the first<tei:lb/>basibranchial. The dorsohyal is surrounded by connective tissue which also connects this element<tei:lb/>to the posterior, ossified section of the basihyal. Posteriorly, the dorsohyal contacts the<tei:lb/>anterohyal at a cartilage interface. The dorsohyal contacts the ventrohyal anteroventrally.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The ventrohyal (Fig. 12) is rectangular, with a prominent anteroventral process. The anterior<tei:lb/>surface of the process is connected by a short, strong ligament to an anterolateral facet on the<tei:lb/>urohyal. The ventrohyal contacts both the dorsohyal anterodorsally and the anterohyal<tei:lb/>posteriorly at a cartilage interface.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The anterohyal (Fig. 12) is waisted in lateral view, with a finger-like process anterodorsally that<tei:lb/>lies between the dorso- and ventrohyals. A blood vessel enters the anterohyal posteriorly, passes<tei:lb/>through a dorsolateral canal and emerges anteriorly at the ventromedial surface of the finger-like<tei:lb/>process.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The posterohyal is quadrilateral; its ventral surface capped with cartilage which is continuous<tei:lb/>anteriorly with the cartilage interface between the postero- and anterohyals. The blood vessel<tei:lb/>associated with the anterohyal (see above) passes medial to the posterodorsal surface of the<tei:lb/>posterohyal, enters the large foramen which pierces the posterohyal dorsally, to run across the<tei:lb/>lateral surface of this bone and the cartilage interface before entering the anterohyal canal.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The interhyal is a short, rod of bone with a lateral cartilaginous cap, it articulates with the<tei:lb/>medial face of the posterohyal and is connected via a short ligament to the cartilage interface of<tei:lb/>the hyomandibula, symplectic and metapterygoid laterally.</tei:p>

The posterior process of this<tei:lb/>basibranchial lies in the mid-line between the tooth plates fused to the paired 5th ceratobranchials<tei:lb/>(Fig. 10).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Hypobranchial 1 is roughly hook shaped, and 2 is a triangular element.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The ossified elements of hypobranchial 3 are roughly triangular in outline and joined posteriorly<tei:lb/>by a large ' W'-shaped cartilage bloc, which is sandwiched between the posterior cartilage elements<tei:lb/>of basibranchial 3 ventrally and the anterior part of basibranchial 4 dorsally (Fig. 10).</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Pectoral girdle</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The extrascapula (Fig. 15) is an ovate bone that contacts both the parietal and pterotic.<tei:lb/>Posteriorly, it overlaps the posttemporal laterally. The sensory canal is bifurcate.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The posttemporal (Fig. 15) is triangular; dorsally, its apex lies posterior to the parietal.<tei:lb/>Medially, there is a short and thick process which is connected ligamentously to the intercalar<tei:lb/>(seep. 169).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The supracleithrum is an elongate bone with short and broad dorsal process which lies across<tei:lb/>the posteromedial surface of the posttemporal (Fig. 15). Ventromedially, and immediately<tei:lb/>posterior to the apex of the ascending limb of the cleithrum, the supracleithrum is connected to<tei:lb/>the basioccipital by Baudelot's ligament.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>The cleithrum (Fig. 15) is an 'L'-shaped element. The ascending limb contacts the medial<tei:lb/>surface of the supracleithrum dorsally and the anterolateral surfaces of postcleithra 1 and 2<tei:lb/>posteriorly (see below).</tei:p>

         <tei:p>There are six hour-glass shaped distal radials. The 3rd bifurcates posteriorly, and the 6th is<tei:lb/>cartilaginous.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Vertebrae</tei:p>
         <tei:p>There are 28-32 abdominal vertebrae, most of which support pleural ribs. The first four con-<tei:lb/>tribute to the Weberian apparatus and support the Weberian ossicles. Hydrocynus Weberian<tei:lb/>apparatus is like that described by Weitzman (1962) in Brycon meeki but the tripus is fan-shaped<tei:lb/>in lateral view, (not triangular) and the neural complex is crescentic (Fig. 17).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The other abdominal vertebrae have a large neural spine. The neural prezygapophysis of the 5th<tei:lb/>vertebra is a thick, nub-like process, whereas those of the 6th to llth vertebrae are elongate<tei:lb/>processes. The neural prezygapophysis of the 12th and all successive vertebrae, including the<tei:lb/>caudal elements, is a short, thumb-like process.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The neural postzygapophyses are short, triangular processes on both abdominal and caudal<tei:lb/>vertebrae.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Parapophyses are present on the 6th to 20th abdominal vertebrae. Each parapophysis supports<tei:lb/>a scimitar-shaped pleural rib in a posterior notch.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The pleural ribs are scimitar-shaped and each is supported dorsally by its respective lateral<tei:lb/>parapophyses up to and including the 20th vertebra.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The transitional development of the haemal arch and spine occurs between the 21st and 31st<tei:lb/>vertebrae (Fig. 18A-C), associated with which are 1 1 pairs of fine, elongate pleural ribs (Fig. 19)<tei:lb/>articulating with the posteroventral surface of the haemal process.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The 32nd and subsequent vertebrae are the caudal vertebrae, each with a fully developed<tei:lb/>haemal arch and spine.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Epineural and epipleural ribs</tei:p>

         <tei:p>There are 27 pairs of epipleural ribs, all of which project posteroventrally; apart from the first<tei:lb/>and last pair, all are forked. The epipleurals lie lateral to the last 1 5 pleural ribs and the series<tei:lb/>continues posteriorly to the base of the caudal fin.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Caudal fin</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Sexual dimorphism is exhibited in the caudal fin skeleton of all species of Hydrocynus. In females,<tei:lb/>the neural spines on the 2nd and 3rd preural centra are of approximately the same length, with<tei:lb/>expanded anterior and posterior flanges (Fig. 20A). The neural spines of preural centra 3 and 4 of<tei:lb/>males are more elongate than in females and curve backwards over the neural spine on the 2nd<tei:lb/>preural centrum (Fig. 20B).</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Dorsal and anal fins</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The dorsal fin comprises 2 soft unbranched and 8 branched fin rays and their supporting<tei:lb/>pterygiophores. The proximal radials articulate with small, rod-shaped, medial radials at a<tei:lb/>cartilage interface. The anterior proximal radial has greatly expanded anterior and posterior<tei:lb/>flanges.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The medial radials articulate with the small, triangular distal radials at a cartilage interface.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The anal fin comprises 3 soft, unbranched and 12 branched fin rays and supporting<tei:lb/>pterygiophores which are similar in morphology to those described for the dorsal fin (see above).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Character analysis</tei:p>
         <tei:p>On the basis of jaw and tooth structure, tooth replacement, three openings to the posttemporal<tei:lb/>fossa and the presence of a tubular orbitosphenoid process, Roberts (1966: 215; 1967: 242; 1969:<tei:lb/>443) considered Hydrocynus to be related to the genus Alestes, stating the former 'very probably<tei:lb/>had Alestes-\ike ancestors'. Therefore, the question to be answered is whether Hydrocynus and<tei:lb/>Alestes are sister-groups, or, whether Hydrocynus is either most closely related to one group of<tei:lb/>Alestes species or some other genus currently included in the Characidae. In order to resolve this<tei:lb/>problem, the derived features of Hydrocynus must be established.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Those features not included in the following discussion are either dealt with later (see p. 193-201)<tei:lb/>or are symplesiomorphies for characids.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Neurocranium</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The ethmoid</tei:p>
         <tei:p>According to Vari (1979: 277) the plesiomorphic condition of the characiform supraethmoid<tei:lb/>process is '. . . an anteriorly triangular bone extending between, and completely or nearly com-<tei:lb/>pletely separating the premaxillae.' Fink &amp; Fink (1981 : 306) make an alternative supposition that<tei:lb/>the absence of a distinct mesethmoid spine (supraethmoid process) and presence of a premaxillary<tei:lb/>articular fossa are primitive features for characiforms.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In Hydrocynus, the supraethmoid process is small and rather indistinct in comparison with<tei:lb/>other characins. It has a shallow depression in the vertical mid-line for articulation of the<tei:lb/>premaxillae (see p. 166). The Citharinidae and Distichodontidae also have a small supraethmoid<tei:lb/>process (Vari, 1979: 277-279) but since the anterior margin is modified into a trifurcate complex,<tei:lb/>it appears that reduction of the supraethmoid process has been independently derived in this<tei:lb/>lineage and Hydrocynus.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Contrary to the opinion of Fink &amp; Fink (op. cit.} my outgroup comparison shows that a<tei:lb/>distinct triangular supraethmoid spine is present and that a premaxillary articular fossa is absent<tei:lb/>in the majority of characiforms. I therefore concur with Vari {op. cit.) that the presence of a<tei:lb/>triangular supraethmoid spine and absence of a premaxillary articular fossa are plesiomorphic<tei:lb/>conditions.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The lateral supraethmoid wings are primitively triangular processes, whereas in Hydrocynus<tei:lb/>these are blunt, thick straight processes.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>A REVIEW OF THE GENUS HYDROCYNUS 1 85</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The morphology of the supraethmoid process and lateral supraethmoid wings of Hydrocynus<tei:lb/>are autapomorphic for this genus.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Dilatator fossa andsphenotic</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The posterior part of the dilatator fossa of the characiforms examined, just encroaches onto the<tei:lb/>anterior face of the pterotic. In Hydrocynus, the posterior part of the dilatator fossa is formed as<tei:lb/>a groove in the face of the pterotic. As this feature is not present amongst the outgroup<tei:lb/>characiforms examined, it is hypothesized to be autapomorphic for Hydrocynus.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The sphenotic process in Hydrocynus is produced laterally as a bluntly triangular shelf which<tei:lb/>slopes anteroventrally; extending medially from the process as a sharp-edged ventral strut.<tei:lb/>Examination of outgroup characiforms reveals that only Serrasalmus possesses a similar strut.<tei:lb/>The strut in Serrasalmus is perforated whereas in Hydrocynus the strut of bone is thick and<tei:lb/>imperforate. As in Hydrocynus, the lateral process in Serrasalmus is anteroventrally directed, but<tei:lb/>is spinous rather than shelf-like. Although the sphenotic in Hydrocynus and Serrasalmus has a<tei:lb/>generally similar morphology, there are marked differences in the shape of the spine, further-<tei:lb/>more, in the absence of any other shared, derived features between these genera, the similarity is<tei:lb/>reasoned to be independently derived and autapomorphic for each genus.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Parasphenoid</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Roberts (1969: 406) considers a straight parasphenoid to be primitive for characiforms, and the<tei:lb/>'strongly ventrally depressed' parasphenoid characteristic of many members of the Characidae<tei:lb/>to be a specialized feature. According to Vari (1979: 288) the characiform parasphenoid is<tei:lb/>plesiomorphically a flat, straight or ventrally convex element extending posteriorly to below the<tei:lb/>basioccipital. My outgroup comparisons suggest that the parasphenoid which is markedly<tei:lb/>convex ventrally, anterior to the ascending processes (which is typical of the Characidae) appears<tei:lb/>/to be the derived condition. I therefore, agree with Vari that the flat, straight parasphenoid is<tei:lb/>'plesiomorphic for characiforms.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In comparison with the parasphenoid in other members of the Characidae, that of Hydrocynus<tei:lb/>is straight, its morphology resembling that of the plesiomorphic type.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Jaw bones</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The plesiomorphic condition for the upper jaw of characiforms is immovably attached to the<tei:lb/>supraethmoid via large, triangular-shaped premaxillary ascending processes, which are almost<tei:lb/>entirely separated in the mid-line by the supraethmoid process (Weitzman, 1962: 32; Vari, 1979:<tei:lb/>271 &amp; pers. obs.) Lepidarchus adonis is exceptional within the Characidae in the extreme<tei:lb/>reduction of the premaxillary ascending processes and triangular supraethmoid spine. The<tei:lb/>supraethmoid region of the cranium of L. adonis closely resembles that of a 10 mm SL specimen<tei:lb/>of Alestes sp. (BMNH 1981.2.17: 1699-1734) in which the cranium is largely cartilaginous and<tei:lb/>the premaxillary ascending processes and supraethmoid spine are not yet fully developed.<tei:lb/>However, Lepidarchus displays reduction in many other morphological characters which<tei:lb/>suggests that reduction of the premaxillary ascending process and supraethmoid spine may be<tei:lb/>paedomorphic features.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In some Alestes species the premaxillae are united by a weak symphysial 'hinge' joint,<tei:lb/>comprising at the most, two interdigitating processes. The distal part of the characid premaxilla<tei:lb/>(excluding Hydrocynus) is tapered (Fig. 21).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The upper jaws of Hydrocynus (described on pp. 171-172) are seen to differ from those of<tei:lb/>other characids in the following and presumably derived features: presence of medial interdigi-<tei:lb/>tations along the vertical length of the premaxillae, forming a loose symphysial hinge; a short<tei:lb/>premaxillary ascending process; the distal part of the premaxilla dorso-ventrally expanded.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Another derived jaw feature in Hydrocynus is the presence of a double premaxillary (ethmoid)<tei:lb/>facet. The anterior facet articulates with the supraethmoid process whilst the posterior facet<tei:lb/>articulates with the lateral supraethmoid wing (see. p. 166).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Vari (1979: 271) reported the presence of a medial premaxillary articular fossa in the Disti-<tei:lb/>chodontidae and Citharinidae, which he hypothesized to be synapomorphic for these families.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>186 B. BREWSTER</tei:p>
         <tei:p>5mm</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 21 Alestes baremose premaxilla, left side, lateral view.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>However, the morphology of this socket differs from that in Hydrocynus in being a deep horizon-<tei:lb/>tal depression, open to its partner across the symphysis in Xenocharax (Distichodontidae). Other<tei:lb/>derived modifications of the premaxillary articular fossa amongst the citharinid-distichodontid<tei:lb/>lineage are: a conical pit; transversely directed pit, or, the fossa roof is reduced to a small shelf at<tei:lb/>the lateral margin of the depression (Vari, 1979: 271-272). The citharinids and distichodontids<tei:lb/>have a single articulation with the ethmoid rather than the double articulation of Hydrocynus. In<tei:lb/>view of these several differences and the absence of any other synapomorphies uniting these taxa,<tei:lb/>it is most likely that the anterior premaxillary facet of Hydrocynus is homoplastic with that in the<tei:lb/>Distichodontidae and Citharinidae.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The maxilla of the African characids is an edentulous comma-shaped bone, considerably<tei:lb/>thickened posteriorly. Posteromedially, the maxilla is grooved for the reception of the maxillary-<tei:lb/>mandibular ligament. In contradistinction, the maxilla of Hydrocynus, although edentulous, is a<tei:lb/>lamellar bone ankylosed to the premaxilla (Roberts, 1969: 415 and personal observation). In<tei:lb/>members of the neotropical characiform family Erythrinidae, the maxilla is also lamellar bone,<tei:lb/>but unlike that of Hydrocynus, it bears a large number of teeth and has an elongate, medially<tei:lb/>curved anterior ascending process. No other synapomorphies were found to unite these taxa,<tei:lb/>thus the apomorphous lamellar nature of the maxillae in Hydrocynus and the Erythrinidae is<tei:lb/>most parsimoniously explained as being independently derived.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The attachment of the maxillary-mandibular ligament to the anterolateral face of the maxilla<tei:lb/>(see p. 172) is unique to Hydrocynus.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The mobile articulation of the upper jaw in Hydrocynus is absent amongst other African<tei:lb/>characids, although found in other characiforms. Like Hydrocynus, the Citharinidae and some<tei:lb/>genera of Distichodontidae have a premaxillary articular fossa (see above), and lack a prominent<tei:lb/>premaxillary ascending process. The mechanism by which mobility of the upper jaw is achieved<tei:lb/>in Citharinidae and Distichodontidae is quite different from that in Hydrocynus (Vari, 1979: 272)<tei:lb/>and must be considered as being independently derived. A mobile premaxillary-supraethmoid<tei:lb/>articulation occurs in the neotropical characiform families Anostomidae, Chilodontidae,<tei:lb/>Prochilodontidae, Parodontidae and Hemiodontidae (Roberts, 1974; 412 &amp; 425; Vari, 1979: 272).<tei:lb/>The morphology of the premaxillae and ethmoid region in these families is dissimilar to that of<tei:lb/>Hydrocynus. In the absence of any other shared, derived characters, the premaxillary mobility in<tei:lb/>these South American families appears to be non-homologous with that in Hydrocynus.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>A REVIEW OF THE GENUS HYDROCYNUS 1 87</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Dentition</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Roberts (1967: 238-239) found the multicuspid teeth of characiforms to be compound elements,<tei:lb/>each cusp representing a separately formed conical element. The individual conical elements<tei:lb/>equate to a single tooth, a group of which are held together by a hard base of variable develop-<tei:lb/>ment. Roberts also thought that conical teeth are primitive for characiforms but the conical teeth<tei:lb/>of Hydrocynus are derived. My observations agree with those of Roberts regarding the formation<tei:lb/>of multicuspid teeth in Alestes and Hydrocynus. The tricuspid condition of Hydrocynus is evi-<tei:lb/>dently derived in each case by fusion of three 'juvenile' conical teeth and the 'adult' conical teeth<tei:lb/>are secondarily derived from the tricuspid teeth in which the median cusps are dominant (see<tei:lb/>p. 172). The juvenile conical dentition found in Hydrocynus and Alestes suggests the presence of<tei:lb/>conical teeth is the primitive condition for characiforms; the multicuspid dentition of non-<tei:lb/>juvenile Alestes and Hydrocynus of more than 23 mm SL but less than 50 mm SL is the derived<tei:lb/>condition. The conical teeth of Hydrocynus are considered to be secondarily derived from the<tei:lb/>tricuspid condition. This is in agreement with Roberts's hypothesis that the 'adult' conical teeth<tei:lb/>of Hydrocynus are derived.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The teeth of the Characidae are usually tightly bunched and contact one another in the<tei:lb/>anterior part of the relatively short jaws. In Hydrocynus the teeth are few in number, and are<tei:lb/>widely and evenly distributed along elongate jaws (see p. 171). Elongate jaws are characteristic of<tei:lb/>a number of supposedly primitive characiform genera, such as Hepsetus, Ctenolucius, Salminus,<tei:lb/>Hoplias and Acestrorhynchus, but with the exception of Hydrocynus and Acestrorhynchus,<tei:lb/>the teeth of these taxa are numerous, usually contiguous and are uniserial. The teeth of<tei:lb/>Acestrorhynchus are more numerous than in Hydrocynus and have large, irregularly situated<tei:lb/>diastemata.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Roberts (1969: 439) considered wide spacing of jaw teeth to have resulted from secondary<tei:lb/>elongation of the jaws. Apart from the wide spacing of the jaw teeth of Hydrocynus, there is no<tei:lb/>other morphological evidence to suggest that the jaws in this taxon are secondarily elongate, or<tei:lb/>indeed that this is the derived condition.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The conical 'juvenile' dentition as found in Hydrocynus is hypothesized to be plesiomorphic for<tei:lb/>characiforms (see above). Apparently such conical 'juvenile' teeth are the first to develop from the<tei:lb/>embryonic dental lamina, which is continuous, so that the emergent teeth all contact one another.<tei:lb/>In specimens of Hydrocynus larger than 16 mm SL, the dental lamina appears to be in regular,<tei:lb/>discrete units that each produce 3 tooth buds; these fuse as the tooth develops and form a tricuspid<tei:lb/>tooth.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Contra Roberts (1969: 439), I consider the wide, even spacing of the teeth of Hydrocynus to be<tei:lb/>the primary derived condition and not a result of secondary elongation of the jaws. Such wide,<tei:lb/>even spacing of the teeth was not found in any other characiform examined and is considered to<tei:lb/>be autapomorphic for Hydrocynus.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Roberts (1966, 1967) dealt with characiform tooth replacement in some detail. He described<tei:lb/>the replacement trenches of Hydrocynus as '. . . roofed over but . . . pierced by large foramina for<tei:lb/>each of the replacement teeth . . . ' For the reason given on p. 171 1 have referred to these trenches<tei:lb/>as 'replacement cavities'. The replacement cavities are most probably derived from a single<tei:lb/>trench because the posterior cavity contains two or more replacement teeth. Roberts (1967: 233)<tei:lb/>described the replacement trenches of five genera of African characids as greatly excavated in the<tei:lb/>lower jaw, with the replacement teeth lying considerably below the bases of the corresponding<tei:lb/>functional teeth. The replacement teeth of Hydrocynus lie horizontally, with their tips projecting<tei:lb/>posteriorly through the cavities. The replacement teeth are also horizontally aligned in the<tei:lb/>replacement trenches of the supposedly primitive characiforms Hepsetus and members of the<tei:lb/>Cynodontini, however, the knowledge of distribution of this character within the characiforms is<tei:lb/>such that no polarity can be assigned to this character.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Teeth are replaced on alternate sides of the jaw in Alestes species, but in several specimens of<tei:lb/>Hydrocynus that are in the process of replacing teeth, the entire functional dentition is lost<tei:lb/>simultaneously. In the jaws of a specimen of 25mm SL (BMNH 1981.2.17: 2600-2609) in the<tei:lb/>process of replacing teeth, the bone of the dentary surrounding the functional teeth and replace-<tei:lb/>ment cavities has been resorbed. The complete loss of the functional dentition is reported by</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>188</tei:p>
         <tei:p>B. BREWSTER</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Begg (1973) who noted high proportions of toothless Hydrocynus being caught by anglers. The<tei:lb/>stomach contents of some of these specimens included shed teeth, so it is assumed the teeth are<tei:lb/>often swallowed. Begg kept eight tigerfish in captivity for a month and found dozens of teeth on<tei:lb/>the floor of the tank at the end of this period; he presumed that all the fish had shed their teeth<tei:lb/>and successfully replaced them. Tweddle (1982) also reported the complete loss of functional<tei:lb/>dentition in a species he called Hydrocynus vittatus. Roberts (1967: 238) reported only Schizodon<tei:lb/>fasciatus (Characiformes: Anostomidae) as losing and replacing its entire dentition in one stage.<tei:lb/>Too few data are available on the occurrence of complete vs incomplete tooth loss amongst<tei:lb/>characiforms for any polarity assignment to be given to these characters.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Circumorbital bones</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Roberts (1969: 419) considers the characiform series to be primitively comprised of eight bones: a<tei:lb/>supraorbital, antorbital and six infraorbitals. All characids examined for outgroup comparison<tei:lb/>in this study have a circumorbital series comprising these eight bones as follows and as<tei:lb/>exemplified by Alestes dentex:</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The antorbital is spear-shaped (Fig. 22) and tightly bound by connective tissue to the antero-<tei:lb/>lateral margin of the frontal; it lies anterior to the supraorbital and the posterodorsal margin of<tei:lb/>the first infraorbital; the first infraorbital, or lachrymal is narrow and rectangular, and lies lateral<tei:lb/>to the ascending process of the maxilla; the second infraorbital is of similar shape, but lies<tei:lb/>posterior to the maxilla; the third, fourth and fifth infraorbital bones are large, cover the cheek,<tei:lb/>and are similar to those described for Hydrocynus (see p. 173); the dermosphenotic is a rectangu-<tei:lb/>lar bone with an anterodorsal process, and is bound by connective tissue to the lateral edge of the<tei:lb/>frontal and to the posterior part of the supraorbital; the supraorbital is an elongate, thick, rod-<tei:lb/>like bone lying anterior to the dermosphenotic; it contacts the lateral edge of the frontal and the v<tei:lb/>antorbital anteriorly.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The laterosensory canal runs along the orbital margin of the circumorbital series and bifurcates in<tei:lb/>the dermosphenotic, as described for Hydrocynus (see p. 173).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The first infraorbital of Hydrocynus is a broad bone relative to that in other characiforms, and<tei:lb/>lies lateral to the premaxilla as well as the ascending limb of the maxilla. The second infraorbital<tei:lb/>is also greatly enlarged, concealing most of the maxilla. Such enlargement of the first and second<tei:lb/>infraorbital bones is unique to Hydrocynus.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DSP</tei:p>
         <tei:p>SUO</tei:p>
         <tei:p>102-5</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 22 Alestes dentex circumorbital series, right side.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>A REVIEW OF THE GENUS HYDROCYNUS 1 89</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Suspensorium</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The hyomandibula of Hydrocynus is similar to that of other characiforms except for the presence<tei:lb/>of a dorsolateral spine (see p. 174). As this spine is not found in any other outgroup characiform<tei:lb/>examined, it is recognized as autapomorphic for Hydrocynus. Amongst other teleosts, there is a<tei:lb/>process on the posterolateral surface of the hyomandibula in the Clupeidae. This process projects<tei:lb/>as an anteriorly directed flange along the length of the hyomandibular shaft, and is unlike that<tei:lb/>found in Hydrocynus. In the absence of any synapomorphies suggesting a relationship between<tei:lb/>these taxa, the hyomandibular spine of Hydrocynus and hyomandibular process in the Clupeidae<tei:lb/>are proposed to be independently derived.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The modal (and presumably plesiomorphic) condition for the ectopterygoid in characiforms is<tei:lb/>an elongate, ovate, toothed bone which contacts the quadrate ventrally and extends along the<tei:lb/>anterior margin of the endopterygoid. The shortened, disc-like ectopterygoid of Hydrocynus was<tei:lb/>not found in any other characiform examined and is hypothesized to be autapomorphic for<tei:lb/>Hydrocynus. Roberts (1969: 418) states that the presence of ectopterygoid teeth in characiforms<tei:lb/>has a mosaic distribution. However, none of the African characids that I examined possess<tei:lb/>ectopterygoid teeth (see also p. 203).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Interrelationships of Hydrocynus</tei:p>
         <tei:p>From the above analysis, the following apomorphic characters have been identified for<tei:lb/>Hydrocynus, and will form the basis of a discussion concerning the interrelationships of the<tei:lb/>genus.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>1 . Supraethmoid process small but specialized with a shallow depression for articulation of<tei:lb/>the premaxillae. The supraethmoid of other characiforms is usually large and triangular,<tei:lb/>separating the premaxillae.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>2. Supraethmoid wings blunt, thick, straight processes. The supraethmoid wings of outgroup<tei:lb/>characiforms are plesiomorphically small triangular processes.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>3. Pterotic grooved posteriorly, such a groove is absent in all other characiforms examined.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>4. Sphenotic with a ventromedial strut, which with the exception of Serrasalmus (see p. 185) is<tei:lb/>absent in all other characiforms.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>5. Upper jaw articulates with the supraethmoid. With the exception of the Citharinidae and<tei:lb/>Distichodontidae (see p. 186), the upper jaw of characiforms is immovably attached to the<tei:lb/>supraethmoid.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>6. Premaxillae with synarthritic connection, such a connection is absent in other characiforms.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>7. Premaxillary ascending processes short, which in other characiforms are present as large<tei:lb/>triangular processes.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>8. Premaxilla deepened distally. The distal part of the premaxilla in other characiforms is<tei:lb/>tapered.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>9. Premaxillary facet present. This facet is absent in other characiforms with the exception of<tei:lb/>the Distichodontidae and the Citharinidae (see p. 186).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>10. Premaxillary dorsomedial facet present, this facet was not found in any other characiform<tei:lb/>examined.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>1 1 . Maxilla lamellar, edentate and ankylosed to premaxilla, whereas the maxilla of other<tei:lb/>characiforms is comma-shaped and thickened posteriorly, with the exception of the<tei:lb/>Erythrinidae (see p. 186).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>12. Maxillary-mandibular ligament attached to anterolateral face of maxilla. In other<tei:lb/>characiforms this ligament is attached to the posteromedial surface of the maxilla.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>13. Tooth replacement cavities present, as opposed to the open trenches found in other<tei:lb/>characiforms.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>14. Conical teeth, secondarily derived from a tricuspid condition. The teeth of other characids<tei:lb/>are multicuspid.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>15. Teeth widely and evenly spaced. In other characiforms, the teeth are usually contiguous, or<tei:lb/>have irregularly situated diastemata.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>190</tei:p>
         <tei:p>B. BREWSTER</tei:p>
         <tei:p>PROX</tei:p>
         <tei:p>PCSB</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 23</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Alestes dentex anal fin, posterior portion of vertebral column and swimbladder, right side,</tei:p>
         <tei:p>lateral view.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>16. 1st and 2nd infraorbital bones enlarged, in other characiforms these are both narrow and<tei:lb/>rectangular, with the latter lying posterior to the maxilla.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>17. Hyomandibular spine present. No such spine occurs in any other characiform.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>18. Ectopterygoid reduced in length. The ectopterygoid of other characiforms is an elongate,<tei:lb/>ovate bone.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Roberts (1966, 1967 &amp; 1969) suggested that Hydrocynus was derived from an Alestes-\ike<tei:lb/>ancestor, interpreting Alestes in the sense used by Boulenger (1907). Myers (1929), Poll (1976)<tei:lb/>and Gery (1968: 1977) recognizing that Boulenger's 'Alestes' is polyphyletic have, however,<tei:lb/>reinstated the genera Brycinus Cuvier &amp; Valenciennes 1849 and Myletes Peters 1852, taxa which<tei:lb/>Boulenger incorporated in his concept of Alestes. My continuing studies on the osteology of<tei:lb/>'Alestes' (sensu Boulenger) tend to confirm the opinions of Myers, Poll and Gery, that it<tei:lb/>incorporates at least three taxa each of which has closer relatives with species in other genera<tei:lb/>and furthermore, that one of these is more closely related to Hydrocynus than are the others.<tei:lb/>This latter group of species, which, pending a revision of the genus, is referred to as Alestes<tei:lb/>sensu stricto, includes the species: dentex (type species of the genus), baremose, ansorgii,<tei:lb/>macrophthalmus, stuhlmanni and liebrechtsii. It is characterized by the two following<tei:lb/>apomorphies:</tei:p>
         <tei:p>19. Posterior chamber of the swimbladder extends to the haemal spine of the third preural<tei:lb/>centrum (Fig. 23). In all other species of 'Alestes' and other outgroup characiforms<tei:lb/>examined, the swimbladder extends to the origin of the anal fin.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>20. The posterior proximal radials of the anal fin are short and curved anteriorly (Fig. 23).<tei:lb/>These radials in the anal fin of all other 'Alestes' species, and other characiforms examined,<tei:lb/>are straight, aligned anterodorsally and not conspicuously shortened.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Those characters which are thought to be synapomorphic features uniting Alestes s.s. with<tei:lb/>Hydrocynus are considered below, and will be compared with those in remaining species in<tei:lb/>Alestes sensu lato, and other characiform taxa.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Dilatator fossa and sphenotic process</tei:p>
         <tei:p>According to Vari (1979: 317, 319 &amp; 326) and Howes (1981: 15) the plesiomorphic characiform<tei:lb/>dilatator fossa is small, is formed by almost equal contributions from the frontal, sphenotic and<tei:lb/>pterotic and is roofed or partially roofed by the frontal. This form of the dilatator fossa is Type 1</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>A REVIEW OF THE GENUS HYDROCYNUS 1 9 1</tei:p>
         <tei:p>sensu Howes (1978: 56). In Hydrocynus, Alestes s.s. and Oligosarcus, the fossa is long and deep,<tei:lb/>with an extensive frontal contribution. It approximates to Type 2 sensu Howes (1978: 56).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The anterior margin of the dilatator fossa is formed by the sphenotic process, which in<tei:lb/>Hydrocynus and Alestes s.s. is thick and ventrally directed (see below) so that the fossa is broad in<tei:lb/>lateral view. In Oligosarcus, the sphenotic process is thin, sickle-shaped and directed backwards,<tei:lb/>thus the fossa is narrow in lateral aspect. Although the dilatator fossae of Hydrocynus, Alestes s.s.<tei:lb/>and Oligosarcus are long and deep, there are differences in the way the sphenotic, frontal and<tei:lb/>pterotic contribute to them, indicative of their independent derivation. Thus, the long, deep<tei:lb/>dilatator fossa shared by Hydrocynus and Alestes s.s. is thought to be synapomorphic for these<tei:lb/>genera.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The sphenotic process of characiforms is thin and directed ventrally forming part of the<tei:lb/>posterior wall of the orbit. In Hydrocynus and Alestes s.s. the process is well-developed and thick-<tei:lb/>ened ventrally. A similar form of the sphenotic process was not found in any other characiform<tei:lb/>examined, and thus is considered a derived feature shared by Hydrocynus and Alestes s.s.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Parasphenoid</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In the absence of any synapomorphies indicating that Hydrocynus is more closely related to any<tei:lb/>characiform taxon outside the Characidae, the straight parasphenoid characteristic of this genus<tei:lb/>is most parsimoniously explained as homoplastic with that of other characiform taxa sharing this<tei:lb/>feature.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Pleural ribs</tei:p>
         <tei:p>All species of Hydrocynus have 10 or 1 1 pairs of fine, elongate pleural ribs that articulate with the<tei:lb/>posteroventral surface of the haemal process and are associated with the transitional develop-<tei:lb/>ment of the haemal arch and spine between the 21st, or 22nd and 31st vertebrae, (see p. 182). In<tei:lb/>Alestes s.s. the counts are: ansorgii, baremose, macrophthalmus 8; stuhlmanni 9; dentex 10-11;<tei:lb/>liebrechtsii 10-11. The counts for Alestes s.l. are: minutus, stolatus 2; lateralis, longipinnis 3;<tei:lb/>carolinae, tholloni, dageti, leuciscus 4; nurse, jacksoni, opisthotaenia, senegalensis 5; nigricauda,<tei:lb/>sadleri, poptae, lemairii, grandisquamis, macrolepidotus 6.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In other characiforms, there are also pleural ribs articulating with the posteroventral surface of<tei:lb/>the haemal process. Counts on those taxa examined are: Charax 2; Rhabdalestes, Bryconaethiops<tei:lb/>2-3; Brycon, Triportheus, Scissor, Lebiasina 3; Acestrorhynchus 4; Hydrolicus 5; and Hepsetus 6.<tei:lb/>Exceptionally, Lepidarchus does not have any pleural ribs articulating with the posteroventral<tei:lb/>surface of the haemal process.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>On the basis of those outgroup comparisons within the Characiformes the higher number of 8<tei:lb/>or more paired 'posterior pleural' ribs occurring in Hydrocynus and Alestes s.s. seem to represent<tei:lb/>the derived condition and is considered to be synapomorphic for these taxa.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Orbitosphenoid tube</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Starks (1926: 167) described a bony orbitosphenoid process surrounding the olfactory tract and<tei:lb/>bulb in Alestes liebrechtsii and A. grandisquamis. Roberts (1969: 441) also observed this process<tei:lb/>in Alestes baremose, A. imberi, A. macrolepidotus, Bryconaethiops and Hydrocynus (see p. 170).<tei:lb/>Vari (1979: 341) added Alestes dentex and A. macrophthalmus to those taxa listed above, and<tei:lb/>suggested that a tubular orbitosphenoid process is an apomorphy shared by these taxa. Although<tei:lb/>Weitzman (1962: 20) and Howes (pers. comm.) noticed that Brycon also has a bony tubular pro-<tei:lb/>cess that encases the olfactory nerve, this is a process developed from the lateral ethmoid and not<tei:lb/>a homologue of the tube under discussion.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In addition to those taxa mentioned, the following also share this feature: Alestes sensu stricto;<tei:lb/>A. nurse; A. grandisquamis; A. macrolepidotus and A. jacksoni. It seems the tubular orbito-<tei:lb/>sphenoid process is either apomorphic for this lineage, or, that this feature has a mosaic distri-<tei:lb/>bution in the African Characidae. However, pending a revision of the genus 'Alestes' no polarity<tei:lb/>can be assigned to this character.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>192 B. BREWSTER</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Caudal neural spines</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Sexual dimorphism in the neural spine morphology of preural vertebrae 1 and 2 as seen in<tei:lb/>Hydrocynus (see p. 183), occurs in all members of the African Characidae examined, except for<tei:lb/>Lepidarchus. Vari (1982, p. 4), describes dimorphism in the neural spines of preural vertebrae 2<tei:lb/>and 3 in the neotropical genus Curimatopsis (Curimatidae). Here, the neural spines in males have<tei:lb/>greatly expanded anterior and posterior flanges, a condition unlike that in the males of African<tei:lb/>characid species which have a reduced neural spine on preural 2 and elongate, curved neural<tei:lb/>spines on preurals 3 and 4. This difference, together with the absence of any other shared, derived<tei:lb/>features, and furthermore since Vari (1983: 46) lists a series of characters indicating curimatids<tei:lb/>are related to the Neotropical family, Prochilodontidae, suggests this form of sexual dimorphism<tei:lb/>has been independently derived in the two groups.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>On the basis of the above analysis, the following characters are proposed as synapomorphies<tei:lb/>for Hydrocynus and Alestes s.s., which taxa are thus considered to be sister-groups (see Fig. 24):</tei:p>
         <tei:p>21 . long, deep dilatator fossa;</tei:p>
         <tei:p>22. well-developed, ventrally thickened sphenotic process;</tei:p>
         <tei:p>23. 8-1 1 pairs of fine, elongate pleural ribs articulating with the posteroventral surface of the<tei:lb/>haemal process.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Gery (1968) studied the classification of 'Alestes', (including Bryconaethiops microstomd),<tei:lb/>utilizing numerical taxonomy to analyse data obtained from a number of morphometric, meristic<tei:lb/>and colour features. His results, produced as a phenogram, show the group of characids referred<tei:lb/>to here as Alestes s.s. to be more closely related to Bryconaethiops than either genus is to any<tei:lb/>other species or group of species included in Alestes sensu lato. Gery's hypothesis that Alestes s.s.<tei:lb/>and Bryconaethiops are sister-groups is in contradiction to the relationship between Alestes s.s.<tei:lb/>and Hydrocynus proposed here. I have been unable to detect any osteological characters to</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 24 Synapomorphy diagram of relationship between Hydrocynus, Alestes s.s. and Alestes s.l.<tei:lb/>Open bars indicate autapomorphies, black bars indicate synapomorphies, numbers 24-26 are as<tei:lb/>follows: (24) unique upper jaw morphology; (25) absence of ectopterygoid teeth; (26) sexual dimor-<tei:lb/>phism exhibited in the posterior neural spines (see p. 183).</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>A REVIEW OF THE GENUS HYDROCYNUS 193</tei:p>
         <tei:p>support Gery's findings and consider Bryconaethiops to be more closely related to some other<tei:lb/>'Alestes' lineage than to either Alestes s.s. or Hydrocynus.<tei:lb/>The proposed relationship of Hydrocynus and Alestes s.s. is discussed further on pp. 202.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Revision of Hydrocynus species</tei:p>
         <tei:p>As stated earlier (see p. 164), there is some confusion regarding the validity of certain nominal<tei:lb/>species of Hydrocynus. Previous authors (Gunther, 1864; Boulenger, 1901; 1907) relied on<tei:lb/>meristic characters to differentiate between species. Four species of Hydrocynus are recognized<tei:lb/>here, based on morphometric, meristic and osteological characters.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Hydrocynus forskahlii Cuvier (Fig. 25B)</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Hydrocynus forskahlii Cuvier 1819 Memoir es du Museum d'Histoire Naturelle 5: 354-357<tei:lb/>Characinus dentex (non Lacepede) Geoffrey Saint Hilaire 1809 Description de I'Egypte 24: 236-244, pi. 4<tei:lb/>IHydrocynus vittatus Castelnau 1861 Memoire sur les Poissons de I'Afrique Australe: 65-66<tei:lb/>Hydrocyon lineatus (attributed to Schlegel) Bleeker, 1 863 Natuurkundige Verhandelingen van de Bataafsche<tei:lb/>Hollandsche Maatschappye der Wetenschappen te Haarlem Z. Verz. 18: 125</tei:p>
         <tei:p>NOTE ON THE SYNONYMY. The type specimen of Hydrocynus vittatus Castelnau 1861 is no longer<tei:lb/>extant (Boulenger, 1907: 106). Castelnau's description is undoubtedly that of a tigerfish; he gives<tei:lb/>the collection locality as Lake Ngami (Okovango drainage, Botswana) and H. forskahlii is the<tei:lb/>only species known to occur in that area. It therefore seems most likely that H. vittatus is a<tei:lb/>synonym of H. forskahlii.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>I have examined the type of H . lineatus, which is in poor condition comprising little more than<tei:lb/>the skull, caudal skeleton and skin. On the basis of morphometric and meristic data, together<tei:lb/>with radiographs of the skull, I am satisfied that H. lineatus is a synonym of H. forskahlii.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>NOTE ON THE HOLOTYPE. The holotype of H. forskahlii is in the collections of the Museum<tei:lb/>National d'Histoire Naturelle, Paris (MNHN 1691). In addition to this specimen, the following<tei:lb/>MNHN specimens A9705, A9708, A9707, A9709, A9710, A8548, A8607, A9711 and 1692 are<tei:lb/>listed by Bertin (1939) as part of a syntypic (sic) series of H. forskahlii and are labelled as such. I<tei:lb/>disagree that these specimens should be included in the type series for the following reasons:<tei:lb/>Specimens A9705 and A9710 were collected from the Nile by Joannis in 1834, subsequent to<tei:lb/>Cuvier's 1819 description. The following specimens were collected from Senegal A9707 by<tei:lb/>Heudelot; A9708, A9711 by Jubelin (the latter in 1828) finally, A9709 and 1692 by Leprieur,<tei:lb/>whereas Cuvier's description gives the type locality as the Nile. Specimens A8548 and A8607<tei:lb/>were collected from the White Nile by Darnaud in 1843.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>I note that Daget et al. (1984: 170) state only MNHN 1691, A9705, A9707, A9708 and A9709<tei:lb/>are syntypes.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The holotype is in poor condition, with few scales remaining on the body; the infraorbitals of<tei:lb/>the right hand side are loose and the right upper jaw is disarticulated.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DIAGNOSIS. The body of H. forskahlii (Fig. 25B) tends to be less deep (mean = 22-6 expressed as<tei:lb/>a percentage of the standard length) than that of its congeners, and the lateral stripes are<tei:lb/>prominent. It differs from H. brevis with which it is sympatric, in having slender gill rakers, which<tei:lb/>are approximately equal to the gill filaments in length.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. Based on the holotype and 175 other specimens, ranging in size from 51-2-442 mm<tei:lb/>standard length. Depth of body 17-2-27-8 (mean = 22-6) per cent of standard length, length of<tei:lb/>head 15-3-25-3 (mean =19-8) per cent. The dorsal profile of the head is straight. Interorbital<tei:lb/>width 6-1-10-8 (mean = 7-9) per cent, width of 4th infraorbital 5-1-7-5 (mean = 6-1); snout length<tei:lb/>6-1-10-2 (mean = 8-3) per cent; depth of premaxilla 1-7-3-9 (mean = 2-7) per cent of standard<tei:lb/>length.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>194 B. BREWSTER</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Gill rakers: Long and slender, approximately as long as the gill filaments; 8-10 (mode 9) rakers</tei:p>
         <tei:p>on the first ceratobranchial.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Scales: Lateral line with 46 (f. 10), 47 (f. 31), 48 (f. 30), 49 (f. 26), 50 (f. 31), 51 (f. 19), 52 (f. 12) or</tei:p>
         <tei:p>53 (f. 7).</tei:p>

         <tei:p>unbranched rays and 1 1 (f. 6), 12 (f. 132), 13 (f. 9) or 14 (f. 1) branched rays.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Teeth: In the upper jaw caniniform, large and widely spaced anteriorly, and smaller and tricuspid</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Osteology: Hydrocynus forskahlii differs from its congeners in the following osteological features:</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The lateral ethmoid wing is dorsally thickened and sickle-shaped in lateral view (Fig. IB). In<tei:lb/>the other Hydrocynus species, it is narrow and crescentic in shape.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In the region of the dilatator fossa, the frontal is produced as a lip that partially roofs the fossa.<tei:lb/>In H. brevis the dilatator fossa is extensively roofed by the frontal (see p. 196) and in H. goliath and<tei:lb/>H. tanzaniae, the dilator fossa is unroofed.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DISTRIBUTION. Nilotic drainage; Senegal, Guinea, Sierra Leone; Liberia, Ivory Coast, Ghana,<tei:lb/>Nigeria, Cameroun, Zaire drainage, Zambesi drainage including L. Malawi, Limpopo, the<tei:lb/>Pongolo River system, Okovango basin, L. Ngami and as far south as L. Sibaya N. Natal.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Hydrocynus brevis Gunther<tei:lb/>(Fig 25 A)</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Hydrocynus brevis Gunther 1864 Catalogue of Fishes 5: 351</tei:p>
         <tei:p>^Hydrocynus somonorum Daget 1954 Memoires de VI. F. A. N. no. 36: 116-117.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>LECTOTYPE. Gunther's description is based on three females in the collections of the BMNH. A<tei:lb/>specimen of 247 mm standard length, from Khartoum (BMNH 1862.6.17: 94) is designated here<tei:lb/>as the lectotype. The remaining two specimens, of 232 mm and 275 mm standard length (BMNH<tei:lb/>1862.6.17: 95-96) thus become paralectotypes.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>NOTE ON THE SYNONYMY. The types of H. somonorum are no longer extant (M. L. Bauchot, pers.<tei:lb/>comm.). I have examined the single non-type specimen labelled as H. somonorum and am satis-<tei:lb/>fied this is a specimen of H. brevis. Daget's description of H. somonorum agrees with that of H.<tei:lb/>brevis, and he comments on the close resemblance of the two nominal forms but considers them<tei:lb/>distinct species on the basis of the short head; very small eye; position of the dorsal fin; number of<tei:lb/>scales above and below the lateral line and the fewer vertebrae in H. somonorum. I find Daget's<tei:lb/>morphometric and meristic data for the types of H . somonorum lie within the ranges of those of<tei:lb/>H. brevis.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DIAGNOSIS. Hydrocynus brevis has a deeper body (mean = 24-4 per cent of the standard length)<tei:lb/>than the other species. The lateral stripes are less conspicuous than those of either H. forskahlii or<tei:lb/>H. tanzaniae. The infraorbital width is greater in this species (mean = 7-7 per cent of the standard<tei:lb/>length) and the gill rakers are short, approximating to one third the length of the gill filaments.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. Based on the lectotype 247 mm SL., paralectotypes 232; 275 mm SL and 83 other<tei:lb/>specimens, ranging from 41 -3-442 mm SL. Depth of body 19-1-29-6 (mean = 24-4) per cent of<tei:lb/>standard length, length of head 18-5-24-5 (mean = 21-3) per cent. The dorsal profile of the head is<tei:lb/>straight to the posterior margin of the parietals, the supraoccipital is slightly crested. Interorbital<tei:lb/>width 8-0-10-3 (mean = 9-2) per cent; 4th infraorbital width 6-9-8-9 (mean = 7-7) per cent; snout<tei:lb/>length 8-1-13-8 (mean=10-0) per cent; depth of premaxilla 3-0-5-3 (mean = 3-8) per cent of<tei:lb/>standard length.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Gill rakers: Short, approximately one third the length of the gill filaments, 7-10 (mode 8) rakers<tei:lb/>on the first ceratobranchial.</tei:p>
