         <tei:p>Osteology: The osteology of H. brevis differs from that of H.forskahlii in the following features:</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The region of the frontals which roofs the dilatator fossa is produced as a thin, flat ledge of bone</tei:p>
         <tei:p>(Fig. 26), this region of the frontals in H.forskahlii partially overlaps the medial border of the fossa,</tei:p>
         <tei:p>whilst in H. goliath and H. tanzaniae the fossa is not overlapped by the frontals (Fig. 28 &amp; 31).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The sphenotic process is very short and peg-like in contrast to the other species of Hydrocynus<tei:lb/>which have a well-developed, ventrally thickened process.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Hydrocynus goliath Boulenger<tei:lb/>(Fig. 27)</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Hydrocynus goliath Boulenger 1898 Annales du Musee du Congo Beige. Zoology 1: 23, plate xi.<tei:lb/>Hydrocynus vittatus (non Castelnau) Boulenger 1898 Annales du Musee du Congo Beige. Zoology 1: 24,</tei:p>
         <tei:p>plate x, fig. 2.<tei:lb/>Hydrocynus vittiger Boulenger 1907 Zoology of Egypt: The Fishes of the Nile. Egyptian Government</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Publishers; as a footnote on p. 106.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>NOTE ON THE SYNONYMY. Boulenger described both H. goliath and H. vittiger from specimens<tei:lb/>collected in the Zaire river system. He distinguished them on morphometric and meristic char-<tei:lb/>acters, in which there is some overlap. In his description, Boulenger stated H. vittiger had only 10<tei:lb/>upper jaw teeth, but the types have at least two additional teeth on each premaxilla at the rictus<tei:lb/>of the jaws, which Boulenger failed to notice. Since the osteology and external morphology of the<tei:lb/>two species are identical, I consider H. vittiger a junior synonym of H. goliath. The latter name<tei:lb/>has precedence because H. vittatus Boulenger 1898 was invalid as it was preoccupied by H.<tei:lb/>vittatus Castelnau 1861; also according to Daget et al. (1984: 171-172) it has been more widely<tei:lb/>used.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>LECTOTYPE. Of the six original specimens described, two were deposited in the collections of the<tei:lb/>BMNH and four in the MRAC, Tervuren. One of the specimens in the BMNH, collected by<tei:lb/>Captain Wilverth from New Antwerp, cannot be traced. The other, a female of 672 mm standard<tei:lb/>length (BMNH 1898.11.17: 4) is designated here as lectotype. The following MRAC, Tervuren<tei:lb/>specimens become paralectotypes: 77(117) Manyanga, coll. Wilverth; 166(267) Umangi, coll.<tei:lb/>Wilverth; 116 Leopoldville, coll. Wilverth and 1 17 Leopoldville coll. Wilverth.<tei:lb/>DIAGNOSIS. The lateral stripes of Hydrocynus goliath are the least distinctive within the species of<tei:lb/>this genus. There are between 2-8 small teeth at the posterior margin of the upper jaw which just<tei:lb/>protrude through the skin. The anal fin has 3 soft unbranched rays and usually 14 branched rays;<tei:lb/>in all other Hydrocynus species there are usually 12 branched rays.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>This species has a higher vertebral count of between 52-54 vertebrae compared with a range of<tei:lb/>45-51 vertebrae in other species. Similarly, there are a larger number of scales in the lateral line<tei:lb/>series, ranging from 53-58 scales, whilst the range for the remaining species is 43-55 scales.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Hydrocynus tanzaniae sp. n. fig. 30</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DIAGNOSIS. The lateral stripes of H. tanzaniae are distinct and it differs from all other Hydrocynus<tei:lb/>species in the presence of elongated 3rd and 4th dorsal and anal fin rays. It is distinguished from<tei:lb/>H. forskahlii and H. brevis by the presence of at least 14 upper and 12 lower jaw teeth and a<tei:lb/>lateral line scale count of 43-47 scales as compared with 46-55 scales in the other two species. The<tei:lb/>body is deeper (mean = 23-8 per cent of standard length) than that of H. forskahlii (mean = 22-6<tei:lb/>per cent of standard length) but does not quite approach the body depth of H. brevis (mean = 24-4<tei:lb/>per cent of standard length).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Hydrocynus tanzaniae has 3 soft, 12-13 (mode 12) branched anal fin rays compared with 3 soft,<tei:lb/>12-16 (mode 14) in H. goliath.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>obscures the vomer in dorsal view and is not markedly narrow.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Nomen dubium<tei:lb/>Salmo dentex (non Linnaeus) Forsskal, 1775 Descriptiones Animalium p. 67, number 97.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The type of Salmo dentex is no longer extant (Klausewitz &amp; Nielsen, 1965); however, Forsskal's<tei:lb/>description of this Nilotic fish clearly refers to a specimen of Hydrocynus. Salmo dentex is usually<tei:lb/>included in the synonymy of H. forskahlii but I consider it a nomen dubium since both H.<tei:lb/>forskahlii and H. brevis are sympatric in the Nile and there are insufficient data in Forsskal's<tei:lb/>description to assign his material to one or other of the two species.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Concluding remarks</tei:p>
         <tei:p>An osteological study of four Hydrocynus species has revealed eighteen features which appear to<tei:lb/>be autapomorphic for the genus.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In view of Roberts's (1966, 1967 &amp; 1969) idea of relationship between Alestes and Hydrocynus,<tei:lb/>species of the former taxon were examined in detail. During the course of this study it became<tei:lb/>apparent that Roberts's Alestes (sensu Boulenger, 1907) is a non-monophyletic assemblage, con-<tei:lb/>taining species having their affinities with species in at least three other genera. Of those genera,<tei:lb/>Alestes sensu stricto (see p. 190) is, on the basis of three synapomorphies, apparently the sister-<tei:lb/>group of Hydrocynus (see p. 192). However, the relationship of Hydrocynus and Alestes s.s. to the<tei:lb/>Alestes sensu lato group of species cannot be resolved until a detailed anatomical revision and<tei:lb/>phylogenetic analysis of all their constituent species has been undertaken.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Roberts (1969: 441) commented on the upper jaw morphology of African Characidae, and<tei:lb/>pointed out that the morphology of the premaxilla and maxilla differs from that of the Neo-<tei:lb/>tropical Characidae. Amongst outgroup Neotropical characids examined, I find the maxilla is<tei:lb/>typically dentate, large, ovate and lamellar, with a medially directed ascending process. The<tei:lb/>maxilla of the African Characidae (excluding Hydrocynus, see p. 172) is edentulous, pediculate<tei:lb/>posteriorly, and has a well-developed, anteriorly curved ascending process; possibly this<tei:lb/>represents the derived condition within the Characiformes.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The majority of Characiformes have two posttemporal fossae, although in concurrence with<tei:lb/>Van (1979: 341) I find that with the exception of Lepidarchus, all African Characidae have a<tei:lb/>third posttemporal fossa contained entirely within the epioccipital. A third posttemporal fossa<tei:lb/>contained entirely within the epioccipital occurs in the Neotropical characiform families<tei:lb/>Hemiodontidae, Paradontidae and Curimatidae (Roberts, 1974: 416 &amp; 425; Vari, 1983: 37).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The African characiform families Distichodontidae and Citharinidae and the neotropical<tei:lb/>characid tribe Cynodontini, also have a third posttemporal fossa, but it is bordered by both the<tei:lb/>epioccipital and exoccipital bones (Vari, 1979: 289; 1983: 37).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Vari (1983) gives a series of characters uniting the Curimatidae to the Prochilodontidae; the<tei:lb/>Hemiodontidae have a rhinosphenoid bone present, in common with various other Neotropical<tei:lb/>characids. In the absence of any synapomorphies indicating a close relationship between the<tei:lb/>Hemiodontidae, Parodontidae, Curimatidae and the African Characidae, the presence of a third<tei:lb/>posttemporal fossa contained entirely within the epioccipital is most parsimoniously explained as<tei:lb/>a homoplasy.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Sexual dimorphism in the posterior neural spines (see p. 183), occurs in all African Characidae<tei:lb/>examined (with the notable exception of Lepidarchus) and in one genus (Curimatopsis) of the<tei:lb/>Neotropical characiform family Curimatidae (Vari, 1982, 4). The morphology of these modi-<tei:lb/>fied neural spines in Curimatopsis, however, differs from that in the African Characidae (see p.<tei:lb/>192). Thus, it is unlikely this feature represents a synapomorphy uniting the African Characidae<tei:lb/>with the Curimatidae. The particular morphology of the modified neural spines, shared<tei:lb/>only amongst African characids, indicates that this character is synapomorphic for those taxa</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>A REVIEW OF THE GENUS HYDROCYNUS 203</tei:p>
         <tei:p>possessing it, namely Hydrocynus, Alestes sensu stricto, Alestes sensu lato, Bryconaethiops,<tei:lb/>Micralestes and Rhabdalestes.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The African characid genus Lepidarchus apparently lacks all those features hypothesized<tei:lb/>above as synapomorphies for African Characidae. It is, therefore possible either that Lepidarchus<tei:lb/>should not be included in the family Characidae, or since it shows reduction in many<tei:lb/>morphological features, this genus is paedomorphic and represents a miniaturized characid.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The unique upper jaw morphology, absence of ectopterygoid teeth (see p. 189) and secondary<tei:lb/>sexual dimorphism exhibited in the posterior neural spines are hypothesized to be synapomor-<tei:lb/>phies that indicate the African characids form a monophyletic subunit within the Characiformes,<tei:lb/>an idea already suggested by Van (1979: 342). However, since a number of African characid<tei:lb/>genera were unavailable for outgroup comparison, formal designation of such a category cannot<tei:lb/>be made at this stage.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>British Museum (Natural History)</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Tilapine fishes of the genera<tei:lb/>Sarotherodon, Oreochromis and Danakilia</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Dr Ethelwynn Trewavas</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The tilapias are cichlid fishes of Africa and the Levant that have become the subjects of<tei:lb/>fish-farming throughout the warm countries of the world. This book described 41 recognized<tei:lb/>species in which one or both parents carry the eggs and embryos in the mouth for safety.<tei:lb/>Substrate-spawning species, of the now restricted genus Tilapia, are not treated here.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Three genera of the mouth-brooding species are included though in one of them, Danakilia, the<tei:lb/>single species is too small to warrant farming. The other two, Sarotherodon, with nine species and<tei:lb/>Oreochromis, with thirty-one, are distinguished primarily by their breeding habits and their<tei:lb/>biogeography, supported by structural features. Each species is described, with its diagnostic<tei:lb/>features emphasised and illustrated, and to this is added a summary of known ecology and<tei:lb/>behaviour. Conclusions on relationships involve assessment of parallel and convergent<tei:lb/>evolution. Dr Trewavas writes with the interests of the fish culturists, as well as those of the<tei:lb/>taxonomists, very much in mind.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>580pp, 188 illustrations include halftones, diagrams, maps and graphs.<tei:lb/>Extensive bibliography. Publication 1983. 50 0565008781</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Cromwell Road</tei:p>
         <tei:p>London SW7 5BD</tei:p>
         <tei:p>England</tei:p>

         <tei:p>The Bulletin of the British Museum (Natural History), instituted in 1949, is issued in four<tei:lb/>scientific series, Botany, Entomology, Geology (incorporating Mineralogy) and Zoology, and<tei:lb/>an Historical series.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Papers in the Bulletin are primarily the results of research carried out on the unique and<tei:lb/>ever-growing collections of the Museum, both by the scientific staff of the Museum and by<tei:lb/>specialists from elsewhere who make use of the Museum's resources. Many of the papers are<tei:lb/>works of reference that will remain indispensable for years to come.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Parts are published at irregular intervals as they become ready, each is complete in itself,<tei:lb/>available separately, and individually priced. Volumes contain about 300 pages and several<tei:lb/>volumes may appear within a calendar year. Subscriptions may be placed for one or more of<tei:lb/>the series on either an Annual or Per Volume basis. Prices vary according to the contents of<tei:lb/>the individual parts.          <tei:p>Introduction</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Although characterised by relatively low levels of faunal diversity, the Arabian peninsula<tei:lb/>occupies an important geographic position at the boundary of three of the world's major<tei:lb/>biogeographic zones the Palaearctic, Afrotropical, and Oriental. It is a region of taxonomic and<tei:lb/>biogeographic discontinuity, not only for land molluscs (Mordan, 1980&amp;) but also for many other<tei:lb/>taxa. Whilst land snails show remarkable distributional stability through time (Solem, 1979), and<tei:lb/>thus represent a highly suitable group for biogeographic analysis, the value of their contribution<tei:lb/>to many such studies has been limited by the inadequacy of our knowledge of their taxonomy<tei:lb/>(Peake, 1978).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The non-marine Mollusca of southern Arabia, an area defined here broadly as that part of<tei:lb/>the peninsula south to the Tropic of Cancer but including the whole of Oman, were last<tei:lb/>comprehensively reviewed by Connolly (1941). To the end of this paper Connolly appended a list<tei:lb/>of 164 species of land and freshwater Mollusca from the region, with the pertinent comment that,<tei:lb/>of those species whose distribution is restricted to southern Arabia, 'half the names are probably<tei:lb/>superfluous and should be relegated to synonymy' (Connolly, 1941:40). Although a small<tei:lb/>number of junior synonyms were included, the list remained essentially uncritical. Subsequently a</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Bull. Br. Mus. nat. Hist. (Zool.) 50 (4): 207-271</tei:p>
         <tei:p>25 September 1986</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>208 P. B. MORDAN</tei:p>
         <tei:p>series of regional reviews has appeared covering South Yemen and Dhofar (Fischer-Piette &amp;<tei:lb/>Metivier, 1972), North Yemen (Verdcourt, 1974), Saudi Arabia (Mordan, 19800), and Oman<tei:lb/>(Smythe &amp; Gallagher, 1977; Mordan, 19806), but all are based essentially on conchological<tei:lb/>material.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Members of the family Enidae s.l. consitute by far the most numerous single element in the<tei:lb/>southern Arabian land-snail fauna, accounting for well over one-third of the total number of<tei:lb/>terrestrial species listed by Connolly. In an earlier paper (Mordan, 1984) the status of subfamily<tei:lb/>units within the Enidae was considered, with especial reference to the Arabian fauna, and the<tei:lb/>anatomical differences between the groups summarised. On the basis of these differences, particu-<tei:lb/>larly of the reproductive and pallial systems, two major groupings were recognised: the northern<tei:lb/>Enidae s.s. comprising the subfamilies Chondrulinae and Eninae, as defined by Forcart (1940);<tei:lb/>and the southern Cerastinae sensu Zilch (1959). These two groups have broadly non-overlapping<tei:lb/>ranges, but both are represented in southern Arabia. From the analysis it was also concluded that<tei:lb/>there was no evidence for the two being sister groups in a cladistic sense, and thus the Enidae s.l.<tei:lb/>could not be considered a monophyletic taxon. Formalising the status of the Cerastinae has,<tei:lb/>however, been deferred until there is a greater knowledge of the related orthurethran families,<tei:lb/>and consequently the present paper retains all three subfamilies within the Enidae s.l.. The bio-<tei:lb/>geographic implications of these conclusions were considered in some detail in the earlier paper<tei:lb/>and will not be repeated here. The name Cerastinae Wenz, 1923 is used as it has priority over<tei:lb/>Pachnodinae Steenberg, 1925, and has the type genus Cerastus Albers, 1860; Cerastus Dejean,<tei:lb/>1821 must be considered a nomen nudum under Article 12 of the International Code of Zoological<tei:lb/>Nomenclature.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The present paper is a review of the taxonomy of the Arabian enids using the admittedly<tei:lb/>incomplete anatomical information available. Whilst it is primarily concerned with genus- and<tei:lb/>species-level distinction, the opportunity is taken to discuss, in greater detail than was possible in<tei:lb/>the earlier paper, the anatomical characters relevant to the higher-level taxonomy of the Enidae<tei:lb/>as exemplified by the Arabian forms. No analysis of phylogeny has been attempted as this will<tei:lb/>form the subject of a further paper reviewing all cerastine genera.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The work is based primarily- on three collections in the British Museum (Natural History)<tei:lb/>[BMNH]: the collection made by Hugh Scott in North and South Yemen, recently supplemented<tei:lb/>by spirit material collected by Peter Heath in the environs of Taizz, N. Yemen in 1978-9; material<tei:lb/>obtained by the author from Dhofar in 1976; and a comprehensive collection made throughout<tei:lb/>Oman by Major Michael Gallagher from 1976 onwards.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Additional material, including many important types, has been loaned from the following<tei:lb/>institutions: Museum National d'histoire Naturelle, Paris [MNHN]; Royal Scottish Museum,<tei:lb/>Edinburgh [RSM]; Zoological Museum, Copenhagen [ZMC]; Museum d'histoire Naturelle,<tei:lb/>Geneva [MHNG]; Academy of Natural Sciences, Philadelphia [ANS]; National Museum of<tei:lb/>Wales, Cardiff [NMW]; and Zoologisches Museum, Berlin [ZMB].</tei:p>
         <tei:p>All conchological measurements are expressed in millimetres as follows: shell height x max.<tei:lb/>shell width x min. shell width; aperture height x aperture width; lip width (if developed);<tei:lb/>number of whorls,<tei:lb/>e.g. 23- 1x10-2x9-4; 9-5x7-0; lip 1-2; 6-7 wh.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Considerable problems have been encountered with Arabic place names and several localities<tei:lb/>could not be traced. In particular it has not proved possible to determine the precise location of<tei:lb/>'Senna', the type locality of Cerastus dinshawi Sykes, 1903; nor a number of Waterson's localities<tei:lb/>in Saudi Arabia and Yemen. In the lists of material, locality names have been copied directly<tei:lb/>from the original labels in almost all cases, but in discussing distributions in the text, spelling<tei:lb/>have usually been taken from the comprehensive edition of the Times Atlas of the World, 1968. In<tei:lb/>particular, the Yemen Arab Republic is referred to as Yemen and the Peoples Republic of<tei:lb/>South Yemen (formerly the Western Aden Protectorate) as South Yemen. Muscat and Oman is<tei:lb/>simply called Oman. Maps of south-western Arabia and of northern Oman showing the principal<tei:lb/>locations are given in Figs 1 and 2.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>DISTRIBUTION (Fig. 3). With the exception of synanthropic lowland sites (between 11 0-400 m<tei:lb/>above sea level) such as Yika, Ghafdi and Wadi Harabin (all cultivated habitats), the species<tei:lb/>appears to be restricted to areas above 1 500 m in the Jebel Akhdar and Jebel Harim ranges of<tei:lb/>northern Oman.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. Dextral, cylindrical conic with blunt, rounded apex; surface glossy with irregular<tei:lb/>transverse striae and numerous fine, denser spiral striae; protoconch smooth; umbilicus closed.<tei:lb/>Aperture oval with reflexed lip; callus often extending across parietal wall. Colour uniform pale<tei:lb/>brown to opaque white, protoconch darker brown.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Shell. Dimensions of Lectotype (Fig. 4a): 23-9 x 12-2x9-7; 12-1 x9-8; lip 2-2; 6-9 wh. Paralectotypes:<tei:lb/>21-2 x 11-2x9-5; 10-8 x 7-7; lip 2-0; 6-5 wh.; 21-6 x 11-9x9-0; 10-8x8-1; lip 1-6; 6-6 wh.; 21-9 x 1-9x9-3;<tei:lb/>10-8 x 8-9; lip 1-9; 6-6 wh.; 20-2 x 11-4x9-0; 1 1 -Ox 8-5; lip 1-9; 6-1 wh.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Although there was generally rather little variation in shell size, and more particularly shape<tei:lb/>(Table 1), a sample of three shells from Wadi Harabin in the eastern Hajar mountains were quite<tei:lb/>exceptionally small, the dimensions of the smallest adult shell being: 12-3 x 7-3 x 6-9; 6-9 x 5-6; lip<tei:lb/>1-2, wh. 5-3. This may be accounted for by the extremely low elevation of the site, 110m, where<tei:lb/>moisture levels are almost certainly considerably lower than at the more usual elevations for this<tei:lb/>species of between 1500-3000 m, even though the locality was a terraced cultivation.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Body. Uniformly cream in colouration.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Radula. (Figs. 5a-b; Table 2). Central tooth noticeably smaller than laterals, and ectocones<tei:lb/>of central relatively reduced. Marginal teeth with very blunt mesocone and wide, pointed<tei:lb/>ectocone which only becomes bicuspid in last three or four teeth in row. Basal plates quadrate<tei:lb/>and extremely broad.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Lung cavity (Fig. 6A). Similar in morphology to Imparietulajousseaumei, but occupying only<tei:lb/>a little over one whorl (Table 3); relatively longer kidney extending 0-95 times cavity length. A<tei:lb/>shallow groove runs from renal orifice along kidney almost to its apex. Mantle gland much less<tei:lb/>prominent than mjousseaumei; outer lung wall unpigmented.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Reproductive system (Figs 7A-B, 8C-D). Hermaphrodite gland comprising between five and<tei:lb/>seven lobes connected linearly to hermaphrodite duct, which bears clump of culs-de-sac and<tei:lb/>massively developed seminal vesicle (Fig. 8D). Talon short, simple and slightly curved; at its<tei:lb/>point of entry on spermoviduct is a large sac or caecum (Fig. 8C). Spermoviduct only slightly<tei:lb/>longer than free oviduct, which in turn is about twice length of vagina. Spermatheca with long,<tei:lb/>strong diverticulum which is continuous with basal stalk, and of equal thickness; spermatheca</tei:p>

         <tei:p>proper continues as thin side-branch with large, globular head. Inserting at base of vagina and<tei:lb/>atrium is well-develpped atrial retractor muscle.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Epiphallus inserting on penis apically with short flagellum at point of entry of vas deferens. A<tei:lb/>short retractor muscle inserts laterally on penis, and originates on lower lung wall. Within penis is<tei:lb/>a hollow papilla or verge occupying most of lumen (Fig. 7B), resembling Hesse's figure of the<tei:lb/>penial papilla of Mastus pupa (Hesse, 1933: fig. 2D).          <tei:p>Two spermatophores were recovered and were closely similar in size and shape (Fig. 9B).<tei:lb/>Spermatophore approximately 10mm long and 0-3-0-4 mm wide; pointed at anterior end and<tei:lb/>bearing a complex spiral fin at other. Rich corneous brown in colour. Both recovered from within<tei:lb/>spermatheca, and oriented with pointed end inserted into diverticulum.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>COMMENTS. The reproductive anatomy of omanensis appears sufficiently similar to that of Mastus<tei:lb/>to be included within that genus; although differing in lacking the short median epiphallar<tei:lb/>caecum of Mastus pupa, it does agree in possessing a relatively large penial papilla and a short,<tei:lb/>blunt terminal epiphallar flagellum.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Of related genera, Adzhar ia Hesse is characterised by an elongate flagellum on the epiphallus<tei:lb/>(Hesse, 1930: 158), and Chondrula Beck and Swertzowia Kobelt by apertural dentition, neither of<tei:lb/>which is found in omanensis.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Subfamily ENINAE Pilsbry &amp; Cooke, 1914</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DIAGNOSIS. Kidney orthurethrous; lung with short renal groove, but lacking rectal fold.<tei:lb/>Hermaphrodite duct with clump of culs-de-sac above seminal vesicle; spermoviduct with serous<tei:lb/>canal; penis with diverticulum.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>DISTRIBUTION (Fig. 10). Widely distributed in the northern Oman mountains from the summit<tei:lb/>of Jabal Akhdar (2980 m) to the foothills; also recorded at a number of lowland synanthropic<tei:lb/>habitats down to 100m in the eastern Hajar Mountains. It has not been recorded from Jebel<tei:lb/>Harim.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. Dextral, elongate pupiform, with tapering blunt apex; surface glossy with fine<tei:lb/>irregular transverse striae and occasional faint spiral striae; protoconch smooth and glossy.<tei:lb/>Distinct columellar fold in the body whorl. Whorls slightly rounded sutures shallowly impressed;<tei:lb/>umbilicus closed. Aperature oval; lip reflected and flattened, sometimes slightly reflexed at<tei:lb/>margin, continuous across parietal wall and internally notched at the parietal/palatal junction.<tei:lb/>Colour transparent pale brown with irregular transverse opaque white streaks becoming progressively<tei:lb/>thicker towards the aperature; protoconch brown; lip white.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Shell. Dimensions of Lectotype (Fig. 4b): 12-1 x 5-3 x 4-5; 4-8 x 3-9; lip 0-8; 7 wh. Remaining type series:<tei:lb/>10-7 x 5-5 x 4-9; 4-7 x 4-0; lip 0-8; 6-2 wh.; 12-4 x 5-3 x 4-6; 4-9 x 3-9; lip 1-0; 7 wh.; 1 1-2 x 5-3 x 4-6; 5-0 x 3-9;<tei:lb/>lip 0-8; 6-4 wh.; 1 1 -4 x 5-0x4-4; 4-5x3-7; lip 0-8; 6-4 wh.; 11-9x5-4x4-8; 4-8x3-6; lip 0-8; 6-7<tei:lb/>wh.; 10-9 x 5-4 x 4-8; 4-8 x 4-1; lip 0-8; 6-3 wh.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>This species appears to show rather greater geographical variation in shell morphology than<tei:lb/>Mastus omanensis, whilst maintaining considerable within-population homogeneity. Six living</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>ARABIAN ENIDAE</tei:p>
         <tei:p>219</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 10 Distribution map of Imparietulajousseaumei.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>populations were examined, using analysis of variance (Table 4), the results proving significant<tei:lb/>for all shell parameters.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Body. Surface pale, almost colourless, but top. of tubercules may be 'peppered' with grey or<tei:lb/>brown pigmentation.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Radula (Figs 5c^d). Teeth relatively slender in comparison with Mastus omanensis but only<tei:lb/>slightly fewer in number (Table 2). Unlike omanensis, marginal ectocones of jousseaumei<tei:lb/>commonly develop multiple cusps.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Lung cavity (Fig. 6B). Of the typical enid type with only a weak groove leading from renal pore<tei:lb/>to about half-way back along kidney margin. Kidney extends about 0-8 times length of entire<tei:lb/>lung. Venation very weak and bearly visible, but well-developed mantle gland present. Total lung<tei:lb/>cavity occupies 1 -8-1 -9 whorls (Table 3).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Reproductive system (Figs 8A-B, 11). Hermaphrodite gland composed of three or four lobes<tei:lb/>imbedded in digestive gland; duct bears clump of culs-de-sac above seminal vesicle (Fig. 8B), and<tei:lb/>terminates as talon formed from simple convolution of duct without any appendages. At point<tei:lb/>of entry of talon is a large sacculate structure (Fig. 8A) as in Mastus omanensis, similar to the<tei:lb/>albumen chamber of Orcula (Steenberg, 1925: pi. VIII). Albumen gland relatively short, about<tei:lb/>2/3 length of spermoviduct, and about half length of the simple spermatheca. Epiphallus greatly<tei:lb/>modified, bearing blunt caecum about half-way along its length and a short terminal flagellum.<tei:lb/>Spermatophore unknown but epiphallus internally complex (Fig. 11B). Epiphallus enters penis<tei:lb/>apically through rounded papilla.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Penis has six, symmetrically arranged longitudinal pilasters, each with enlarged portion at top,<tei:lb/>separated from main body of pilaster by slight constriction; penis waisted at this point. Tops of<tei:lb/>pilasters form six-pointed coronet on tip of everted penis (Fig. 11C). Retractor muscle inserts<tei:lb/>laterally about half-way down penis on opposite side to appendix; latter of typical pupillacean</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Fig. 11 Imparietula jousseaumei, Saiq, Oman, 1980: A, genital system; B, penis and epiphallus; C,</tei:p>
         <tei:p>everted genitalia. Scale 1 mm.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>structure but relatively short (about 3-4 times length of penis). Appendicular retractor muscle<tei:lb/>inserts slightly below top of basal portion and originates next to but separate from penis<tei:lb/>retractor, on lower lung wall; base of appendix without strong pilasters. Atrium short and<tei:lb/>undifferentiated internally.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>222 P. B. MORDAN</tei:p>
         <tei:p>COMMENTS. The absence of a spermathecal diverticulum, combined with the presence of a penial<tei:lb/>appendix and an epiphallus with a short terminal flagellum and blunt median caecum, which<tei:lb/>inserts apically on the penis, place this species within Imparietula. Conchologically it is quite<tei:lb/>distinct from any other named species of the genus (Forcart, 1940; Gittenberger, 1967). Smythe &amp;<tei:lb/>Gallagher's (1977: 223) record of Ena schahrudensis from Jabal Sham is erroneous, being merely<tei:lb/>an elongate form of Imparietula jousseaumei.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>PARAMASTUS P. Hesse, 1933<tei:lb/>TYPE SPECIES. Bulimus episomus Bourguignat [monotypy].</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Paramastus episomus (Bourguignat, 1857)</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Buliminus episomus Bourguignat, 1857: 10, pi. 3, figs 5-7. [Nazareth].</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Buliminus hedjazicus Bourguignat, 1882: 24, pi. 1, fig. 12. [between Jeddah and Mecca].</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Paramastus episoma hedjazicus Bourguignat. Zilch, 1951: 42, pi. 3a, fig. 16.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>? Paramastus episomus Bourguignat. Mordan, 1980:360, fig. 21.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>TYPE MATERIAL. Holotype, Between Jeddah and Mecca, Saudi Arabia, MHNG.<tei:lb/>DISTRIBUTION. Known only from the type locality (above).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. Dextral, pupiform; surface with weak, irregular radial striae and weaker, but more<tei:lb/>regular, spiral striae, and occasional malleations, especially on body whorl; umbilicus closed.<tei:lb/>Aperture elongate oval, with flared, sharp lip; small parietal denticle near palatal/parietal<tei:lb/>boarder, and weak, broad columellar fold. Shell opaque white, but periostracum lacking.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Shell. Dimensions of holotype (Fig. 4c): 15-2 x 6-5 x 6-4; 5-8 x 4-3; 6-7 wh.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Anatomy. Unknown.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>COMMENTS. See below under P. sabaeanus.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Paramastus sabaeanus (Bourguignat, 1876)</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Bulimus sabaeanus Bourguignat, 1876:19 [Sabeens, near Mareb.]; Bourguignat, 1882:23, fig. 14.<tei:lb/>Buliminus (Petraeus) sabaeanus Bourguignat. Rossmassler, 1888:31, pi. 99, fig. 560. non sabaeanus (Bgt.)<tei:lb/>Rolle &amp; Kobelt, in Rossmassler, 1895-97:58, pi. 1 1, figs 8-11.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>TYPE MATERIAL. Lectotype (here selected) and Paralectotype, Sabeens, near Mareb, Yemen,<tei:lb/>MHNG.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DISTRIBUTION. Known only from the type locality (above).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. As for hedjazicus, but aperture relatively larger and rounder, with flattened lip;<tei:lb/>spiral striae and parietal denticle absent.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Shell. Dimensions of lectotype (Fig. 4d): 23-1x10-2x9-4; 9-5x7-0; 7-7 wh. Paralectotype:<tei:lb/>21-7 x 10-0 x 8-8; 9-0 x 7-2; 7-5 wh.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Anatomy. Unknown.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>COMMENTS. The positions of this and the previous taxon at both the specific and generic levels is<tei:lb/>uncertain. Until recently both would have been referred to Paramastus Hesse on conchological<tei:lb/>and geographic grounds. This is still the most likely eventuality and they are provisionally<tei:lb/>retained here. However, Heller (1971) has demonstrated that Paramastus sensu lato comprises<tei:lb/>two groups: one, which he named Cyrenaicus, lacking a penial appendix and with a distribution<tei:lb/>centered on Cyrenaica; and Paramastus sensu stricto possessing a penial appendix and containing<tei:lb/>with certainty only two species (P. episomus (Bourguignat, 1857) and P. cyprius Zilch, 1959).<tei:lb/>The true affinity of the Arabian snails is thus in doubt. Zilch (1951) relegated hedjazicus to a<tei:lb/>subspecies of episomus and this is retained here. Certainly both Arabian taxa fall broadly within<tei:lb/>the wide morphological range of episomus. Without anatomical information further speculation<tei:lb/>is valueless.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>ARABIAN ENIDAE 223</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Subfamily CERASTINAEWenz, 1923</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DIAGNOSIS. Kidney orthurethrous; lung with well-developed renal and rectal folds.<tei:lb/>Hermaphrodite duct without culs-de-sac, and spermoviduct without serous canal. Penis with<tei:lb/>appendix.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>CERASTUS Albers, 1 860<tei:lb/>TYPE SPECIES. Bulimus distans Pfeiffer [orig. desig.]</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DIAGNOSIS. Shell lip undifferentiated or weakly flared. -Penis with long, pointed caecum; penis<tei:lb/>retractor inserts on epiphallus. Penial appendix with moderately developed papilla; appendicular<tei:lb/>retractor inserts on enclosed basal portion of central stalk. Penial and appendicular retractors<tei:lb/>united prior to attachment to lung wall.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Cerastus schweinfurthi (Martens, 1895)</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Buliminus schweinfurthi Martens, 1895:129. [Menaha].</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Buliminus schweinfurthi var. gracilior Martens, 1895:129. [Menaha].</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Buliminus (Cerastus) schweinfurthi Martens. Kobelt, 1902:893, pi. 127, figs 19-21; Rossmassler, 1903:51, pi.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>291, fig. 1862.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Buliminus (Cerastus) schweinfurthi var. menahensis Kobelt, 1902:894, pi. 127, figs 22, 23. [Menaha].<tei:lb/>Buliminus (Cerastus) schweinfurthi var. menahensis Kobelt. Rossmassler, 1903:52, pi. 291, fig. 1863.<tei:lb/>Cerastus schweinfurthi Kobelt. Connolly, 1941:26.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Cerastus schweinfurthi var. maxima Connolly, 1941:26. [pass between Saiyani and Ibb].<tei:lb/>Cerastua (Euryptyxis) schweinfurthi Martens. Hesse, 1933:220.<tei:lb/>Cerastua schweinfurthiiKobell. Verdcourt, 1974:5.<tei:lb/>Cerastua schweinfurthii var. maxima Connolly. Verdcourt, 1974:5.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>TYPE MATERIAL. Lectotype (here selected, BMNH 1895.8.20.1), Paralectotype (BMNH<tei:lb/>1 895.8.20.2), and 5 possible Paralectotypes (BMNH 1 937. 1 2.30.2 1 27-3 1) ofschweinfirthi Martens,<tei:lb/>Menaha, Yemen, leg. Schweinfurth. Holotype and 6 Paratypes (BMNH 1939.4.19.34-40) of var.<tei:lb/>maxima Connolly, Naqil Mahrras Pass, between Seyani to Ibb, Yemen, leg. Scott, 30.xii.1937,<tei:lb/>c. 2200 m.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>OTHER MATERIAL. Yemen: Taizz District, 1250-1550 m. leg. H. Scott, BMNH 1939.4.19.53-54 (2 specs.);<tei:lb/>Wadi Dhulla, 10 km. NW. Saana, 2500 m., Leg. H. Scott, BNMH 1939.4.19.41^2 (2 specs); Thawilah, leg.<tei:lb/>L. Merucci, vi.1945, BMNH 1956.1 1.26-30 (5 specs + 20 unregistered); 8 km from Taizz, leg. M. Brunt &amp; J.<tei:lb/>Mather, (1 spec); Wadi Shabau, leg. Brunt &amp; Mather, (2 specs.); As Saiyani, 32 km N Taizz, leg. Lavranos,<tei:lb/>1977, MNHN (9 specs); 32 km. N. Sanaa, leg. M Al-Safadi, (1 spec); Jebel Sumara, north of Ibb, 2950 m.,<tei:lb/>leg. M. Lavranos, 1974, MNHN, (5 specs., 1 reconstituted and dissected). South Yemen: Jebel Harir,<tei:lb/>x-xi.1937, 2300m., 1 paratype maxima Connolly, BMNH 1939.4.19.27, leg. H. Scott.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DISTRIBUTION (Fig. 12). The species extends from near Dhala in South Yemen westwards to the<tei:lb/>region of Taizz, and northwards to the type locality of Menaha and to Sana. It is thus the most<tei:lb/>widely distributed Arabian Cerastus, and by far the most abundant in museum collections.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. Dextral; acuminate ovate, apex blunt, sutures shallow. Protoconch smooth, later<tei:lb/>with regular radial ribs; radial sculpture becoming progressively weaker, and in combination<tei:lb/>with spiral striae giving glossy appearance. Aperture not flared, slightly thickened internally,<tei:lb/>callus extending across parietal wall. Periostracum corneous olive-brown, variegated in<tei:lb/>occasional radial streaks of darker brown.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Shell. Dimensions of Lectotype, schweinfurthi von Martens (Fig. 13a): 31-4 x 17-4 x 1-1; 17-0x11-9;<tei:lb/>7-lwh. Dimensions of holotype, var maxima Connolly (Fig. 13b): 47-3 x 26-5 x 23-4; 26-2 x 18-5; 7-8 wh.<tei:lb/>Largest specimen, Wadi Shabau, Yemen, leg. Brunt &amp; Mather: 42-7 x 25-2 x 20-6; 24-4 x 17-3; 7-3 wh.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Cerastus schweinfurthi shows considerable variation in both shell size and shape, particularly<tei:lb/>in the region between Taizz and Ibb in Yemen, from where Connolly described var maxima.<tei:lb/>There was a general correlation between shell size and thickness, the very largest shells being up<tei:lb/>to 1-8 mm thick.</tei:p>

      </tei:div>

         <tei:p>Fig. 13 a, Lectotype of Buliminus schweinfurthi Martens, BMNH 1895.8.20.1, x 1-5; b, holotype of<tei:lb/>Cerastusschweinfurthivar. maxima Connolly, BMNH 1939.4.19.34, x 1-5; c, paratype of C. schwein-<tei:lb/>furthi var. maxima, BMNH 1939.4.19.27, x 1-5; d, lectotype of C. girwanensis Connolly, BMNH<tei:lb/>1934.4.19.9, x 3; e, holotype of Cerastua albonotata Verdcourt, BMNH 19731, x 1-5; f, holotype of<tei:lb/>Polychordia pulcherrima Connolly, BMNH 1939.4.19.55, x 3;g, holotype of Cerastus scotti Connolly,<tei:lb/>BMNH 1939.4. 19. 11, x 3.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>226 P. B. MORDAN</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 14 Radula of Cerastus schweinfurthi, reconstituted specimen, Jebel Sumara, Yemen, 1974: a,<tei:lb/>central and lateral teeth; b, marginal teeth; x 720.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Body. Darkly pigmented in uniform browny-black.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Radula (Fig. 14; Table 2). Part of a radula was extracted from the reconstituted specimen.<tei:lb/>Although the teeth were immature, it was possible to confirm that in all teeth the mesocones are<tei:lb/>relatively elongate, and the bases narrow in comparison with Euryptyxis. The marginal ectocones<tei:lb/>have a single cusp, which appears to be lacking from the outermost teeth.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Lung cavity. As far as could be established, complete renal and rectal folds are developed,<tei:lb/>united at the top of the lung. The kidney is 0-85 times the cavity length, and venation is<tei:lb/>noticeably more prominent near the mantle collar. The outer lung wall is dark brown/black with<tei:lb/>irregular patches and streaks of opaque white.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Reproductive system (Fig. 15). Hesse (1933:220-221, fig. 43.) has figured the reproductive<tei:lb/>system of Cerastus' schweinfurthi and also gave a comprehensive description of its gross<tei:lb/>morphology. Three important features of this description are: the spermatheca which is clearly<tei:lb/>separated into head and stalk regions, the well-developed, elongate penial caecum; and the penis<tei:lb/>retractor muscle which is united with the appendicular retractor for some distance after its<tei:lb/>origin on the lower lung wall, and which inserts singly on the epiphallus. Hesse also figures a<tei:lb/>spermatophore, and a detail of a single spermatophore 'scale' (Hesse, 1933: fig. 43, D-E).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Only a single, reconstituted specimen was available for dissection in the present study. From<tei:lb/>this, it has been able to confirm many features of Hesse's description, and also describe in more<tei:lb/>detail the internal morphology of the penial appendix. It is clear (Fig. 1 5 A) that the morphology<tei:lb/>of the junction of the basal and middle portions of the appendix is similar to Zebrinops although<tei:lb/>the projection of the papilla into the basal lumen is shorter. Additionally, the penial muscle<tei:lb/>sheath is single-layered. Internally, the epiphallus appeared to have spiral ornamentation and<tei:lb/>Hesse states that there is a single row of 'scales' on the spermatophore, which appears to be<tei:lb/>spirally arranged in his figure.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>COMMENTS. The shell of schweinfurthi most closely resembles that of Cerastus scotti but is readily<tei:lb/>separable by its much greater size; the two species co-occur at Jebel Harir, near Dhala, South<tei:lb/>Yemen.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>COMMENTS. Although the material is limited, it does show remarkable uniformity in shell form,<tei:lb/>and in view of the similar lack of conchological variability in the other small Arabian species<tei:lb/>of Cerastus, must be considered a valid taxon. Morphologically, the shell is closest to that of<tei:lb/>girwanensis from which it differs principally in the details of its ribbing and colouration.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Cerastus girwanensis Connolly, 1941<tei:lb/>Cerastus girwanensis Connolly, 1941:27, pi. 3, fig. 13.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>TYPE MATERIAL. Lectotype (here selected, BMNH 1939.4.19.9) and Paralectotype (BMNH<tei:lb/>1939.4.19.10), Jebel Girwan, Yemen, 2700m., leg. P. W. R. Petrie, 17.ii.1938.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DISTRIBUTION (Fig. 12). Known only from the type locality, Jebel Girwan, near Ghaiman, about<tei:lb/>17 km SE. of Sana, Yemen.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. Dextral; elongate conic; apex blunt, sutures deep; protoconch initially smooth<tei:lb/>becoming ribbed, ribbing regularly spaced, sharp and of even thickness (2-3 ribs/mm.), spiral<tei:lb/>striae absent. Umbilicus narrow and shallow, aperture sharp, unthickened. Colour uniform pale<tei:lb/>brown, ribs paler.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Shell. Dimensions of Lectotype (Fig. 13d): 17-0 x 9-5 x 8-0; 7-6 x 5-2; 7 wh.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Anatomy. The radula and jaw of the smaller, immature, type were described by Connolly<tei:lb/>(1941:28); the radula closely resembles that of C. scotti but had considerably fewer teeth (Table<tei:lb/>2). Like scotti the marginal ectocones were multicuspid in all but the first two teeth.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>COMMENTS. See C. albonotatus above.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Cerastus scotti Connolly, 1941<tei:lb/>Cerastus scotti Connolly, 1941:22, pi. 3, fig. 14, text fig. 7. [Jebel JihafJ.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>TYPE MATERIAL. Holotype (BMNH 1939.4.19.11) and 6 paratypes (BMNH 1939.4.19.12-17),<tei:lb/>Jebel Jihaf, South Yemen, 2000-2300 m; 2 paratypes, Jebel Harir, South Yemen, BMNH, leg.<tei:lb/>Scott &amp; Britton, x-xi. 1937.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>DISTRIBUTION (Fig. 12). Recorded from elevated sites in the region of Taizz, Yemen and Dhala,<tei:lb/>South Yemen, and it is thus more widely distributed than other small Cerastus species.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. Dextral; elongate ovoid; ribs fine, initally strong and regularly spaced, becoming<tei:lb/>much weaker and less regular (6-9 ribs/mm.); weak spiral striae giving glossy surface appearance.<tei:lb/>Apex sharp, protoconch initially smooth; sutures not deeply impressed; narrow, shallow<tei:lb/>umbilicus; aperture sharp, unthickened. Colourless to very pale brown, semi-transparent;<tei:lb/>stronger early ribs may be translucent white.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Shell. Dimensions of Lectotype (Fig. 13g): 19-3 x 10-6 x 9-1; 9-6 x 6-2; 6-9 wh. Largest specimen: Naqd al<tei:lb/>Ahmar, Yemen: 22-7 x 1 1 1 x 9-8; 10-0 x 6-6; 8 wh.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Anatomy. The jaw and radula were described by Connolly (1941:27, fig. 7); see Table 2. The<tei:lb/>remaining anatomy is unknown.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>COMMENTS. C. scotti differs from the other small Arabian Cerastua by its shell shape and ribbing,<tei:lb/>resembling a small C. schweinfurthi. It is easily separated from the latter by its considerably<tei:lb/>smaller size for an equivalent number of whorls.</tei:p>

Origin of penial and appendicular retractors<tei:lb/>separate or adjacent.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Buliminus (Petraeus) candidus Lamarck. Kobelt, 1902:407, pi. 72, figs 2, 3.<tei:lb/>Euryptyxis forskalii Pfeiffer. Connolly, 1941:30; Verdcourt, 1974:8.<tei:lb/>Pupa Candida Lamarck. Mermod, 1951:717, fig. 71.<tei:lb/>Euryptyxis Candida Lamarck. Verdcourt, 1974:6; Mordan, 1980a: 360, fig. 2k.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>TYPE MATERIAL. Possible Syntypes of arata Recluz, 'Yemen', Petit Collection, BMNH<tei:lb/>1943. 10.2.53-55, (3 specs.).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The Lamarck types of Candida have not been examined but a representative specimen is well<tei:lb/>illustrated by Mermod (1951, Fig. 71), leaving no doubt as to the identity of the species. The<tei:lb/>name Helix arabica Forskal predates Candida but is here considered a nomen dubium. It is based</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>230 P. B. MORDAN</tei:p>
         <tei:p>on material collected by Forskal himself which is now housed in the Copenhagen Museum<tei:lb/>(together with the material which forms the basis of the accounts of Martini and Chemnitz (1786)<tei:lb/>and Beck (1837)). The original description of arabica refers to a smooth, white shell ('alba 1 and<tei:lb/>'glabrd'), whereas the specimens purporting to be the types are strongly ribbed (Fig. 16d) as in<tei:lb/>typical Candida, and are mostly pale brown in colour. The description better fits labiosa Miiller,<tei:lb/>and the true identity of arabica Forskal is clearly uncertain.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>One specimen of Candida from the Spengler collection, ZMC, is probably the original of a poor<tei:lb/>figure labelled Helix sulcata Miiller in Martini and Chemnitz (1786, fig. 1231). This figure is cited<tei:lb/>as an indication by Beck (1837) for the name Buliminus forskalii and thus the Spengler specimen<tei:lb/>is most likely the type of forskalii Beck.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>DISTRIBUTION (Fig. 17). Restricted to the south-west of Arabia. The range is similar to that of E.<tei:lb/>fragosa, but extends further northwards into the Asir province of Saudi Arabia, and westwards<tei:lb/>to the Farsan Islands in the Red sea. The southern- and eastern-most localities are respectively<tei:lb/>near Musaymir and in the region of Dhala, both in South Yemen. A number of early records are<tei:lb/>from Socotra, but these have not been confirmed in recent years.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. Dextral; pupiform, surface with strong sharp regular radial ribs, and very fine,<tei:lb/>wavy spiral striae which cross the ribbing; protoconch initially smooth becoming radially ribbed;<tei:lb/>umbilicus closed. Aperture with flared lip, never recurved, and often thickened just internal to<tei:lb/>the margin; strong internal columellar fold in body whorl (Fig. 18D), not visible from aperture.<tei:lb/>Colourless opaque to brown, lip may be more deeply pigmented.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Shell. Dimensions of figured syntype Candida Lamarck (Mermod, 1951:717, fig. 71): shell height 24-5;<tei:lb/>shell diam. 13-0; apert. height 12-0; whorls 7-5. Syntypes arata Recluz: 25-9 x 13-4 x 12-1; 12-9x9-4; lip<tei:lb/>1-9; 7-7 wh.; 25-4x13-1x1 1-9; 13-2 x 10-1; lip 1-8; 7-6 wh.; 25-7x13-5x11-2; 13-1 x 9-4; lip 2-1; 7-7 wh.<tei:lb/>Largest specimen, Dhala, South Yemen, leg H. Scott (Fig. 16a): 40-1 x 19-9 x 16-1; 18-5 x 14-0; lip 3-4;<tei:lb/>8-9 wh. Smallest specimen, Dumsuk Island, Red Sea, leg Macfadyen (Fig. 16c): 16-8 x 10-0 x 8-2; 9-8 x 6-2;<tei:lb/>lip 1-0; 6-4 wh.</tei:p>

      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>ARABIAN ENIDAE</tei:p>
         <tei:p>233</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 18 X-ray photograph of Euryptyxis species to show relative development of the columellar fold;<tei:lb/>A, latireflexa; B, revoili; C, labiosa; D, Candida; E,fragosa; x 1-4.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In fresh specimens the protoconch and early whorls are always a rich, glossy brown,<tei:lb/>typically becoming progressively paler until the shell becomes a milky transluscent grey/white.<tei:lb/>Occasionally the entire shell retains the brown colouration. In most specimens the internal lip<tei:lb/>surface has a brown rim, well illustrated by Delessert (1847, pi. 27, fig. 10).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Sculpture does not in general appear to vary greatly, with the sharp, regular radial striae<tei:lb/>always predominating. There is, however, a considerable range in size and, to a lesser extent,<tei:lb/>shape, although the latter is always characteristically pupiform. Material from coastal sites such<tei:lb/>as Jizan and Dumsuk Island are remarkable for their small size, and a more-elongate shape<tei:lb/>(theforskali form) appears to be characteristic of shells from the upland region of Asir in Saudi<tei:lb/>Arabia.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Body. Taizz specimens of a uniform cream colouration, with slightly darker pigmentation on<tei:lb/>tubercles of head.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Radula (Figs 19a-b). Total number of teeth per row in Candida from Taizz between 40-42<tei:lb/>(Table 2), although Hesse (1933) quoted a figure of 50 teeth for Menaha material. Central and<tei:lb/>lateral ectocones clearly developed but small. Mesocone of marginals rounded and ectocones<tei:lb/>bear single, wide pointed cusp; in some most marginal teeth ectocone becomes bicuspid.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Lung cavity. As in E. latireflexa (see below), with kidney extending 0-75-0-8 times cavity<tei:lb/>length. Outer lung wall weakly pigmented with brown and white streaks close to mantle collar.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Reproductive system. (Figs 20, 21). Hermaphrodite gland composed of 4 to 5 diffuse lobes,<tei:lb/>loosely imbedded in digestive gland and lying against parietal and columellar shell walls.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>234</tei:p>
         <tei:p>P. B. MORDAN</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 19 Radular teeth of Euryptyxis: a, E. Candida, Taizz, Yemen, 1979, central and lateral teeth; b,<tei:lb/>marginal teeth; c, E.fragosa, Taizz, Yemen, 1979, central and lateral teeth; d, marginal teeth; x 720;<tei:lb/>e, entire side of radula; x 80.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>ARABIAN ENIDAE</tei:p>
         <tei:p>235</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 20 Genital system of Euryptyxis Candida, Taizz, 1979. Scale 2 mm.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Hermaphrodite duct long and thin, with elongate seminal vesicle (sensu Bayne, 1973) which is<tei:lb/>only weakly convoluted; talon simple fold in duct (Fig. 2 IB). Albumen gland relatively large for<tei:lb/>genus, 1-1-1-3 times length of spermoviduct.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Free oviduct about three times length of vagina, continuous with the atrium and darkly<tei:lb/>pigmented almost throughout its length; both attached to body wall by numerous short muscles.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>236</tei:p>
         <tei:p>P. B. MORDAN</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 21 Euryptyxis Candida, Taizz, 1978: A, epiphallus; B, talon region; C, penis. Scale 1 mm.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Atrial retractor fairly strong, branching from main columellar muscle. Internally free oviduct<tei:lb/>bears weak, irregular longitudinal pillasters. Spermatheca approximately same length as free<tei:lb/>oviduct and not obviously separable into distinct head and stalk regions. Terminal portion of vas<tei:lb/>deferens differentiated into long epiphallus, about five times length of penis; externally with<tei:lb/>longitudinal row of transverse ridges, corresponding to line of internal pits, varying in number<tei:lb/>between 75 and 77 in the Taizz material (Fig. 21 A). Epiphallus enters penis just below caecum.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Penis characterised externally by large, bulbous, thin-walled caecum at top; caecum may be<tei:lb/>globular or pointed. Penis small relative to other Euryptyxis, and has muscular collar or sheath,<tei:lb/>attached above and recurved at base to form two-layered structure (Fig. 2 1C). Internally are four<tei:lb/>principal glandular areas, three large and one very much smaller, which continue downwards as<tei:lb/>tapering pilasters. Pattern asymetrical: two large glandular patches, which appear almost fused,</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>ARABIAN ENIDAE 237</tei:p>
         <tei:p>lie to one side of epiphallar pore, with remaining large and small areas quite distinct on other<tei:lb/>side, overlying point of entry of the appendix at base of penis. Appendix clearly divisible into<tei:lb/>three distinct regions: a short, wide basal portion, internally lined with reticulum of predomi-<tei:lb/>nantly longitudinal pilasters; a long, thin, thick-walled central portion with a narrow lumen; and<tei:lb/>a wider, terminal saculate portion of similar length but with very thin, transparent wall. Penis<tei:lb/>and appendicular retractor muscles originate together about one third way up lower lung wall;<tei:lb/>may initially be fused over a short distance. Penis retractor bifurcates prior to insertion,<tei:lb/>one branch inserting transversely at penis/epiphallus junction, other laterally just above, on<tei:lb/>epiphallus proper.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>COMMENTS. Although Candida exhibits enormous variation in size, the shape and sculpture of<tei:lb/>the shell are relatively constant, and it is significant that all smaller specimens are from coastal<tei:lb/>locations. Its pupiform shell shape and strong, regular radial ribbing combined with aflared but<tei:lb/>unreflexed lip distinguish it from other Euryptyxis.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Reproductive anatomy is most similar to E. fragosa, but at Taizz, Yemen where both are<tei:lb/>sympatric, there are differences in the epiphallus and penial appendix.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Euryptyxis fragosa (Pfeiffer, 1 842)</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Bulimus fragosa Ferussac, 1821-2:59. [Les Grandes Indes], nomen nudum.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Buliminus fragrosus Ferussac. Beck, 1837:68. nomen nudum.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Bulimus fragosus Ferussac. Pfeiffer, 1842:45. [Yemen].</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Bulimus fragosus Ferussac. Kuster &amp; Pfeiffer, 1845-55:62, pi. 18, figs 1, 2; Bourguignat, 1882:14, fig. 19.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Buliminus fragosus Ferussac. Rossmassler, 1880:41, pi. 198, fig. 1983.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Buliminus fragosus Pfeiffer. Westerlund, 1887:64.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Buliminus ( Petraeus ) fragosus Ferussac. Kobelt, 1902:406, pi. 172, fig. 1.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Euryptyxis fragosus Pfeiffer. Fisher-Piette &amp; Metivier, 1972:1293.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>TYPE MATERIAL. Ferussac's original naming of this species was invalid, the first comprehensive<tei:lb/>description being given by Pfeiffer (1842). However, Pfeiffer's original material has not been<tei:lb/>traced and may possibly have been lost with the destruction of the Dohrn Collection in the<tei:lb/>Stettin Museum during the 1839 1 45 war (Dance, 1966:285). Two shells labelled Helix fragosa<tei:lb/>Per. were found in the Ferussac collection in Paris but these are E. Candida.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>OTHER MATERIAL. Yemen: El Kubar, leg. G. W. Berry, BMNH 1908.2.21.3, (1 spec.); Wadi Dhulla, 5 miles<tei:lb/>NW. Sana, c. 2500m., leg. P. W. R. H. Scott, BMNH 1939.4.19.1-8, (7 specs.); Jebel Jihaf, 1200m.,<tei:lb/>leg. H. Scott, BMNH 1939.4.19.21-24, (4 specs., 2 dissected); Jebel Jihaf, 1950-2050 m., Leg. H. Scott,<tei:lb/>BMNH 1939.4.19.25, (1 registered spec., 14 further unregistered specs.); Dhala, 1500m., leg. H. Scott,<tei:lb/>12-14.ix.1937, BMNH, (4 specs.); Mukieras, leg. G. Lancaster Harding, 1958, (1 1 specs).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DISTRIBUTION (Fig. 22). The most restricted of the four recognised Arabian Euryptyxis<tei:lb/>species. Almost all of the material examined here was collected in and around Taizz, although<tei:lb/>conchologically similar material has been recorded as far north as Manakha. The relatively<tei:lb/>smooth, slender form was found abundantly at Jebel Jihaf, near Dahla in in South Yemen, but<tei:lb/>also near Hamman Ali, between Yarim and Sana'a.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>238</tei:p>
         <tei:p>P. B. MORDAN</tei:p>
         <tei:p>o</tei:p>
         <tei:p>O</tei:p>
         <tei:p>cx<tei:lb/>a</tei:p>
         <tei:p>s</tei:p>
         <tei:p>_o</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>ARABIAN ENIDAE 239</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. Dextral, rounded elongate conical; surface with regular radial ribbing varying from<tei:lb/>coarse to very fine, becoming less evenly spaced, wavy, and usually weaker towards the aperture,<tei:lb/>and fine wavy spiral striae; protoconch smooth, becoming ribbed; umbilicus closed. Aperture<tei:lb/>with flared lip, never recurved; no internal columellar tooth (Fig. 18E). Colour opaque white, rarely<tei:lb/>greyish brown with white ribs.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Shell. Dimensions: Taizz, Yemen, leg. Waterson, 1946: 30-0 x 15-1 x 13-3; 14-2x10-0; lip 1-6;<tei:lb/>8-7 wh. Taizz, Yemen, leg. Heath, 1979: 35-8 x 19-1 x 16-6; 19-1 x 13-7; lip 2-9; 8-6 wh. Jebel Jihaf, South<tei:lb/>Yemen, leg. Scott: 28-8 x 15-0 x 12-7; 14-5 x 10-0; lip 1-3, 8-2 wh.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The principal sculptural variation is found in the radial ribbing which may be very strong (Fig.<tei:lb/>16F), as in some populations around Taizz, or almost absent (Jebel Jihaf); other populations in<tei:lb/>the Taizz region have intermediate sculpture. Coarse ribbing appears in general to be correlated<tei:lb/>with a rather wider shell than normal, whereas the smoother shells are more slender in profile;<tei:lb/>individual populations show little variation in either respect.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Body. Uniformly pale cream, with slight dark pigmentation on tubercles in head region.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Radula (Figs. 19c-e). Very similar to E. Candida (see Table 2).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Lung cavity. Typical for genus (see E. latireflexa below), but kidney relatively shorter than<tei:lb/>other species, occupying 0-7 times lung length (Table 3). Outer wall of lung unpigmented.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Reproductive system (Figs 23, 24). Albumen gland relatively short and narrow, approximately<tei:lb/>1/3 length of spermoviduct. Spermatheca about 1-5 times as long as free oviduct; in one specimen<tei:lb/>it contained fragments of a partly dissolved spermatophore: tail region complete and shaped<tei:lb/>much as in Zebrinops; one complete digitiform spine also present (Figs. 24C-D).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Epiphallus thick, externally bears strong transverse ridges corresponding internally to<tei:lb/>longitudinal row of 40 to 57 pits (Figs. 24A). Penis with globular caecum (smaller than in E.<tei:lb/>Candida) and sharply recurved muscular sheath. Penis and appendicular retractor muscles<tei:lb/>originating separately but at same leve on lung wall; former has multiple insertion on epiphallus,<tei:lb/>and main branch to penis. Internally, penis with rather variable pilaster pattern. Within<tei:lb/>individual Taizz populations, overall distribution of glandular patches and pilasters similar, but<tei:lb/>differences present in degree to which the two lateral glandular areas embraced epiphallar pore,<tei:lb/>and also in separation of these from their downward extensions by the transverse groove. Also<tei:lb/>differences in number, strength and regularity of pilasters in base of penial appendix.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Penial appendix characterised by thickening of basal region of central stalk (Fig. 23), unlike<tei:lb/>other Arabian Euryptyxis; thickening more marked in Taizz specimens than in those from Jebel<tei:lb/>Jihaf, but clearly developed in latter. Insertion of appendicular retractor muscle normal for<tei:lb/>genus.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>COMMENTS. Despite considerable inter-population variation in shell sculpture and shape,<tei:lb/>specimens from three populations from Taizz showed a close degree of similarity in their<tei:lb/>anatomy. The sample from Jebel Jihaf, whilst being much smoother and more slender than any<tei:lb/>of the Taizz examples, was anatomically similar, and the number of epiphallar pits (48-49) was<tei:lb/>within the range shown by the Taizz material (40-57) (Table 5).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The species is characterised both by the number of epiphallar pits and the enlarged base of the<tei:lb/>central part of the penial appendix. All the forms agree in having a generally conical outline to<tei:lb/>the shell (as opposed to the pupiform shape of Candida), a lip which is flared but never reflexed, a<tei:lb/>very weakly developed columellar fold, and distinct, regular spiral striae. These conchological<tei:lb/>similarities, in conjunction with the overall anatomical similarities, suggest that in the present<tei:lb/>state of knowledge all the forms should be included within a single species.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>The material described by Muller in his Vermium terrestrium et flu\iatilum ... of 1 774 is housed<tei:lb/>in the Zoological Museum, Copenhagen but I have been unable to trace the types of labiosa there.<tei:lb/>Although the original description of labiosa is arguably ambiguous in the absence of types, the<tei:lb/>subsequent usage of the Miiller's name is clear and his authorship is retained. Pilsbry's naming of<tei:lb/>hypodon clearly arose from an erroneous locality.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>OTHER MATERIAL. Yemen: Menaka, leg. Schweinfurth, BMNH 1895.8.20.3, (1 spec.); Senna [? = Sana, Wadi<tei:lb/>Hadramaut, c 500m], leg.          <tei:p>DISTRIBUTION (Fig. 22). It is difficult to assess the precise range of this species, which could occur<tei:lb/>almost continuously from Dhofar to the south-eastern coast of the Red Sea, although almost all<tei:lb/>the material considered here is either from localities in Dhofar, or the environs of Aden where<tei:lb/>the snail seems to be particularly abundant. Odd specimens from the Hadramaut [Preston],<tei:lb/>and the Tihama of Yemen [Waterson] and Saudi Arabia [Lavranos] do, however, suggest such<tei:lb/>continuity. In addition to the continental distribution, labiosa has been recorded from the<tei:lb/>Farsan and Perim Islands in the Red Sea, as well as Socotra (the type locality ofjehennei), Cape<tei:lb/>Gardafui, Somalia (Bourguignat, 1882:31), and one doubtful record from the Seychelles. All<tei:lb/>records are from sites at comparatively low elevations, the highest being 800 m in Dhofar.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. Dextral, elongate pupiform, surface with regular radial ribs becoming weaker and<tei:lb/>more widely spaced towards aperture, and fine wavy spiral striae; protoconch smooth becoming<tei:lb/>strongly ribbed; umbilicus closed. Aperture with recurved lip, and strong internal columellar tooth<tei:lb/>(Fig. 18C). Colour usually transparent or opaque white, may be tinged with brown; lip white.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Shell. Dimensions of syntype jehenni Recluz: 25-1 x 12-0x9-2; 12-1 x9-3; lip 2-5; 8 wh. Holotype of<tei:lb/>hypodon Pilsbry (Fig. 25e): 25 x 12-5; 13-0 x 10-2; lip 2-3; 7-5 wh. Largest specimen, Aden, leg. Baynham:<tei:lb/>23-9x11-5x9-3; 11-5x9-1; lip 2-2; 7-3 wh. Tawai Atair, Dhofar. leg. Gallahagher: largest:<tei:lb/>21-4 x 10-8 x 9-3; 9-9 x 8-7; lip 1-5; 7-2 wh. Smallest: 15-9 x 8-9 x 8-1; 7-8 x 6-0; lip 1-0; 6-2 wh.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Shell shape varies considerably within the species, even within samples from a single site. This<tei:lb/>is exemplified by the results of a statistical comparison of three 'age classes' of shells: living,</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>ARABIAN ENIDAE</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Fig. 25 Euryptyxis labiosa: a-c, Tawai Atair, Oman, BMNH; d, Dumsuk Island, Read Sea, BMNH<tei:lb/>1928.3.16.29; e, holotype of Bulimulus hypodon Pilsbry, ANS 23029. All x 1-5.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>recently dead (intact periostracum), and long dead (periostracum lost) collected at Tawai Atair<tei:lb/>in 1978, (Table 6). Despite small sample size, there were highly significant differences between<tei:lb/>the long-dead category and each of the other two categories for almost all shell parameters,<tei:lb/>whilst there were few significant differences between the latter pair. There also appear to be<tei:lb/>geographical differences, at least for some shell characters. Whereas shells from Aden seem to<tei:lb/>show a similar degree of shape variation to those from Dhofar, the former are typically more<tei:lb/>elongate and tend to have a wider lip (c. 2 mm. compared with 1-1-5 mm. for Dhofar).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Body. Darkly pigmented, becoming almost black towards head region.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Lung cavity. Similar to other Euryptyxis species, with kidney extending 0-75-0-8 times length<tei:lb/>of lung (Table 3). Outer lung wall strongly pigmented with black and opaque white irregularly<tei:lb/>shaped patches, clearly visible through shell (Mordan, 1980b: pi. 2).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Radula (Figs 26a-b). Overall size of radula markedly smaller than in other Euryptyxis, but<tei:lb/>individual tooth size not greatly different; total number of teeth per row lower because fewer<tei:lb/>marginals (Table 2).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Reproductive system (Fig. 27). Albumen gland about half length of spermoviduct, and<tei:lb/>spermatheca only slightly longer than free oviduct. Relative length of vagina/atrium complex<tei:lb/>as in other Euryptyxis but much wider, being more than twice width of free oviduct. Details of<tei:lb/>terminal male system similar to E. latireflexa, although overall dimensions much smaller.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Differences as follows: number of pits in epiphallus greater, being 30-31 (Table 5; Fig. 27c);<tei:lb/>appendix enters higher up penis; appendicular retractor does not ensheath mid-portion of<tei:lb/>appendix prior to insertion. Arrangement of pillasters within penis similar to E. latireflexa, but<tei:lb/>a well-defined transverse groove just below epiphallar pore separates pair of lateral pillasters<tei:lb/>surrounding pore from their downward extensions (Fig. 27B); pilaster pattern within base of<tei:lb/>appendix as in E. latireflexa.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>COMMENTS. In both shell and soft anatomy this species most closely resembles latireflexa, with<tei:lb/>which it co-occurs in Dhofar, but at Tawai Atair there are differences in penial and epiphallar<tei:lb/>anatomy, and the adult shell is distinctly smaller throughout its range.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>TYPE MATERIAL. Lectotype (Here selected, BMNH 1984159) and 2 Paralectotypes (BMNH<tei:lb/>1984160) latireflexus Reeve, ? Muscat, Oman, Cuming Collection. Two syntypes lunti<tei:lb/>Melvill, Hadramaut, leg. W. Lunt, 1893-4, BMNH 1895.7.10.2-3. Lectotype and 3<tei:lb/>Paralectotypes socialis Jousseaume, Schoukra, South Yemen, MNHN (selected Fisher-Piette &amp;<tei:lb/>Metivier, 1972:1302). Lectotype and 2 Paralectotypes schoukraensis Jousseaume, Schoukra,<tei:lb/>South Yemen, MNHN (Selected Fisher-Piette &amp; Metivier, 1972:1300). Holotype deflersi<tei:lb/>Jousseaume, Jebel el Arays, leg. Deflers, MNHN. Holotype dinshawi Sykes, Senna, ? South<tei:lb/>Yemen, leg. E. R. Shopland, BMNH 1903.6.8.7.          <tei:p>The type locality of the senior synonym, latireflexa Reeve, is uncertain, but clearly that of<tei:lb/>Muscat given in the Conchologica Iconica is erroneous. Specimens closely resembling the types<tei:lb/>have been recorded from localities as far apart as Qairoon Hairiti, Dhofar (Fig. 28b) and<tei:lb/>Qubesh, Yemen.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>DISTRIBUTION (Fig. 17). This species would appear to be the most widely distributed Arabian<tei:lb/>Euryptyxis, extending right across the south of the peninsula from the near the west coast to eas-<tei:lb/>ternmost Dhofar. It inhabits both coastal and mountainous situations.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. Dextral; globose to elongate conic; surface with regular radial ribbing, typically<tei:lb/>becoming weaker and less regular towards the aperture, and fine regular spiral striae which cross<tei:lb/>the ribs; protoconch starting smooth, becoming ribbed; umbilicus closed. Aperture with well-<tei:lb/>developed recurved lip, may be greatly expanded; strong columellar tooth in body whorl (Fig.<tei:lb/>ISA). Colour opaque white to brown, aperture more deeply pigmented.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Shell. Dimensions: Lectotype latireflexus Reeve (Fig. 28a): 31-8 x 15-1 x 12-5; 14-9x11-1; lip 2-2;<tei:lb/>8-2 wh. Paralectotypes: 28-1 x 14-0 x 11-6; 14-0 x 10-6; lip 2-3; 7-9 wh.; 28-9 x 14-0 x 12-1; 13-4 x 10-4; lip 2-2;<tei:lb/>8 wh. Syntype lunti Melvill (Fig. 28c): 27-6 x 15-2 x 12-0; 14-8 x 1 1-5; lip 3-1; 8 wh. Lectotype socialis<tei:lb/>Jousseaume: 34-3 x 17-3 x 12-7; 19-2x14-2; lip 3-5; 8-1 wh. Lectotype schoukraensis Jousseaume:<tei:lb/>34-8 x 17-7 x 13-0; 18-7 x 13-7; lip 3-6; 8 wh. Holotype deflersi Jousseaume: 30-7 x 16-8 x 12-7; 16-5 x 12-5;</tei:p>

         <tei:p>lip 2-8; 8 wh. Holotype dinshawi Sykes (Fig. 28d): 36-1 x 22-0 x 14-3; 20-7 x 17-5; lip 5-8; 8 wh. Largest<tei:lb/>Syntype leesi Pallary: 27-8x17-3x13-7; 15-8x13-0; lip 3-5; 7-4 wh. Figured syntype (Fig. 28f):<tei:lb/>24-7 x 16-0 x 13-5; 15-8 x 13-0; lip 2-7; 7-5 wh.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Shell shape varies considerably within the species, apparently along broad clines. In Dhofar,<tei:lb/>from where there is a considerable amount of material, statistically significant differences in<tei:lb/>shell shape were found between roughly contemporary representative samples from the three<tei:lb/>prinicipal mountain ranges; Jabal Qamar, Jabal Qara and Jabal Samhan (Table 7).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Specimens from Yemen and South Yemen to the west are noticeably more conical than the<tei:lb/>'globular' form leesi Pallary from Dhofar (Fig. 28f), and often have a very much more developed<tei:lb/>apertural lip, culminating in the extreme form dinshawi Sykes found at Dhala, South Yemen<tei:lb/>(Fig. 28d).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Body. Pigmentation restricted to tips of tubercles, which are speckled with pale brown; colour<tei:lb/>darker in head region.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Radula (Figs 26c-f). Table 2 clearly shows distinct differences in tooth size between specimens<tei:lb/>from Tawai Atair and from Ain Arzat, Dhofar, the former being the larger; it may or may not be<tei:lb/>significant that at Tawai Atair E. latireflexa is sympatric with the smaller E. labiosa. Overall<tei:lb/>radular morphology similar to E. Candida.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Lung cavity (Figs 29A-B). Kidney of typical orthurethran form, extending from top of lung<tei:lb/>downwards about 0-75-0-8 times length of cavity (Table 3). Fold of epithelium, up to 1 mm tall,<tei:lb/>runs from renal pore upwards parallel with kidney almost to top as renal fold, and then reflexes<tei:lb/>to run downwards alongside rectum as rectal fold. At pneumostome fold turns sharply away<tei:lb/>from rectum before stopping. Pneumostome fairly complex (Fig. 29B) bearing transverse fold of<tei:lb/>mantle which emerges from anus. Venation strongly developed, as in other cerastuines. Outer<tei:lb/>lung wall weakly pigmented with occasional flecks of opaque white.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Reproductive system (Figs 30, 31) Hermaphrodite gland composed of between 5 and 7<tei:lb/>irregularly shaped lobes. Albumen gland from 0-5 and 0-8 times length of spermoviduct, and<tei:lb/>free oviduct about twice length of vagina and two-thirds length of spermatheca. Epiphallus<tei:lb/>approximately 4 to 5 times length of penis, and only weakly ridged externally; internally with<tei:lb/>single row of 17 to 28 pits (Fig. 31 A; Table 5). Penial caecum conical, varying in height from 0-3<tei:lb/>to 0-5 times length of penis; muscular sheath never recurved. Penial and appendicular retractor<tei:lb/>muscles originate separately on lower lung wall; former divides, two small branches inserting on<tei:lb/>epiphallus, and main branch on penis proper.</tei:p>

      </tei:div>

         <tei:p>pilasters. Also two large glandular patches with associated pilasters, just as in E. Candida, and<tei:lb/>one or two intermediate glandular areas.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>COMMENTS. E. latireflexa as recognised here includes numerous synonyms, and in most cases their<tei:lb/>present inclusion is based on shell characters alone. Anatomical information is only available<tei:lb/>from Dhofar, but a detailed study of this suggests that only one species is present there, despite<tei:lb/>considerable shell variation (Table 7). West of Dhofar there appears to be a clinal increase in<tei:lb/>shell height and lip width. Although there are a relatively large number of shells recorded from<tei:lb/>the region of Dhala, the intervening area of South Yemen is poorly known with only the shells</tei:p>

      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>ARABIAN ENIDAE 253</tei:p>
         <tei:p>collected by Little around Al Mukalla (figured in Pallary, 1925) and Lunt from the Hadramaut<tei:lb/>(Melvill, 1894).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>This species most closely resembles E. labiosa. It is however always larger than labiosa and<tei:lb/>differs from it anatomically in the number of epiphallar pits and the relative proportions of the<tei:lb/>penis, as well as mantle and body pigmentation.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>E. revoili Bourguignat from Somalia has a similar number of epiphallar pits to latireflexa<tei:lb/>(Table 5), as well as showing conchological similiarities, but differs significantly in the<tei:lb/>reproductive anatomy, notably in the insertion of the penis retractor muscle which is on the<tei:lb/>epiphallus only.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A CHA T1NELLOIDES Nevill, 1 878<tei:lb/>TYPE SPECIES. Bulimus socotrensis Pfeiffer [monotypy].</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DIAGNOSIS. Shell with undifferentiated lip. Penis with short, rounded caecum; penial retractor<tei:lb/>inserts on epiphallus; appendicular retractor inserts on basal portion of appendix, and originates<tei:lb/>separatey from penial retractor.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Achatinelloides sebasmia (Jousseaume, 1889)</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Ovella sebasmia Jousseaume, 1889:350. [Aden].<tei:lb/>Ovella sebasmia Jousseaume, 1890:94, pi. 3, fig. 9-1 1 .</tei:p>
         <tei:p>TYPE MATERIAL. 2 Syntypes, Aden, South Yemen, Jousseaume Collection, MNHN.<tei:lb/>DISTRIBUTION. Known only from the type locality (above).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. Dextral, elongate-conic; surface with regular, coarse, radial ribs, protoconch<tei:lb/>smooth; umbilicus open. Aperture elongate-oval, lip undifferentiated, thick callus extending<tei:lb/>across parietal wall and small parietal denticle developed near palatal/parietal border; weak<tei:lb/>columellar fold. Colour creamy white with irregular, brown, flammulate patterning, weaker on<tei:lb/>body whorl; protoconch white, sometimes pale brown on lower part of whorl; aperture pale<tei:lb/>brown internally.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Anatomy. Unknown.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>COMMENTS. See under jousseaumei below.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Achatinelloides jousseaumei (Jousseaume, 1890)<tei:lb/>Ovella jousseaumei Jousseaume, 1890:93, pi. 3, figs 7, 8. [Mahala].</tei:p>

         <tei:p>DISTRIBUTION. Known only from the type material.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. As for sebasmia above but more globose, with stronger colour patterning on body<tei:lb/>whorl, and more-developed columellar fold.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Shell. Dimensions of the two mature syntypes (Fig. 32b): 20-2x11-9x10-1; 11-3x6-3; 7 wh.<tei:lb/>[Bourguignat Colin., possible figure.] 19-4 x 1 1-0 x 10-1; 1 1-1 x 6-3; 7 wh. [Jousseaume Colin.] Remaining<tei:lb/>syntypes immature.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Anatomy. Unknown.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>COMMENTS. The genus Achatinelloides represents an endemic radiation on the islands of Socotra<tei:lb/>and Abd al Kuri, with a total of seventeen species being recognised by Smith (1903). They occupy<tei:lb/>a variety of habitats at elevations up to 1 500 m. It is tempting to think of the Arabian specimens<tei:lb/>as merely introduced Socotran forms because of their highly restricted distribution around the<tei:lb/>major South Yemen port of Aden, and the similarity of their shells. Certainly sebasima is close to<tei:lb/>A. balfouri Godwin Austen, 1881 (dimensions of the figured syntype, BMNH 1881.12.14.20:</tei:p>

         <tei:p>P. B. MORDAN</tei:p>

         <tei:p>20-9 x 10-5 x 10-0; 11-2x6-2; 6-7 wh.), and jousseaumei is near to both A. dahamisensis<tei:lb/>Smith, 1898 (figured syntype, BMNH 1899.12.20.20: 20-2 x 12-6 x 10-6; 12-4 x 7-3; 6-9 wh.) and<tei:lb/>A. homhilensis Smith, 1 898 (figured syntype, BMNH 1899.12.20.51: 19-5 x 11-4 x 10-3; 11-5x7-5;<tei:lb/>7wh.).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>As the anatomy of Achatinelloides has never been investigated, the opportunity has been taken<tei:lb/>to dissect a poorly preserved aestivating specimen of the type species of the genus, A. socotrensis<tei:lb/>Pfeiffer, 1881, collected on ll.viii.1956 near Hadibu, Socotra (BMNH 1957.7.10.1). The pallial<tei:lb/>and reproductive anatomy are of the typical cerastine type. The penial and appendicular<tei:lb/>retractor muscles are separate, but the latter inserts well below the top of the basal portion of<tei:lb/>the appendix. The penis itself differs from other cerastuines in having three pairs of well-<tei:lb/>defined longitudinal pilasters in the penial caecum. The caecum is exceptionally thin-walled, the<tei:lb/>pilaster pattern being clearly visible externally. The penial retractor inserts about one-quarter the<tei:lb/>way up the epiphallus, which is similar in form to that of Zebrinops, and has a single row of<tei:lb/>approximately 19 internal pits.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>ZEBRINOPS Thiele, 1 93 1<tei:lb/>TYPE SPECIES. Limicolaria revoili Bourguignat [orig. desig.]</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DIAGNOSIS. Shell without differentiated lip. Penis with well-developed, elongate caecum; penial<tei:lb/>retractor inserts on epiphallus. Appendix with long papilla; appendicular retractor inserts on<tei:lb/>enclosed base of central stalk. Penial and appendicular retractors originate separately.</tei:p>
