         <tei:p>DISTRIBUTION (Fig. 33). Almost all the material examined originates from two principal areas: the<tei:lb/>environs of Taizz in North Yemen and Dhala in South Yemen. This may well represent collecting<tei:lb/>bias, but interestingly the former represents typical albata whilst the latter material is largely<tei:lb/>referable to ventricosa. Additional material from near Aden and from Al Kubar, to the south and<tei:lb/>east of Dhala respectively, is of the ventricosa type.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. Dextral; elongate conic; umbilicus narrow and shallow. Protoconch smooth;<tei:lb/>subsequent ornamentation consists only of weak growth lines and occasional malleations;<tei:lb/>texture glossy. Colour of protoconch often light to dark brown, may be white, remainder opaque<tei:lb/>white with two interrupted spiral bands of brown (the lower only visible on body whorl) which<tei:lb/>may extend radially as streaks.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Shell. Dimensions of lectotype (albata Ferussac) (Fig. 32c): 26-3 x 1 1 -4 x 10-6; 1 0- 1 x 6-6; 8-9 wh. Paralecto-<tei:lb/>types: 24-6 x 11-3 x 10-0; 10-3 x 6-2 x 8 wh.; 22-5 x 11-3 x 10-6; 9-5 x 5-5; 8-4 wh. Dimensions of figured<tei:lb/>syntype (ventricosa Connolly) (Fig. 32d): 16-6 x 8-3 x 7-5; 6-7 x 4-6; 7-5 wh. Dimensions of largest complete<tei:lb/>specimen, Taizz, leg. Brunt and Mather: 35-2 x 16-5 x 15-7; 15-0 x 9-5; 9-6 wh.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The specific distinction of ventricosa was justified by Connolly largely on conchological<tei:lb/>grounds, principally shell size and shape, but this does not seem to hold up to analysis. The<tei:lb/>sample collected by Hugh Scott from 2 km north of Taizz comprises mainly good albata, but also<tei:lb/>includes a few smaller specimens. Height against whorl number of this material is plotted in<tei:lb/>fig. 34, and it can be seen that the two show a clear linear relationship. Additionally, the type<tei:lb/>specimens of albata and ventricosa are included in the plot and tend to confirm that the two<tei:lb/>nominal taxa merely represent age classes of the same species. A statistical comparison of the<tei:lb/>type series of albata and ventricosa in respect of the H/D ratio of the shells failed to demonstrate<tei:lb/>that the latter were significantly more ventricose (Student's t test: 18 degrees of freedom,<tei:lb/>t = 0-973, not significant).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Body. Generally pale, becoming darker towards head region.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Radula (Fig. 35). Both radulae examined extremely worn in central and lateral areas, but<tei:lb/>marginal teeth in good condition. In all except first two or three marginals of row, ectocones<tei:lb/>multicuspid. Tooth dimensions and numbers given in Table 2.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Lung cavity. Of typical cerastuine type with well-developed complete renal and rectal folds and<tei:lb/>prominent venation. Kidney 0-85 times the length of lung. Outer wall clear and unpigmented in<tei:lb/>most individual, but some have distinct pigmentation in pneumostome region and along rectum.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Reproductive system (Figs 36, 37). Hermaphrodite gland composed of 5 to 6 lobes, and talon<tei:lb/>sharply curved (fig. 36A). Albumen gland large, approximately 0-9 times length of spermoviduct;<tei:lb/>latter wider, relative to its length, than in Euryptyxis, as is spermatheca which has distinctly</tei:p>

A number of continental<tei:lb/>African Zebrinops have been dissected and all clearly belong to species other than albata.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>Fig. 37 Zebrinops albata Taizz, Yemen, 1978: A, everted genitalia; B, epiphallus, external; C,<tei:lb/>epiphallus, internal; D, penis and base of appendix. Scale 1 mm.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Discussion</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The following discussion addresses two, essentially distinct problems involving decisions made at<tei:lb/>different taxonomic levels: the problems of species discrimination in arid-habitat land snails; and<tei:lb/>secondly a consideration of the differences which have been shown to exist between the two<tei:lb/>major family-group taxa, the Enidae sensu stricto and the Cerastinae.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Discrimination at the species level</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Shell characters, and more recently characters of the terminal genitalia, have been used to<tei:lb/>discriminate species of land snails. The former are clearly subject to the effects of environmental</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>260 P. B. MORDAN</tei:p>
         <tei:p>variation, but the relationship between the latter and possible species-isolating mechanisms<tei:lb/>would appear to be a direct one. Certainly the genitalia have been found pragmatically to be<tei:lb/>useful in providing low-level taxonomic characters in pulmonates, as well as other animal groups<tei:lb/>(Arnold, 1973). However, Mayr (1963:103) discounts the importance of mechanical isolation<tei:lb/>in most cases, suggesting that the observed differences in genital structure are the result of<tei:lb/>pleiotropy. A possible mechanism by which such pleiotropic changes could be stored by the<tei:lb/>genitalia, rather than be eliminated by normalizing selection as in other organ systems, has been<tei:lb/>explored by Arnold (1973, 1983).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Clearly information on soft anatomy is desirable wherever possible, but despite examination of<tei:lb/>almost all the Arabian pulmonate material currently available in museums, most or all Arabian<tei:lb/>species in the genera Cerastus, Achatinelloides, Paramastus and Polychordia are known only from<tei:lb/>shells, and even for species whose internal anatomy is known, our principal information regard-<tei:lb/>ing their geographical distribution and variation is derived from shell collections. Nevertheless,<tei:lb/>morphological groupings based on shell characters have emerged which are consistent with the<tei:lb/>limited anatomical information available, and consequently must be regarded as species. The<tei:lb/>level of confidence in the taxonomy presented here varies greatly between taxa: in the case of the<tei:lb/>northern Oman enids it is high, for living material was available from a relatively large number of<tei:lb/>sites throughout the known geographical range, but is much lower in groups such as Euryptyxis<tei:lb/>which are conchologically highly variable and for which anatomical information is available<tei:lb/>from only a limited part of their assumed distribution.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The high levels of shell variation, so characteristic of many desert groups, stem primarily from<tei:lb/>two factors: the extreme climatic variability, both spatial and temporal, characteristic of desert<tei:lb/>environments, and the genetic isolation resulting from the wide separation of most of the popu-<tei:lb/>lations, often combined with small population size. Reduced gene flow between populations will<tei:lb/>be accentuated in the larger pulmonate snails considered here, where vagility is reduced further<tei:lb/>by their habit of aestivating for most of the year attached to rocks or trees, and the analogy has<tei:lb/>been drawn between the genetics of such semi-sessile animals and that of plants (Selander &amp;<tei:lb/>Hudson, 1976). Moreover, in periods of extensive mortality these various factors may combine to<tei:lb/>produce a temporal form of genetic drift, the 'bottle-neck' effect (Cain, 1983), which can cause local<tei:lb/>fixation of the genotype into a new mode.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Family-level groups</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The elucidation of higher-level taxonomy typically requires different, or at least additional<tei:lb/>characters to those used at the species level, and in this study the pallial cavity and proximal<tei:lb/>genital system (proximal to the gonad) have been found to be of particular value. Two family-<tei:lb/>level groups are here defined for the purposes of the discussion: the Enidae sensu s trie to<tei:lb/>(comprising the nothern families Eninae and Chondrulinae, sensu (Forcart, 1940); and the<tei:lb/>Cerastinae (or Pachnodinae) sensu Zilch, 1959). Any subsequent unqualified use of the term<tei:lb/>Enidae refers to the former group alone. Consideration of the relationships within the Cerastinae<tei:lb/>will be deffered until an anatomical study of all the component genera has been completed, and a<tei:lb/>phylogenetic analysis can be undertaken (Mordan, in prep.).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In the remainder of this discussion, the characters studied are considered system-by-system,<tei:lb/>rather than in terms of their value at the various taxonomic levels.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Shell</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Limited information on shell variation is available for some living populations from Dhofar and<tei:lb/>northern Oman, where the greatest collecting effort was directed. However, no detailed statistical<tei:lb/>analysis of shell form and its possible correlation with environmental variables has been<tei:lb/>attempted, partly because of the lack of an adequate range of sufficiently large samples. Also, no<tei:lb/>climatic or vegetational data are available for the relevant areas (see Taha et al., 1981, for a<tei:lb/>summary of Arabian climate). Assemblages of dead shells are generally unsuitable because of<tei:lb/>uncertainty of their provenance owing to the possibility of passive transport, and even living</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>ARABIAN ENID AE 261</tei:p>
         <tei:p>populations, at least of some species, appear to comprise adults from a number of year classes<tei:lb/>which may themselves vary (see below).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Woodruff (1978) has summarised knowledge of variation in the shells ofCerion, a West Indian<tei:lb/>genus with a ecology in many respects similar to the Arabian species, and many of the points he<tei:lb/>made are relevant to the present discussion. Woodruff considered the shell under the following<tei:lb/>headings, which for convenience are used here:</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Shell size and shape</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Populations of all species appear to vary considerably in size of both the adult shell and the<tei:lb/>aperture, and this is known to be correlated with climate in some other land snail groups<tei:lb/>(Heller, 1975; Woodruff, 1978; Tillier, 1981). Subtleties of shape are more difficult to quantify<tei:lb/>accurately, and here the simple height/diameter ratio (the spire index of Cain, 1977) is used; in the<tei:lb/>subsequent discussion 'shape' refers to this parameter.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Spatial variation in both size and shape is demonstrated in E. latireflexa from Dhofar, where<tei:lb/>roughly contemporary (1976-78) living samples are available from each of the three principal<tei:lb/>mountain ranges: Jabal Qamar, Jabal Qara, and Jabal Samhan. Analysis of variance shows<tei:lb/>highly significant inter-population variation in shell height and aperture size between the three<tei:lb/>mountains, whereas comparison of two living samples from separate sites within the Jabal Qara<tei:lb/>range shows no such differences (Table 7). Concomitant changes in shape are also recorded<tei:lb/>between the three mountains, with significantly more globose forms (leesi Pallary, Fig. 28f) in<tei:lb/>Jabal Qamar; as with size, there is no significant difference between populations within the Jabal<tei:lb/>Qara range (Table 7). This variation may be viewed in a number of ways: as vicariant, in the sense<tei:lb/>that topography has divided the species into a number of allopatric units which are starting to<tei:lb/>diverge morphologically, or as points on a cline of increasing shell size running west to east in<tei:lb/>Dhofar. If the latter is true then the cline reverses for, on the evidence of dead shells, both<tei:lb/>height and spire index increase westwards from Jebel Qamar, culminating in the form known as<tei:lb/>dinshawi Sykes (Fig. 28d). Alternatively, the observed inter-population differences could be<tei:lb/>purely random.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Populations of E. Candida on the Red Sea island of Dumsuk, in the Farsan group, and from the<tei:lb/>coastal Tihama of Saudi Arabia and Yemen are noticeably smaller than the more typical popu-<tei:lb/>lations at higher elevations where rainfall is greater, approaching the size of E. labiosa (a small,<tei:lb/>essentially coastal species, which also occurs on Dumsuk). Shell size and shape are also compared<tei:lb/>in living populations ofMastus omanensis and Imparietulajousseaumei in N. Oman; in the former<tei:lb/>no highly significant differences were found (Table 1), but in /. jousseaumei significant results<tei:lb/>have been obtained for all parameters, particularly for shell diameter and spire index (Table 4).<tei:lb/>Unusually elongate forms of jousseaumei were collected from a remote gorge on Jabal Sira (part<tei:lb/>of Jabal Akhdar, N. Oman) at an elevation of 2400 m. This may simply be part of the general<tei:lb/>trend for increasing spire index with elevation in this species, but Pupilla c.f. annandalei Pilsbry<tei:lb/>also occurs at this site (the species is otherwise known only from the type locality in Nepal)<tei:lb/>suggesting perhaps a long-isolated relict fauna in the area (Mordan, 19806).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Temporal variation in size, but not shape, is also evident in some populations. Significant<tei:lb/>differences are found for all size parameters (excepting lip width) in comparisons of living and<tei:lb/>very recently dead shells with shells which had been dead at least one dry season, from a single<tei:lb/>sample of Euryptyxis labiosa from Jabal Samhan, Dhofar. No significant differences exist<tei:lb/>between the living and freshly dead samples (Table 6). Climate, and in particular precipitation,<tei:lb/>varies considerably from year to year so that size variation between age classes is not at all<tei:lb/>unexpected; feeding activity, and thus shell growth, is known to be highly correlated with soil<tei:lb/>moisture levels in desert snails (Shackak, Orr &amp; Steinburger, 1975).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Shell shape formed the basis of Connolly's separation of Zebrinops ventricosa from Z. albata<tei:lb/>(Connolly, 1941), but a statistical comparison of spire indices of the two type series failed to<tei:lb/>separate them. Similarly, a plot of shell height/maximum shell diameter for the types and a<tei:lb/>relatively large sample from Dhala, S. Yemen suggests that all form part of a continuous growth<tei:lb/>series (Fig. 34).</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>262 P. B. MORDAN</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Certain aspects of shape which the spire index fails to distinguish may be useful in separating<tei:lb/>closely related species: in particular Euryptyxis fragosa and E. Candida, which commonly<tei:lb/>co-occur in SW. Arabia, differ in the curvature of the sides of the spire profile, the former being<tei:lb/>more or less flat from the apex down to the body whorl giving a conical profile (Figs 16e-g), the<tei:lb/>latter markedly convex between the nepionic and body whorls (Figs 16a-d). Eyryptyxis Candida<tei:lb/>in particular shows clear ontogenetic shape changes similar to those described by Cain (1981)<tei:lb/>for certain Cerion species, but knowledge of Euryptyxis ecology is insufficient to make any<tei:lb/>suggestions regarding possible adaptive values.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Lip size and shape</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Lip expansion is known to be inversely correlated with moisture in some desert snails (Heller,<tei:lb/>1975; Woodruff, 1978), and varies greatly between Arabian cerastine species. Although there is<tei:lb/>no thickening of the lip region in any species, other than the general thickening of the shell which<tei:lb/>occurs with age in many pulmonate groups (Pollard, 1975), the degree of lip flare or curvature<tei:lb/>proved a valuable diagnostic character at both generic and specific levels. No lip differentiation<tei:lb/>is found in either Zebrinops and Achatinelloides , and is only weakly developed or absent in<tei:lb/>Cerastus. The absence of a differentiated lip makes it impossible to establish maturity from the<tei:lb/>shell alone, but does not necessarily imply that growth is indeterminate.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Mature Euryptyxis always develop a lip, although this ranges from a simple flairing of the<tei:lb/>aperture in E. Candida and E. fragosa (Figs 1 6a-g) to a truly recurved structure in E. labiosa<tei:lb/>and E. latireflexa (Figs 25, 28). In this last species, the lip can become greatly expanded in some<tei:lb/>western populations (described under the name dinshawi Sykes). The apertural region appears to<tei:lb/>thicken progressively in most adult snails, and under suitable conditions this could give rise to<tei:lb/>such extreme lip development. In Euryptyxis overall thickening of the shell with age in certain<tei:lb/>geographical regions also appears to correlate positively with the degree of development of the<tei:lb/>columellar fold, although there do appear to be overriding intrinsic differences in the size of the<tei:lb/>fold between individual species (Fig. 18). Both enid species develop lips as adults, but Mastus<tei:lb/>omanensis shows a greater degree of variation in this respect, with one specimen from Qasaydot,<tei:lb/>Jebel Harim, N. Oman, having an exceptionally expanded lip reminiscent of the dinshawi form of<tei:lb/>Euryptyxis latireflexa.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Shell sculpture</tei:p>
         <tei:p>This again proved a valuable character in determining species of Cerastus and Euryptyxis. Of<tei:lb/>particular value was the relative development and regularity of radial ribbing, and in the case of<tei:lb/>the latter genus, the extent of development of spiral striae. Three of the species of Euryptyxis<tei:lb/>showed the development of very coarse ribbing in some populations which has given rise to a<tei:lb/>number of new names from Bourguignat and Jousseaume, but on the evidence of dissections of<tei:lb/>both smooth and ribbed forms of E. fragosa from Taizz, Yemen, such variation comes within the<tei:lb/>limits of the species as recognised here.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Apical shell sculpture of all species was examined under the stereoscan microscope but showed<tei:lb/>no taxonomically valuable characters.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Visceral mass</tei:p>
         <tei:p>There is considerable variation in the absolute and relative proportions of the shell and visceral<tei:lb/>mass between genera, expressed in terms of whorl number. Table 3 shows typical lengths of the<tei:lb/>three principal regions of the visceral mass: the mantle to the top of the lung; the top of the lung<tei:lb/>to the top of the stomach; and the region above the stomach, which is composed of the posterior<tei:lb/>lobe of the digestive gland and contains the gonad.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In all genera it is clear that the total visceral mass does not extend to the apex of the shell, and<tei:lb/>in many species the apical shell whorls are sealed off internally by a septum. In the more-elongate<tei:lb/>genera Imparietula and Zebrinops the visceral mass extends relatively further towards the apex<tei:lb/>than in the shorter forms where the differential between shell and total visceral mass may exceed<tei:lb/>three whorls. The observed differences between species with elongate and short shells appears to<tei:lb/>be accounted for by the greater length of the region above the stomach in the former although</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>ARABIAN ENIDAE 263</tei:p>
         <tei:p>additionally the lung of Imparietula extends almost two whorls, compared with about one<tei:lb/>whorl for the other taxa. On the evidence from the species examined, variation does not seem to<tei:lb/>correlate with family-level groupings.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Alimentary system</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Radula</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In all species for which there was anatomical material available the radula was examined but<tei:lb/>little useful information emerged. It is significant that in recent taxonomic revisions of genera in<tei:lb/>both the Enidae (eg. Heller, 1974; 1975) and Cerastinae (eg. Verdcourt, 1966, 1970; van Mol &amp;<tei:lb/>Coppois, 1980) the radula has not been used as a diagnostic character at either specific or generic<tei:lb/>levels. The results are summarized in Table 2.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In Euryptyxis both tooth size and number were generally fairly uniform between the three<tei:lb/>larger species, but in the smallest, E. labiosa, where overall radula size was smaller, tooth number,<tei:lb/>but not size, was distinctly reduced. In Cerastus the number of teeth was much as in Euryptyxis,<tei:lb/>but the teeth were generally smaller and the difference in size between the centrals and laterals<tei:lb/>more pronounced. In Zebrinops and Cerastus tooth number appeared to vary with size. The<tei:lb/>radula formula of both enid species was similar, but interestingly the size of individual teeth in<tei:lb/>Mastus omanensis, the larger species, was approximately twice that of Imparietula jousseaumei.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>At higher taxonomic levels there appear to be no consistent differences between the radulae of<tei:lb/>many orthurethran groups, although some authors, for example Solem (1962), have referred to a<tei:lb/>characteristic 'enid' radula. Watson (1920) pointed out the close similarities between the enid<tei:lb/>radula and that of other Pupillacea, and referred to a generalised radular type characteristic<tei:lb/>of 'the less-specialised members of the Orthurethra'. This has rows possessing a symetrically<tei:lb/>tricuspid central tooth with a large, usually pointed, mesocone and markedly smaller outer cusps,<tei:lb/>and bicuspid laterals with a reduced ectocone, which are typically larger than the central tooth.<tei:lb/>The marginal mesocone is often very blunt and the ectocone typically with a single cusp which<tei:lb/>becomes progressively more subdivided towards the edge of the radular row. In particular,<tei:lb/>there seem to be no reliable radular differences between the Enidae and the Cerastinae. Both<tei:lb/>Watson and Steenberg, in separating the Cerastinae ( = Pachnodinae of Steenberg, 1925) from<tei:lb/>the Enidae, made much of the distinctly angled disposition of the tooth rows in Pachnodus.<tei:lb/>However this genus is clearly specialised in its dentition, being arboreal, and is not typical of the<tei:lb/>family as a whole (see for example Euryptyxis fragosa, Fig. 19e); such radular modifications to an<tei:lb/>arboreal mode of life have been shown to be convergent in a number of cerastines (Solem, 1973).<tei:lb/>The total number of teeth in a row is normally higher in the Cerastinae than in enids and the<tei:lb/>latter group never approaches the total of 463 teeth recorded by van Mol &amp; Coppois (1980) for<tei:lb/>Pachnodus velutinus Pfeiffer. An analysis of enid tooth number from Hesse's review of the family<tei:lb/>(Hesse, 1933) shows an average number of 62 (s.d. 17-7; range 29-107) teeth per row.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Crop and Intestinal Folds</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Beck (1912: pi. 9, fig. 23.) illustrated the internal structure of the stomach region of Zebrina<tei:lb/>detrita Miiller, clearly showing two longitudinal crop folds passing posteriorly to the digestive<tei:lb/>gland ducts, and two intestinal folds running posteriorly from the ducts to the small intestine. It<tei:lb/>is evident from Beck's figure that, as in Arabian Mastus and Imparietula, the intestinal fold<tei:lb/>emanating from the posterior duct opening is more highly developed than that coming from the<tei:lb/>anterior duct, and in the Arabian enids it develops into a true flap which extends some distance<tei:lb/>along the intestine. This is in marked contrast to the cerastines Euryptyxis and Zebrinops where<tei:lb/>both intestinal 'folds' are little more than thickenings of the epithelium. The paired crop folds are<tei:lb/>similarly reduced, and are often barely visible. Such differences are of potential taxonomic signifi-<tei:lb/>cance, but require more-detailed investigation.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Lung cavity</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Lung folds are more highly developed in the Cerastinae than in any orthurethran group. A<tei:lb/>short renal fold is found in some Eninae (Weigmann, 1901; Beck, 1912) and Chrondrulinae</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>264 P. B. MORDAN</tei:p>
         <tei:p>(pers. obs.), as well as certain Pupillidae (Steenberg, 1925), but apparently occurs in all cerastines.<tei:lb/>Typically it extends the full length of the kidney, and in Amimopina (Solem, 1964) and a number<tei:lb/>of African cerastine genera (pers. obs.) is closed throughout its length, giving rise to the<tei:lb/>'pseudosigmurethrous' condition described by Solem (1964); a similar arrangement was also<tei:lb/>reported for Acanthinula aculeata by Watson (1920). In cerastines the renal fold normally joins<tei:lb/>the rectal fold at the apex of the pallial cavity, the latter then extending downwards to the<tei:lb/>pneumostome. In juvenile Euryptyxis the rectal fold was found to extend down only to the level<tei:lb/>of the tip of the kidney, but is fully developed in the adult. Rectal folds are found in all Arabian<tei:lb/>genera, Pachnodus (van Mol &amp; Coppois, 1980), Rachis (Seshaia, 1932), Amimopina (Solem, 1964),<tei:lb/>as well as Conulinus, Rhachidina and Rhachistia (pers. obs.). It is known to be lacking only in<tei:lb/>Edouardia (Connolly, 1925; and pers. obs.).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The lung folds of cerastines serve to partition the lung cavity into functionally distinct<tei:lb/>respiratory and excretory areas, and this is correlated with the development of prominent pallial<tei:lb/>venation, particularly of the respiratory area. By contrast, no vessels are macroscopically visible<tei:lb/>on the outer lung wall of enids. Tillier (1982) concluded that in slugs an extensive lung<tei:lb/>vascularisation increased effective respiratory surface area, and is developed in response to a<tei:lb/>reduction in lung size. Whilst the cerastine lung is not relatively shorter than in many enids (Table<tei:lb/>3), its respiratory area is reduced laterally by the lung folds. Differences in size between the vessels<tei:lb/>of the lung wall and those within the folds, which are typically narrower, were also noted and<tei:lb/>may be related to the differing functions of the two areas: the large vessels may, for example,<tei:lb/>serve to increase the blood supply to resorbtive region of the lung, which would potentially be of<tei:lb/>great importance in xerophilic taxa. The groove clearly has a resorbtive function in cerastines, as<tei:lb/>van Mol &amp; Coppois (1980:22) have described a well-developed brush border epithelium in the renal<tei:lb/>groove of Pachnodus.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Reproductive system</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Hermaphrodite Duct Diverticulae</tei:p>
         <tei:p>These structures were first described from Ena and Zebrina by Martens &amp; Wiegmann (1898:82),<tei:lb/>who also pointed out that they were absent from Pachnodus; subsequently Wiegmann (1901:282,<tei:lb/>pi. X, figs 12 &amp; 23) figured similar structures from Subzebrinus. Hesse (1933:154) also figured the<tei:lb/>diverticulae in Chondrula and their taxonomic importance was discussed by Steenberg (1925) who<tei:lb/>referred to them as culs-de-sac. They are presented in both Arabian enid species but are absent from<tei:lb/>the cerastines, and take the form of a clump of blind-ended sacs situated on the hermaphrodite<tei:lb/>duct between the hermaphrodite gland and the seminal vesicles (Figs 7A, 8B-D, 11 A). Their<tei:lb/>function is unknown although Wiegmann noted that they contained sperm. They are not<tei:lb/>known from any other Stylommatophora, and appear to form a synapomorphy separating the<tei:lb/>Enidae from other Orthurethra. Similar-looking structures have been reported in certain<tei:lb/>Basommatophora by Hubendick (1978) who referred to them as seminal vesicles; whether or not<tei:lb/>these are homologous with the stylommatophoran seminal vesicles as defined by Bayne (1973) is<tei:lb/>unknown. What is clear is that the Enidae possess a true seminal vesicle (sensu Bayne) in addition<tei:lb/>to the culs-de-sac, suggesting that the latter might be an independent structure formed de novo.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Spermoviduct</tei:p>
         <tei:p>On the basis of a figure and description in Beck (1912:223, pi. 9, fig. 31), Steenberg (1925) has<tei:lb/>stated that the enid spermoviduct is differentiated into three grooves; he had earlier described a<tei:lb/>similar organisation in the Clausiliidae (Steenberg, 1914), and was later to record the presence of<tei:lb/>this third groove in the Pupillidae (Steenberg, 1929). A similar configuration is found in northern<tei:lb/>Oman enids. Steenberg referred to this third groove as the 'serous canal' and suggested that it<tei:lb/>originated from the oviducal portion of the spermoviduct. In cerastines the organisation is<tei:lb/>different: van Mol &amp; Coppois (1980) described only two grooves in Pachnodus, a spermiduct and<tei:lb/>an oviduct, agreeing with my own histological observations on Euryptyxis and Zebrinops.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Visser (1977) has recently reviewed the systematic importance of the spermoviduct in the<tei:lb/>Pulmonata, and although he tentatively included the Clausiliidae in his 'incomplete triaulic'</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>ARABIAN ENIDAE 265</tei:p>
         <tei:p>category, I consider that Steenberg's observations on the clausiliids are better interpreted as<tei:lb/>the 'semi-diaulic' condition, as the serous canal remains a groove throughout its length, never<tei:lb/>separating as a free duct. Visser recognised two principal lineages in the Stylommatophora, both<tei:lb/>containing groups showing the semi-diaulic, monotrematic condition, but apparently differing in<tei:lb/>the homology of the free male duct (vas deferens). Group A, where the vas deferens forms from<tei:lb/>the sperm groove, included the Clausiliidae (the 'serous canal' is presumably equivalent to<tei:lb/>Visser's 'seminal groove' which serves to transport exogenous sperm). Group B incorporates<tei:lb/>some Pacific Orthurethra (Achatinellidae) and the Succineidae and is characterised by a vas<tei:lb/>deferens originating from the seminal groove.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Although both the Enidae and the Cerastinae are at Visser's semidiaulic level of organisation<tei:lb/>and would appear to fall within the stylommatophoran group A, they clearly differ fundamen-<tei:lb/>tally by the presence of a third spermoviducal canal in the former. Visser (1977:48) admits that<tei:lb/>'two morphologically widely divergent types of reproductive system as displayed by (i) Achatina<tei:lb/>and (ii) Agriolimax'' are included under the semi-diaulic designation in group A. The differences<tei:lb/>between the spermoviducts of enids and cerastines are analogous with those of Visser's examples:<tei:lb/>Achatina with a spermoviduct 'consisting of a female channel, a sperm groove and a seminal<tei:lb/>groove', and Agriolimax where 'the spermoviduct consists solely of a female channel and the<tei:lb/>sperm groove'.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Terminal Genitalia</tei:p>
         <tei:p>It is generally recognised that the terminal genitalia of land molluscs provide a rich source of<tei:lb/>taxonomically important information below the family level (Solem, 1978), and Cain (1982)<tei:lb/>referred specifically to gastropod genitalia in exemplifying what he termed 'privileged characters'<tei:lb/>in a taxonomic sense.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Some useful family-level characteristics also are to be found in the terminal region, particularly<tei:lb/>in the Cerastinae where the overall pattern appears to be remarkably constant. A highly<tei:lb/>characteristic brown spongy tissue is found lining the atrium and vagina of most cerastines.<tei:lb/>This was first noted by Seshaiya (1932) in Rachis, but is also present in Amimopina (Solem,<tei:lb/>1964), Pachnodus (van Mol &amp; Coppois, 1980), Euryptyxis, Cerastus, Zebrinops, Acatinelloides,<tei:lb/>Edouardia, Rachistia, and probably most other members of the subfamily; the only group for<tei:lb/>which it has been noted as absent is in central African Cerastus (Verdcourt, 1970). The histology<tei:lb/>has been described by van Mol &amp; Coppois (1980), who noted the presence of melanin and elastic<tei:lb/>fibres in the cells, and similar tissue has not been described in any other group. Additionally, a<tei:lb/>spermatheca lacking a diverticulum is always developed, and typically joins the free oviduct close<tei:lb/>to or at the commencement of the melanic tissue.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Of particular interest within the Orthurethra is the development of a penial appendix. An<tei:lb/>appendix with a remarkably uniform basic structure is found in many pupillacean groups in<tei:lb/>addition to the Enidae and Cerastinae: the Achatinellidae, Orculidae, Cionellidae, Amastridae,<tei:lb/>Pupillidae, Vertiginidae and Valloniidae. It seems probable that it is homologous within these<tei:lb/>various groups, and its presence defines Shilieko's suborder Pupillina (Shileiko, 1979).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>At lower taxonomic levels too, the anatomy of the penial appendix provides valuable<tei:lb/>information. It is broadly separable into three regions; a relatively short basal portion which<tei:lb/>internally bears pilasters, and which may be everted (Figs 1 1C, 37 A); a long, thin central stalk<tei:lb/>which is thick-walled and has only a very narrow lumen; and an elongate, apical sac which is thin<tei:lb/>walled with a large lumen. Shilieko (1979, fig. 4) recognised a total of five regions by including<tei:lb/>areas of differentiation at the top of the basal portion, and at the base of the central stalk. The<tei:lb/>function of the penial appendix is unknown, although Shileiko (1979) suggested it might act as a<tei:lb/>receptacle for autosperm prior to its injection into the spermatophore, and Forcart (1940) that it<tei:lb/>acted as a mechanical stimulator. It is present in all cerastines. The region of transition between<tei:lb/>the basal and central portions of the appendix, where the retractor muscle inserts, has proved to<tei:lb/>be of considerable taxonomic value in the cerastines, particularly at the generic level. In Arabian<tei:lb/>Euryptyxis the muscle inserts more-or-less directly at the junction, although it may expand and<tei:lb/>embrace the base of the central stalk just prior to attachment. In Zebrinops (Fig. 37D), and to a</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>266 P. B. MORDAN</tei:p>
         <tei:p>greater extent in Cerastus (Fig. 15A), the muscle actually encloses the stalk base, becoming<tei:lb/>attached at the top and forming an enclosed lumen; this trend is even more pronounced in the<tei:lb/>Ethiopian and Somali species of Cerastus and Zebrinops (Mordan, pers. obs.). The enclosure of<tei:lb/>the base of the central stalk is accompanied by the projection of the tip of the stalk into the basal<tei:lb/>lumen as an elongate papilla, which is particularly prominent in Zebrinops (Fig. 37D). The degree<tei:lb/>of contraction of the muscular sheath would partly determine the extent of protrusion of the<tei:lb/>papilla, and this may in turn be related to the state of relaxation of the individual specimen.<tei:lb/>Nevertheless, real differences in the length of the sheath, and of the papilla, do appear to exist<tei:lb/>between taxa.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The penis has a muscular sheath which is attached only at its top, and which in certain species<tei:lb/>appears always to be reflected to form a double-layered structure. There is a clearly demarked<tei:lb/>caecum which varies greatly in both size and shape between species, ranging from a short bulb in<tei:lb/>Euryptyxis to a long, so-called 'flagellum' in Cerastus. Whilst there are marked differences in<tei:lb/>both pilaster pattern and degree of differentiation of the caecal area between genera, within<tei:lb/>Euryptyxis, for example, pilaster pattern is fairly uniform: paired glandular pads embrace the<tei:lb/>epiphallar pore and extend downwards as pilasters which may bifurcate. Normally there is a<tei:lb/>further pair of glandular pads and associated pilasters opposite the pore. In two of the four<tei:lb/>species of Euryptyxis the glandular pads are divided latitudinally by a groove running round the<tei:lb/>penis below the level of the pore (Figs 24B, 27B), but in E. Candida and E. latireflexa (Figs 2 1C,<tei:lb/>3 1C) the groove appears to be absent. This groove is also well defined in Zebrinops, but in this<tei:lb/>genus no large glandular pads extend above it and the whole area of the caecum is dotted with<tei:lb/>small glandular areas (Fig. 37D).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The epiphallus itself has a relatively simple structure in Euryptyxis and Zebrinops, and appears<tei:lb/>externally as a thickened portion of the vas deferens with a series of transverse ridges or folds<tei:lb/>corresponding to a single internal row of pits which mould the spines of the spermatophore. The<tei:lb/>number of these pits does vary within species, but in the case of the four recognised Arabian<tei:lb/>species of Euryptyxis at least, there is no overlap in range between individual species (Table 5).<tei:lb/>An analysis of variance of the data shows highly significant between-species variation<tei:lb/>(F= 168-53, d.f. 3,31. p&lt;0-001). The African species E. revoili (24-25 pits) falls within the<tei:lb/>range of the Arabian E. latireflexa (17-28 pits), but the geographical distributions of these two<tei:lb/>species do not overlap. Moreover, their terminal genitalia differ in a number of other significant<tei:lb/>respects. Although most of the data in Table 5 were obtained from sympatric populations, where<tei:lb/>interspecific differences might be expected to be more pronounced, the limited evidence from<tei:lb/>allopatric populations of E. fragosa and E. latireflexa suggests this is not the case. Much larger<tei:lb/>epiphallar differences existed between syntopic E. fragosa and E. Candida at Taizz, than between<tei:lb/>latireflexa and labiosa in Dhofar, but whereas the latter species pair differ in size quite markedly,<tei:lb/>the former are of very similar adult size and some alternative species-isolation mechanism such as<tei:lb/>genital or spermatophore incompatibility might need to be invoked.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The spermatophore of Euryptyxis is unknown except for a few broken fragments found in<tei:lb/>the spermatheca of E. fragosa. These suggest that the structure is similar to Zebrinops, but at<tei:lb/>least some of the spines have five points at their tip rather than two as in Z. albata. Cerastus<tei:lb/>schweinfurthi also has a single row of spines on the spermatophore. It is tempting to speculate on<tei:lb/>the possibility of the spermatophore structure acting as, or reinforcing, some form of species-<tei:lb/>isolation mechanism, but direct evidence is lacking, and the importance of inter-specific genital<tei:lb/>differences in mechanical isolation may well have been overestimated (Mayr, 1963). Nevertheless,<tei:lb/>such characters can be of considerable empirical value in taxonomy (Arnold, 1973, 1983), and<tei:lb/>although the epiphallus cannot be considered a part of the external genitalia, it does secrete the<tei:lb/>spermatophore and presumably would be subject to selection pressures similar to those acting on<tei:lb/>the external genital organs.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The Enidae exhibit a rather greater variety of terminal genital structures than do the cerastines.<tei:lb/>In particular, the penial appendix is lacking in a number of genera, and a diverticulum is often<tei:lb/>developed on the spermathecal stalk. Forcart (1940) based his separation or the Turkish Enidae<tei:lb/>into the two subfamilies Eninae and Chondrulinae on the presence or absence of the penial<tei:lb/>appendix, and on this basis both subfamilies are represented in northern Oman. The generic</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>ARABIAN ENIDAE 267</tei:p>
         <tei:p>placement of the two northern Oman enids is based principally on the anatomy of the terminal<tei:lb/>genitalia.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Ecology</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Extremely little is known of the ecology of the southern Arabian land snail fauna. In common<tei:lb/>with most desert pulmonates much of the year is spent in aestivation, with only limited periods of<tei:lb/>activity during the wet seasons. The main precipitation results from the south-west monsoon<tei:lb/>during July and August, but there may be other shorter periods of rainfall during the year. In<tei:lb/>general, cerastines aestivate in shaded situations on trees and rocks, normally some distance<tei:lb/>above ground level. Mastus and Imparietula are known to utilise similar sites, but they are also<tei:lb/>commonly found in considerable numbers in soil under rocks and ground litter such as palm<tei:lb/>fronds. Whilst most cerastine species are largely restricted to upland regions, Imparietula and<tei:lb/>Mastus also occupy synanthropic sites in plantations and near houses at lower elevations.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A consideration of population structure of Euryptyxis during aestivation suggests that in most<tei:lb/>species maturity is normally reached in two growing seasons (not necessarily annual), and that<tei:lb/>the adults subsequently live for a number of years. Only two size classes appear to be represented,<tei:lb/>and adults greatly outnumber the juveniles.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The importance of predation is uncertain, but many aestivating colonies of Euryptyxis in<tei:lb/>Dhofar showed evidence of some snails having been broken off, leaving the shell lip adhering to<tei:lb/>the substrate. The most likely predators are small mammals such as the spiny mouse Acomys,<tei:lb/>three species of which are recorded from Dhofar (Harrison, 1980), and the rat (Rattus rattus)<tei:lb/>which is now present in many areas. Rodents in particular are known to exert a significant effect<tei:lb/>on population densities of desert snails in the Negev (Yom-Tov, 1970).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Sympatry of closely related species is of considerable taxonomic interest as it can provide the<tei:lb/>only situations in which the species may interact. In the problematical genus Euryptyxis the<tei:lb/>syntopic occurrence of the following species pairs was recorded: E.fragosa and E. Candida [Taizz,<tei:lb/>1978-79]; and E. latireflexa and E. labiosa [Tawi Atair, 1977-78]. It also seems likely that<tei:lb/>Cerastus schweinfurthi and C. scotti co-occur at Jebel Harir (Connolly, 1941), but living material<tei:lb/>is lacking. From the limited information available, however, there was no real evidence for<tei:lb/>any form of character displacement in sympatric populations when compared with those from<tei:lb/>allopatric situations.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Summary and conclusions</tei:p>
         <tei:p>A total of sixteen species of Enidae sensu lato are recognised in the present revision, from a list of<tei:lb/>thirty nominal species given by Connolly (1941); only one, Cerastus albonotata Verdcourt, has<tei:lb/>been newly described since that time. Whilst these figures appear to represent a synonymy ratio<tei:lb/>of 2: 1 , such a conclusion would be false, as almost the entire synonymy is accounted for within<tei:lb/>the single genus Euryptyxis.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The most significant taxonomic changes presented concern the generic repositioning of a<tei:lb/>number of taxa listed by Connolly under Euryptyxis, and in particular the placement of the<tei:lb/>northern Oman species into genera belonging to the essentially Palaearctic subfamilies Eninae<tei:lb/>and Chondrulinae. Within the Cerastinae, the existing generic units have been retained, but have<tei:lb/>now been defined in terms of anatomical as well as shell characters. In doing this it has been<tei:lb/>necessary to use additional corroborative data on closely related taxa from Somalia, Ethiopia<tei:lb/>and Socotra. In general there has been insufficient material to assess anatomical variation<tei:lb/>adequately. Indeed the anatomy of a large number of the taxa remains unknown. The greatest<tei:lb/>information was available for the commonest and most-widespread genus Euryptyxis and here<tei:lb/>in was demonstrated that species were better defined in terms of epiphallar rather than penial<tei:lb/>anatomy, suggesting that spermatophore morphology might play a role in species isolation.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Consistent, and I believe significant, anatomical differences have been shown to exist between<tei:lb/>the northern subfamilies Eninae and Chondrulinae (referred to here as the Enidae sensu stricto) ,</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>268 P. B. MORDAN</tei:p>
         <tei:p>and the southern Cerastinae. The former are uniquely defined by the possession of a clump of<tei:lb/>culs-de-sac on the hermaphrodite duct, whilst the latter show a number of specialisations of the<tei:lb/>pallial complex, apparently related to water conservation, as well as of the reproductive system.<tei:lb/>Moreover, there appear to be no synapomorphic characters to unite the two as sister groups in a<tei:lb/>cladistic sense, normalization of these differences in nomenclature is, however, deferred until<tei:lb/>there is greater knowledge of the anatomy of related orthurethran families.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>British Museum (Natural History)</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The littorinid molluscs of mangrove forests in the<tei:lb/>Indo-Pacific region</tei:p>
         <tei:p>D. G. Reid</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Periwinkles (family Littorinidae) are amongst the most intensively studied of marine gastropods,<tei:lb/>because of their worldwide distribution and abundance in the intertidal environment. This<tei:lb/>monograph is a comprehensive account of the taxonomy, biology and biogeography of the<tei:lb/>'Littorina scabra' species complex, a hitherto poorly-known group of littorinids ubiquitous in<tei:lb/>mangrove habitats throughout the Indo-Pacific region. Twenty species are recognized, where<tei:lb/>most previous authors have distinguished only three. Detailed accounts of the anatomy of each<tei:lb/>species are given, with particular attention to the reproductive system, by which the species can<tei:lb/>be reliably distinguished. A key to shells is provided, and 100 figures and plates illustrate the<tei:lb/>range of shell variation, anatomical characters and geographical distribution of each species.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Drawing on comparisons with ten littorinid genera, evolutionary trends in the morphology of<tei:lb/>male and female reproductive tracts, sperm nurse cells, egg capsules, reproductive modes and<tei:lb/>radulae are discussed. These features are assessed as taxonomic characters, and the large<tei:lb/>literature on the morphology of the family is reviewed. Cladistic analysis of anatomical<tei:lb/>characters supports a reclassification of the Littorinidae, including placement of the 'scabra'<tei:lb/>group in the genus Littoraria.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>In addition to molluscan systematists, this monograph should be of interest to marine<tei:lb/>biogeographers and ecologists working in the mangrove environment. In recent years much<tei:lb/>ecological and genetical research has been stimulated by the discovery of sibling species of<tei:lb/>Littorina in Europe. The Littoraria species have a similar potential in the Indo-Pacific, where up<tei:lb/>to ten species may occur sympatrically. Several of the species are polymorphic for shell colour<tei:lb/>and have already been used as material for the study of mechanisms of natural selection.<tei:lb/>1986, 240pp, 99 illustrations, 1 colour plate. 565 00978 8 35.00.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p/>

         <tei:p>Zoology series VolSO No 5 30 October 1986</tei:p>

      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>The Bulletin of the British Museum (Natural History), instituted in 1949, is issued in four<tei:lb/>scientific series, Botany, Entomology, Geology (incorporating Mineralogy) and Zoology, and<tei:lb/>an Historical series.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Papers in the Bulletin are primarily the results of research carried out on the unique and<tei:lb/>ever-growing collections of the Museum, both by the scientific staff of the Museum and by<tei:lb/>specialists from elsewhere who make use of the Museum's resources. Many of the papers are<tei:lb/>works of reference that will remain indispensable for years to come.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Parts are published at irregular intervals as they become ready, each is complete in itself,<tei:lb/>available separately, and individually priced. Volumes contain about 300 pages and several<tei:lb/>volumes may appear within a calendar year. Subscriptions may be placed for one or more of<tei:lb/>the series on either an Annual or Per Volume basis. Prices vary according to the contents of<tei:lb/>the individual parts. Orders and enquiries should be sent to:</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Publications Sales,</tei:p>
         <tei:p>British Museum (Natural History),<tei:lb/>Cromwell Road,</tei:p>
         <tei:p>London SW7 5BD,<tei:lb/>England.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>World List abbreviation: Bull. Br. Mus. nat. Hist. (Zool.)</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Trustees of the British Museum (Natural History), 1986</tei:p>

         <tei:p>r 2 DEC 19</tei:p>
         <tei:p>PRESENTFJ</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Revision of the western African earthworm genus<tei:lb/>Millsonia (Octochaetidae: Oligochaeta) with notes<tei:lb/>on two new species of the genus Agastrodrilus<tei:lb/>(Octochaetidae) from Ghana</tei:p>
         <tei:p>R.W. Sims</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Department of Zoology, British Museum (Natural History), Cromwell Road, London SW7 5BD</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Contents</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Synopsis.<tei:lb/>Introduction .<tei:lb/>Morphology .<tei:lb/>Taxonomy<tei:lb/>The genus Millsonia.<tei:lb/>The genus Agastrodrilus .<tei:lb/>Acknowledgements .<tei:lb/>References</tei:p>
         <tei:p>273<tei:lb/>273<tei:lb/>274<tei:lb/>276<tei:lb/>277<tei:lb/>309<tei:lb/>312<tei:lb/>312</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The western African earthworm genus Millsonia Beddard, 1894 is revised. A table of diagnostic characters,<tei:lb/>descriptions and figures are provided for 27 species regarded as valid, of which 14 are described as new.<tei:lb/>Three taxa previously associated with the genus are excluded: meridionalis Omodeo, 1972 is transferred to<tei:lb/>Dichogaster Beddard, 1888 s.l. and schlegeli Horst, 1884 to Benhamia Michaelsen, 1889; Dichogaster insignis<tei:lb/>Michaelsen, 1922 is regarded as a species incertae sedis. Notes are also provided on the allied genus<tei:lb/>Agastrodrilus Omodeo &amp; Vaillaud, 1967 with descriptions of two new species.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Introduction</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Earthworms of the genus Millsonia inhabit the soils of the wet savannah and forest regions of<tei:lb/>western Africa from the Ivory Coast eastwards to Nigeria. Although the ranges partly overlap, the<tei:lb/>genus is replaced in the soils of the drier savannahs to the north and west by the allied Benhamia;<tei:lb/>both, however, are sympatric with their more widely distributed mutual relative, Dichogaster.<tei:lb/>Until comparatively recently only a few species of Millsonia were recognized, but following surveys<tei:lb/>in the Ivory Coast (Omodeo, 1958) and especially Ghana, the situation has changed radically.<tei:lb/>Collections were made in connection with the ecological surveys carried out by the laboratory at<tei:lb/>Lamto, Ivory Coast, while Miss M. Tazelaar collected earthworms around Kumasi, Ghana (Sims,<tei:lb/>1965a). Even more recently, Professor J.J. Niles made further collections in the Kumasi region,<tei:lb/>then, in 1966, Dr J.D. Plisko Winkworth visited Ghana and sampled earthworm populations<tei:lb/>throughout the country. These latter series were eventually presented to the British Museum<tei:lb/>(Natural History) where they were found to contain so many new species of Millsonia that it<tei:lb/>became necessary to undertake a special study of the genus. The results of this investigation form<tei:lb/>the subject of the present report with the genus here recognized as comprising some 27 species<tei:lb/>of which 14 are described as new to science. Additionally, it emerges that the species occur in a<tei:lb/>wide range of habitats, spreading from the forests and wet savannahs into cultivation, i.e. from</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Bull. Br. Mus. nat. Hist. (Zool.) 50 (5): 273-313</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Issued 30 October 1986</tei:p>
         <tei:p>273</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>274 R. w. SIMS</tei:p>
         <tei:p>plantations to village fields, gardens and even to the Botanic Gardens at Aburi,Ghana. Two new<tei:lb/>species of the morphologically similar genus Agastrodrilus were also collected and are described<tei:lb/>later in the report.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Morphology</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Size. Most species of Millsonia are large, a few being over 500 mm in length, and several having<tei:lb/>more than 500 body segments. However, some have small-sized individuals seldom exceeding<tei:lb/>100 mm in length with fewer than 200 body segments.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Setae. Usually small and uniform in size and shape, not always to be seen anteriorly in mature<tei:lb/>individuals. (The ventral setae in the anterior region are considerably enlarged in species of the<tei:lb/>genus Agastrodilus.)</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Dorsal pores. The first dorsal pore is variably located in a pre-clitellar furrow according to species.<tei:lb/>Its situation seems not to be correlated with the thickening, i.e. the muscularization, of the anterior<tei:lb/>septa (see below) that reflects the burrowing prowess of a species.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Clitellum. Chiefly saddle-shaped; present over at least segments xiv-xvii, commonly extending<tei:lb/>xiii-xix. The genital field, which is enclosed between the ventral borders of the clitellum, is highly<tei:lb/>variable in appearance, being characteristically developed for each species. The clitellum is often<tei:lb/>well developed in immature individuals (gonads poorly developed, seminal vesicles absent).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Male terminalia. Species of the genus form two assemblages according to the arrangement of the<tei:lb/>male and prostatic pores. Over half of the species have an acanthodriline arrangement (male pores<tei:lb/>paired xviii, prostatic pores paired xvii and xix), while the remainder have a microscolecine<tei:lb/>reduction with the male and prostatic pores paired only on segment xvii. The male pores are<tei:lb/>sometimes carried on porophores but mostly they are superficial and commonly discharge into<tei:lb/>paired genital pouches or perhaps a single median pouch. (The pouches are everted during<tei:lb/>copulation, and often during relaxing, killing and fixing when preserving specimens).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Spermathecal pores. In species with an acanthodriline arrangement of the male terminalia, the<tei:lb/>spermathecae occur in two segments, vii and ix, and consequently open into furrows 7/8/9. Among<tei:lb/>other species that have a microscolecine reduction of the male terminalia, there is only a single pair<tei:lb/>of spermathecae. Apart from two exceptions, hemina and oracapensis, that have the spermathecae<tei:lb/>opening into 7/8, the spermathecae open into furrow 8/9. The spermathecal pores are located<tei:lb/>the same distance apart as the male pores. The furrows into which they open are appropriately<tei:lb/>modified whenever the genital field is specialized, since the pores of the opposing systems of<tei:lb/>concopulants are closely applied during sperm transfer. Whenever the male pores open into deep,<tei:lb/>pit-like, genital pouches that are everted during copulation, the latter are accommodated by<tei:lb/>imaginations (vestibula, i.e. 'vestibules') that develop in the furrow in the vicinity of each<tei:lb/>spermathecal pore. The spermathecal pore is rarely single (median ventral only in ditheca), but a<tei:lb/>single mid- ventral vestibule may develop with maturity (as in nigra) although the pores within are<tei:lb/>paired (Sims, 19656).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Female pores. Located on segment xiv, the female pores are usually paired near setal lines a or b,<tei:lb/>sometimes within aa; the pore is rarely single (mid-ventral in ditheca and nilesf).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Genital papillae. Papillate and transverse, glandular pads are common ventrally in the clitellar<tei:lb/>region and on nearby segments in fully adult individuals of most species. The papillae are mostly<tei:lb/>large with a single pair occurring on each segment, but occasionally they are small and irregularly<tei:lb/>scattered in discrete areas on the segments. Although papillae are usually arranged in discrete<tei:lb/>species-distinct patterns, there is considerable intraspecific variation in their development so that<tei:lb/>the full specific complement is seldom realized.</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>THE EARTHWORM GENUS Millsonia 275</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Septa. Interspecific differences in the thickening (muscularization) of the anterior septa are wide-<tei:lb/>spread, but even so they are not employed taxonomically. They do, however, signify that the<tei:lb/>included species have highly variably burrowing abilities and divergent life styles.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Gizzards. Two simple gizzards are present, and they are invariably separate. The anterior gizzard is<tei:lb/>in segment v and separated by septum 5/6 from the posterior gizzard in vi. They are usually<tei:lb/>uniform, but occasionally one is reduced being either smaller or less muscular, or exceptionally,<tei:lb/>vestigial as in anomala. (Both gizzards are reduced in Agastrodrilus.)</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Calciferous glands. These are stalked, lamellate structures, paired dorso-laterally on the<tei:lb/>oesophagus in segments ;cv, xvi, xvii. Occasionally the anterior pair is reduced, e.g. anomala,<tei:lb/>brevicingulata* centralis.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Intestine. The last oesophageal segment is clearly xvii (i.e. the location of the posterior pair of<tei:lb/>(oesophageal) calciferous glands), in segment xviii the gut is transitional in structure since it begins<tei:lb/>to dilate while becoming thinner-walled so that in xix it forms the anterior end of the intestine.<tei:lb/>Apart from young juveniles, paired segmental caeca occur in 1 to 36 segments of the anterior<tei:lb/>intestine with the foremost pair located between segments xxiv and xxx. The number of caecal<tei:lb/>pairs is more or less constant for each species, but where there is a long series of caeca, the posterior<tei:lb/>pairs become progressively smaller until the posteriormost may be difficult to detect. The caeca<tei:lb/>are morphologically digitate and never saccular, thus the condition of the anterior intestine differs<tei:lb/>from the pouch-like shape present in contracted specimens of many western African species of<tei:lb/>Dichogaster.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Testes. Holandric, within paired testis sacs in segments x and xi.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Prostates. These may be paired in segments xvii and xix (acanthodriline arrangement) or in<tei:lb/>segment xvii only (miscroscolecine reduction). They may form a simple 'U' and be confined to a<tei:lb/>single segment of become highly convoluted and encroach into nearby segments. Ectally each<tei:lb/>gland becomes muscular to form a slender duct that leads to the exterior; generally the length of the<tei:lb/>duct varies according to the species.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Spermathecae. Invariably located in segments viii and/or ix. Spermathecae (usually paired) occur<tei:lb/>in both segments in species with an acanthodriline arrangement of the male terminalia but in<tei:lb/>species with a microscolecine reduction of the male terminalia, there is a single pair in only one<tei:lb/>segment. The species ditheca is exceptional in having only a single, unpaired, median ventral<tei:lb/>spermatheca in each of segments viii and ix (see above Spermathecal pores). About half of the<tei:lb/>species have simple, adiverticulate Spermathecae. The Spermathecae of most other species have one<tei:lb/>or two diverticula, mostly arising from the duct but occasionally on the ampulla; the diverticula are<tei:lb/>usually unilocular and only rarely multilocular.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Nephridia. Closed exonephric meronephridia are present on the parietes throughout most of the<tei:lb/>body; they are usually reduced or absent from the anterior, mainly pre-testicular, segments where<tei:lb/>they may occur on the septa. In the oesophageal segments the meronephrida tend to be diffuse, but<tei:lb/>in the intestinal segments they become more saccular and discrete with perhaps 10 to 12 forming a<tei:lb/>row along the equator of a segment, with each discharging directly to the exterior. Many species<tei:lb/>have an additional pair of open holonephridia in each intestinal segment. Each holonephridium<tei:lb/>consists of a long duct leading peripherally over the parietes to the dorsum from a ventral<tei:lb/>nephrostome opening in the preceding segment; the duct is looped and becomes convoluted before<tei:lb/>discharging to the exterior on the equator of the segment about setal distance aa from the mid-<tei:lb/>dorsal line. Terminal vesicle or bladder not seen, presumably absent from each holonephridium.<tei:lb/>The coiled portion of the holonephridial duct has subsidiary convolutions that possibly function as<tei:lb/>closed meronephridia. An additional specialization was reported in M. anomala by Omodeo</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>276 R. w. SIMS</tei:p>
         <tei:p>(1955); here the holonephridial ducts of each side communicate with a (paired) longitudinal canal<tei:lb/>on the parietes on both sides of the ventral nerve cord.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The discrete meronephric condition occurs in most species with numerous intestinal caeca (over<tei:lb/>ten pairs), whereas the combined meronephric and holonephric condition is present in the majority<tei:lb/>of species with fewer than ten pairs of intestinal caeca. This correlation between the presence of<tei:lb/>numerous pairs of intestinal caeca and the absence of holonephridia makes the causal factors<tei:lb/>seemingly physiological, i.e. species adaptations to their habitat niches. Among earthworms of the<tei:lb/>family Megascolecidae, the structure of the excretory system appears to be highly plastic with<tei:lb/>frequent partial or complete replacement of holonephridia by meronephridia, even among con-<tei:lb/>generic species. The occurrence of meronephridia does not therefore appear to represent a highly<tei:lb/>significant phylogenetic event, but instead a common phenomenon that may occur even within a<tei:lb/>minor radiation whenever it is advantageous for emergent groups to adjust to changing conditions<tei:lb/>or to exploit new niches. The incidence of both nephridial conditions among species of the genus<tei:lb/>Millsonia thus parallels comparable situations in the family Megascolecidae and, in doing so, casts<tei:lb/>doubts on the validity of the family Octochaetidae that is currently delineated from the family<tei:lb/>Acanthodrilidae with only holonephridia by having only meronephridia.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Taxonomy</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The megascolecoid genus Millsonia was erected by Beddard (1894) for two non-perichaetine,<tei:lb/>meronephric species lacking penial setae but with paired male and prostatic pores combined on<tei:lb/>segment xvii, two gizzards, three pairs of calciferous glands and paired intestinal caeca. At that<tei:lb/>time, megascolecoid earthworms with a lumbricine arrangement of the setae and spermathecae in<tei:lb/>the pre-testicular segments were divided into the family Cryptodrilidae (male and prostatic pores<tei:lb/>paired on segment xvii or xviii) and the Acanthodrilidae (male pores paired on segment xviii and<tei:lb/>the prostatic pores paired on segments JCVH and xix). Thus Millsonia was originally assigned to<tei:lb/>the family Cryptodrilidae to join, among others, its ally Dichogaster Beddard, 1888 (type species<tei:lb/>D. damonis Beddard, 1 888 from Fiji having male pores on xvii with prostatic pores and further<tei:lb/>prostatic pores on *v7and xix but internally lacking intestinal caeca). When Michaelsen produced<tei:lb/>his monograph (1900), he considered the location and arrangement of the male terminalia to be<tei:lb/>relatively unimportant systematically and instead gave greater weighting to internal characters.<tei:lb/>Thus he united all of the meronephridial species of the Cryptodrilidae previously accommodated<tei:lb/>in Dichogaster, Millsonia and Balanta (the last was a related monotypic genus separated by<tei:lb/>Michaelsen in 1898 for a species with combined, paired male and prostatic pores on segment xix).<tei:lb/>Furthermore, they were placed with species of the genus Benhamia previously assigned to the<tei:lb/>family Acanthodrilidae to form a vast, heterogenous genus, Dichogaster, that accommodated all<tei:lb/>species with two gizzards and three pairs of calciferous glands. One result was that three species<tei:lb/>with an acanthodriline arrangement of the male terminalia and possessing intestinal caeca, inermis<tei:lb/>Michaelsen, caecifera Benham and heteronephra Michaelsen (all previously assigned to the genus<tei:lb/>Benhamia), were first brought together with nigra Beddard, the type species of Millsonia that<tei:lb/>has a microscolecine arrangement of the male terminalia (i.e. male and prostatic pores paired on<tei:lb/>segment JCVH only).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>During the next few decades, many new species were described in the genus Dichogaster to form<tei:lb/>what was clearly an heterogenous assemblage containing nearly two hundred nominal species.<tei:lb/>Eventually, Omodeo (1955) divided Dichogaster s.l. into six genera by separating the species on the<tei:lb/>number and location of the calciferous glands together with the occurrence of penial setae and<tei:lb/>intestinal caeca. The genus Millsonia was resurrected for the species without penial setae but,<tei:lb/>among other characters, possessing intestinal caeca. Subsequently, small-bodied species similar to<tei:lb/>Millsonia, but with enlarged ventral setae and rudimentary gizzards, were separated in the genus<tei:lb/>Agastrodrilus by Omodeo and Vaillaud, 1967 (see p. 309). This group of genera is now assigned to<tei:lb/>the family Octochaetidae sensu Gates (1959).</tei:p>
         <tei:pb/>
      </tei:div>
      <tei:div>
         <tei:p>THE EARTHWORM GENUS Millsonia 217</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Genus MILLSONIA Beddard, 1894</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Millsonia Beddard, 1894: 380; Beddard, 1895: 479; Omodeo, 1955: 218; Omodeo, 1958: 22 &amp; 59.<tei:lb/>Dichogaster (part): Michaelsen, 1900: 334; Stephenson, 1930: 851.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>TYPE SPECIES. Millsonia nigra Beddard, 1894 (Omodeo, 1955, by subsequent designation).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DIAGNOSIS. Octochaetidae lacking penial setae with two simple, well-developed gizzards, the<tei:lb/>anterior gizzard in segment v and the posterior gizzard in vi\ stalked, lamellate, calciferous glands<tei:lb/>paired on the oesophagus in segments xv, xvi, xvii (the anterior pair occasionally reduced); paired<tei:lb/>digitate caeca present in at least one, usually several adjacent segments, of the anterior region of the<tei:lb/>intestine; typhlosole present; setal couples ab and cd similar in size, small.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DISTRIBUTION. Ivory Coast, Ghana, Togo, Benin and Nigeria.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>REMARKS. The genus is restricted to contain only species with two large, simple gizzards (never<tei:lb/>fused or partly fused gizzards) and lacking penial setae; thus species such as schlegeli Horst, 1884<tei:lb/>and meridionalis Omodeo, 1973 with penial setae and fused gizzards are excluded. Examination of<tei:lb/>the types of schlegeli in the Rijksmuseum van Natuurlijke Historic, Leiden, reveals that Horst<tei:lb/>miscounted the segments and the species should be assigned to the genus Benhamia Michaelsen,<tei:lb/>1889 (sensu Omodeo, 1955); this is clearly a taxon requiring a separate study (Michaelsen, 19140).<tei:lb/>While meridionalis is seemingly a species of Dichogaster Beddard, 1888 (sensu Omodeo, 1955), a<tei:lb/>genus in which the anterior region of the intestine is commonly saccular in each segment (in<tei:lb/>contracted specimens this condition gives the appearance of the intestine being caecate).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>On the other hand, the identity of Dichogaster insignis Michaelsen, 1922, known only from a<tei:lb/>single subadult collected at Juring on the Sulima River in eastern Sierra Leone, is problematic.<tei:lb/>Unlike meridionalis and schlegeli, this species from Juring possesses separate gizzards in v and vi<tei:lb/>and lacks penial setae, but intestinal caeca were not recorded by Michaelsen. (Examination of<tei:lb/>the unique holotype in the Rijksmuseum van Natuurlijke Historic, Leiden, does not resolve the<tei:lb/>question of the occurrence of intestinal caeca since the fore-gut has been removed and only the<tei:lb/>oesophageal portion has been separately retained in an included vial.) Unfortunately, compari-<tei:lb/>sons with other species are made different because of the immaturity of the individual, with the<tei:lb/>clitellum, spermathecae and prostates being only poorly developed. Omodeo (1958: 59) assigned<tei:lb/>insignis to the genus Millsonia on the characters already noted, and also on the absence of diver-<tei:lb/>ticula from the spermathecae, a condition rarely encountered among western African octochaetids<tei:lb/>outside of the genus Millsonia. Examination during this current investigation of the holotype<tei:lb/>revealed additionally the absence of holonephridia, the occurrence of the female pores in setal line<tei:lb/>a and the lack of papillae and other external specializations; thus insignis would appear to resemble<tei:lb/>M. centralis from Central Ghana, a species with 14 pairs of intestinal caeca. However, to unite<tei:lb/>these two taxa would imply that Michaelsen either omitted to record or overlooked the presence of<tei:lb/>numerous intestinal caeca, clearly unacceptable requirements. In these circumstances, I propose to<tei:lb/>regard the taxon Dichogaster insignis Michaelsen, 1922 as a species incertae sedis; hopefully the<tei:lb/>acquisition of additional material from the type locality will, in time, help elucidate the problem of<tei:lb/>its generic identity. In any case, support for this decision is provided by distributional evidence as<tei:lb/>species of Millsonia have not been reported from Sierra Leone nor from the adjacent state of<tei:lb/>Liberia, the most westerly recorded so far coming from the Ivory Coast.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The comparative characters of the valid species currently included in the genus Millsonia are<tei:lb/>listed in Table 1.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>DIAGNOSIS. Spermathecal pores paired in furrows 7/8/9 in setal lines bb; male pores paired jcvm,<tei:lb/>prostatic pores paired xvii and xix; female pores paired near a immediately within aa; small closely<tei:lb/>paired papillae present fvm and in furrows 13/14 and 14/15, single transverse pads present<tei:lb/>mid-ventrally xxi-xxv; nine pairs of intestinal caeca Jtjcv/-xxjc/v; holo- and meronephric.</tei:p>

         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. External characters. Length 11 0-1 24 mm, diameter 5-6 mm. Segments 180-249,<tei:lb/>multiannulate. First dorsal pore 5/6. Citellum (f xiii) xiv-xix, saddle-shaped extending ventrally to<tei:lb/>within setal lines be. Male pores paired xviii discharging into paired seminal grooves joining the<tei:lb/>(paired) prostatic pores in xvii and xix where they are located between setal lines ab. Female pores<tei:lb/>paired slightly within setal lines aa. Spermathecal pores paired in furrows 7/8/9 in setal line b, the<tei:lb/>anterior pair being larger. Papillae closely paired on the posterior surface of the mid-ventrum of viii<tei:lb/>and by the mid-ventral line in furrows 1 3/ 1 4/ 1 5; a swollen transverse pad occurs between setal lines<tei:lb/>aa on each of segments xxi-xxv, the more posterior pads being less well defined.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Setae uniform, small, closely paired, ventral; post-clitellar formula aa : ab : be : cd =<tei:lb/>14: 1 -2 : 9 1 where dd = three-quarters of the body circumference.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Internal characters First septum 4/5, septa 5/6/7 greatly thickened, septa 7/8/9/10 less so. The<tei:lb/>anterior gizzard is weakly developed, being little more than a slight thickening of the oesophageal<tei:lb/>wall, whereas the posterior gizzard is strongly muscularized; the anterior pair of calciferous glands<tei:lb/>is often reduced in size. Intestinal caeca, nine pairs, present xxvi-xxxiv. Prostates paired xvii and<tei:lb/>xix, tightly convoluted each with a slender muscular ectal duct. Spermathecae paired viii and ix,<tei:lb/>adiverticulate, the duct and ampulla of each are approximately the same length. Nephridia:<tei:lb/>meronephridia occur throughout the body, a pair of holonephridia are additionally present in each<tei:lb/>intestinal segment where their ducts unite with paired longitundinal canals lying one on each side of<tei:lb/>the ventral nerve cord.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>TYPE LOCALITY. Gagnoa, southern Ivory Coast.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>RECORDS. 4C Gagnoa (604'N, 555'W), southern Ivory Coast; Nov. 1954 (syntypes of Millsonia anomala),<tei:lb/>(see Remarks below).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DISTRIBUTION. Southern Ivory Coast.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>REMARKS. The description and the text-figure are based on Omodeo (1955). See also the Remarks in<tei:lb/>the account of the morphologically similar species M. omodeoi.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Lavelle (1971) recorded that M. anomala constitutes a major part of the oligochaete fauna in the<tei:lb/>low-lying, wet areas of the Lam to savannah of the Ivory Coast where breeding occurs twice a year<tei:lb/>at the end of the rainy seasons. (For further ecological information see also: Lavelle, Douhalei &amp;<tei:lb/>Sow, 1974; Lavelle &amp; Meyer, 1976.)</tei:p>

         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. External characters. Length (aclitellate specimens 60-198 mm) clitellate specimens<tei:lb/>186, 210mm; diameter 5-8 mm. Female pores paired xiv within aa<tei:lb/>about 5 aa apart, approximately equidistant from the other and the adjacent seta a; located slightly<tei:lb/>anteriorly to the setal ring. In addition to the papillae on the pads associated with<tei:lb/>the male and spermathecal pores, one syntype has midventral pads in furrows 13/14, 14/15 and<tei:lb/>15/16 that carry between four and eight papillae.          <tei:p>Internal characters. Septa 4/5 and 5/6 strongly thickened, other anterior septa membranous.<tei:lb/>Gizzards large, strongly muscularized. Intestinal caeca of variable number 6-12 xxix-xxxiv to<tei:lb/>xxix-xl. Prostates, single pair jcv, highly convoluted filling most of xviii and xix with a long,<tei:lb/>muscular ectal region. Single pair of spermathecae in ix, with a stout duct and distinct ampulla; two<tei:lb/>diverticula present on the duct, the lateral diverticulum arises more ectally than the more highly<tei:lb/>convoluted medial diverticulum.          <tei:p>TYPE LOCALITY. North Ejura, Ghana.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>MATERIAL EXAMINED. 2C* 24A* Near the river, north of Ejura (723'n 115"W), central Ghana; coll. J.D.<tei:lb/>Plisko, date? BM(NH) 1984.4.1: 1-26 (syntypes of Millsonia artesetosa).</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DISTRIBUTION. Known only from the type locality.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>REMARKS. The near equidistant location of the setae on the ventral surface and the reduction in the<tei:lb/>number of strongly muscularized anterior septa may indicate that this is a weakly burrowing<tei:lb/>species; possibly it is a denizen of forest litter and crevices among tree roots.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>Millsonia ashantiensis sp. nov.<tei:lb/>(Figs 3C &amp; 4C)</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DIAGNOSIS.          <tei:p>282 R. w. SIMS</tei:p>
         <tei:p>DESCRIPTION. External changes. Length (aclitellate specimens 60-255 mm) clitellate specimens<tei:lb/>21 1-330 mm; diameter (aclitellate specimens 2-5 mm) clitellate specimens 5-8 mm. Segments<tei:lb/>400-51 7 (402 in juvenile 60 mm long); commonly triannulate. First dorsal pore 4/5 but very small,<tei:lb/>large dorsal pores beginning in furrow (1 1/12) 12/13, 13/14, (14/15).          <tei:p>REMARKS. The precise location of the type locality may be open to speculation, since subsequent<tei:lb/>records of the species are from central Ghana. It is possible that the only connection between the<tei:lb/>holotype and the currently recognized type locality, Axim, is that the town was either the place of<tei:lb/>residence of the collector or the port of dispatch to Europe.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>The spelling of the name caecifera needs comment since Reynolds and Cook (1976) erroneously<tei:lb/>reverted to the original orthography which was merely a printer's error of transcription. In the<tei:lb/>original description, the spelling in the title and second paragraph, page 103, (also the 'running<tei:lb/>head' at the top of each alternate page) was inadvertently printed as 'caecifera'' whereas the<tei:lb/>etymologically correct 'caciferd appears on page 107. Clearly the former is in error so the first<tei:lb/>spelling of the name is not to be retained but that on page 107 should be employed instead<tei:lb/>(International Code of Zoological Nomenclature (3rd edition 1985), Article 32 (b) (i)). In any case<tei:lb/>there is a first reviser since Beddard (1900)* validated the name caecifera Article 24 (C). The<tei:lb/>author, Benham, a nineteenth-century graduate of Oxford University was, of necessity, well<tei:lb/>schooled in the classical languages and clearly would not have employed the stem ''coed- in a<tei:lb/>Latin-derived scientific name to designate a 'caeca-bearing' species. It seems likely that the error<tei:lb/>arose where the author made use of ligature to join the vowels when writing his original descrip-<tei:lb/>tion, subsequently the printer mis-read the '#' for an V in the hand-written manuscript, so V was<tei:lb/>set instead of '#'.</tei:p>
         <tei:p>* Although Michaelsen (1900: 366) indicated that he was correcting the spelling, Beddard's action took priority since his<tei:lb/>paper was published in the February of 1900 whereas Michaelsen's monograph was not published until the October.</tei:p>
